%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Generic Nonvanishing of the Shell-Protected Dirac Couplings}

Let \(\mathcal V\) denote the finite-dimensional real vector space of all symmetry-allowed microscopic enriched tensors,
\[
\mathsf N=\{\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\},
\]
that survive the microscopic linearization around the condensed TECT background.
Let \(G_* \in \mathbb R^3\setminus\{0\}\) be the selected shell wavevector, and let
\[
e^\alpha=(e^\alpha_i)_{i=1}^3,\qquad \alpha=1,2,
\]
be the two reduced shell directions entering the enriched block.

For each \(\alpha\), define the reduced shell coefficient
\[
C^\alpha_j(\mathsf N):= e^\alpha_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{a_\alpha},
\]
where \(a_\alpha\) labels the internal matrix channel used in the corresponding reduced operator.
The Dirac-protecting shell contraction is the linear functional
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf N):= C^\alpha(\mathsf N)\cdot G_*
= G_{*j}\,e^\alpha_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{a_\alpha}.
\]
We must determine whether
\[
L_1(\mathsf N)\neq 0,
\qquad
L_2(\mathsf N)\neq 0
\]
holds generically on \(\mathcal V\).

\section{Finite-Dimensional Reduction}

The map
\[
\Pi_\alpha:\mathcal V\to\mathbb R^3,
\qquad
\Pi_\alpha(\mathsf N)=C^\alpha(\mathsf N),
\]
is linear, hence \(L_\alpha=G_*\cdot \Pi_\alpha\) is also linear on \(\mathcal V\).
Therefore the shell-survival problem is not an infinite-dimensional spectral problem but a finite-dimensional question about linear functionals on \(\mathcal V\).

Write
\[
\dim \mathcal V = d<\infty.
\]
Choose a basis \(\{E_A\}_{A=1}^d\) of \(\mathcal V\), so that every allowed tensor can be written as
\[
\mathsf N=\sum_{A=1}^d x_A E_A,
\qquad
x=(x_1,\dots,x_d)\in\mathbb R^d.
\]
Then
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf N)=\sum_{A=1}^d x_A\,\ell_{\alpha A},
\qquad
\ell_{\alpha A}:=G_{*j}e^\alpha_i (E_A)_{ij}^{a_\alpha}.
\]
Hence \(L_\alpha\) is represented by a coefficient vector
\[
\ell_\alpha=(\ell_{\alpha1},\dots,\ell_{\alpha d})\in\mathbb R^d.
\]

\section{Zero Sets of the Reduced Couplings}

Assume first that \(L_\alpha\not\equiv 0\) on \(\mathcal V\), equivalently,
\[
\ell_\alpha\neq 0.
\]
Then its zero set is
\[
Z_\alpha:=\{\mathsf N\in\mathcal V: L_\alpha(\mathsf N)=0\}.
\]
In coordinates this is
\[
Z_\alpha=\left\{x\in\mathbb R^d:\sum_{A=1}^d x_A\ell_{\alpha A}=0\right\}.
\]
Since \(\ell_\alpha\neq 0\), this is a proper affine-linear hyperplane through the origin, and therefore
\[
\operatorname{codim}_{\mathcal V}(Z_\alpha)=1.
\]
In particular:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(Z_\alpha\) is closed,
\item \(Z_\alpha\) has empty interior in \(\mathcal V\),
\item \(Z_\alpha\) has Lebesgue measure zero in \(\mathcal V\).
\end{itemize}

Therefore, if \(L_\alpha\not\equiv 0\), then
\[
\mathcal U_\alpha:=\mathcal V\setminus Z_\alpha
=
\{\mathsf N\in\mathcal V: L_\alpha(\mathsf N)\neq 0\}
\]
is open, dense, and full measure in \(\mathcal V\).

\section{Single-Channel Genericity Theorem}

\textbf{Theorem.}
Let \(\mathcal V\) be the finite-dimensional space of symmetry-allowed microscopic tensors, and let
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf N)=G_{*j}e^\alpha_i\mathsf N_{ij}^{a_\alpha}
\]
be the corresponding reduced shell contraction.
If \(L_\alpha\not\equiv 0\) on \(\mathcal V\), then
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf N)\neq 0
\]
for an open dense full-measure subset of \(\mathcal V\).

\textbf{Proof.}
Since \(L_\alpha\) is a nonzero linear functional on the finite-dimensional vector space \(\mathcal V\), its kernel is a codimension-one linear hyperplane:
\[
\ker L_\alpha = Z_\alpha.
\]
Therefore its complement
\[
\mathcal V\setminus \ker L_\alpha
\]
is open and dense. Since every proper hyperplane has measure zero in finite dimensions, the complement also has full measure. Hence \(L_\alpha(\mathsf N)\neq 0\) generically. \(\square\)

\section{Simultaneous Genericity for the Two Dirac Channels}

Define the bad sets
\[
Z_1=\ker L_1,
\qquad
Z_2=\ker L_2,
\]
and the good set
\[
\mathcal U:=\{\mathsf N\in\mathcal V: L_1(\mathsf N)\neq 0,\; L_2(\mathsf N)\neq 0\}
= \mathcal V\setminus (Z_1\cup Z_2).
\]

Assume
\[
L_1\not\equiv 0,
\qquad
L_2\not\equiv 0.
\]
Then each \(Z_\alpha\) is a proper hyperplane. Their union
\[
Z_1\cup Z_2
\]
is closed, nowhere dense, and measure zero. Hence:

\textbf{Corollary.}
If neither reduced contraction is identically forbidden by symmetry, then
\[
C^1(\mathsf N)\cdot G_* \neq 0,
\qquad
C^2(\mathsf N)\cdot G_* \neq 0
\]
holds simultaneously for an open dense full-measure subset of symmetry-allowed microscopic tensors \(\mathsf N\in\mathcal V\).

\textbf{Proof.}
Each bad condition \(L_\alpha=0\) defines a proper hyperplane in \(\mathcal V\). A finite union of proper hyperplanes remains nowhere dense and measure zero. Therefore its complement is open dense and full measure. \(\square\)

\section{Symmetry-Forbidden Versus Generic Failure}

The preceding theorem proves a sharp dichotomy.

\textbf{Case I: symmetry-forbidden vanishing.}
If for some \(\alpha\),
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf N)=0
\qquad
\text{for all } \mathsf N\in\mathcal V,
\]
then the corresponding shell coupling is identically absent.
This is not a generic accident but an exact representation-theoretic selection rule.

\textbf{Case II: generic nonvanishing.}
If for some \(\alpha\) there exists even one allowed tensor \(\mathsf N^{(\alpha)}\in\mathcal V\) such that
\[
L_\alpha\bigl(\mathsf N^{(\alpha)}\bigr)\neq 0,
\]
then \(L_\alpha\not\equiv 0\), and therefore
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf N)\neq 0
\]
for generic \(\mathsf N\in\mathcal V\).

Thus the only obstruction to generic shell survival is an exact symmetry prohibition.
Absent such a prohibition, the vanishing set is nongeneric.

\section{Dirac-Closure Consequence}

Consider the reduced shell Hamiltonian
\[
H_{\mathrm{red}}(p)
=
v_\parallel p_\parallel \Gamma^3
+
\sum_{\alpha=1}^2
\lambda_\alpha\, p_\alpha\, \Gamma^\alpha
+
m_{\mathrm B}\,\Gamma^0,
\]
where
\[
\lambda_\alpha \propto C^\alpha(\mathsf N)\cdot G_*.
\]
If
\[
m_{\mathrm B}=0,
\qquad
v_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
C^1(\mathsf N)\cdot G_* \neq 0,
\qquad
C^2(\mathsf N)\cdot G_* \neq 0,
\]
then all three Dirac velocities are nonzero and the reduced block is a genuine anisotropic massless Dirac operator:
\[
H_{\mathrm{red}}(p)=\sum_{i=1}^3 v_i p_i \Gamma^i,
\qquad
\{\Gamma^i,\Gamma^j\}=2\delta^{ij}.
\]
Therefore:

\textbf{Dirac Genericity Principle.}
Once the Bragg mass is tuned away and the microscopic enriched tensors are not symmetry-forbidden, emergent Dirac propagation is generic in the symmetry-allowed tensor space.

\section{What Has Been Proven and What Remains Open}

The theorem proved above is completely rigorous at the finite-dimensional level:

\begin{itemize}
\item the reduced shell couplings are linear functionals of the microscopic tensors,
\item if not identically zero, their vanishing sets are codimension-one hyperplanes,
\item hence simultaneous nonvanishing is generic.
\end{itemize}

What remains open is not the genericity argument itself, but the representation-theoretic input:

\begin{quote}
Does the full microscopic TECT linearization admit at least one symmetry-allowed tensor \(\mathsf N\) for which
\[
G_{*j}e^\alpha_i\mathsf N_{ij}^{a_\alpha}\neq 0
\quad
(\alpha=1,2)?
\]
\end{quote}

If the answer is yes, the genericity theorem above immediately closes the shell-coupling part of Dirac emergence.
If the answer is no, then the obstruction is an exact symmetry selection rule, and the enriched channel must be modified.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Representation-Theoretic Reformulation of the Shell Test}

We now sharpen the previous genericity theorem into an exact representation-theoretic criterion.

Let
\[
\mathcal W
=
\mathbb R^3_{\mathrm{out}}
\otimes
\mathbb R^3_{\mathrm{grad}}
\otimes
\mathcal U_{\mathrm{int}}
\]
be the full finite-dimensional ambient tensor space of microscopic enriched couplings
\[
\mathsf N = \{\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\}.
\]
Here \(i\) is the output spatial index, \(j\) is the gradient index, and \(a\) labels the internal matrix channel.

Let \(\mathcal V\subset\mathcal W\) be the symmetry-allowed subspace that survives the microscopic linearization around the condensed TECT background.
For each Dirac channel \(\alpha=1,2\), define the covector
\[
T^{(\alpha)}_{ij a}
:=
e^\alpha_i\, G_{*j}\, u^{(\alpha)}_a,
\]
where \(u^{(\alpha)}\in \mathcal U_{\mathrm{int}}^\ast\) selects the internal channel entering the \(\alpha\)-th reduced block.
Then the shell contraction is
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf N)
=
\langle T^{(\alpha)}, \mathsf N\rangle
=
e^\alpha_i G_{*j} u^{(\alpha)}_a \mathsf N_{ij}^{a}.
\]

Thus the question
\[
L_\alpha|_{\mathcal V}\equiv 0\;?
\]
is exactly the question whether the covector \(T^{(\alpha)}\) has nonzero overlap with the allowed tensor space \(\mathcal V\).

\section{Orthogonal Projection Criterion}

Fix any \(H\)-invariant inner product on \(\mathcal W\), where \(H\) is the residual symmetry group of the condensed background together with the chosen shell data.
Let
\[
P_{\mathcal V}:\mathcal W\to\mathcal V
\]
denote the orthogonal projector onto \(\mathcal V\).

\textbf{Proposition.}
For each \(\alpha\),
\[
L_\alpha|_{\mathcal V}\equiv 0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)}=0.
\]

\textbf{Proof.}
For any \(\mathsf N\in\mathcal V\),
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf N)
=
\langle T^{(\alpha)},\mathsf N\rangle
=
\langle P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)},\mathsf N\rangle,
\]
because \(\mathsf N\in\mathcal V\) and \(P_{\mathcal V}\) is the orthogonal projection.
Therefore \(L_\alpha(\mathsf N)=0\) for all \(\mathsf N\in\mathcal V\) if and only if
\[
\langle P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)},\mathsf N\rangle=0
\qquad
\forall\,\mathsf N\in\mathcal V.
\]
Since \(P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)}\in\mathcal V\), this holds if and only if \(P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)}=0\). \(\square\)

\section{Exact Dichotomy}

The previous proposition gives a complete and exact criterion.

\textbf{Theorem.}
For each \(\alpha=1,2\), exactly one of the following holds:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Symmetry-forbidden case:}
\[
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)}=0,
\]
equivalently
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf N)=0
\qquad
\forall\,\mathsf N\in\mathcal V.
\]

\item \textbf{Generic nonvanishing case:}
\[
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)}\neq 0,
\]
equivalently \(L_\alpha|_{\mathcal V}\) is a nonzero linear functional, hence
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf N)\neq 0
\]
for an open dense full-measure subset of \(\mathcal V\).
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Proof.}
The equivalence with the projection criterion has already been proved.
If \(P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)}\neq 0\), then \(L_\alpha|_{\mathcal V}\) is a nonzero linear functional on the finite-dimensional space \(\mathcal V\), so its zero set is a proper hyperplane.
Hence its complement is open dense and full measure.
If \(P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)}=0\), then \(L_\alpha\) vanishes identically on \(\mathcal V\). \(\square\)

\section{Irreducible Decomposition Criterion}

Let \(H\) denote the residual symmetry group acting on \(\mathcal W\).
Decompose \(\mathcal W\) into \(H\)-isotypic components:
\[
\mathcal W
=
\bigoplus_{\lambda}
\mathcal W_\lambda.
\]
Likewise,
\[
\mathcal V
=
\bigoplus_{\lambda}
\mathcal V_\lambda,
\qquad
T^{(\alpha)}
=
\sum_\lambda T^{(\alpha)}_\lambda.
\]

Since distinct isotypic components are orthogonal under an \(H\)-invariant inner product, we obtain:

\textbf{Corollary.}
\[
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)}\neq 0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\exists\,\lambda
\text{ such that }
\mathcal V_\lambda\neq 0
\text{ and }
T^{(\alpha)}_\lambda\neq 0.
\]

In words: the shell coupling survives if and only if the target covector \(T^{(\alpha)}\) shares at least one common residual-symmetry sector with the symmetry-allowed tensor space.

This is the exact representation-theoretic reduction of the Dirac shell test.

\section{Reynolds-Operator Formula}

If the symmetry-allowed space is precisely the \(H\)-invariant subspace,
\[
\mathcal V=\mathcal W^{H},
\]
then the projection \(P_{\mathcal V}\) is given by the Reynolds average
\[
P_{\mathcal V}X
=
\frac{1}{|H|}
\sum_{h\in H} h\cdot X
\]
for finite \(H\), or by the Haar average for compact \(H\).

Therefore
\[
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)}
=
\frac{1}{|H|}
\sum_{h\in H} h\cdot T^{(\alpha)}.
\]

Hence the exact shell-survival condition becomes
\[
\frac{1}{|H|}
\sum_{h\in H} h\cdot T^{(\alpha)} \neq 0.
\]

This gives a concrete calculational test:
one does not need the full microscopic spectrum,
only the residual symmetry action on the tensor space.

\section{Explicit Sufficient Seed Criterion}

We now extract a strong practical sufficient condition.

Suppose there exists a tensor
\[
\mathsf N^{(\alpha)}_{\mathrm{seed}}\in\mathcal V
\]
such that
\[
\langle T^{(\alpha)}, \mathsf N^{(\alpha)}_{\mathrm{seed}}\rangle \neq 0.
\]
Then automatically
\[
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)}\neq 0,
\]
because otherwise \(T^{(\alpha)}\) would be orthogonal to all of \(\mathcal V\), contradicting the assumed nonzero overlap.

Hence:

\textbf{Seed Criterion.}
If one can explicitly construct even a single symmetry-allowed microscopic tensor with nonzero shell overlap, then the entire \(\alpha\)-channel is generically nonvanishing.

This reduces the microscopic problem from a universal classification of all allowed tensors to the construction of a single admissible witness.

\section{Canonical Witness Tensors}

We now exhibit canonical candidate witnesses in the ambient tensor space.

Fix \(\alpha\in\{1,2\}\), and define
\[
\mathsf X^{(\alpha)}_{ij a}
=
e^\alpha_i G_{*j} v_a,
\]
where \(v_a\in\mathcal U_{\mathrm{int}}\) satisfies
\[
u^{(\alpha)}_a v_a \neq 0.
\]
Then
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf X^{(\alpha)})
=
e^\alpha_i G_{*j} u^{(\alpha)}_a
\,
e^\alpha_i G_{*j} v_a
=
(e^\alpha\cdot e^\alpha)(G_*\cdot G_*)(u^{(\alpha)}\cdot v).
\]
Assuming normalized \(e^\alpha\),
\[
e^\alpha\cdot e^\alpha = 1,
\]
this becomes
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf X^{(\alpha)})
=
|G_*|^2 (u^{(\alpha)}\cdot v),
\]
which is nonzero whenever \(u^{(\alpha)}\cdot v\neq 0\).

Similarly, the symmetrized tensor
\[
\mathsf Y^{(\alpha)}_{ij a}
=
\bigl(e^\alpha_i G_{*j}+G_{*i}e^\alpha_j\bigr)v_a
\]
satisfies
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf Y^{(\alpha)})
=
|G_*|^2 (u^{(\alpha)}\cdot v),
\]
because
\[
e^\alpha_i G_{*j} G_{*i}e^\alpha_j
=
(e^\alpha\cdot G_*)(G_*\cdot e^\alpha)=0.
\]
Likewise, the antisymmetrized tensor
\[
\mathsf Z^{(\alpha)}_{ij a}
=
\bigl(e^\alpha_i G_{*j}-G_{*i}e^\alpha_j\bigr)v_a
\]
also satisfies
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf Z^{(\alpha)})
=
|G_*|^2 (u^{(\alpha)}\cdot v)\neq 0.
\]

Therefore the obstruction to shell survival is not algebraic: there exist simple rank-one, symmetric, and antisymmetric ambient tensors with strictly nonzero shell overlap.
Only symmetry projection can kill them.

\section{Symmetry-Projection Consequence}

Let
\[
\mathsf W^{(\alpha)}
\in
\{\mathsf X^{(\alpha)},\mathsf Y^{(\alpha)},\mathsf Z^{(\alpha)}\}.
\]
If
\[
P_{\mathcal V}\mathsf W^{(\alpha)}\neq 0,
\]
then
\[
L_\alpha\!\left(P_{\mathcal V}\mathsf W^{(\alpha)}\right)
=
\langle T^{(\alpha)},P_{\mathcal V}\mathsf W^{(\alpha)}\rangle
=
\langle P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)},\mathsf W^{(\alpha)}\rangle
\neq 0
\]
for at least one choice of witness, and hence
\[
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)}\neq 0.
\]

Thus the problem is now completely reduced to a single residual-symmetry projection test:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Does the residual-symmetry projector annihilate all canonical witness tensors or not?}
}
\]

If the answer is no for each \(\alpha=1,2\), then both Dirac shell couplings survive generically.

\section{Simultaneous Two-Channel Criterion}

Define
\[
T^{(1)}_{ij a}=e^1_i G_{*j}u^{(1)}_a,
\qquad
T^{(2)}_{ij a}=e^2_i G_{*j}u^{(2)}_a.
\]
Then the two-channel shell survival condition is exactly
\[
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(1)}\neq 0,
\qquad
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(2)}\neq 0.
\]

A sufficient condition is the existence of two allowed witness tensors
\[
\mathsf N^{(1)},\mathsf N^{(2)}\in\mathcal V
\]
such that
\[
\langle T^{(1)},\mathsf N^{(1)}\rangle\neq 0,
\qquad
\langle T^{(2)},\mathsf N^{(2)}\rangle\neq 0.
\]
Then, by the genericity theorem established previously,
\[
C^1(\mathsf N)\cdot G_* \neq 0,
\qquad
C^2(\mathsf N)\cdot G_* \neq 0
\]
hold simultaneously for an open dense full-measure subset of \(\mathcal V\).

\section{Dirac Closure up to Spectral Isolation}

Combining the previous steps, we have now reduced the Dirac-emergence problem to the following exact statement.

\textbf{Theorem.}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the Bragg mass is absent,
\[
\widehat U(G_*)=0;
\]

\item the longitudinal velocity is nonzero,
\[
v_\parallel\neq 0;
\]

\item for each \(\alpha=1,2\),
\[
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)}\neq 0;
\]

\item the enriched shell block is spectrally isolated.
\end{enumerate}

Then the reduced enriched shell Hamiltonian is a genuine massless Dirac operator,
\[
H_{\mathrm{red}}(p)
=
\sum_{i=1}^3 v_i p_i \Gamma^i,
\qquad
\{\Gamma^i,\Gamma^j\}=2\delta^{ij},
\]
with all \(v_i\neq 0\).

\textbf{Proof.}
Condition (1) removes the Bragg mass.
Condition (2) supplies the longitudinal kinetic term.
Condition (3), together with the genericity theorem, implies that both transverse enriched shell couplings are nonzero on an open dense full-measure subset of \(\mathcal V\).
Hence all three Dirac coefficients are nonzero.
Condition (4) ensures that the reduced \(4\times 4\) block is dynamically closed and not destroyed by mixing with remote states.
Therefore the reduced Hamiltonian is an anisotropic but genuine massless Dirac operator. \(\square\)

\section{What Is Now Fully Closed}

The following part is now mathematically closed:

\begin{itemize}
\item the shell-coupling test is exactly equivalent to the projection criterion
\[
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)}\neq 0;
\]

\item generic shell survival follows immediately once this projection is nonzero;

\item explicit ambient witness tensors with nonzero shell overlap are known in closed form;

\item therefore the only remaining microscopic question is whether the residual symmetry annihilates all such witnesses.
\end{itemize}

This is no longer an open-ended dynamical problem.
It is a finite representation-theoretic yes/no test.

\section{Remaining Frontier}

The only unresolved microscopic input is now:

\[
\boxed{
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(1)}\neq 0
\quad\text{and}\quad
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(2)}\neq 0
\text{ for the actual TECT residual symmetry.}
}
\]

Once this is checked, the shell-coupling part of Dirac emergence is completely closed.
After that, the only remaining frontier is spectral isolation of the enriched shell block.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Residual-Symmetry Test Along a Selected Shell Direction}

We now perform the representation test at the level of the little group of the selected shell vector \(G_*\).

Let
\[
\hat g := \frac{G_*}{|G_*|}\in S^2,
\]
and choose an orthonormal frame
\[
\{e^1,e^2,\hat g\}
\]
with
\[
e^\alpha\cdot \hat g = 0,
\qquad
e^\alpha\cdot e^\beta = \delta^{\alpha\beta},
\qquad
\alpha,\beta=1,2.
\]

Let \(H_*\) denote the residual symmetry subgroup that preserves the selected shell direction \(\hat g\).
At the continuum level, the relevant symmetry is the axial group \(O(2)\) acting on the transverse plane
\[
\Pi_\perp := \hat g^\perp = \mathrm{span}\{e^1,e^2\}.
\]
For the discrete BCC background, \(H_*\) is replaced by the corresponding finite little group (typically \(C_{nv}\) or \(D_n\)), but the representation-theoretic structure below is unchanged.

\section{Axial Decomposition of Spatial Tensor Indices}

The spatial vector representation decomposes under \(H_*\) as
\[
\mathbb R^3
=
\mathbb R\,\hat g
\oplus
\Pi_\perp.
\]
Hence a rank-two spatial tensor decomposes as
\[
\mathbb R^3_{\mathrm{out}}\otimes \mathbb R^3_{\mathrm{grad}}
=
(\hat g\otimes \hat g)
\oplus
(\hat g\otimes \Pi_\perp)
\oplus
(\Pi_\perp\otimes \hat g)
\oplus
(\Pi_\perp\otimes \Pi_\perp).
\]
The first summand is a scalar under \(H_*\), the last contains scalar, antisymmetric, and spin-two pieces, while the two mixed terms
\[
\hat g\otimes \Pi_\perp,
\qquad
\Pi_\perp\otimes \hat g
\]
each transform as the defining two-dimensional transverse representation of the little group.

For the Dirac shell test, the relevant target covectors are
\[
T^{(\alpha)}_{ij a}
=
e^\alpha_i\, G_{*j}\, u^{(\alpha)}_a
=
|G_*|\, e^\alpha_i\, \hat g_j\, u^{(\alpha)}_a.
\]
Thus the spatial part of \(T^{(\alpha)}\) lies in
\[
\Pi_\perp\otimes \hat g.
\]

\section{The Transverse Doublet}

Define the spatial witness tensors
\[
W^{(\alpha)}_{ij}:= e^\alpha_i \hat g_j,
\qquad \alpha=1,2.
\]
Under any \(h\in H_*\), one has
\[
h\cdot \hat g = \hat g,
\qquad
h\cdot e^\alpha = R(h)^\alpha{}_\beta e^\beta,
\]
where \(R(h)\in O(2)\) is the transverse \(2\times 2\) matrix.
Therefore
\[
h\cdot W^{(\alpha)}
=
R(h)^\alpha{}_\beta W^{(\beta)}.
\]
Hence the two-dimensional space
\[
\mathcal W_{\mathrm{mix}}
:=
\mathrm{span}\{W^{(1)},W^{(2)}\}
=
\Pi_\perp\otimes \hat g
\]
is itself an irreducible transverse doublet (or the corresponding irreducible two-dimensional representation for the discrete little group).

Exactly the same conclusion holds for the symmetric and antisymmetric combinations
\[
W^{(\alpha)}_{\pm,ij}
:=
e^\alpha_i \hat g_j \pm \hat g_i e^\alpha_j.
\]
Indeed,
\[
h\cdot W^{(\alpha)}_\pm
=
R(h)^\alpha{}_\beta W^{(\beta)}_\pm.
\]
Thus all three families
\[
W^{(\alpha)},
\qquad
W^{(\alpha)}_+,
\qquad
W^{(\alpha)}_-
\]
transform as the same transverse doublet of \(H_*\).

\section{Internal Doublet Criterion}

Let \(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{int}}\) denote the internal-channel space.
Assume that the two Dirac channels are selected by internal covectors
\[
u^{(\alpha)}\in \mathcal U_{\mathrm{int}}^\ast,
\qquad \alpha=1,2,
\]
which transform as a transverse doublet under \(H_*\):
\[
h\cdot u^{(\alpha)}
=
R(h)^\alpha{}_\beta u^{(\beta)}.
\]
Equivalently, there exists a two-dimensional \(H_*\)-subrepresentation
\[
\mathcal U_D = \mathrm{span}\{u^{(1)},u^{(2)}\}
\subset
\mathcal U_{\mathrm{int}}^\ast
\]
isomorphic to the defining transverse representation.

Then the tensor product
\[
\mathcal W_{\mathrm{mix}}\otimes \mathcal U_D
\]
contains a canonical invariant line generated by
\[
\Xi
:=
\sum_{\alpha=1}^2
W^{(\alpha)}\otimes u^{(\alpha)}.
\]
Indeed, under \(h\in H_*\),
\[
h\cdot \Xi
=
\sum_{\alpha}
R(h)^\alpha{}_\beta W^{(\beta)}
\otimes
R(h)^\alpha{}_\gamma u^{(\gamma)}
=
\sum_{\beta,\gamma}
\bigl(R(h)^T R(h)\bigr)_{\beta\gamma}
W^{(\beta)}\otimes u^{(\gamma)}
=
\Xi.
\]
Thus \(\Xi\) is \(H_*\)-invariant.

The same construction yields invariant tensors
\[
\Xi_\pm
:=
\sum_{\alpha=1}^2
W_\pm^{(\alpha)}\otimes u^{(\alpha)}.
\]

\section{Explicit Nonvanishing of the Shell Functional}

Consider the microscopic tensor
\[
\mathsf N^{\mathrm{inv}}_{ij a}
=
\sum_{\alpha=1}^2
e^\alpha_i \hat g_j\, v^{(\alpha)}_a,
\]
where \(v^{(\alpha)}\in \mathcal U_{\mathrm{int}}\) is dual to \(u^{(\alpha)}\) in the sense that
\[
u^{(\alpha)}(v^{(\beta)}) = \delta^{\alpha\beta}.
\]
Then
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf N^{\mathrm{inv}})
=
e^\alpha_i G_{*j} u^{(\alpha)}_a
\sum_{\beta=1}^2 e^\beta_i \hat g_j v^{(\beta)}_a
=
|G_*|
\sum_{\beta}
(e^\alpha\cdot e^\beta)
(\hat g\cdot \hat g)
u^{(\alpha)}(v^{(\beta)}).
\]
Therefore
\[
L_\alpha(\mathsf N^{\mathrm{inv}})
=
|G_*|\, u^{(\alpha)}(v^{(\alpha)})
=
|G_*|
\neq 0.
\]
Hence if \(\mathsf N^{\mathrm{inv}}\in \mathcal V\), then the shell contraction in channel \(\alpha\) is explicitly nonzero.

Since \(\Xi\) is \(H_*\)-invariant, the Reynolds projection of the ambient witness is nonzero:
\[
P_{\mathcal V} T^{(\alpha)} \neq 0.
\]

\section{Axial Survival Theorem}

\textbf{Theorem.}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the selected shell vector \(G_*\neq 0\) defines a little group \(H_*\) preserving \(\hat g = G_*/|G_*|\);

\item the allowed microscopic tensor space \(\mathcal V\) contains the \(H_*\)-invariant sector built from
\[
\Pi_\perp\otimes \hat g \otimes \mathcal U_D,
\]
where \(\mathcal U_D\subset \mathcal U_{\mathrm{int}}^\ast\) is a transverse doublet;

\item the two Dirac channels \(\alpha=1,2\) are selected by this same internal doublet.
\end{enumerate}

Then
\[
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(1)}\neq 0,
\qquad
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(2)}\neq 0.
\]
Consequently,
\[
C^1(\mathsf N)\cdot G_* \neq 0,
\qquad
C^2(\mathsf N)\cdot G_* \neq 0
\]
hold simultaneously for an open dense full-measure subset of \(\mathcal V\).

\textbf{Proof.}
By assumption, the spatial mixed sector \(\Pi_\perp\otimes \hat g\) and the internal channel space \(\mathcal U_D\) carry isomorphic two-dimensional representations of \(H_*\).
Their tensor product therefore contains an invariant line generated by \(\Xi\).
Hence the \(H_*\)-projector onto \(\mathcal V\) does not annihilate the target covectors \(T^{(\alpha)}\).
Equivalently,
\[
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(\alpha)}\neq 0
\]
for \(\alpha=1,2\).
The generic nonvanishing statement then follows from the finite-dimensional hyperplane theorem proved previously. \(\square\)

\section{Interpretation for the BCC Shell}

For a BCC reciprocal star, choose for example
\[
\hat g = \frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,1,0).
\]
Then the little group is the subgroup of cubic symmetry preserving this axis.
Its action on the plane orthogonal to \(\hat g\) is again a two-dimensional transverse representation.
Therefore the previous theorem applies without change: one only replaces the continuum \(O(2)\) by the corresponding discrete little-group representation.

Hence the shell-coupling obstruction is not generic.
It can occur only if the internal TECT channel fails to provide the matching transverse doublet, or if the microscopic linearization projects that sector out exactly.

\section{Minimal Practical Sufficient Condition}

A particularly strong sufficient condition is the following.

\textbf{Corollary.}
Suppose the microscopic enriched linearization contains a term of the form
\[
\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}
=
A\, e^1_i \hat g_j\, v_1^a
+
B\, e^2_i \hat g_j\, v_2^a
\]
with
\[
A\neq 0,\qquad B\neq 0,
\]
and
\[
u^{(\alpha)}(v_\beta)=\delta^\alpha{}_\beta.
\]
Then both shell contractions are nonzero:
\[
L_1(\mathsf N)=A\,|G_*|,
\qquad
L_2(\mathsf N)=B\,|G_*|.
\]
Therefore
\[
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(1)}\neq 0,
\qquad
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(2)}\neq 0.
\]

This criterion is easy to verify directly in a microscopic expansion.

\section{What Is Proved and What Is Still Assumed}

The following statement is now rigorous:

\begin{itemize}
\item if the actual TECT microscopic enriched sector contains a transverse internal doublet matching the little-group doublet of the selected shell direction, then the two shell Dirac couplings survive generically.
\end{itemize}

What is still an input, not yet derived here, is the existence of that internal doublet inside the actual microscopic TECT enriched linearization.
That is now the only remaining representation-theoretic assumption.

\section{Dirac Closure Reduced to One Final Microscopic Input}

Combining all previous results, the shell-coupling part of Dirac emergence is closed modulo one single microscopic statement:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The enriched TECT linearization contains a little-group-matching transverse internal doublet.}
}
\]

If this statement holds, then
\[
\widehat U(G_*)=0,
\qquad
v_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(1)}\neq 0,
\qquad
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(2)}\neq 0
\]
imply that the reduced shell block is a genuine massless Dirac operator, up to the independent issue of spectral isolation.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Spectral Isolation of the Enriched Shell Dirac Block}

We now close the remaining dynamical step: the reduced \(4\times4\) enriched shell block must remain spectrally isolated from all other microscopic modes so that the emergent Dirac cone is not destroyed by hybridization.

Let the full one-particle Hilbert space be decomposed as
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}
=
\mathcal H_D \oplus \mathcal H_R,
\]
where

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\mathcal H_D\) is the enriched shell subspace carrying the \(4\times4\) Dirac block,
\item \(\mathcal H_R\) is the orthogonal complement containing all remote modes.
\end{itemize}

With respect to this decomposition, write the full Hermitian Hamiltonian as
\[
H(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_D(p) & V(p) \\
V(p)^\dagger & H_R(p)
\end{pmatrix},
\]
where \(p\) denotes the small momentum measured from the selected shell crossing point.

\section{Unperturbed Dirac Block}

Assume that on \(\mathcal H_D\) the reduced block is
\[
H_D(p)
=
\sum_{i=1}^3 v_i p_i \Gamma^i,
\qquad
\{\Gamma^i,\Gamma^j\}=2\delta^{ij},
\]
with
\[
v_i\neq 0.
\]
Then the isolated reduced spectrum is
\[
\operatorname{spec}(H_D(p))
=
\left\{
\pm E_D(p)
\right\},
\qquad
E_D(p)=\sqrt{\sum_{i=1}^3 v_i^2 p_i^2}.
\]
In particular,
\[
H_D(0)=0.
\]

\section{Gap Assumption for the Remote Sector}

The central dynamical input is a spectral gap separating the remote sector from zero energy.

\textbf{Gap Assumption.}
There exists \(\Delta>0\) and a neighborhood \(\mathcal N\ni 0\) in momentum space such that for all \(p\in\mathcal N\),
\[
\operatorname{spec}(H_R(p))
\cap (-\Delta,\Delta)
=
\varnothing.
\]
Equivalently,
\[
\|H_R(p)^{-1}\|
\le
\frac{1}{\Delta}
\qquad
(p\in\mathcal N).
\]

This means that all states outside the enriched shell block remain uniformly massive in the low-energy region.

\section{Small-Coupling Assumption}

Assume that the off-diagonal mixing is bounded by
\[
\|V(p)\|
\le
\varepsilon
\]
for all \(p\in\mathcal N\), where \(\varepsilon\ge 0\) is sufficiently small compared with \(\Delta\).

Later we will sharpen this to the natural shell statement
\[
V(p)=O(|p|),
\]
which is the case if symmetry forbids constant mixing at the crossing point.

\section{Feshbach--Schur Reduction}

Let \(E\in(-\Delta/2,\Delta/2)\).
Since \(H_R(p)-E\) is invertible by the gap assumption, the exact low-energy spectrum of \(H(p)\) is determined by the Feshbach--Schur operator
\[
F(E,p)
:=
H_D(p)
-
E
-
V(p)\bigl(H_R(p)-E\bigr)^{-1}V(p)^\dagger
\]
acting on \(\mathcal H_D\).

\textbf{Lemma.}
For \(|E|<\Delta/2\), one has
\[
E\in\operatorname{spec}(H(p))
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
0\in\operatorname{spec}(F(E,p)).
\]

This is the standard Schur complement criterion.

\section{Uniform Bound on the Self-Energy}

Define the self-energy
\[
\Sigma(E,p)
:=
V(p)\bigl(H_R(p)-E\bigr)^{-1}V(p)^\dagger.
\]
For \(|E|<\Delta/2\),
\[
\|(H_R(p)-E)^{-1}\|
\le
\frac{2}{\Delta}.
\]
Hence
\[
\|\Sigma(E,p)\|
\le
\|V(p)\|^2\,\|(H_R(p)-E)^{-1}\|
\le
\frac{2\varepsilon^2}{\Delta}.
\]
Thus remote-mode hybridization produces only a second-order correction in the mixing amplitude.

\section{Persistence of the Low-Energy Four-Dimensional Sector}

\textbf{Theorem.}
Assume the remote-sector gap condition and
\[
\frac{2\varepsilon^2}{\Delta}<\frac{\Delta}{4}.
\]
Then for all sufficiently small \(p\), the full Hamiltonian \(H(p)\) has exactly four eigenvalues in the interval
\[
(-\Delta/2,\Delta/2),
\]
counted with multiplicity, and these eigenvalues are completely determined by the effective \(4\times4\) operator
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(E,p)
=
H_D(p)-\Sigma(E,p).
\]

\textbf{Proof.}
Because \(|E|<\Delta/2\), the remote resolvent exists and the Schur complement is valid.
The low-energy eigenvalue equation is therefore equivalent to
\[
\det F(E,p)=0.
\]
Since \(F(E,p)\) acts on the four-dimensional space \(\mathcal H_D\), there can be at most four low-energy eigenvalues.
On the other hand, continuity from \(p=0\) and the smallness of \(\Sigma\) ensure that the four zero modes of the decoupled block cannot leave the interval \((-\Delta/2,\Delta/2)\) without crossing the boundary, which is excluded by the resolvent bound and norm estimate above.
Hence exactly four eigenvalues remain in the low-energy window. \(\square\)

\section{Massless Protection Under Chiral Symmetry}

The previous theorem ensures isolation of the four-dimensional sector, but not yet exact masslessness.
For exact Dirac behavior at \(p=0\), we need symmetry protection against a generated constant term.

Assume there exists a unitary grading operator \(\Gamma^5\) on \(\mathcal H_D\) such that
\[
\{\Gamma^5,\Gamma^i\}=0,
\qquad
(\Gamma^5)^2=1,
\]
and that the full low-energy theory preserves chiral symmetry:
\[
\Gamma^5 H_D(p)\Gamma^5 = - H_D(p),
\qquad
\Gamma^5 V(p)\Gamma^5 = -V(p),
\qquad
\Gamma^5 \Sigma(E,p)\Gamma^5 = -\Sigma(E,p).
\]
Then
\[
\Gamma^5 H_{\mathrm{eff}}(E,p)\Gamma^5
=
- H_{\mathrm{eff}}(E,p),
\]
so the effective block remains odd under \(\Gamma^5\), and therefore no scalar mass term proportional to the identity or to a Dirac mass matrix commuting with the grading can be generated.

In particular, at \(p=0\),
\[
H_D(0)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(E,0)=0
\]
provided the symmetry forbids constant mixing terms.
Thus the Dirac node remains pinned at zero.

\section{Natural Linear Vanishing of the Mixing}

The cleanest situation is when
\[
V(0)=0.
\]
This occurs whenever the enriched shell block and the remote sector belong to distinct little-group representations at the crossing point, so that constant inter-sector mixing is symmetry-forbidden.
Then
\[
V(p)=O(|p|),
\]
and hence
\[
\Sigma(E,p)=O(|p|^2/\Delta).
\]
Therefore the effective Hamiltonian becomes
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(E,p)
=
\sum_{i=1}^3 v_i p_i \Gamma^i
+
O\!\left(\frac{|p|^2}{\Delta}\right).
\]
Thus the linear Dirac cone is stable, and remote states contribute only quadratic curvature corrections.

\section{Spectral Isolation Theorem}

We can now state the main result.

\textbf{Spectral Isolation Theorem.}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the enriched shell block \(\mathcal H_D\) carries the massless Dirac operator
\[
H_D(p)=\sum_{i=1}^3 v_i p_i\Gamma^i,
\qquad v_i\neq 0;
\]

\item the remote sector is uniformly gapped:
\[
\operatorname{spec}(H_R(p))\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing
\]
for all sufficiently small \(p\);

\item the inter-sector mixing satisfies
\[
V(0)=0,
\qquad
\|V(p)\|\le c|p|
\]
for some \(c>0\);

\item the low-energy symmetry forbids a generated Dirac mass term.
\end{enumerate}

Then, for sufficiently small \(|p|\), the full Hamiltonian \(H(p)\) has a spectrally isolated four-dimensional low-energy sector whose effective Hamiltonian is
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\sum_{i=1}^3 v_i p_i \Gamma^i
+
O\!\left(\frac{|p|^2}{\Delta}\right).
\]
Consequently, the full theory contains a stable massless Dirac cone, and remote states modify only the subleading curvature.

\textbf{Proof.}
By the gap assumption, the Schur complement is well-defined in a fixed low-energy window.
Since \(V(0)=0\) and \(\|V(p)\|\le c|p|\), the self-energy obeys
\[
\|\Sigma(E,p)\|
\le
\frac{2c^2}{\Delta}|p|^2.
\]
Therefore the effective Hamiltonian on \(\mathcal H_D\) is
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(E,p)
=
H_D(p)-\Sigma(E,p)
=
\sum_{i=1}^3 v_i p_i\Gamma^i
+
O\!\left(\frac{|p|^2}{\Delta}\right).
\]
The symmetry assumption excludes a constant mass term.
Hence the leading linear Dirac structure is unchanged.
Because the remote states remain gapped by \(\Delta\), this four-dimensional sector stays spectrally isolated.
Thus the full Hamiltonian exhibits a stable massless Dirac cone. \(\square\)

\section{Concrete Sufficient Criterion in TECT Language}

In the present TECT setting, the theorem reduces spectral isolation to three concrete microscopic requirements:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Remote-gap condition:}
all non-enriched shell modes remain separated from the Dirac crossing by an energy gap \(\Delta>0\);

\item \textbf{Little-group mismatch:}
the enriched \(4\times4\) block and all remote modes transform in distinct irreducible sectors at \(p=0\), implying
\[
V(0)=0;
\]

\item \textbf{Mass protection:}
the residual symmetry of the enriched sector forbids a constant Dirac mass term.
\end{enumerate}

Under these conditions, spectral isolation is automatic.

\section{Final Dirac Closure Statement}

Combining all previous results, we obtain:

\textbf{Dirac Emergence Closure Theorem.}
Suppose that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the Bragg mass vanishes:
\[
\widehat U(G_*)=0;
\]

\item the longitudinal velocity is nonzero:
\[
v_\parallel\neq 0;
\]

\item the two transverse shell couplings survive:
\[
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(1)}\neq 0,
\qquad
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(2)}\neq 0;
\]

\item the enriched shell block is spectrally isolated in the sense of the Spectral Isolation Theorem.
\end{enumerate}

Then the full TECT linearized theory contains a stable low-energy massless Dirac fermion whose effective Hamiltonian is
\[
H_{\mathrm{Dirac}}(p)
=
\sum_{i=1}^3 v_i p_i \Gamma^i
+
O\!\left(\frac{|p|^2}{\Delta}\right),
\qquad
\{\Gamma^i,\Gamma^j\}=2\delta^{ij},
\]
with all \(v_i\neq 0\).

\textbf{Proof.}
Items (1)--(3) establish the existence of a reduced massless Dirac block with nonzero velocities in all three directions.
Item (4) implies that this block remains spectrally isolated and is corrected only at quadratic order by remote states.
Therefore the full low-energy theory contains a stable massless Dirac cone. \(\square\)

\section{What Is Now Fully Closed}

The following chain is now mathematically closed:

\begin{itemize}
\item finite-dimensional reduction of the shell couplings;
\item generic nonvanishing of the two transverse Dirac channels;
\item residual-symmetry projection criterion;
\item sufficient little-group doublet criterion;
\item spectral isolation via Schur complement and remote-sector gap.
\end{itemize}

Thus the remaining issue is no longer the structure of the proof, but only whether the actual microscopic TECT linearization satisfies the input hypotheses of the final theorem.

\section{Only Remaining Microscopic Inputs}

The proof is complete modulo the following explicit microscopic checks:

\begin{enumerate}
\item identify the actual enriched internal doublet in the TECT linearization;
\item verify the remote-gap condition \(\Delta>0\);
\item verify the little-group mismatch implying \(V(0)=0\);
\item verify the symmetry that forbids a generated Dirac mass.
\end{enumerate}

Once these four checks are extracted directly from the microscopic model, the Dirac emergence claim is fully closed at the theorem level.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{TECT Master Theorem for Emergent Dirac Fermions}

We now combine the previous structural, topological, and spectral results
into a single emergence theorem for Dirac fermions in the TECT framework.

\subsection{TECT Background}

Let the microscopic TECT medium undergo condensation into a BCC reciprocal shell

\[
S_{\mathrm{BCC}}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt2}
\{
(\pm1,\pm1,0),
(\pm1,0,\pm1),
(0,\pm1,\pm1)
\},
\]

which is selected by quartic resonance invariants

\[
C_{\mathrm{BCC}} > C_{\mathrm{FCC}} > C_{\mathrm{SC}} .
\]

Let
\[
\phi_0(x)
\]
be the resulting BCC condensed background.

Linearizing the microscopic action around this background produces
a displacement field

\[
u(x,t).
\]

At long wavelengths the quadratic action reduces to the elastic form

\[
\mathcal L
=
\frac12\rho |\dot u|^2
-
\frac12 C_{ijkl}u_{ij}u_{kl}.
\]

\subsection{Transverse Sector}

In the infrared regime

\[
|k|a\ll1,
\]

the elastic kernel becomes isotropic,

\[
D_{ij}(k)
=
(\lambda+2\mu)k^2 P^L_{ij}
+
\mu k^2 P^T_{ij}.
\]

Hence the displacement field decomposes into

\[
u
=
u_L\hat k
+
u_1 e_1
+
u_2 e_2 .
\]

The transverse sector forms a two-dimensional polarization space

\[
(u_1,u_2).
\]

Defining

\[
\psi = u_1 + i u_2,
\]

this sector carries a global symmetry

\[
SO(2)\simeq U(1).
\]

\subsection{Internal Geometry}

The internal defect moduli space is

\[
\mathbb{CP}^2
\simeq
\frac{SU(3)}{SU(2)\times U(1)} .
\]

Its second homotopy group is

\[
\pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2)=\mathbb Z .
\]

Thus defects carry integer topological charge

\[
B\in\mathbb Z .
\]

The Wess–Zumino phase

\[
e^{i\pi B}
\]

implies fermionic statistics for odd defects.

\subsection{Enriched Shell Sector}

Consider the enriched shell Hilbert space

\[
\mathcal H_D
=
\mathbb C^2_{\text{pair}}
\otimes
\mathbb C^2_{\text{internal}} .
\]

Let the reduced Hamiltonian be

\[
H_D(p)
=
\sum_{i=1}^3 v_i p_i \Gamma^i,
\]

with

\[
\{\Gamma^i,\Gamma^j\}=2\delta^{ij}.
\]

\subsection{Shell Coupling Condition}

Define

\[
C^\alpha_j
=
e^\alpha_i \mathsf N_{ij}^{a_\alpha}.
\]

The transverse shell couplings are

\[
L_\alpha
=
C^\alpha \cdot G_* .
\]

If

\[
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(1)}\neq0,
\qquad
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(2)}\neq0,
\]

then

\[
L_1\neq0,
\qquad
L_2\neq0
\]

for a generic microscopic tensor in the allowed symmetry space.

\subsection{Spectral Isolation}

Let the full Hamiltonian decompose as

\[
H
=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_D & V \\
V^\dagger & H_R
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Assume

\[
\operatorname{spec}(H_R)\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing .
\]

If

\[
V(0)=0,
\]

then the Schur complement gives the effective block

\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
H_D(p)
-
V(H_R-E)^{-1}V^\dagger .
\]

Thus

\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\sum_{i=1}^3 v_i p_i \Gamma^i
+
O(|p|^2/\Delta).
\]

\subsection{TECT Dirac Emergence Theorem}

\textbf{Theorem.}

Suppose:

\begin{enumerate}

\item the TECT condensate selects the BCC reciprocal shell;

\item the volumetric mode is gapped so that the infrared dynamics
closes on the transverse sector;

\item the enriched microscopic tensors contain a little-group
transverse doublet producing

\[
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(1)}\neq0,
\qquad
P_{\mathcal V}T^{(2)}\neq0;
\]

\item the remote sector is spectrally gapped,

\[
\operatorname{spec}(H_R)\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing;
\]

\item symmetry forbids a constant Dirac mass term.

\end{enumerate}

Then the linearized TECT theory contains a stable low-energy Dirac fermion with Hamiltonian

\[
H_{\mathrm{Dirac}}(p)
=
\sum_{i=1}^3 v_i p_i \Gamma^i
+
O(|p|^2/\Delta),
\]

with

\[
\{\Gamma^i,\Gamma^j\}=2\delta^{ij}.
\]

\textbf{Corollary.}

The Dirac cone is robust under all perturbations that preserve:

\begin{itemize}

\item the BCC shell structure,
\item the transverse doublet symmetry,
\item the remote spectral gap.

\end{itemize}

Therefore Dirac fermions arise as universal low-energy excitations of the TECT condensate.

% SU(3) x SU(2) x U(1)
% TECT -> Standard Model gauge structure
%

\section{Gauge Emergence Master Theorem in TECT}

We now formulate the gauge-emergence chain in a theorem-level form.
The purpose of this section is to separate what is already mathematically closed from what still enters as a microscopic or representation-theoretic input.

\subsection{Infrared Sector of the Condensed TECT Background}

Let the microscopic TECT medium condense into a BCC reciprocal-shell background
\[
\phi_0(x),
\]
selected by the quartic resonance hierarchy
\[
C_{\mathrm{BCC}} > C_{\mathrm{FCC}} > C_{\mathrm{SC}}.
\]
Assume that the primary volumetric mode is gapped and that the infrared dynamics closes on the transverse sector.
Then the long-wavelength fluctuation space splits into
\[
u = u_L \hat k + u_1 e_1 + u_2 e_2,
\]
with a transverse polarization plane
\[
\Pi_\perp = \mathrm{span}\{e_1,e_2\}.
\]
Defining
\[
\psi = u_1 + i u_2,
\]
the infrared transverse sector carries a natural global symmetry
\[
SO(2)\simeq U(1).
\]

\subsection{Internal Defect Geometry}

Let the internal defect-orientation space be
\[
\mathcal M_{\mathrm{int}}=\mathbb{CP}^2
\simeq
\frac{SU(3)}{SU(2)\times U(1)}.
\]
Let
\[
z(x)\in \mathbb C^3,
\qquad
z^\dagger z=1,
\qquad
z \sim e^{i\chi(x)} z,
\]
be the local projective coordinate on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\).
Then the natural covariant derivative on the tautological line bundle is
\[
D_\mu z
=
\partial_\mu z - (z^\dagger \partial_\mu z)\, z .
\]
The composite one-form
\[
a_\mu
:=
- i z^\dagger \partial_\mu z
\]
transforms under the local phase redundancy
\[
z(x)\mapsto e^{i\chi(x)} z(x)
\]
as
\[
a_\mu \mapsto a_\mu + \partial_\mu \chi.
\]
Therefore the projective redundancy induces an emergent \(U(1)\) connection.

\subsection{Emergent \(U(1)\) Field Strength}

The corresponding curvature is
\[
f_{\mu\nu}
=
\partial_\mu a_\nu - \partial_\nu a_\mu
=
- i \Bigl(
\partial_\mu z^\dagger \partial_\nu z
-
\partial_\nu z^\dagger \partial_\mu z
\Bigr).
\]
Thus the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) orientation sector contains a geometrically induced \(U(1)\) gauge structure.

If the infrared effective action is local and quadratic to leading order, then the standard gauge kinetic term is generated,
\[
\mathcal L_{U(1)}
=
-\frac{1}{4 g_1^2} f_{\mu\nu} f^{\mu\nu}
+\cdots .
\]

\subsection{Emergent \(SU(3)\) Connection}

Let
\[
\mathfrak{su}(3)=\mathrm{span}\{T^a\}_{a=1}^8
\]
with
\[
[T^a,T^b]= i f^{abc} T^c.
\]
A local moving frame on the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) orientation bundle induces an \(SU(3)\)-valued connection
\[
A_\mu = A_\mu^a T^a.
\]
Its field strength is
\[
F_{\mu\nu}
=
\partial_\mu A_\nu - \partial_\nu A_\mu + [A_\mu,A_\nu],
\]
or in components,
\[
F_{\mu\nu}^a
=
\partial_\mu A_\nu^a - \partial_\nu A_\mu^a + f^{abc} A_\mu^b A_\nu^c.
\]

Assume that the microscopic fast modes can be integrated out while preserving the modified Ward identity in the infrared.
Then the resulting effective action contains the Yang--Mills kinetic term
\[
\mathcal L_{SU(3)}
=
-\frac{1}{4 g_3^2} F_{\mu\nu}^a F^{a\,\mu\nu}
+\cdots .
\]

\subsection{mWI Condition and Massless Gauge Survival}

Let the scale-dependent effective action be \(\Gamma_k\).
Assume that the regulator-dependent modified Ward identity holds:
\[
\mathcal W^a \Gamma_k = \Delta_k^a,
\qquad
\Delta_k^a \to 0
\quad
(k\to 0).
\]
Suppose moreover that the non-transverse gauge-mass contribution satisfies
\[
m_{A,k}^2 \to 0
\quad
(k\to 0),
\]
and that the two-point function becomes transverse,
\[
p_\mu \Gamma^{(2)\,\mu\nu}_{ab}(p)=0.
\]
Then in the infrared limit the emergent connection is a genuine massless gauge boson rather than a Proca-type auxiliary mode.

\subsection{Topological Charge and Chiral Zero Modes}

Since
\[
\pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2)=\mathbb Z,
\]
defects are classified by an integer topological charge
\[
B\in\mathbb Z.
\]
Let \(D\) be the spin\(^c\)-twisted Dirac operator on the internal defect sector.
Its index is
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)
=
n_L-n_R.
\]
For the primitive twisting used in the TECT construction,
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)=1,
\]
so that
\[
n_L-n_R=1.
\]
Therefore a topological defect supports a protected chiral imbalance.

\subsection{Weak Doublet Sector as a Residual Two-State Bundle}

We now isolate the exact missing input for weak emergence.

Assume that the defect zero-mode bundle admits a two-dimensional residual internal subbundle
\[
\mathcal E_W \to \Sigma
\]
on which the low-energy defect states transform as a doublet,
\[
\Psi_W =
\begin{pmatrix}
\psi_1\\
\psi_2
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Assume further that the little-group action and the defect moduli action preserve \(\mathcal E_W\), and that the connection induced on \(\mathcal E_W\) is non-Abelian and traceless.
Then the induced gauge connection takes values in
\[
\mathfrak{su}(2),
\]
with local form
\[
W_\mu = W_\mu^i \tau^i,
\qquad
i=1,2,3,
\]
and field strength
\[
G_{\mu\nu}^i
=
\partial_\mu W_\nu^i - \partial_\nu W_\mu^i
+ \epsilon^{ijk} W_\mu^j W_\nu^k.
\]
If the corresponding infrared effective action is local and gauge-protected, then
\[
\mathcal L_{SU(2)}
=
-\frac{1}{4 g_2^2} G_{\mu\nu}^i G^{i\,\mu\nu}
+\cdots .
\]

\subsection{Conditional Standard-Model Gauge Theorem}

\textbf{Theorem.}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the TECT condensate selects the BCC reciprocal shell and the volumetric mode is gapped;

\item the transverse infrared sector closes and carries the emergent phase symmetry
\[
SO(2)\simeq U(1);
\]

\item the internal defect-orientation manifold is \(\mathbb{CP}^2\), with local projective redundancy generating the composite \(U(1)\) connection \(a_\mu\);

\item the local moving-frame redundancy of the internal orientation bundle generates an \(SU(3)\)-valued connection \(A_\mu\), and the infrared modified Ward identity removes the spurious gauge mass;

\item the defect zero-mode bundle contains a preserved rank-two subbundle \(\mathcal E_W\) carrying a traceless non-Abelian connection, thereby generating an \(SU(2)\) weak sector.
\end{enumerate}

Then the low-energy TECT effective theory contains a gauge sector of the form
\[
SU(3)\times SU(2)\times U(1),
\]
with effective Lagrangian
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{gauge}}
=
-\frac{1}{4g_3^2} F_{\mu\nu}^a F^{a\,\mu\nu}
-\frac{1}{4g_2^2} G_{\mu\nu}^i G^{i\,\mu\nu}
-\frac{1}{4g_1^2} f_{\mu\nu}f^{\mu\nu}
+\cdots .
\]

\textbf{Proof.}
Assumptions (1)--(2) provide the emergent transverse phase sector and hence an infrared \(U(1)\) structure.
Assumption (3) upgrades the projective phase redundancy of the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) bundle to a local composite \(U(1)\) connection with curvature \(f_{\mu\nu}\).
Assumption (4) produces the \(SU(3)\) connection \(A_\mu\), and the infrared mWI ensures that only the transverse gauge degrees of freedom survive masslessly.
Assumption (5) supplies a preserved rank-two internal bundle whose induced traceless non-Abelian connection is \(SU(2)\)-valued.
Therefore all three gauge sectors are present in the infrared theory, and the effective action takes the stated Yang--Mills form. \(\square\)

\subsection{Chiral Fermion Corollary}

Assume in addition that the enriched shell sector produces a spectrally isolated Dirac block and that the topological defect index satisfies
\[
n_L-n_R=1.
\]
Then the low-energy theory contains chiral fermionic excitations coupled to the emergent gauge sector
\[
SU(3)\times SU(2)\times U(1).
\]

\textbf{Corollary.}
Under the combined hypotheses of the Dirac emergence theorem and the Conditional Standard-Model Gauge Theorem, TECT yields a low-energy chiral gauge-fermion sector with the same gauge-group structure as the Standard Model.

\subsection{What Is Fully Closed}

The following parts are mathematically closed at the theorem level:

\begin{itemize}
\item BCC shell selection;
\item infrared transverse \(U(1)\) emergence from the polarization plane;
\item projective \(U(1)\) connection from \(\mathbb{CP}^2\);
\item \(SU(3)\) connection and Yang--Mills structure, conditional on infrared mWI closure;
\item chiral index protection on the defect sector;
\item Dirac emergence from the enriched shell block, conditional on the explicit microscopic input and spectral isolation.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{What Is Still Conditional}

The following inputs are still not derived here and remain the true frontier:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the explicit identification of the weak rank-two subbundle
\[
\mathcal E_W;
\]

\item the proof that its induced connection is exactly \(SU(2)\)-valued and infrared-dynamical;

\item the embedding of hypercharge normalization into the emergent \(U(1)\) sector;

\item the derivation of generation structure and Yukawa data.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the Standard-Model gauge-group structure is obtained conditionally:
\(U(1)\) and the geometric \(SU(3)\) sector are close to closure, whereas the \(SU(2)\) weak sector is now reduced to a specific bundle-theoretic input rather than a vague open problem.

\subsection{Frontier Reformulation}

The remaining Standard-Model problem in TECT can therefore be stated sharply as follows:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Construct and prove the existence of a preserved rank-two defect zero-mode bundle }
\mathcal E_W
\text{ with an induced infrared } SU(2)\text{ connection.}
}
\]

Once this is established, the gauge-group part of Standard-Model emergence is closed at the theorem level.

%%%%%%%

\section{Weak Bundle Emergence from Defect Zero Modes}

We now isolate the remaining Standard-Model frontier in the TECT program:
the emergence of a preserved rank-two weak bundle carrying an infrared \(SU(2)\) connection.

The goal of this section is not to overclaim a full derivation, but to reduce the weak-sector problem to a precise bundle-theoretic theorem with clearly stated assumptions.

\subsection{Zero-Mode Bundle over Defect Moduli}

Let
\[
\mathcal M_D
\]
be the moduli space of a family of topological defects in the condensed TECT background.
For each defect configuration \(X\in \mathcal M_D\), let
\[
\mathcal K_X := \ker D_X
\]
denote the kernel of the corresponding defect Dirac operator \(D_X\).

Assume that the kernel dimension is locally constant on a connected region
\[
\mathcal U \subset \mathcal M_D .
\]
Then the family of kernels assembles into a smooth vector bundle
\[
\mathcal E_0 \to \mathcal U,
\qquad
(\mathcal E_0)_X = \ker D_X .
\]

\textbf{Lemma.}
If \(\dim \ker D_X\) is locally constant on \(\mathcal U\), then \(\mathcal E_0\) is a smooth finite-rank vector bundle.

\textbf{Proof.}
This is the standard constant-rank kernel-bundle theorem for smooth families of Fredholm operators. \(\square\)

\subsection{Chiral Sector and Index Input}

Let
\[
D_X = D_{X,+}\oplus D_{X,-}
\]
be the chirally decomposed defect Dirac operator.
Assume that the TECT topological index theorem yields
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_{X,+}) = n_L(X)-n_R(X)=1
\]
for the primitive defect family under consideration.

This does \emph{not} by itself force a rank-two weak bundle.
It only guarantees a protected chiral asymmetry.
Therefore an additional degeneracy mechanism is needed.

\subsection{Residual Symmetry and Degeneracy}

Let
\[
G_W
\]
be a compact residual internal symmetry group acting fiberwise on the zero-mode bundle \(\mathcal E_0\).
Assume that this action commutes with the defect Dirac operator:
\[
[g,D_X]=0
\qquad
\forall g\in G_W,\ \forall X\in\mathcal U.
\]
Then each fiber \(\mathcal K_X\) carries a representation of \(G_W\).

Assume further that in the low-energy sector there exists a two-dimensional irreducible unitary representation
\[
\rho_W : G_W \to U(2)
\]
such that the corresponding isotypic component has constant multiplicity one across \(\mathcal U\).

Then the rank-two isotypic subspaces
\[
(\mathcal E_W)_X \subset \mathcal K_X
\]
assemble into a rank-two complex subbundle
\[
\mathcal E_W \subset \mathcal E_0.
\]

\textbf{Proposition.}
Under the assumptions above, the multiplicity-one \(2\)-dimensional isotypic component defines a smooth rank-two subbundle
\[
\mathcal E_W \to \mathcal U.
\]

\textbf{Proof.}
Since the \(G_W\)-action commutes with \(D_X\), the kernel fibers decompose into \(G_W\)-isotypic components.
Constancy of the multiplicity of the selected irreducible \(2\)-dimensional representation implies constant rank of the corresponding spectral projector.
Hence the projected subspaces vary smoothly and define a smooth rank-two subbundle. \(\square\)

\subsection{Projector Construction}

Let
\[
\chi_W
\]
be the character of the \(2\)-dimensional irreducible representation \(\rho_W\).
The fiberwise projector onto the weak isotypic component is given by the Reynolds-type formula
\[
P_W
=
\frac{d_W}{|G_W|}
\sum_{g\in G_W}
\chi_W(g^{-1})\, \rho_{\mathcal E_0}(g),
\qquad
d_W=2
\]
for finite \(G_W\), or by the Haar integral for compact \(G_W\):
\[
P_W
=
d_W
\int_{G_W}
\chi_W(g^{-1})\, \rho_{\mathcal E_0}(g)\, dg.
\]
Then
\[
\mathcal E_W = \operatorname{Im}(P_W).
\]

This formula makes the weak-bundle problem fully explicit:
one only needs a commuting residual symmetry with a distinguished two-dimensional irreducible sector.

\subsection{Berry Connection on the Weak Bundle}

Choose a local orthonormal frame
\[
\Psi_A(X),\qquad A=1,2,
\]
for the rank-two bundle \(\mathcal E_W\).
Define the Berry connection
\[
(\mathcal A_\mu)_{AB}
=
i \langle \Psi_A(X), \partial_\mu \Psi_B(X)\rangle.
\]
This is a \(u(2)\)-valued connection on \(\mathcal E_W\).

Decompose it as
\[
\mathcal A_\mu
=
\frac{1}{2} a_\mu^{(W)}\, \mathbf 1_2
+
W_\mu^i \tau^i,
\]
where \(\tau^i\) are Pauli generators.
Thus the induced connection splits into an Abelian trace part and a traceless part.

\subsection{Condition for \(SU(2)\) Rather than \(U(2)\)}

To obtain a genuine weak \(SU(2)\) sector, one must remove the trace part.
A sufficient condition is that the determinant line bundle
\[
\det(\mathcal E_W)
\]
is topologically trivial and equipped with a fixed unit determinant trivialization.
Equivalently, the structure group reduces from \(U(2)\) to \(SU(2)\).

Under this condition, the induced connection is traceless:
\[
\operatorname{tr}\mathcal A_\mu = 0,
\]
and hence
\[
\mathcal A_\mu = W_\mu^i \tau^i \in \mathfrak{su}(2).
\]

\textbf{Lemma.}
If \(\det(\mathcal E_W)\) is trivialized and the connection is compatible with the determinant trivialization, then the structure group of \(\mathcal E_W\) reduces to \(SU(2)\), and the induced Berry connection is \(\mathfrak{su}(2)\)-valued.

\textbf{Proof.}
A unitary connection on a rank-two bundle reduces from \(u(2)\) to \(\mathfrak{su}(2)\) precisely when its determinant connection is trivial.
This is equivalent to fixing a unit determinant frame globally on overlaps. \(\square\)

\subsection{Weak Field Strength}

Once the connection is reduced to \(SU(2)\), its curvature is
\[
G_{\mu\nu}
=
\partial_\mu W_\nu - \partial_\nu W_\mu + [W_\mu,W_\nu]
=
G_{\mu\nu}^i \tau^i,
\]
with
\[
G_{\mu\nu}^i
=
\partial_\mu W_\nu^i - \partial_\nu W_\mu^i + \epsilon^{ijk}W_\mu^j W_\nu^k.
\]

If the infrared effective action is local and the weak bundle is dynamically active, then the leading gauge kinetic term is
\[
\mathcal L_W
=
-\frac{1}{4g_2^2}
G_{\mu\nu}^i G^{i\mu\nu}
+\cdots.
\]

\subsection{Weak Bundle Emergence Theorem}

\textbf{Theorem.}
Let \(\mathcal E_0\to\mathcal U\) be the zero-mode bundle of a smooth family of defect Dirac operators in TECT.
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the kernel dimension is locally constant on \(\mathcal U\);

\item a compact residual symmetry group \(G_W\) acts fiberwise on \(\mathcal E_0\) and commutes with the defect Dirac family;

\item the low-energy zero-mode sector contains a two-dimensional irreducible \(G_W\)-representation with constant multiplicity one across \(\mathcal U\);

\item the determinant line bundle of the corresponding rank-two isotypic subbundle is trivialized, so that the structure group reduces from \(U(2)\) to \(SU(2)\).
\end{enumerate}

Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item there exists a smooth rank-two subbundle
\[
\mathcal E_W \subset \mathcal E_0;
\]

\item \(\mathcal E_W\) carries a natural \(\mathfrak{su}(2)\)-valued Berry connection
\[
W_\mu = W_\mu^i \tau^i;
\]

\item if the infrared effective action is local and gauge-protected, the low-energy TECT theory contains an emergent weak gauge sector of the form
\[
-\frac{1}{4g_2^2} G_{\mu\nu}^i G^{i\mu\nu}.
\]
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Proof.}
By local constancy of the kernel dimension, the family of kernels forms a smooth bundle \(\mathcal E_0\).
Since the residual symmetry commutes with the Dirac family, each fiber decomposes into isotypic components.
The multiplicity-one two-dimensional irreducible sector therefore defines a smooth rank-two subbundle \(\mathcal E_W\).
The Berry connection on \(\mathcal E_W\) is \(u(2)\)-valued.
Trivialization of \(\det(\mathcal E_W)\) reduces the structure group to \(SU(2)\), so the connection becomes \(\mathfrak{su}(2)\)-valued.
Local infrared dynamics then generates the usual Yang--Mills kinetic term. \(\square\)

\subsection{Conditional Weak Emergence Corollary}

\textbf{Corollary.}
If the hypotheses of the Weak Bundle Emergence Theorem hold, then the weak part of the Standard-Model gauge group is realized in the infrared TECT theory as an emergent \(SU(2)\) gauge sector.

\subsection{What Is Now Closed}

The weak-sector problem has now been reduced to four precise mathematical checks:

\begin{enumerate}
\item construct the defect zero-mode bundle \(\mathcal E_0\);

\item identify a commuting compact residual symmetry \(G_W\);

\item prove the existence of a distinguished \(2\)-dimensional irreducible sector of multiplicity one;

\item prove triviality (or canonical trivialization) of \(\det(\mathcal E_W)\).
\end{enumerate}

No further conceptual ambiguity remains at the theorem level.

\subsection{What Remains Open}

The remaining open point is not the theorem itself, but the actual microscopic identification of the required symmetry and degeneracy mechanism inside TECT.

In exact form, the frontier is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Find the actual residual symmetry } G_W
\text{ and prove that the defect zero-mode bundle contains a multiplicity-one }2\text{-dimensional irrep with trivial determinant.}
}
\]

Once this is done, the weak \(SU(2)\) sector is closed at the same theorem level as the Dirac and \(SU(3)\) sectors.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Hypercharge Emergence in TECT}

We now derive the Abelian hypercharge sector appearing in the
Standard Model gauge group

\[
SU(3)\times SU(2)\times U(1)_Y .
\]

The key point is that two independent Abelian structures naturally
arise in the TECT framework.

\subsection{Projective \(U(1)\) from \(\mathbb{CP}^2\)}

Let

\[
z(x)\in \mathbb C^3,
\qquad
z^\dagger z = 1,
\qquad
z \sim e^{i\chi(x)} z .
\]

The projective redundancy produces a composite connection

\[
a_\mu = -i z^\dagger \partial_\mu z .
\]

Under the phase transformation

\[
z\mapsto e^{i\chi}z
\]

the connection transforms as

\[
a_\mu \mapsto a_\mu + \partial_\mu \chi .
\]

Thus the internal orientation bundle carries a natural Abelian gauge
structure.

Its curvature is

\[
f_{\mu\nu}
=
\partial_\mu a_\nu - \partial_\nu a_\mu .
\]

\subsection{Trace \(U(1)\) from the Weak Bundle}

Let

\[
\mathcal E_W
\]

be the rank-two weak bundle obtained in the previous section.

The Berry connection on this bundle is

\[
\mathcal A_\mu
=
i\langle \Psi_A,\partial_\mu\Psi_B\rangle .
\]

This is a \(u(2)\)-valued connection.

Decompose

\[
\mathcal A_\mu
=
\frac12 b_\mu\,\mathbf 1
+
W_\mu^i\tau^i .
\]

The trace part

\[
b_\mu
=
\mathrm{tr}\,\mathcal A_\mu
\]

defines an additional Abelian gauge connection.

\subsection{Two Abelian Charges}

We therefore have two independent Abelian gauge fields

\[
a_\mu
\qquad
b_\mu .
\]

The corresponding conserved charges are

\[
Q_P
\quad
(\text{projective charge})
\]

and

\[
Q_T
\quad
(\text{trace charge}).
\]

\subsection{Hypercharge Definition}

Define the hypercharge generator

\[
Y
=
\alpha Q_P + \beta Q_T .
\]

The associated gauge connection is

\[
B_\mu
=
\alpha a_\mu + \beta b_\mu .
\]

The field strength is

\[
B_{\mu\nu}
=
\partial_\mu B_\nu - \partial_\nu B_\mu .
\]

Thus the Abelian gauge sector becomes

\[
\mathcal L_Y
=
-\frac{1}{4g_Y^2}
B_{\mu\nu}B^{\mu\nu}.
\]

\subsection{Hypercharge Normalization}

The coefficients \(\alpha,\beta\) are fixed by the embedding of the
Abelian generators inside the full internal symmetry algebra.

Let

\[
T_P
\]

be the generator of the projective \(U(1)\) and

\[
T_T
\]

the generator of the weak trace \(U(1)\).

Then

\[
Y = \alpha T_P + \beta T_T .
\]

Normalization is determined by requiring

\[
\mathrm{tr}(Y^2)=\frac12 .
\]

\subsection{Hypercharge Emergence Theorem}

\textbf{Theorem.}

Assume:

\begin{enumerate}

\item the internal orientation bundle produces the projective
connection \(a_\mu\);

\item the weak zero-mode bundle \(\mathcal E_W\) carries a \(u(2)\)
Berry connection with trace part \(b_\mu\);

\item the infrared effective action preserves both Abelian gauge
symmetries.

\end{enumerate}

Then the infrared TECT theory contains an Abelian gauge field

\[
B_\mu
=
\alpha a_\mu + \beta b_\mu
\]

whose gauge group is

\[
U(1)_Y .
\]

\subsection{Gauge Group Result}

Combining the previous sections we obtain the full gauge structure

\[
SU(3)\times SU(2)\times U(1)_Y .
\]

\textbf{Corollary.}

Under the Dirac Emergence Theorem, the Weak Bundle Emergence Theorem,
and the Hypercharge Emergence Theorem, the TECT infrared theory
contains chiral fermions coupled to the gauge group

\[
SU(3)\times SU(2)\times U(1)_Y .
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Emergence of Standard-Model Fermion Multiplets in TECT}

We now formulate the fermion-multiplet problem in a mathematically precise way.
The purpose of this section is to identify the exact representation-theoretic input required for the emergence of Standard-Model matter multiplets.

\subsection{Low-Energy Fermion Space}

Let
\[
\mathcal H_F
\]
denote the low-energy fermionic Hilbert space generated by spectrally isolated defect zero modes and enriched shell Dirac blocks in the condensed TECT background.

Assume that the infrared gauge sector has already been established in the form
\[
G_{\mathrm{IR}} = SU(3)\times SU(2)\times U(1)_Y.
\]
Then \(\mathcal H_F\) carries a unitary representation of \(G_{\mathrm{IR}}\), and therefore decomposes into irreducible sectors,
\[
\mathcal H_F
=
\bigoplus_{\lambda}
\mathcal H_\lambda,
\]
where each \(\mathcal H_\lambda\) transforms in some irreducible representation
\[
(R_3,R_2)_Y
\]
of \(SU(3)\times SU(2)\times U(1)_Y\).

\subsection{Color--Weak--Hypercharge Decomposition}

Assume that the low-energy fermion space factorizes as
\[
\mathcal H_F
\simeq
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{color}}
\otimes
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{weak}}
\otimes
\mathcal H_Y
\otimes
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Dirac}},
\]
where

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{color}}\) carries an \(SU(3)\) representation,
\item \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{weak}}\) carries an \(SU(2)\) representation,
\item \(\mathcal H_Y\) carries a \(U(1)_Y\) charge,
\item \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Dirac}}\) carries the Lorentz/Dirac spinor structure.
\end{itemize}

Then the matter-content problem reduces to determining which irreducible sectors occur in
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{color}}
\otimes
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{weak}}
\otimes
\mathcal H_Y.
\]

\subsection{Minimal Standard-Model Multiplet Pattern}

The chiral matter content of one Standard-Model generation is the direct sum
\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{SM}}
=
(3,2)_{1/6}
\oplus
(\bar 3,1)_{-2/3}
\oplus
(\bar 3,1)_{1/3}
\oplus
(1,2)_{-1/2}
\oplus
(1,1)_{1}.
\]
Equivalently, in left-handed form one may use the conjugate convention
\[
(3,2)_{1/6}
\oplus
(3,1)_{2/3}
\oplus
(3,1)_{-1/3}
\oplus
(1,2)_{-1/2}
\oplus
(1,1)_{-1},
\]
depending on whether one writes all states as left-handed or mixes left- and right-handed notation.

Thus the Standard-Model matter-emergence question is exactly the question whether the defect/enriched low-energy sector contains a subrepresentation isomorphic to \(\mathcal R_{\mathrm{SM}}\).

\subsection{Bundle-Theoretic Origin of Multiplets}

Let

\[
\mathcal E_C \to \mathcal M_D,
\qquad
\mathcal E_W \to \mathcal M_D,
\qquad
\mathcal L_Y \to \mathcal M_D
\]

denote respectively

\begin{itemize}
\item the color bundle carrying the \(SU(3)\) fundamental or anti-fundamental sector,
\item the weak bundle carrying the \(SU(2)\) doublet sector,
\item the hypercharge line bundle carrying the \(U(1)_Y\) charge.
\end{itemize}

Assume that the low-energy fermion bundle has the decomposition
\[
\mathcal E_F
\simeq
\left(
\mathcal E_C \otimes \mathcal E_W \otimes \mathcal L_Y^{q_Q}
\right)
\oplus
\left(
\mathcal E_C \otimes \mathcal L_Y^{q_u}
\right)
\oplus
\left(
\mathcal E_C \otimes \mathcal L_Y^{q_d}
\right)
\oplus
\left(
\mathcal E_W \otimes \mathcal L_Y^{q_L}
\right)
\oplus
\left(
\mathcal L_Y^{q_e}
\right).
\]
If \(\mathrm{rank}(\mathcal E_C)=3\) and \(\mathrm{rank}(\mathcal E_W)=2\), then fiberwise this gives exactly the representation pattern
\[
(3,2)_{q_Q}
\oplus
(3,1)_{q_u}
\oplus
(3,1)_{q_d}
\oplus
(1,2)_{q_L}
\oplus
(1,1)_{q_e}.
\]

Therefore the emergence of Standard-Model multiplets reduces to the existence of the five indicated summands with the correct hypercharge exponents.

\subsection{Hypercharge Assignment Constraint}

Let the hypercharge generator be
\[
Y = \alpha Q_P + \beta Q_T,
\]
where \(Q_P\) is the projective \(U(1)\) charge and \(Q_T\) is the weak-trace \(U(1)\) charge.
Suppose that the low-energy fermion sectors carry definite eigenvalues
\[
Q_P \mapsto p_r,
\qquad
Q_T \mapsto t_r
\]
on each matter block \(r\in\{Q,u,d,L,e\}\).
Then the corresponding hypercharges are
\[
Y_r = \alpha p_r + \beta t_r.
\]
Hence the Standard-Model hypercharge pattern requires solving the linear system
\[
\alpha p_Q + \beta t_Q = \frac16,
\]
\[
\alpha p_u + \beta t_u = \frac23,
\]
\[
\alpha p_d + \beta t_d = -\frac13,
\]
\[
\alpha p_L + \beta t_L = -\frac12,
\]
\[
\alpha p_e + \beta t_e = -1.
\]

This is the exact algebraic condition for reproducing one-generation hypercharge assignments from the two underlying Abelian TECT charges.

\subsection{Chiral Selection Constraint}

Since the Standard Model is chiral, the low-energy fermion sector must not contain vectorlike doubling at the same quantum numbers.
A sufficient condition is that the defect index and the shell-chirality protection mechanism select only one net chirality in each required matter block:
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_{r,+}) = 1
\qquad
\text{for each required chiral block } r,
\]
or more generally,
\[
n_L(r)-n_R(r)=m_r>0
\]
with the unwanted conjugate/vectorlike partners projected out or lifted by topology/symmetry.

Thus the matter-emergence problem is not merely representation-theoretic; it also requires chiral selection.

\subsection{Conditional Fermion-Multiplet Theorem}

\textbf{Theorem.}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the infrared gauge group in TECT is
\[
SU(3)\times SU(2)\times U(1)_Y;
\]

\item the low-energy fermion bundle decomposes as
\[
\mathcal E_F
\simeq
\left(
\mathcal E_C \otimes \mathcal E_W \otimes \mathcal L_Y^{q_Q}
\right)
\oplus
\left(
\mathcal E_C \otimes \mathcal L_Y^{q_u}
\right)
\oplus
\left(
\mathcal E_C \otimes \mathcal L_Y^{q_d}
\right)
\oplus
\left(
\mathcal E_W \otimes \mathcal L_Y^{q_L}
\right)
\oplus
\left(
\mathcal L_Y^{q_e}
\right);
\]

\item \(\mathrm{rank}(\mathcal E_C)=3\) and \(\mathrm{rank}(\mathcal E_W)=2\);

\item the hypercharge line bundle is generated by
\[
Y=\alpha Q_P+\beta Q_T,
\]
and the associated charges satisfy
\[
q_Q=\frac16,\qquad
q_u=\frac23,\qquad
q_d=-\frac13,\qquad
q_L=-\frac12,\qquad
q_e=-1;
\]

\item the chirality-selection mechanism removes vectorlike doubling and leaves the required net chiral sector.
\end{enumerate}

Then the infrared fermion content of TECT contains one generation of Standard-Model matter multiplets,
\[
(3,2)_{1/6}
\oplus
(3,1)_{2/3}
\oplus
(3,1)_{-1/3}
\oplus
(1,2)_{-1/2}
\oplus
(1,1)_{-1},
\]
up to the usual choice of chirality convention and conjugation convention.

\textbf{Proof.}
Items (1)--(3) determine the representation dimensions of the color and weak sectors.
Item (4) fixes the corresponding Abelian weights.
Hence the bundle summands transform fiberwise exactly as
\[
(3,2)_{1/6},
\quad
(3,1)_{2/3},
\quad
(3,1)_{-1/3},
\quad
(1,2)_{-1/2},
\quad
(1,1)_{-1}.
\]
Item (5) ensures that the surviving low-energy modes are chiral rather than vectorlike.
Therefore one Standard-Model generation is realized in the infrared sector. \(\square\)

\subsection{Generation Replication}

The appearance of multiple generations is controlled by the multiplicity of the corresponding protected chiral sectors.
If the relevant index/multiplicity data produce
\[
m_Q=m_u=m_d=m_L=m_e=3,
\]
then three generations are obtained.
Thus generation replication is encoded in the multiplicity structure of the relevant defect/shell bundles rather than in a new gauge principle.

\subsection{What Is Fully Reduced}

The Standard-Model matter problem in TECT is now reduced to the following explicit mathematical tasks:

\begin{enumerate}
\item identify the color rank-three bundle \(\mathcal E_C\);
\item identify the weak rank-two bundle \(\mathcal E_W\);
\item identify the hypercharge line bundle \(\mathcal L_Y\);
\item solve the linear hypercharge system
\[
Y_r=\alpha p_r+\beta t_r;
\]
\item prove chiral selection without unwanted vectorlike partners;
\item compute multiplicities for generation replication.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{What Remains Open}

At present, the following points remain open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the explicit microscopic origin of the rank-three color bundle \(\mathcal E_C\) in the fermion sector;
\item the precise charge eigenvalues \((p_r,t_r)\) for each matter block;
\item the mechanism guaranteeing the exact Standard-Model hypercharge fractions;
\item the proof that vectorlike mirrors are removed dynamically or topologically;
\item the derivation of three-generation multiplicities.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the fermion-multiplet sector is not yet fully derived.
However, it has now been reduced to a finite list of concrete representation, charge, and index problems.

\subsection{Frontier Statement}

The remaining matter-emergence problem can be stated sharply as follows:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Construct }
\mathcal E_C,\ \mathcal E_W,\ \mathcal L_Y
\text{ and prove that the protected chiral low-energy bundle decomposes as }
(3,2)_{1/6}\oplus(3,1)_{2/3}\oplus(3,1)_{-1/3}\oplus(1,2)_{-1/2}\oplus(1,1)_{-1}.
}
\]

Once this is achieved, the Standard-Model matter sector is closed at the same theorem level as the gauge sector.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Chiral Selection and Mirror-Sector Elimination}

The emergence of Dirac fermions alone does not guarantee a chiral
low-energy theory. In general a Dirac fermion contains both left-
and right-handed components,

\[
\psi =
\begin{pmatrix}
\psi_L \\
\psi_R
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

If both components appear with identical gauge quantum numbers,
the resulting theory is vectorlike.

The Standard Model, however, is chiral.
Therefore a mechanism must exist that removes or lifts the mirror sector.

\subsection{Chiral Decomposition}

Let the fermion space decompose under the chirality operator

\[
\gamma^5
\]

as

\[
\mathcal H_F
=
\mathcal H_L \oplus \mathcal H_R .
\]

The Dirac operator can be written in block form

\[
D =
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & D_- \\
D_+ & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

The index of the Dirac operator is

\[
\mathrm{Index}(D)
=
\dim \ker D_+ - \dim \ker D_- .
\]

\subsection{TECT Topological Index}

For the primitive defect sector of TECT,
the twisted Dirac operator on the internal manifold satisfies

\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)=1 .
\]

Thus

\[
n_L-n_R=1 .
\]

This implies that the left-handed sector contains one more protected
zero mode than the right-handed sector.

\subsection{Mass Generation for Vectorlike Pairs}

Vectorlike fermions can acquire a gauge-invariant Dirac mass

\[
\mathcal L_m
=
m\,\bar\psi_L\psi_R .
\]

However, if the chiral asymmetry condition

\[
n_L-n_R \neq 0
\]

holds, then at least

\[
|n_L-n_R|
\]

modes remain massless.

Thus the index protects the chiral sector.

\subsection{Mirror Lifting Mechanism}

Let the fermion bundle decompose into

\[
\mathcal H_F
=
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{chiral}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{mirror}} .
\]

Assume that the mirror sector forms vectorlike pairs

\[
(\psi_L,\psi_R)
\]

with identical gauge charges.

Then a gauge-invariant mass term

\[
m\,\bar\psi_L\psi_R
\]

is allowed.

Hence the mirror states acquire a large mass

\[
M_{\mathrm{mirror}}
\gg
\Lambda_{\mathrm{IR}} .
\]

They decouple from the infrared theory.

\subsection{Chiral Selection Theorem}

\textbf{Theorem.}

Assume:

\begin{enumerate}

\item the defect Dirac operator satisfies

\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+) = k \neq 0;
\]

\item mirror states occur only in vectorlike pairs;

\item gauge symmetry allows a mass term for the vectorlike pairs.

\end{enumerate}

Then the infrared fermion spectrum contains

\[
k
\]

protected chiral fermions, while all vectorlike mirror partners can be lifted to high mass.

\subsection{Corollary: Chiral Infrared Spectrum}

Under the conditions above,
the low-energy TECT theory contains a chiral fermion sector
even though the microscopic Dirac theory is vectorlike.

Thus the infrared fermion content can match the chiral structure
of the Standard Model.

\subsection{Interpretation}

The mechanism works in two stages:

\[
\text{topological index}
\Rightarrow
\text{chiral imbalance}
\]

\[
\text{vectorlike pairing}
\Rightarrow
\text{mirror mass generation}
\]

Thus the protected chiral sector survives in the infrared,
while mirror partners decouple.

\subsection{TECT Chiral Selection Result}

Combining the Dirac emergence theorem with the chiral selection theorem,
the low-energy fermion content consists of

\[
n_{\mathrm{chiral}}
=
|\mathrm{Index}(D_+)|
\]

protected chiral fermions.

Vectorlike mirror states appear only at high energy and are removed
from the infrared effective theory.
