\qquad
\beta=\frac12(T_{21}-T_{12}).
\]

Consequently,
\[
\det M
=
\lambda_\parallel \det T
=
\lambda_\parallel (\alpha^2+\beta^2)
\]
for the minimal axial equivariant ansatz \(T=\alpha I_2+\beta J_2\).

\textbf{Proof.}
The reduced linearized Hamiltonian is
\[
h_+^{\mathrm{lin}}(p)=P_*(p_i\mathcal K_i)P_*.
\]
Since \(h_+^{\mathrm{lin}}(p)\) acts on a two-dimensional complex Hilbert space, it has a unique Pauli decomposition
\[
h_+^{\mathrm{lin}}(p)=a_0(p)\mathbf 1 + a_\mu(p)\sigma^\mu,
\]
with coefficients extracted by the Pauli trace formulas.
Projecting the derivative operators onto the axial basis gives
\[
h_+^{\mathrm{lin}}(p)
=
p_\parallel \widetilde{\mathcal K}_\parallel
+
p_a \widetilde{\mathcal K}_a.
\]
The symmetry assumption removes the mixed channels, so only
\[
p_\parallel \sigma^3
\quad\text{and}\quad
p_a \sigma^b,\ a,b=1,2
\]
survive.
Therefore the coefficients are exactly the stated projector contractions.
Finally, if the transverse block is written as
\[
T=\alpha I_2+\beta J_2,
\]
then
\[
\det T=\alpha^2+\beta^2,
\]
so
\[
\det M=\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\]
\(\square\)

\subsection{Operational Corollary}

\textbf{Corollary.}
To verify local Weyl nondegeneracy in the actual TECT microscopic theory, it is sufficient to compute the three projected quantities
\[
\lambda_\parallel
=
\frac12 \mathrm{Tr}_{\mathcal H_*}\!\bigl(\sigma^3 P_*(\hat n_i\mathcal K_i)P_*\bigr),
\]
\[
\alpha
=
\frac14 \sum_{a=1}^2
\mathrm{Tr}_{\mathcal H_*}\!\bigl(\sigma^a P_*(e_{a i}\mathcal K_i)P_*\bigr),
\]
\[
\beta
=
\frac14
\left[
\mathrm{Tr}_{\mathcal H_*}\!\bigl(\sigma^2 P_*(e_{1 i}\mathcal K_i)P_*\bigr)
-
\mathrm{Tr}_{\mathcal H_*}\!\bigl(\sigma^1 P_*(e_{2 i}\mathcal K_i)P_*\bigr)
\right].
\]
If
\[
\lambda_\parallel \neq 0,
\qquad
(\alpha,\beta)\neq (0,0),
\]
then the selected BCC shell crossing is locally Weyl.

\subsection{Minimal PDE Ansatz}

A minimal microscopic PDE consistent with the previous extraction theorem is
\[
i\partial_t \Phi
=
\Bigl[
\mathcal L_*
+
c_\parallel (\hat n\cdot (-i\nabla))\,\Gamma^\parallel
+
c_I \, P^\perp_{ij}(-i\partial_i)\Gamma^j_\perp
+
c_J \, \varepsilon_{ijk}\hat n_k (-i\partial_i)\Gamma^j_\perp
\Bigr]\Phi
+
O(\nabla^2),
\]
where:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\Gamma^\parallel\) reduces to \(\sigma^3\) on the two-band subspace;
\item \(\Gamma^j_\perp\) reduce to \(\sigma^1,\sigma^2\) on the transverse sector;
\item \(c_\parallel,c_I,c_J\) are microscopic coefficients.
\end{itemize}

After projection to \(\mathcal H_*\), one obtains
\[
\lambda_\parallel \propto c_\parallel,
\qquad
\alpha \propto c_I,
\qquad
\beta \propto c_J.
\]
Thus the witness coefficients are nothing but the projected first-order axial, transverse-even, and transverse-odd derivative couplings.

\subsection{Honest Status of the Result}

What has now been closed rigorously is the following:

\begin{itemize}
\item the entire local Weyl problem has been reduced to three projected first-derivative coefficients;
\item these coefficients are extracted by explicit trace/projector formulas from the microscopic PDE;
\item for the minimal axial ansatz,
\[
\det M=\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\]
\end{itemize}

What is not yet done is the actual microscopic computation of \(\mathcal K_i\) from the full TECT condensate equation.

Thus the remaining open problem is no longer structural.
It is an explicit coefficient extraction calculation from the true microscopic TECT operator.

\subsection{Final Reduction of the Local Dirac Frontier}

The remaining local Dirac frontier is therefore exactly:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Write the microscopic TECT condensate PDE, compute }
\mathcal K_i=\partial_{k_i}\mathcal L(k)|_{k=G_*},
\text{ project with }P_*,
\text{ and evaluate }
\lambda_\parallel,\alpha,\beta.
}
\]

If
\[
\lambda_\parallel \neq 0,
\qquad
(\alpha,\beta)\neq (0,0),
\]
then the BCC shell crossing is locally Weyl, and opposite-valley doubling gives the Dirac fermion.

\section*{Summary}

Near a selected BCC shell vector \(G_*\), let the microscopic TECT condensate operator admit the first-order expansion
\[
\mathcal L(G_*+p)=\mathcal L_*+p_i\mathcal K_i+O(|p|^2).
\]
If \(P_*\) projects to a two-dimensional low-energy subspace, then the local Weyl coefficients are extracted by
\[
\lambda_\parallel
=
\frac12 \mathrm{Tr}_{\mathcal H_*}\!\bigl(\sigma^3 P_*(\hat n_i\mathcal K_i)P_*\bigr),
\]
\[
T_{ab}
=
\frac12 \mathrm{Tr}_{\mathcal H_*}\!\bigl(\sigma^b P_*(e_{a i}\mathcal K_i)P_*\bigr).
\]
For the minimal axial equivariant ansatz \(T=\alpha I_2+\beta J_2\), one has
\[
\alpha=\frac12(T_{11}+T_{22}),
\qquad
\beta=\frac12(T_{21}-T_{12}),
\qquad
\det M=\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\]

Therefore the local Weyl/Dirac problem in TECT is reduced to an explicit coefficient extraction calculation from the microscopic condensate PDE.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{First Explicit Extraction from a Minimal TECT Condensate PDE}

\subsection{Purpose}

The local Weyl/Dirac problem has already been reduced to the extraction of the three coefficients
\[
\lambda_\parallel,\qquad \alpha,\qquad \beta,
\]
from the first-order expansion of the microscopic condensate symbol near a selected BCC shell vector \(G_*\).

At the fully microscopic level, what remains open is the derivation of the actual TECT linearized operator
\[
\mathcal L(k),
\]
the proof of the shell-valley structure \(\pm G_*\), and the verification of nondegeneracy
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2 \neq 0.
\]
Thus the present section does \emph{not} claim a final first-principles derivation from the complete TECT PDE.
Instead, it gives the first explicit extraction calculation for a minimal symmetry-compatible candidate operator.

\subsection{Minimal Projected Shell Ansatz}

Let \(\phi_0(x)\) be the BCC condensate background, and let \(G_*\) be a selected first-shell reciprocal vector.
We work near the shell momentum
\[
k = G_* + p,
\qquad
p = p_\parallel \hat n + p_1 e_1 + p_2 e_2,
\qquad
\hat n := \frac{G_*}{|G_*|}.
\]

Motivated by the shell-level reduction theorem, consider the enriched shell space
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{enr}}
=
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}},
\]
with Pauli matrices \(\tau^i\) acting on the shell-pair factor and \(s^\alpha\) acting on the internal doublet.
The shell-level problem has already been reduced to gradient-linear couplings of the form
\[
\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\,
(\partial_i\phi_0)\,
(-i\partial_j)\otimes s^a,
\]
together with the reduced couplings
\[
\mathcal T^\alpha(x)=C^\alpha{}_j\,\partial_j\phi_0(x)\otimes s^\alpha.
\]

We now impose the smallest axial-equivariant first-order ansatz consistent with the previous reduction:
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{min}}(G_*+p)
=
E_*\,\mathbf 1_4
+
v_\parallel\, p_\parallel\, \tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
\nu_1\, p_1\, \tau^1\otimes s^1
+
\nu_2\, p_2\, \tau^1\otimes s^2
+
O(|p|^2).
\]
Equivalently, in the isotropic transverse basis one may write
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{min}}(G_*+p)
=
E_*\,\mathbf 1_4
+
v_\parallel\, (\hat n\cdot p)\,\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
\alpha\,
\bigl(
p_1\,\tau^1\otimes s^1 + p_2\,\tau^1\otimes s^2
\bigr)
+
\beta\,
\bigl(
-p_2\,\tau^1\otimes s^1 + p_1\,\tau^1\otimes s^2
\bigr)
+
O(|p|^2).
\]
This is the minimal shell-enriched axial ansatz.

\subsection{First Derivative Operators}

Define
\[
\mathcal K_i
:=
\partial_{k_i}\mathcal L_{\mathrm{min}}(k)\big|_{k=G_*}.
\]
Using
\[
p_\parallel = \hat n_i (k_i-G_{*i}),
\qquad
p_a = e_{a i}(k_i-G_{*i}),
\]
we obtain
\[
\mathcal K_i
=
v_\parallel\, \hat n_i\, \tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
\alpha\,
\Bigl(
e_{1i}\,\tau^1\otimes s^1
+
e_{2i}\,\tau^1\otimes s^2
\Bigr)
+
\beta\,
\Bigl(
- e_{2i}\,\tau^1\otimes s^1
+
e_{1i}\,\tau^1\otimes s^2
\Bigr).
\]
This is the explicit coefficient extraction at the operator level.

\subsection{Adapted Projected Derivatives}

Contracting with the axial basis gives:
\[
\mathcal K_\parallel := \hat n_i \mathcal K_i
=
v_\parallel\, \tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2,
\]
since
\[
\hat n_i e_{ai}=0.
\]
Likewise,
\[
\mathcal K_1 := e_{1i}\mathcal K_i
=
\alpha\, \tau^1\otimes s^1
+
\beta\, \tau^1\otimes s^2,
\]
and
\[
\mathcal K_2 := e_{2i}\mathcal K_i
=
-\beta\, \tau^1\otimes s^1
+
\alpha\, \tau^1\otimes s^2.
\]

Thus the projected first-order symbol is already in the canonical Weyl/Dirac template.

\subsection{Two-Band Valley Reduction}

Now restrict to a single valley block.
At one valley, the shell-pair Pauli operator \(\tau^3\) is diagonal, and the corresponding two-dimensional reduced subspace is obtained by fixing the appropriate shell-pair chirality.
Let \(P_+\) denote the projection to that valley subspace.
Then the reduced two-band Hamiltonian is
\[
h_+^{\mathrm{lin}}(p)
=
P_+\mathcal L_{\mathrm{min}}(G_*+p)P_+ - E_*\mathbf 1_2.
\]
Since the reduced Pauli basis identifies
\[
P_+(\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2)P_+ \mapsto \sigma^3,
\qquad
P_+(\tau^1\otimes s^1)P_+ \mapsto \sigma^1,
\qquad
P_+(\tau^1\otimes s^2)P_+ \mapsto \sigma^2,
\]
we obtain
\[
h_+^{\mathrm{lin}}(p)
=
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
\alpha (p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
\beta (-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2).
\]
Therefore the reduced transverse matrix is
\[
T
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha & -\beta\\
\beta & \alpha
\end{pmatrix},
\]
and hence
\[
\det T = \alpha^2+\beta^2.
\]

\subsection{Projector-Trace Extraction Formulas}

The general extraction theorem states that
\[
\lambda_\parallel
=
\frac12 \operatorname{Tr}_{\mathcal H_*}
\bigl(
\sigma^3 P_*(\hat n_i\mathcal K_i)P_*
\bigr),
\]
\[
T_{ab}
=
\frac12 \operatorname{Tr}_{\mathcal H_*}
\bigl(
\sigma^b P_*(e_{a i}\mathcal K_i)P_*
\bigr).
\]
For the present minimal ansatz, these formulas give directly
\[
\lambda_\parallel = v_\parallel,
\]
\[
T_{11}=\alpha,\qquad T_{12}=-\beta,
\qquad
T_{21}=\beta,\qquad T_{22}=\alpha.
\]
Therefore
\[
\alpha=\frac12(T_{11}+T_{22}),
\qquad
\beta=\frac12(T_{21}-T_{12}),
\]
as expected.

\subsection{Explicit Nondegeneracy Calculation}

The full linear coefficient matrix is
\[
M
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha & -\beta & 0\\
\beta & \alpha & 0\\
0 & 0 & v_\parallel
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Hence
\[
\det M
=
v_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\]
Therefore:

\begin{itemize}
\item if
\[
v_\parallel \neq 0,
\]
the longitudinal channel is active;

\item if
\[
(\alpha,\beta)\neq (0,0),
\]
the transverse block is invertible;

\item if both hold, then
\[
\det M\neq 0,
\]
and the crossing is Weyl.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Valley Doubling and Dirac Form}

Assume there exists an opposite valley at \(-G_*\) satisfying the standard opposite-valley relation
\[
h_-(p) = -h_+(-p).
\]
Then the doubled four-component Hamiltonian is
\[
H_D(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
After a standard chiral basis change, this becomes
\[
H_D(q)
=
q_1 \alpha^1 + q_2 \alpha^2 + q_3 \alpha^3,
\qquad
\{\alpha^i,\alpha^j\}=2\delta^{ij}\mathbf 1_4,
\]
which is the massless Dirac Hamiltonian.

Thus the minimal extracted coefficients
\[
v_\parallel,\alpha,\beta
\]
already control the full Weyl/Dirac transition.

\subsection{Extraction Theorem: Explicit Minimal Form}

\textbf{Theorem.}
Suppose the microscopic TECT linearized symbol near a selected BCC shell vector \(G_*\) is, to first order, unitarily reducible to the minimal shell-enriched ansatz
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{min}}(G_*+p)
=
E_*\,\mathbf 1_4
+
v_\parallel\, p_\parallel\, \tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
\alpha\,
\bigl(
p_1\,\tau^1\otimes s^1 + p_2\,\tau^1\otimes s^2
\bigr)
+
\beta\,
\bigl(
-p_2\,\tau^1\otimes s^1 + p_1\,\tau^1\otimes s^2
\bigr)
+
O(|p|^2).
\]
Then the first derivative operators satisfy
\[
\mathcal K_\parallel = v_\parallel\, \tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2,
\]
\[
\mathcal K_1 = \alpha\, \tau^1\otimes s^1 + \beta\, \tau^1\otimes s^2,
\]
\[
\mathcal K_2 = -\beta\, \tau^1\otimes s^1 + \alpha\, \tau^1\otimes s^2,
\]
and the reduced Weyl coefficients are exactly
\[
\lambda_\parallel = v_\parallel,
\qquad
\det T = \alpha^2+\beta^2.
\]
Consequently,
\[
\det M = v_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\]
If
\[
v_\parallel \neq 0,
\qquad
(\alpha,\beta)\neq (0,0),
\]
then the selected BCC shell crossing is locally Weyl.
If, in addition, an opposite valley exists with the standard pairing symmetry, the doubled low-energy theory is Dirac.

\textbf{Proof.}
All statements follow from the explicit derivative computation above, followed by projection to the two-band valley subspace and direct determinant evaluation. \(\square\)

\subsection{What Is Proved and What Remains Open}

What is proved here is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item for the minimal symmetry-compatible shell-enriched ansatz, the coefficients \(\lambda_\parallel,\alpha,\beta\) are extracted explicitly;
\item the Weyl nondegeneracy condition reduces exactly to
\[
v_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2)\neq 0;
\]
\item opposite-valley doubling then yields Dirac.
\end{enumerate}

What remains open is the microscopic matching problem:

\begin{enumerate}
\item derive the full TECT condensate PDE in final closed form;
\item prove that its linearization near \(G_*\) reduces to the minimal enriched ansatz above, or identify the exact corrections;
\item prove existence of the opposite shell-pair valley and its symmetry relation.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the local Weyl/Dirac problem has now been reduced to a concrete microscopic coefficient-matching calculation.

\subsection{Final Honest Reduction}

The remaining local Dirac frontier is now exactly:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Derive the true TECT linearized symbol and match its first-order projected coefficients to }
v_\parallel,\alpha,\beta.
}
\]

If the microscopic matching gives
\[
v_\parallel \neq 0,
\qquad
(\alpha,\beta)\neq (0,0),
\]
then local Weyl nondegeneracy follows immediately, and Dirac emergence follows after valley doubling.

\section*{Final Summary}

The local Weyl/Dirac problem in TECT has been reduced to the first-order expansion of the microscopic condensate symbol near a selected BCC shell vector \(G_*\).

For the minimal shell-enriched candidate operator
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{min}}(G_*+p)
=
E_*\,\mathbf 1_4
+
v_\parallel\, p_\parallel\, \tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
\alpha\,
\bigl(
p_1\,\tau^1\otimes s^1 + p_2\,\tau^1\otimes s^2
\bigr)
+
\beta\,
\bigl(
-p_2\,\tau^1\otimes s^1 + p_1\,\tau^1\otimes s^2
\bigr)
+
O(|p|^2),
\]
the projected first-derivative coefficients are
\[
\lambda_\parallel = v_\parallel,
\qquad
T=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha & -\beta\\
\beta & \alpha
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\det T=\alpha^2+\beta^2.
\]
Therefore
\[
\det M = v_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\]

Hence:

\[
v_\parallel\neq 0,\quad (\alpha,\beta)\neq (0,0)
\Longrightarrow
\text{local Weyl nondegeneracy}.
\]

If, in addition, the opposite shell valley exists and satisfies the standard pairing symmetry, then valley doubling yields the massless Dirac operator.

Thus the remaining microscopic task is no longer structural:
it is the explicit matching of the true TECT linearized symbol to the coefficients \(v_\parallel,\alpha,\beta\).

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Microscopic Matching Theorem for the Weyl/Dirac Coefficients}

\subsection{Purpose}

The local Weyl/Dirac problem has already been reduced to the three coefficients
\[
\lambda_\parallel,\qquad \alpha,\qquad \beta,
\]
through the determinant identity
\[
\det M=\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\]
The remaining task is therefore to match these coefficients directly to the microscopic enriched tensor couplings of the TECT condensate linearization.

The shell-level enriched coupling has the generic gradient-linear form
\[
\delta \mathcal L_{\mathrm{enr}}
=
\sum_{a}
\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\,
(\partial_i \phi_0)\,
(-i\partial_j)\otimes s^a,
\]
where:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\phi_0\) is the condensed BCC background,
\item \(\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\) is the finite microscopic tensor coefficient,
\item \(s^a\) acts on the internal reduced doublet sector.
\end{itemize}

We now show that, after projection to a selected shell pair \(\pm G_*\), these tensors determine the effective Weyl/Dirac coefficients by explicit contractions.

\subsection{Selected Shell Pair and Adapted Frame}

Fix a reciprocal pair
\[
\pm G_* \in S_{\mathrm{BCC}},
\qquad
\hat n := \frac{G_*}{|G_*|},
\]
and choose an orthonormal frame
\[
\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\}.
\]
Decompose a small momentum as
\[
p = p_\parallel \hat n + p_1 e_1 + p_2 e_2.
\]

Near the selected shell mode, write the BCC background locally as
\[
\phi_0(x)
=
\Phi_* e^{iG_*\cdot x}
+
\overline{\Phi_*}\, e^{-iG_*\cdot x}
+\cdots,
\]
so that the gradient on the selected shell component is
\[
\partial_i \phi_0
=
i G_{*i}\Phi_* e^{iG_*\cdot x}
-
i G_{*i}\overline{\Phi_*} e^{-iG_*\cdot x}
+\cdots.
\]
Thus, on the selected shell component, \(\partial_i\phi_0\) contributes a factor proportional to \(G_{*i}\).

\subsection{Shell-Reduced Vector Coefficients}

Define the reduced vector coefficients
\[
C_j^{a}
:=
G_{*i}\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}.
\]
Equivalently,
\[
C^{a} = (C_j^{a})_{j=1}^3 \in \mathbb R^3.
\]
Then the enriched shell operator projected to the selected shell pair acquires the form
\[
\delta h_{\mathrm{enr}}(p)
\sim
\sum_{a}
C_j^{a}\, p_j\, \Sigma^{a},
\]
where \(\Sigma^{a}\) denotes the projected \(2\times2\) Pauli-sector generator induced by the microscopic channel \(s^a\).

This formula is the precise microscopic origin of the shell-level coefficients.
All local Weyl data are determined by the three vectors
\[
C^1,\qquad C^2,\qquad C^3.
\]

\subsection{Longitudinal/Transverse Decomposition of the Reduced Coefficients}

Decompose each \(C^a\) along the adapted frame:
\[
C^a
=
(C^a\cdot \hat n)\,\hat n
+
(C^a\cdot e_1)\,e_1
+
(C^a\cdot e_2)\,e_2.
\]

For the minimal axial-equivariant ansatz, the symmetry-adapted matching is:

\begin{itemize}
\item channel \(a=3\) contributes to the longitudinal scalar piece,
\item channels \(a=1,2\) contribute to the transverse doublet piece.
\end{itemize}

Accordingly, define
\[
\lambda_\parallel
:=
\kappa_*
\,(C^3\cdot \hat n),
\]
where \(\kappa_*\) is the shell-projection normalization factor (proportional to the selected-shell condensate amplitude).

Likewise define the transverse \(2\times2\) matrix
\[
T_{ab}
:=
\kappa_*
\,(C^b\cdot e_a),
\qquad
a,b=1,2.
\]
Explicitly,
\[
T=
\kappa_*
\begin{pmatrix}
C^1\cdot e_1 & C^2\cdot e_1\\
C^1\cdot e_2 & C^2\cdot e_2
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Thus the reduced linear Hamiltonian becomes
\[
h_+^{\mathrm{lin}}(p)
=
\lambda_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
\sum_{a,b=1}^2 T_{ab}\, p_a \sigma^b.
\]

\subsection{Direct Contraction Formulas}

Substituting \(C_j^a=G_{*i}\mathsf N_{ij}^a\), we obtain
\[
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_*\,
\hat n_j G_{*i}\mathsf N_{ij}^{3}.
\]
Since \(G_{*i}=|G_*|\hat n_i\),
\[
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_* |G_*|\,
\hat n_i\hat n_j\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{3}.
\]

Similarly,
\[
T_{ab}
=
\kappa_*\,
e_{aj} G_{*i}\mathsf N_{ij}^{b}
=
\kappa_* |G_*|\,
e_{aj}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{b},
\qquad
a,b=1,2.
\]

Therefore the microscopic matching formulas are
\[
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_* |G_*|\,
\hat n_i\hat n_j\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{3}
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
T_{ab}
=
\kappa_* |G_*|\,
e_{aj}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{b},
\qquad a,b=1,2.
}
\]

These are the fundamental coefficient-extraction formulas.

\subsection{Extraction of \(\alpha\) and \(\beta\)}

For the minimal axial-equivariant transverse ansatz, write
\[
T=\alpha I_2+\beta J_2,
\qquad
J_2=
\begin{pmatrix}
0&-1\\
1&0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then
\[
\alpha = \frac12 \operatorname{tr}(T),
\qquad
\beta = \frac12 \operatorname{tr}(J_2^{\!\top}T).
\]
Equivalently,
\[
\alpha = \frac12(T_{11}+T_{22}),
\qquad
\beta = \frac12(T_{21}-T_{12}).
\]

Using the microscopic formulas for \(T_{ab}\), this gives
\[
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr),
\]
and
\[
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr).
\]

Thus the complete matching is
\[
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_* |G_*|\,
\hat n_i\hat n_j\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{3},
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr),
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr).
}
\]

\subsection{Microscopic Matching Theorem}

\textbf{Theorem.}
Let the TECT enriched microscopic linearization near a selected BCC shell pair \(\pm G_*\) contain the gradient-linear coupling
\[
\delta \mathcal L_{\mathrm{enr}}
=
\sum_{a=1}^3
\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\,
(\partial_i \phi_0)\,
(-i\partial_j)\otimes s^a.
\]
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the shell pair \(\pm G_*\) defines an adapted frame \(\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\}\);

\item the selected shell projection maps the microscopic channels \(s^a\) to the reduced Pauli basis so that \(a=3\) is longitudinal and \(a=1,2\) are transverse;

\item forbidden mixed channels are absent by the little-group symmetry.
\end{enumerate}

Then the reduced Weyl coefficients are given by
\[
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_* |G_*|\,
\hat n_i\hat n_j\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{3},
\]
\[
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr),
\]
\[
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr).
\]
Consequently,
\[
\det M
=
\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\]

\textbf{Proof.}
The background gradient contributes a factor \(G_{*i}\) on the selected shell component, so the microscopic tensor \(\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\) reduces to the shell vectors \(C_j^a=G_{*i}\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\).
Projecting these vectors onto the adapted frame \((e_1,e_2,\hat n)\) gives the longitudinal scalar coefficient and the transverse \(2\times2\) matrix:
\[
\lambda_\parallel=\kappa_*(C^3\cdot \hat n),
\qquad
T_{ab}=\kappa_*(C^b\cdot e_a).
\]
Substituting \(C_j^a=G_{*i}\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\) gives the stated contraction formulas.
The formulas for \(\alpha,\beta\) follow from the decomposition
\[
T=\alpha I_2+\beta J_2.
\]
Finally,
\[
\det T=\alpha^2+\beta^2,
\]
hence
\[
\det M=\lambda_\parallel\det T=\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\]
\(\square\)

\subsection{Immediate Corollary}

\textbf{Corollary.}
If the actual microscopic tensor coefficients satisfy
\[
\hat n_i\hat n_j\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{3}\neq 0,
\]
and
\[
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr)^2
+
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr)^2
\neq 0,
\]
then
\[
\lambda_\parallel \neq 0,
\qquad
(\alpha,\beta)\neq (0,0),
\]
and therefore the selected BCC shell crossing is locally Weyl.

If, in addition, an opposite valley exists with the standard doubling symmetry, then the doubled low-energy theory is Dirac.

\subsection{Matching Table}

The microscopic-to-effective dictionary is therefore:

\[
\begin{array}{c|c}
\text{Microscopic contraction} & \text{Effective coefficient} \\
\hline
\hat n_i\hat n_j\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{3} & \lambda_\parallel \\
\frac12\left(e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}+e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}\right) & \alpha/(\kappa_*|G_*|) \\
\frac12\left(e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}-e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}\right) & \beta/(\kappa_*|G_*|) \\
\end{array}
\]

Thus the local Weyl problem has been converted into a finite list of explicit tensor contractions.

\subsection{What Is Closed and What Is Still Open}

What is now closed:

\begin{itemize}
\item the exact matching formulas from microscopic enriched tensors \(\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\) to the effective Weyl/Dirac coefficients \(\lambda_\parallel,\alpha,\beta\);
\item the identity
\[
\det M=\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2);
\]
\item the finite tensor-contraction criterion for local Weyl nondegeneracy.
\end{itemize}

What remains open:

\begin{itemize}
\item derive the actual microscopic TECT condensate PDE in final closed form;
\item compute the true \(\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\) generated by that PDE;
\item verify the shell-pair valley existence and opposite-valley doubling relation.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Final Honest Reduction}

The remaining local Dirac frontier is now exactly:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Compute the actual microscopic tensor coefficients } \mathsf N_{ij}^{a}
\text{ of the TECT condensate linearization and evaluate the three contractions above.}
}
\]

If they satisfy
\[
\hat n_i\hat n_j\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{3}\neq 0,
\]
and
\[
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr)^2
+
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr)^2
\neq 0,
\]
then local Weyl nondegeneracy follows immediately.

\section*{Summary}

For the enriched microscopic shell coupling
\[
\delta \mathcal L_{\mathrm{enr}}
=
\sum_{a=1}^3
\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\,
(\partial_i \phi_0)\,
(-i\partial_j)\otimes s^a,
\]
the selected BCC shell pair \(\pm G_*\) reduces the microscopic tensors to the shell vectors
\[
C_j^a=G_{*i}\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}.
\]
After projection to the adapted frame \(\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\}\), the effective Weyl coefficients are
\[
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_* |G_*|\,
\hat n_i\hat n_j\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{3},
\]
\[
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr),
\]
\[
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr).
\]
Hence
\[
\det M=\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\]

Therefore the local Weyl/Dirac problem in TECT is no longer a qualitative question.
It is an explicit finite tensor-contraction test on the microscopic coefficients \(\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\).

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{TECT Emergent Dirac Fermion Theorem}

\subsection{Purpose}

We now assemble the previously established ingredients into a single theorem-level statement for emergent Dirac fermions in TECT.

The logic consists of four layers:

\begin{enumerate}
\item topological chirality from the defect index;
\item dynamical mirror lifting;
\item local Weyl-valley emergence from the BCC shell matching;
\item opposite-valley doubling to a Dirac fermion.
\end{enumerate}

The purpose of this section is to state precisely what is already closed at the theorem level, and which microscopic inputs still remain to be checked in the full TECT condensate PDE.

\subsection{Topological Chiral Sector}

Let \(D_+\) denote the chirally projected defect Dirac operator on the relevant internal topological sector.
Assume the primitive twisting of the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\)-based defect bundle gives
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+) = I > 0.
\]
Then
\[
n_L-n_R = I.
\]
Equivalently, the low-energy defect sector contains a net protected left-handed chiral subspace of index \(I\).

\subsection{Mirror Lifting}

Let the low-energy fermion space decompose as
\[
\mathcal H_F
=
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{prot}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{mir}},
\]
where \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{prot}}\) is the topologically protected chiral subspace and \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{mir}}\) consists of vectorlike mirror pairs.

Assume there exists a mirror-lifting operator
\[
M_{\mathrm{mir}}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 0 & \mu^\dagger\\
0 & \mu & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\mu \neq 0,
\]
acting on the mirror sector, such that all vectorlike pairs acquire nonzero gap.
Then the infrared spectrum reduces to the protected chiral sector
\[
n_L = I,
\qquad
n_R = 0,
\]
up to the surviving opposite-valley doubling discussed below.

\subsection{Local BCC Weyl Matching}

Fix a reciprocal shell pair
\[
\pm G_* \in S_{\mathrm{BCC}},
\qquad
\hat n = \frac{G_*}{|G_*|},
\]
and choose an adapted orthonormal frame
\[
\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\}.
\]

Assume the enriched microscopic shell coupling near the selected shell pair has the form
\[
\delta \mathcal L_{\mathrm{enr}}
=
\sum_{a=1}^3
\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\,
(\partial_i\phi_0)\,
(-i\partial_j)\otimes s^a,
\]
where \(\phi_0\) is the BCC condensate background and \(s^a\) acts on the reduced internal shell doublet.

Define the three effective coefficients by
\[
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_* |G_*|\,
\hat n_i \hat n_j \,\mathsf N_{ij}^{3},
\]
\[
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr),
\]
\[
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr).
\]
Then the local reduced valley Hamiltonian is
\[
h_+(p)
=
\lambda_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
\alpha (p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
\beta (-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\]
Equivalently, the transverse matrix is
\[
T=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha & -\beta\\
\beta & \alpha
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\det T = \alpha^2+\beta^2.
\]
Hence the full linear coefficient determinant is
\[
\det M
=
\lambda_\parallel (\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\]

Therefore, if
\[
\lambda_\parallel \neq 0,
\qquad
(\alpha,\beta)\neq (0,0),
\]
then the local shell crossing is Weyl.

\subsection{Opposite-Valley Doubling}

Assume there exists an opposite shell valley at \(-G_*\) with the standard pairing relation
\[
h_-(p) = - h_+(-p).
\]
Then the doubled low-energy Hamiltonian becomes
\[
H_D(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
After a standard basis change, this is the massless Dirac Hamiltonian
\[
H_D(q)
=
q_1 \Gamma^1 + q_2 \Gamma^2 + q_3 \Gamma^3,
\qquad
\{\Gamma^i,\Gamma^j\}=2\delta^{ij}.
\]

\subsection{TECT Emergent Dirac Fermion Theorem}

\textbf{Theorem.}
Assume the following:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the TECT condensate selects the BCC reciprocal shell \(S_{\mathrm{BCC}}\);

\item the internal topological defect sector carries a chirally twisted Dirac operator with
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+) = I > 0;
\]

\item all vectorlike mirror pairs are lifted by a nondegenerate mirror operator
\[
\mu \neq 0;
\]

\item there exists a selected shell pair \(\pm G_*\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}}\) together with a two-band enriched valley sector;

\item the enriched microscopic shell tensors \(\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\) satisfy
\[
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_* |G_*|\,
\hat n_i \hat n_j \,\mathsf N_{ij}^{3}
\neq 0,
\]
and
\[
(\alpha,\beta)\neq (0,0),
\]
where
\[
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr),
\]
\[
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr);
\]

\item an opposite valley exists at \(-G_*\) with the standard doubling relation
\[
h_-(p) = -h_+(-p).
\]
\end{enumerate}

Then the infrared TECT theory contains a protected chiral Dirac fermion sector.
More precisely:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the selected shell crossing at \(G_*\) is Weyl;

\item the opposite shell crossing at \(-G_*\) has opposite chirality;

\item valley doubling yields a massless Dirac fermion;

\item the mirror sector is lifted, so the surviving low-energy fermion content is the protected chiral Dirac sector of multiplicity \(I\).
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Proof.}
By assumption (2),
\[
n_L-n_R=I>0,
\]
so there exists a net protected chiral sector.
By assumption (3), all vectorlike mirror pairs are gapped out, leaving only the protected sector in the infrared.

By assumption (5), the reduced shell Hamiltonian near \(G_*\) has coefficients
\[
\lambda_\parallel \neq 0,
\qquad
\det T = \alpha^2+\beta^2 > 0.
\]
Hence
\[
\det M = \lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2)\neq 0.
\]
Therefore the local crossing is linearly nondegenerate and hence Weyl.

By assumption (6), the opposite valley at \(-G_*\) satisfies the standard opposite-valley relation, so the two Weyl nodes combine into a doubled four-component Hamiltonian of Dirac type.
Thus the infrared theory contains a massless Dirac fermion.

Finally, because the mirror sector has already been lifted while the topological index protects the chiral sector, the surviving low-energy fermionic content is precisely the protected chiral Dirac sector with multiplicity \(I\).
\(\square\)

\subsection{Corollary: Minimal Local Criterion}

\textbf{Corollary.}
At the local valley level, the entire TECT Dirac-emergence problem reduces to the finite coefficient test
\[
\hat n_i \hat n_j \,\mathsf N_{ij}^{3}\neq 0,
\]
and
\[
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr)^2
+
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr)^2
\neq 0,
\]
together with the existence of the opposite valley.

If these conditions hold, then local Weyl nondegeneracy and Dirac doubling follow automatically.

\subsection{What Is Closed}

The following structure is now closed at the theorem level:

\[
\mathbb{CP}^2
\Longrightarrow
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)=I
\Longrightarrow
\text{protected chiral sector}
\Longrightarrow
\text{mirror lifting}
\Longrightarrow
\text{local Weyl valley on BCC shell}
\Longrightarrow
\text{Dirac doubling}.
\]

Equivalently, the Dirac-emergence architecture is no longer open-ended.
It has been reduced to a finite set of explicit microscopic checks.

\subsection{What Remains Open}

What remains genuinely open is not the theorem architecture, but the microscopic realization:

\begin{enumerate}
\item derive the full TECT condensate PDE in final closed form;
\item compute the actual enriched microscopic coefficients \(\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\);
\item verify the shell-pair valley existence at \(\pm G_*\);
\item verify the opposite-valley pairing relation;
\item match the resulting Dirac sector to the gauge and matter bundle decomposition.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Final Honest Statement}

The local fermion problem in TECT has now been reduced to a finite tensor-and-valley verification problem.
No further conceptual ingredient is needed at the theorem level.

The remaining frontier is exactly:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Compute the true microscopic enriched tensors } \mathsf N_{ij}^{a}
\text{ and verify the shell-pair valley doubling relation.}
}
\]

\section*{Summary}

The emergent Dirac fermion problem in TECT is now controlled by four ingredients:

\begin{enumerate}
\item topological chirality:
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+) = I > 0;
\]

\item mirror lifting:
\[
\mu \neq 0;
\]

\item local BCC-shell Weyl matching:
\[
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_* |G_*|\,
\hat n_i\hat n_j\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{3},
\]
\[
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr),
\]
\[
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i\,\mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr),
\]
with
\[
\det M = \lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2);
\]

\item opposite-valley doubling:
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p).
\]
\end{enumerate}

If these conditions hold, then the infrared TECT theory contains a protected chiral Dirac fermion sector.

Therefore the remaining microscopic task is finite and explicit:
compute the true enriched tensor coefficients \(\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\) and verify the opposite-valley shell pairing.

\section{TECT Chiral Gauge Fermion Theorem}

\subsection{Purpose}

We now combine the gauge-emergence chain and the fermion-emergence chain into a single theorem-level statement.

The aim is to show that, under the finite set of microscopic assumptions already isolated in previous sections, the infrared TECT theory contains a chiral gauge-fermion sector of the form
\[
SU(3)\times SU(2)\times U(1)_Y
\]
coupled to protected Dirac fermions whose vectorlike mirrors are lifted.

This theorem does \emph{not} yet claim the full Standard Model matter content, anomaly cancellation, or exact generation multiplicity.
Its purpose is narrower and more precise:

\[
\boxed{
\text{TECT yields a chiral gauge-fermion architecture once the finite bundle/tensor/valley inputs are satisfied.}
}
\]

\subsection{Gauge Sector Inputs}

Assume the following gauge-geometric ingredients.

\paragraph{(G1) Abelian projective bundle.}
The low-energy orientational degree of freedom is represented by
\[
z(x)\in \mathbb C^3,\qquad z^\dagger z=1,\qquad z\sim e^{i\alpha(x)}z,
\]
so that the physical target is
\[
[z]\in \mathbb{CP}^2.
\]
This produces a principal \(U(1)\) bundle with local connection
\[
a_\mu = -i z^\dagger \partial_\mu z
\]
and curvature
\[
f_{\mu\nu}=\partial_\mu a_\nu-\partial_\nu a_\mu.
\]

\paragraph{(G2) Color connection.}
The internal projective geometry
\[
\mathbb{CP}^2 \simeq \frac{SU(3)}{U(2)}
\]
admits the already-derived \(SU(3)\)-valued connection
\[
A_\mu = A_\mu^a T^a,
\qquad
F_{\mu\nu}^a
=
\partial_\mu A_\nu^a-\partial_\nu A_\mu^a+f^{abc}A_\mu^bA_\nu^c.
\]

\paragraph{(G3) Weak bundle.}
The rank-two orthogonal complement bundle
\[
Q \to \mathbb{CP}^2
\]
admits a local orthonormal frame with \(U(2)\) redundancy.
Assume the corresponding Berry connection reduces to a genuine \(SU(2)\) connection
\[
W_\mu = W_\mu^i \tau^i
\]
on a preserved rank-two weak subbundle \(\mathcal E_W\).

\paragraph{(G4) Hypercharge embedding.}
Assume there exists a hypercharge generator
\[
Y=\alpha Q_P+\beta Q_T
\]
or, in the stronger canonical case, a traceless \(3+2\) embedding such that the Abelian gauge field is
\[
B_\mu
=
\alpha\, a_\mu+\beta\, b_\mu,
\]
with curvature
\[
B_{\mu\nu}=\partial_\mu B_\nu-\partial_\nu B_\mu.
\]

Under these assumptions, the infrared gauge sector is
\[
G_{\mathrm{gauge}} = SU(3)\times SU(2)\times U(1)_Y.
\]

\subsection{Topological Chiral Inputs}

Assume the topological defect sector satisfies:

\paragraph{(F1) Chiral index.}
For the relevant defect Dirac operator,
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+) = I > 0.
\]
Hence
\[
n_L-n_R = I.
\]

\paragraph{(F2) Mirror lifting.}
There exists a nondegenerate mirror-lifting operator
\[
M_{\mathrm{mir}}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 0 & \mu^\dagger\\
0 & \mu & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\mu\neq 0,
\]
which gaps out all vectorlike mirror pairs.

Therefore the infrared fermion content reduces to the topologically protected chiral sector.

\subsection{Local BCC Valley Inputs}

Fix a shell pair
\[
\pm G_*\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}},
\qquad
\hat n = \frac{G_*}{|G_*|},
\]
and choose an adapted frame
\[
\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\}.
\]

Assume the enriched microscopic shell coupling near the selected shell pair is
\[
\delta \mathcal L_{\mathrm{enr}}
=
\sum_{a=1}^3
\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}
(\partial_i\phi_0)
(-i\partial_j)\otimes s^a.
\]
Define
\[
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_*|G_*|\, \hat n_i\hat n_j \mathsf N_{ij}^{3},
\]
\[
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i \mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i \mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr),
\]
\[
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i \mathsf N_{ij}^{1}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i \mathsf N_{ij}^{2}
\Bigr).
\]
Then the reduced local valley Hamiltonian is
\[
h_+(p)
=
\lambda_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
\alpha (p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
\beta (-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2),
\]
and its linear nondegeneracy is controlled by
\[
\det M = \lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\]

Assume
\[
\lambda_\parallel \neq 0,
\qquad
(\alpha,\beta)\neq (0,0).
\]
Then the selected shell crossing is Weyl.

\paragraph{(F3) Opposite valley doubling.}
Assume there exists an opposite valley at \(-G_*\) satisfying
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p).
\]
Then the doubled four-component Hamiltonian is Dirac.

\subsection{Gauge-Covariant Dirac Structure}

Because the fermionic valley sector descends from the defect sector and the latter already carries the internal gauge data, the covariant derivative acting on the emergent Dirac fermion is of the form
\[
\mathcal D_\mu
=
\partial_\mu
- i g_3 A_\mu^a T^a
- i g_2 W_\mu^i t^i
- i g_Y Y B_\mu,
\]
where:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(T^a\) are generators of the color representation;
\item \(t^i\) are generators of the weak representation;
\item \(Y\) is the hypercharge generator.
\end{itemize}

Therefore the low-energy Dirac equation is
\[
(i\gamma^\mu \mathcal D_\mu - M_*)\Psi = 0,
\]
or, in the massless shell-emergent case,
\[
i\gamma^\mu \mathcal D_\mu \Psi = 0
\]
before additional symmetry-breaking effects are introduced.

\subsection{TECT Chiral Gauge Fermion Theorem}

\textbf{Theorem.}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the TECT condensate selects the BCC reciprocal shell;
\item the projective orientational sector yields the \(U(1)\) bundle over \(\mathbb{CP}^2\);
\item the internal geometry yields an \(SU(3)\) connection;
\item the rank-two weak bundle exists and yields an \(SU(2)\) connection;
\item hypercharge \(U(1)_Y\) is defined by the Abelian embedding \(Y\);
\item the topological defect Dirac operator satisfies
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)=I>0;
\]
\item all vectorlike mirror pairs are lifted by
\[
\mu\neq 0;
\]
\item the selected BCC shell pair \(\pm G_*\) admits enriched microscopic coefficients satisfying
\[
\lambda_\parallel \neq 0,
\qquad
(\alpha,\beta)\neq (0,0);
\]
\item an opposite shell valley exists with the doubling relation
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p).
\]
\end{enumerate}

Then the infrared TECT theory contains a chiral gauge-fermion sector with the following properties:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the gauge group is
\[
SU(3)\times SU(2)\times U(1)_Y;
\]

\item the protected chiral sector has multiplicity \(I\);

\item each local nondegenerate shell crossing is Weyl;

\item opposite-valley doubling yields a Dirac fermion;

\item the resulting Dirac fermion is gauge-covariantly coupled through
\[
\mathcal D_\mu
=
\partial_\mu
- i g_3 A_\mu^a T^a
- i g_2 W_\mu^i t^i
- i g_Y Y B_\mu.
\]
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Proof.}
Items (1)--(5) establish the infrared gauge architecture
\[
SU(3)\times SU(2)\times U(1)_Y.
\]
Item (6) gives a protected net chiral sector of index \(I\).
Item (7) removes vectorlike mirror partners from the infrared spectrum.
Item (8) implies
\[
\det M=\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2)\neq 0,
\]
so the selected shell crossing is Weyl.
Item (9) doubles the local Weyl node into a Dirac fermion.
Because the fermionic state descends from the same internal sector carrying the gauge bundle data, the resulting Dirac operator is gauge-covariant under the full gauge group.
Thus the infrared theory contains a protected chiral gauge-fermion sector. \(\square\)

\subsection{Corollary: Finite Microscopic Checklist}

\textbf{Corollary.}
The full chiral gauge-fermion emergence problem reduces to the finite microscopic checklist:

\begin{enumerate}
\item compute the actual weak-bundle data \(\mathcal E_W\);
\item determine the hypercharge embedding \(Y\);
\item compute the actual enriched tensor coefficients \(\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\);
\item verify
\[
\lambda_\parallel \neq 0,
\qquad
(\alpha,\beta)\neq (0,0);
\]
\item verify opposite-valley doubling;
\item match the resulting fermion bundle to the desired matter representation.
\end{enumerate}

No further conceptual structure is needed at the theorem level.

\subsection{What Is Closed}

The following architecture is now closed:

\[
\text{BCC condensate}
\Longrightarrow
\mathbb{CP}^2
\Longrightarrow
U(1)\ \text{bundle}
\Longrightarrow
SU(3)\ \text{connection}
\Longrightarrow
SU(2)\ \text{weak bundle}
\Longrightarrow
U(1)_Y
\]
\[
\Longrightarrow
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)
\Longrightarrow
\text{mirror lifting}
\Longrightarrow
\text{local Weyl valley}
\Longrightarrow
\text{Dirac doubling}
\Longrightarrow
\text{chiral gauge fermion sector}.
\]

\subsection{What Remains Open}

The genuinely open frontier is now the microscopic realization of the finite inputs:

\begin{enumerate}
\item exact weak bundle construction;
\item exact hypercharge normalization;
\item actual TECT tensor coefficients \(\mathsf N_{ij}^{a}\);
\item shell-pair valley existence and doubling;
\item matter representation decomposition;
\item generation multiplicity and anomaly cancellation.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Final Honest Statement}

The emergence of a chiral gauge-fermion architecture in TECT has now been reduced to a finite bundle/tensor/valley matching problem.
At the theorem level, no conceptual gap remains.

\section*{Summary}

Under the finite set of assumptions consisting of:

\begin{itemize}
\item projective \(U(1)\) bundle over \(\mathbb{CP}^2\),
\item emergent \(SU(3)\) connection,
\item rank-two weak bundle giving \(SU(2)\),

\[
\lambda_\parallel \neq 0,\qquad (\alpha,\beta)\neq (0,0),
\]

\end{itemize}

the infrared TECT theory contains a protected chiral gauge-fermion sector with gauge group
\[

\]
and Dirac fermions coupled by the covariant derivative
\[
\mathcal D_\mu
=
\partial_\mu
- i g_3 A_\mu^a T^a
- i g_2 W_\mu^i t^i
- i g_Y Y B_\mu.
\]

Thus the remaining frontier is no longer conceptual.
It is the explicit microscopic realization of the finite bundle/tensor/valley inputs.

h(p)

then the local shell crossing is Weyl because

\section{BCC Shell Valley Existence Theorem}

\subsection{Aim and honest scope}

We now formulate the strongest rigorously provable statement currently available for the
fermion-emergence sector of TECT.

The goal is not yet to derive the full valley spectrum from a completely explicit microscopic
TECT PDE with all coefficients computed ab initio.
That stronger result remains open.

What we prove here is the following conditional-but-rigorous theorem:

\begin{center}
\emph{
If the microscopic condensate dynamics admits a BCC-condensed background,
if the linearized Bloch operator near a reciprocal-shell point $\pm G_*$ possesses a
two-band crossing isolated from the rest of the spectrum,
and if the linear linearized coefficient matrix is nondegenerate,
then the effective Hamiltonian near $\pm G_*$ is necessarily of Weyl type.
}
\end{center}

This is the precise mathematical closure behind the previously stated
``BCC Weyl Valley Theorem.''

\subsection{Microscopic condensate equation and BCC background}

Let the condensate field be a complex vector order parameter
\begin{equation}
\Phi(x,t)\in \mathbb{C}^N,
\end{equation}
with $N\ge 2$ sufficient for a two-band crossing sector.
Assume the microscopic dynamics is generated by a local translation-invariant PDE
of the form
\begin{equation}
i\partial_t \Phi = \mathcal{L}(-i\nabla,\Phi)\Phi,
\end{equation}
or, for dissipative/gradient-flow dynamics, by an equivalent linearized spectral problem
around a static background.
The only structure used below is the linearization around a stationary BCC-condensed state.

Let $\Phi_0(x)$ be a static BCC background with reciprocal support on the BCC star
\begin{equation}
S_{BCC}
=
\frac{q_*}{\sqrt{2}}
\left\{
(\pm 1,\pm 1,0),\,
(\pm 1,0,\pm 1),\,
(0,\pm 1,\pm 1)
\right\},
\qquad
|S_{BCC}|=12.
\end{equation}
We write
\begin{equation}
\Phi(x,t)=\Phi_0(x)+\delta\Phi(x,t),
\end{equation}
and linearize:
\begin{equation}
i\partial_t \delta\Phi
=
\mathbb{L}_{\Phi_0}\,\delta\Phi.
\end{equation}

Because $\Phi_0$ is periodic with BCC reciprocal structure, the linearized operator
$\mathbb{L}_{\Phi_0}$ is periodic and therefore admits Bloch decomposition.

\subsection{Bloch reduction near a reciprocal-shell point}

Fix one shell point
\begin{equation}
G_* \in S_{BCC}.
\end{equation}
A fluctuation mode near this point is parameterized as
\begin{equation}
k = G_* + p,
\qquad
|p|\ll |G_*|.
\end{equation}
By Bloch-Floquet theory, the spectral problem reduces locally to
\begin{equation}
\mathbb{L}_{\Phi_0}(G_*+p)\,u_{n,p}
=
E_n(p)\,u_{n,p}.
\end{equation}

Assume that at $p=0$ there exists an isolated two-band crossing:
\begin{equation}
E_1(0)=E_2(0)=E_*,
\end{equation}
and that all other bands are separated by a nonzero gap:
\begin{equation}
\inf_{m\ge 3}|E_m(0)-E_*|>0.
\end{equation}
Then Kato perturbation theory allows one to project the full Bloch operator onto the
two-dimensional crossing subspace.
Hence there exists a smooth rank-two spectral projector $P(p)$ for sufficiently small $p$,
and the effective Hamiltonian is
\begin{equation}
h(p):=P(p)\,\mathbb{L}_{\Phi_0}(G_*+p)\,P(p).
\end{equation}
At $p=0$, $h(0)=E_*\,\mathbf{1}_2$.

\subsection{General Hermitian form of the two-band effective Hamiltonian}

Since $h(p)$ acts on a two-dimensional complex space and is Hermitian,
it has the unique Pauli decomposition
\begin{equation}
h(p)=\varepsilon_0(p)\,\mathbf{1}_2+\sum_{a=1}^3 d_a(p)\sigma^a.
\end{equation}
Because $p=0$ is a band-touching point, we have
\begin{equation}
d_a(0)=0,
\qquad a=1,2,3.
\end{equation}
Taylor expansion gives
\begin{equation}
d_a(p)=\sum_{i=1}^3 M_{ai}p_i+\mathcal{O}(|p|^2),
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
M_{ai}:=\left.\frac{\partial d_a}{\partial p_i}\right|_{p=0}.
\end{equation}
Therefore
\begin{equation}
h(p)
=
E_*\,\mathbf{1}_2
+
\sum_{a,i}M_{ai}p_i\,\sigma^a
+
\mathcal{O}(|p|^2).
\end{equation}

\subsection{Weyl criterion}

\begin{theorem}[Local Weyl criterion]
Let
\begin{equation}
h(p)=E_*\,\mathbf{1}_2+\sum_{a,i}M_{ai}p_i\,\sigma^a+\mathcal{O}(|p|^2)
\end{equation}
be the effective two-band Hamiltonian near a band-touching point.
If
\begin{equation}
\det M\neq 0,
\end{equation}
then the touching point is a Weyl point.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The eigenvalues are
\begin{equation}
E_\pm(p)=E_*+\varepsilon_0(p)\pm |d(p)|,
\qquad
d(p):=(d_1(p),d_2(p),d_3(p)).
\end{equation}
To first order,
\begin{equation}
d(p)=Mp+\mathcal{O}(|p|^2).
\end{equation}
If $\det M\neq 0$, then the linear map $p\mapsto Mp$ is invertible.
Hence there exists $c>0$ such that for sufficiently small $p$,
\begin{equation}
|Mp|\ge c|p|.
\end{equation}
Therefore
\begin{equation}
E_+(p)-E_-(p)=2|d(p)|\ge c|p|+\mathcal{O}(|p|^2),
\end{equation}
so the touching is isolated and linear in all three momentum directions.
This is precisely a Weyl cone.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Adapted decomposition at a BCC shell point}

Choose an orthonormal frame adapted to $G_*$:
\begin{equation}
\hat n := \frac{G_*}{|G_*|},
\qquad
\{e_1,e_2\}\subset \hat n^\perp,
\qquad
\{\hat n,e_1,e_2\}\ \text{orthonormal}.
\end{equation}
Decompose
\begin{equation}
p=p_\parallel \hat n + p_1 e_1 + p_2 e_2.
\end{equation}
Then the linearized Hamiltonian can always be rewritten as
\begin{equation}
h(p)
=
E_*\,\mathbf{1}_2
+
p_\parallel V_\parallel
+
p_1 V_1
+
p_2 V_2
+
\mathcal{O}(|p|^2),
\end{equation}
with Hermitian $2\times 2$ matrices $V_\parallel,V_1,V_2$.
After subtraction of the trace part and a unitary basis change in the two-band subspace,
one may choose the Pauli basis so that
\begin{equation}
h(p)
=
E_*\,\mathbf{1}_2
+
\lambda_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
(\alpha p_1-\beta p_2)\sigma^1
+
(\beta p_1+\alpha p_2)\sigma^2
+
\mathcal{O}(|p|^2).
\end{equation}
Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
d(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha p_1-\beta p_2\\
\beta p_1+\alpha p_2\\
\lambda_\parallel p_\parallel
\end{pmatrix}
+
\mathcal{O}(|p|^2).
\end{equation}
Thus
\begin{equation}
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & \alpha & -\beta\\
0 & \beta & \alpha\\
\lambda_\parallel & 0 & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\end{equation}
and therefore
\begin{equation}
\det M=\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\end{equation}
Hence the Weyl criterion becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2)\neq 0.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Why this form is natural at a shell point}

At a shell point $G_*$, the distinguished direction is the radial direction $\hat n$.
The tangent plane is two-dimensional, and to linear order the effective Hamiltonian must split
into one longitudinal coefficient and one transverse complex coefficient.
The pair
\begin{equation}
(\alpha,\beta)
\end{equation}
is the real representation of the complex transverse slope
\begin{equation}
\alpha+i\beta.
\end{equation}
Thus the BCC shell geometry naturally organizes the linear crossing into
\begin{equation}
1 \text{ longitudinal slope} + 2 \text{ transverse slopes},
\end{equation}
which is exactly the structure required for a three-dimensional Weyl cone.

\subsection{Topological charge of the Weyl point}

Define the normalized vector
\begin{equation}
\hat d(p):=\frac{d(p)}{|d(p)|}
\end{equation}
on a small sphere $S^2_\epsilon$ around $p=0$.
The Weyl topological charge is
\begin{equation}
\chi
=
\frac{1}{4\pi}
\int_{S^2_\epsilon}
\hat d \cdot
\left(
\partial_{\theta}\hat d\times \partial_{\phi}\hat d
\right)
\,d\theta\,d\phi.
\end{equation}
For the linear model $d(p)=Mp$, this degree is
\begin{equation}
\chi=\operatorname{sgn}(\det M).
\end{equation}
Hence if $\det M\neq 0$, the crossing is topologically protected:
it cannot be removed by a sufficiently small smooth perturbation unless it annihilates
with another Weyl point of opposite charge.

\subsection{Opposite shell point and valley pairing}

Consider the opposite shell point
\begin{equation}
-G_* \in S_{BCC}.
\end{equation}
Suppose the linearized Bloch operator satisfies the valley-pairing relation
\begin{equation}
h_{-}(p)=-h_{+}(-p)
\end{equation}
after subtraction of the common scalar part.
Then the opposite point carries the opposite chirality:
\begin{equation}
\chi_-=-\chi_+.
\end{equation}
The doubled Hamiltonian is
\begin{equation}
H(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\end{equation}
To linear order this is unitarily equivalent to a massless Dirac Hamiltonian.
Thus opposite BCC shell valleys produce Dirac doubling.

\subsection{Main theorem}

\begin{theorem}[BCC Shell Valley Existence Theorem]
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item A microscopic TECT condensate PDE admits a periodic BCC-condensed background $\Phi_0$.

\item The corresponding Bloch-linearized operator $\mathbb{L}_{\Phi_0}(k)$ has, at some shell point
$G_*\in S_{BCC}$, an isolated two-band crossing separated by a spectral gap from all other bands.

\item The projected two-band Hamiltonian is Hermitian and smooth in $p=k-G_*$.

\item The linear coefficient matrix $M$ of the Pauli-vector part satisfies
\begin{equation}
\det M\neq 0.
\end{equation}
Equivalently, in the adapted shell basis,
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2)\neq 0.
\end{equation}
\end{enumerate}

Then $G_*$ is a Weyl valley of the BCC shell spectrum.
Its local effective Hamiltonian is
\begin{equation}
h(p)
=
E_*\,\mathbf{1}_2
+
\lambda_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
(\alpha p_1-\beta p_2)\sigma^1
+
(\beta p_1+\alpha p_2)\sigma^2
+
\mathcal{O}(|p|^2),
\end{equation}
and its chirality is
\begin{equation}
\chi=\operatorname{sgn}\!\bigl(\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2)\bigr).
\end{equation}
If, in addition, the opposite shell point $-G_*$ satisfies the valley-pairing relation
\begin{equation}
h_{-}(p)=-h_{+}(-p),
\end{equation}
then the two Weyl valleys combine into a Dirac fermion.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Items (1)--(3) guarantee the existence of the local rank-two projected Hamiltonian.
Item (4) implies, by the Local Weyl Criterion, that the crossing is isolated and linear,
hence a Weyl point.
The chirality formula follows from the degree of the map $\hat d:S^2\to S^2$.
The final Dirac statement follows by block doubling under the valley-pairing relation.
\end{proof}

\subsection{What is proven and what remains open}

The theorem above is mathematically closed once assumptions (1)--(4) hold.
Therefore the remaining open problem is now sharply localized:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Derive the two-band crossing and compute }M
\text{ directly from the full microscopic TECT PDE.}
}
\end{equation}

Equivalently, the true remaining microscopic frontier is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item derive the explicit condensate PDE,
\item compute the Bloch operator around the BCC background,
\item prove existence of isolated crossings at $\pm G_*$,
\item compute $\lambda_\parallel,\alpha,\beta$ and verify
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2)\neq 0.
\end{equation}
\end{enumerate}

Once these are completed, the fermion-emergence chain
\begin{equation}
\text{BCC condensate}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{Weyl valley}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{Dirac fermion}
\end{equation}
is fully microscopic.

\section{Final Summary}

We have rigorously reduced the BCC-shell fermion problem to a local two-band spectral theorem.

Given a periodic BCC-condensed background, Bloch reduction near a shell point $G_*$ yields
a projected $2\times 2$ Hermitian effective Hamiltonian
\[
h(p)=E_*\mathbf{1}_2+\sum_{a,i}M_{ai}p_i\sigma^a+\mathcal{O}(|p|^2).
\]
If
\[
\det M\neq 0,
\]
then the crossing at $G_*$ is necessarily a Weyl point.
In the shell-adapted basis this criterion becomes
\[
\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2)\neq 0.
\]
The Weyl topological charge is
\[
\chi=\operatorname{sgn}(\det M).
\]
If the opposite shell point $-G_*$ satisfies
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p),
\]
then the two Weyl valleys combine into a Dirac fermion.

Therefore the remaining open frontier is no longer conceptual.
It is the explicit microscopic computation of the Bloch crossing and the coefficient matrix
$M$ from the full TECT condensate PDE.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Microscopic TECT Condensate PDE and Bloch Operator Around the BCC Background}

\subsection{Aim and scope}

We now derive the strongest mathematically controlled microscopic formulation currently available
for the condensate sector of TECT.

The aim of this section is fourfold:

\begin{enumerate}
\item to write the minimal translation-invariant and rotation-invariant condensate PDE capable of
supporting a finite-shell instability,

\item to construct a stationary BCC-condensed background,

\item to compute the exact linearized operator around that background,

\item to reduce the local valley problem near a shell point $G_*$ to a finite-dimensional Bloch matrix problem.
\end{enumerate}

This does not yet prove the existence of a two-band crossing.
What it does prove is that, once the condensate PDE is specified, the remaining Weyl/Dirac problem
is an explicit spectral problem for a periodic linear operator.

\subsection{Minimal microscopic condensate functional}

Let the condensate order parameter be
\begin{equation}
\Phi(x,t)\in \mathbb{C}^N,
\qquad x\in \mathbb{R}^3,
\end{equation}
with $N\ge 1$ at the condensate level, and $N\ge 2$ required later for a nontrivial two-band crossing sector.

We impose the following minimal microscopic principles:

\begin{enumerate}
\item translation invariance,
\item spatial isotropy before condensation,
\item local polynomial interactions,
\item quartic stabilization,
\item finite-wavevector instability.
\end{enumerate}

The minimal rotationally invariant free-energy functional with these properties is
\begin{equation}
\mathcal{F}[\Phi]
=
\int d^3x
\left[
r\, \Phi^\dagger \Phi
+
\kappa\, \partial_i \Phi^\dagger \partial_i \Phi
+
\lambda\, (\nabla^2\Phi)^\dagger (\nabla^2\Phi)
+
\frac{u}{2}(\Phi^\dagger\Phi)^2
\right],
\label{eq:Fmin}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\lambda>0,
\qquad
u>0.
\end{equation}

The Euler-Lagrange equation for static configurations is
\begin{equation}
\frac{\delta \mathcal F}{\delta \Phi^\dagger}
=
r\Phi
-\kappa \nabla^2\Phi
+\lambda \nabla^4\Phi
+u(\Phi^\dagger\Phi)\Phi
=0.
\label{eq:staticEL}
\end{equation}

If one wishes to use conservative dynamics, the corresponding microscopic PDE is
\begin{equation}
i\partial_t \Phi
=
\frac{\delta \mathcal F}{\delta \Phi^\dagger}
=
r\Phi
-\kappa \nabla^2\Phi
+\lambda \nabla^4\Phi
+u(\Phi^\dagger\Phi)\Phi.
\label{eq:microPDE}
\end{equation}

If one prefers dissipative relaxation, one may instead use
\begin{equation}
\partial_t \Phi
=
-
\frac{\delta \mathcal F}{\delta \Phi^\dagger}.
\end{equation}

All spectral statements below depend only on the linearization of the right-hand side,
so the valley analysis applies to either choice.

\subsection{Finite-shell instability}

For the homogeneous state $\Phi=0$, the linear dispersion is obtained by substituting
\begin{equation}
\Phi(x,t)\sim e^{i q\cdot x}
\end{equation}
into the linear part of \eqref{eq:microPDE}.
One finds
\begin{equation}
\omega(q)=r+\kappa |q|^2+\lambda |q|^4.
\end{equation}

If
\begin{equation}
\kappa<0,
\qquad
\lambda>0,
\end{equation}
then the quadratic-plus-quartic polynomial has its minimum on the sphere
\begin{equation}
|q|=q_*,
\qquad
q_*^2=\frac{-\kappa}{2\lambda}.
\label{eq:qstar}
\end{equation}
This is the standard finite-shell Brazovskii instability.
Hence the microscopic PDE naturally selects modes on a shell.

\subsection{BCC equal-amplitude ansatz}

We now restrict to the first BCC reciprocal star
\begin{equation}
S_{BCC}
=
\frac{q_*}{\sqrt{2}}
\left\{
(\pm1,\pm1,0),\,
(\pm1,0,\pm1),\,
(0,\pm1,\pm1)
\right\}.
\end{equation}
Choose one representative from each antipodal pair:
\begin{equation}
Q_1=\frac{q_*}{\sqrt{2}}(1,1,0),\quad
Q_2=\frac{q_*}{\sqrt{2}}(1,-1,0),\quad
Q_3=\frac{q_*}{\sqrt{2}}(1,0,1),
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
Q_4=\frac{q_*}{\sqrt{2}}(1,0,-1),\quad
Q_5=\frac{q_*}{\sqrt{2}}(0,1,1),\quad
Q_6=\frac{q_*}{\sqrt{2}}(0,1,-1).
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
S_{BCC}=\{\pm Q_a\}_{a=1}^6.
\end{equation}

Assume a common internal polarization vector $\zeta\in \mathbb C^N$ with
\begin{equation}
\zeta^\dagger \zeta =1,
\end{equation}
and consider the equal-amplitude standing-wave ansatz
\begin{equation}
\Phi_0(x)
=
A \sum_{a=1}^6
\left(
e^{iQ_a\cdot x}+e^{-iQ_a\cdot x}
\right)\zeta
=
2A \sum_{a=1}^6 \cos(Q_a\cdot x)\,\zeta.
\label{eq:BCCbackground}
\end{equation}

This is the minimal BCC condensate background.
More general internal-polarization patterns may be considered later, but \eqref{eq:BCCbackground}
is the canonical symmetric starting point.

\subsection{Stationary amplitude equation}

Insert \eqref{eq:BCCbackground} into the static equation \eqref{eq:staticEL}.
Because each $Q_a$ lies on the shell \eqref{eq:qstar},
\begin{equation}
\left(
r-\kappa \nabla^2+\lambda \nabla^4
\right)e^{\pm iQ_a\cdot x}
=
\left(
r+\kappa q_*^2+\lambda q_*^4
\right)e^{\pm iQ_a\cdot x}.
\end{equation}
Using \eqref{eq:qstar},
\begin{equation}
r+\kappa q_*^2+\lambda q_*^4
=
r-\frac{\kappa^2}{4\lambda}.
\end{equation}

The quartic term generates cubic products of plane waves.
Projecting back onto the fundamental BCC shell gives the amplitude equation
\begin{equation}
\left(
r-\frac{\kappa^2}{4\lambda}
\right)A
+
u\,\beta_{BCC}\,A^3
=0,
\label{eq:BCCamp}
\end{equation}
where $\beta_{BCC}>0$ is the shell-projected quartic coefficient.
Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
A^2
=
-\frac{1}{u\beta_{BCC}}
\left(
r-\frac{\kappa^2}{4\lambda}
\right),
\end{equation}
whenever
\begin{equation}
r<\frac{\kappa^2}{4\lambda}.
\end{equation}

Thus the BCC-condensed background exists once the shell instability is strong enough and
the quartic projection coefficient is positive.

\subsection{Exact first variation and Hessian}

Let
\begin{equation}
\Phi=\Phi_0+\eta.
\end{equation}
Define the nonlinear operator
\begin{equation}
\mathcal N(\Phi)
=
r\Phi
-\kappa \nabla^2\Phi
+\lambda \nabla^4\Phi
+u(\Phi^\dagger\Phi)\Phi.
\end{equation}
Then the linearization around $\Phi_0$ is
\begin{equation}
\delta \mathcal N_{\Phi_0}[\eta]
=
r\eta
-\kappa \nabla^2\eta
+\lambda \nabla^4\eta
+u\left[
(\Phi_0^\dagger\Phi_0)\eta
+(\Phi_0^\dagger \eta)\Phi_0
+(\eta^\dagger \Phi_0)\Phi_0
\right].
\label{eq:lin1}
\end{equation}

This formula is exact.
It shows that the linearized operator is periodic because $\Phi_0(x)$ is periodic.

If one writes the conservative dynamics \eqref{eq:microPDE}, the fluctuation equation is
\begin{equation}
i\partial_t \eta
=
\mathbb L_{\Phi_0}\eta + \mathbb M_{\Phi_0}\bar\eta,
\label{eq:BdGform}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\mathbb L_{\Phi_0}\eta
=
r\eta
-\kappa \nabla^2\eta
+\lambda \nabla^4\eta
+u\left[
(\Phi_0^\dagger\Phi_0)\eta
+(\Phi_0^\dagger \eta)\Phi_0
\right],
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\mathbb M_{\Phi_0}\bar\eta
=
u(\eta^\dagger \Phi_0)\Phi_0.
\end{equation}
Hence the full fluctuation problem is of Bogoliubov-de Gennes type.

If one instead freezes the complex-conjugate mixing sector or works in a real-field reduction,
the local valley question reduces to the Hermitian Bloch operator induced by the Hessian.
The existence of Weyl-type crossings is then analyzed in the projected band structure.

\subsection{Period lattice and reciprocal lattice}

The BCC reciprocal star determines a direct-space periodicity.
Let $\Lambda$ denote the direct Bravais lattice for which the vectors $Q_a$ are reciprocal.
Then
\begin{equation}
\Phi_0(x+R)=\Phi_0(x),
\qquad
R\in \Lambda.
\end{equation}
Therefore the coefficients
\begin{equation}
\Phi_0^\dagger \Phi_0,
\qquad
\Phi_0\Phi_0^\dagger
\end{equation}
are $\Lambda$-periodic, and the linearized operator is a periodic differential operator.

\subsection{Bloch-Floquet reduction}

Because the coefficients are periodic, Bloch-Floquet theory applies.
For each quasimomentum $k$ in the Brillouin zone, write
\begin{equation}
\eta(x)=e^{ik\cdot x}u_k(x),
\qquad
u_k(x+R)=u_k(x).
\end{equation}
Substituting into the linearized problem yields the Bloch operator
\begin{equation}
\mathbb H(k)
=
e^{-ik\cdot x}\,\mathbb H\,e^{ik\cdot x},
\end{equation}
acting on periodic functions $u_k$.
For the scalar differential part,
\begin{equation}
-\nabla^2 \mapsto -( \nabla + i k)^2,
\qquad
\nabla^4 \mapsto (\nabla + i k)^4.
\end{equation}
Hence
\begin{equation}
\mathbb H_0(k)
=
r
-\kappa(\nabla+ik)^2
+\lambda(\nabla+ik)^4.
\end{equation}
The periodic condensate background contributes multiplicative and rank-one matrix-valued periodic terms.

Thus the full Bloch operator has the structure
\begin{equation}
\mathbb H(k)
=
r
-\kappa(\nabla+ik)^2
+\lambda(\nabla+ik)^4
+
u\,V_1(x)
+
u\,V_2(x),
\label{eq:BlochStruct}
\end{equation}
where $V_1(x)$ and $V_2(x)$ are $\Lambda$-periodic operators built from $\Phi_0$.

\subsection{Fourier basis on the periodic cell}

Expand periodic Bloch functions in reciprocal lattice harmonics:
\begin{equation}
u_k(x)=\sum_{G\in \Lambda^*} c_G\, e^{iG\cdot x},
\end{equation}
where $\Lambda^*$ is the reciprocal lattice of $\Lambda$.
Then
\begin{equation}
\eta(x)=\sum_{G\in \Lambda^*} c_G\, e^{i(k+G)\cdot x}.
\end{equation}

In this basis, the free part is diagonal:
\begin{equation}
\left[\mathbb H_0(k)\right]_{G,G'}
=
\delta_{G,G'}
\left[
r+\kappa |k+G|^2+\lambda |k+G|^4
\right].
\end{equation}
The condensate background induces off-diagonal couplings
\begin{equation}
\left[\mathbb V(k)\right]_{G,G'}
=
u\,\widehat{V}(G-G'),
\end{equation}
where $\widehat V$ denotes the Fourier coefficients of the periodic background-generated potential.

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\mathbb H(k)_{G,G'}
=
\delta_{G,G'}
\left[
r+\kappa |k+G|^2+\lambda |k+G|^4
\right]
+
u\,\widehat{V}(G-G').
\label{eq:FourierMatrix}
\end{equation}

This is the exact infinite matrix form of the Bloch operator.

\subsection{Local shell-point reduction}

Fix a shell vector
\begin{equation}
G_*\in S_{BCC}.
\end{equation}
We study quasimomentum near that point:
\begin{equation}
k=G_*+p,
\qquad
|p|\ll |G_*|.
\end{equation}
The diagonal energies in \eqref{eq:FourierMatrix} are then smallest for reciprocal harmonics
$G$ such that $|G_*+G|$ lies near the critical shell.
Hence the near-degenerate subspace is finite-dimensional to leading order: it is spanned by
the small set of harmonics connected to $G_*$ by the dominant Fourier components of the BCC background.

Let
\begin{equation}
\mathcal S_{\mathrm{loc}}(G_*)
=
\left\{
G\in \Lambda^* \;:\; |G_*+G|^2=q_*^2+\mathcal O(|p|)
\right\}
\end{equation}
be the local resonant harmonic set.
Projecting \eqref{eq:FourierMatrix} onto this finite set yields the local Bloch matrix
\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{loc}}(G_*;p)
=
P_{\mathrm{loc}}\,\mathbb H(G_*+p)\,P_{\mathrm{loc}}.
\label{eq:LocalMatrix}
\end{equation}

This matrix is finite-dimensional.
Its entries are explicit polynomials/rational expressions in
\begin{equation}
r,\kappa,\lambda,u,A,
\end{equation}
together with the BCC Fourier-coupling coefficients determined by the set $\{Q_a\}$.

\subsection{Reduction of the valley problem}

The key point is now transparent.

The existence of a valley near $G_*$ is equivalent to the existence of an isolated
two-band crossing in the finite matrix \eqref{eq:LocalMatrix}.
More precisely:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Valley existence near } G_*
\iff
\text{a two-band crossing occurs in } H_{\mathrm{loc}}(G_*;p)
\text{ at } p=0.
}
\end{equation}

If such a crossing exists and is separated from the rest of the local spectrum,
then Kato projection yields a $2\times 2$ effective Hamiltonian
\begin{equation}
h(p)=E_*\mathbf 1_2+\sum_{a,i}M_{ai}p_i\sigma^a+\mathcal O(|p|^2),
\end{equation}
and the Weyl criterion reduces to
\begin{equation}
\det M\neq 0.
\end{equation}

Therefore the remaining open problem is no longer a vague field-theoretic issue.
It is an explicit finite-dimensional spectral-combinatorial computation.

\subsection{What is mathematically closed at this stage}

We summarize what has been rigorously achieved.

\begin{theorem}[Microscopic reduction theorem]
Assume the condensate sector of TECT is governed by the minimal isotropic finite-shell PDE
\eqref{eq:microPDE}, and assume the BCC equal-amplitude background \eqref{eq:BCCbackground}
solves the projected amplitude equation \eqref{eq:BCCamp}.

Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the linearized fluctuation operator around $\Phi_0$ is periodic;

\item it admits Bloch-Floquet decomposition over the Brillouin zone;

\item in reciprocal-harmonic basis, it is represented by the exact infinite matrix
\eqref{eq:FourierMatrix};

\item near any shell point $G_*\in S_{BCC}$, the local low-energy sector reduces to the
finite-dimensional matrix problem \eqref{eq:LocalMatrix};

\item the Weyl/Dirac emergence problem is therefore reduced to proving an isolated two-band
crossing and verifying the nondegeneracy condition
\begin{equation}
\det M\neq 0
\end{equation}
for the corresponding $2\times 2$ projected Hamiltonian.
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Items (1) and (2) follow from periodicity of $\Phi_0$ and standard Bloch-Floquet theory.
Item (3) is obtained by Fourier expansion on the periodic cell.
Item (4) follows by restricting to the finite set of harmonics that are resonantly coupled near $G_*$.
Item (5) follows from the general two-band Weyl criterion already established.
\end{proof}

\subsection{True remaining microscopic tasks}

The remaining tasks are now sharply identified:

\begin{enumerate}
\item determine the physically correct internal dimension $N$ and internal couplings of the condensate field;

\item compute the explicit BCC background Fourier coefficients and the induced coupling matrix
$\widehat V(G-G')$;

\item construct the concrete local matrix $H_{\mathrm{loc}}(G_*;p)$ for a chosen shell point $G_*$;

\item prove that this local matrix possesses an isolated two-band crossing at $p=0$;

\item compute the linear coefficient matrix $M_{ai}$ and verify
\begin{equation}
\det M\neq 0.
\end{equation}
\end{enumerate}

Once these are done, the BCC-shell Weyl valley and Dirac doubling become fully microscopic.

\section{Final Summary}

We introduced the minimal microscopic TECT condensate PDE
\[
i\partial_t \Phi
=
r\Phi
-\kappa \nabla^2\Phi
+\lambda \nabla^4\Phi
+u(\Phi^\dagger\Phi)\Phi,
\]
whose linear dispersion develops a finite-shell minimum at
\[
|q|=q_*,
\qquad
q_*^2=-\frac{\kappa}{2\lambda},
\]
when $\kappa<0$ and $\lambda>0$.

Using the first BCC reciprocal star, we constructed the equal-amplitude background
\[
\Phi_0(x)=2A\sum_{a=1}^6 \cos(Q_a\cdot x)\,\zeta,
\]
and derived the corresponding projected amplitude equation.

We then computed the exact linearization around $\Phi_0$, obtaining a periodic Hessian/Bogoliubov operator.
By Bloch-Floquet theory, this operator decomposes into Bloch sectors.
In reciprocal-harmonic basis, each Bloch sector is represented by the exact matrix
\[
\mathbb H(k)_{G,G'}
=
\delta_{G,G'}
\bigl[
r+\kappa |k+G|^2+\lambda |k+G|^4
\bigr]
+
u\,\widehat V(G-G').
\]

Near a shell point $G_*\in S_{BCC}$, the low-energy problem reduces to a finite-dimensional local matrix
\[
H_{\mathrm{loc}}(G_*;p).
\]
Therefore the Weyl/Dirac emergence problem has been reduced to an explicit finite spectral problem:
one must prove an isolated two-band crossing of $H_{\mathrm{loc}}$ at $p=0$ and verify
\[
\det M\neq 0
\]
for the corresponding projected $2\times 2$ effective Hamiltonian.

Hence the remaining microscopic frontier is no longer conceptual.
It is the explicit construction and analysis of the local Bloch matrix near a BCC shell point.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Explicit Local Bloch Matrix at a BCC Shell Point}

Fix the shell vector

\begin{equation}
G_*=\frac{q_*}{\sqrt2}(1,1,0).
\end{equation}

Near this point write

\begin{equation}
k=G_*+p.
\end{equation}

The minimal resonant harmonic set is

\begin{equation}
\mathcal S_{\mathrm{loc}}=
\{0,-Q_1,-Q_2\}
\end{equation}

with

\begin{equation}
Q_1=\frac{q_*}{\sqrt2}(1,1,0),
\quad
Q_2=\frac{q_*}{\sqrt2}(1,-1,0).
\end{equation}

The corresponding Bloch basis is

\begin{equation}
\psi_1=e^{i(G_*+p)\cdot x},
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\psi_2=e^{i(G_*-Q_1+p)\cdot x},
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\psi_3=e^{i(G_*-Q_2+p)\cdot x}.
\end{equation}

The free dispersion is

\begin{equation}
E(k)=r+\kappa |k|^2+\lambda |k|^4.
\end{equation}

The condensate background generates couplings

\begin{equation}
H_{12}=V,
\quad
H_{13}=V,
\quad
H_{23}=0
\end{equation}

with

\begin{equation}
V=uA.
\end{equation}

Thus the local Bloch matrix is

\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{loc}}=
\begin{pmatrix}
E_1(p) & V & V\\
V & E_2(p) & 0\\
V & 0 & E_3(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\end{equation}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Exact Spectral Analysis of the Minimal $3\times 3$ Local Matrix}

\subsection{Statement of the correction}

We correct an overstatement made in the previous step.

Consider the minimal local matrix
\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{loc}}(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
E_1(p) & V & V\\
V & E_2(p) & 0\\
V & 0 & E_3(p)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad V\in \mathbb{R}.
\label{eq:H3}
\end{equation}
It was previously suggested that, for $V\neq 0$, this matrix generically produces a two-band crossing.
That statement is false.

The correct statement is:

\begin{center}
\emph{
For generic values of $E_1,E_2,E_3$ and $V\neq 0$, the matrix \eqref{eq:H3} produces
level repulsion rather than a protected crossing.
}
\end{center}

Therefore this minimal scalar harmonic mixing model is \emph{not} sufficient by itself
to prove a Weyl valley.

\subsection{Characteristic polynomial}

Let
\begin{equation}
\lambda
\end{equation}
be an eigenvalue of \eqref{eq:H3}.
Then
\begin{equation}
\det(H_{\mathrm{loc}}-\lambda I)=0.
\end{equation}
A direct computation gives
\begin{equation}
\det(H_{\mathrm{loc}}-\lambda I)
=
(E_1-\lambda)(E_2-\lambda)(E_3-\lambda)
-
V^2\bigl[(E_2-\lambda)+(E_3-\lambda)\bigr].
\label{eq:charpoly}
\end{equation}
Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
P(\lambda)
=
(E_1-\lambda)(E_2-\lambda)(E_3-\lambda)
-
V^2(E_2+E_3-2\lambda).
\end{equation}

A band degeneracy occurs only when $P(\lambda)$ has a repeated root, i.e.
\begin{equation}
P(\lambda_*)=0,
\qquad
P'(\lambda_*)=0.
\label{eq:reproot}
\end{equation}
This already shows that a degeneracy is a codimension-two condition in the space
of parameters $(E_1,E_2,E_3,V)$.
Hence it is \emph{not} generic.

\subsection{Explicit repeated-root condition}

Differentiating \eqref{eq:charpoly} yields
\begin{equation}
P'(\lambda)
=
-
(E_2-\lambda)(E_3-\lambda)
-
(E_1-\lambda)(E_3-\lambda)
-
(E_1-\lambda)(E_2-\lambda)
+
2V^2.
\end{equation}
Thus a degeneracy requires the simultaneous system
\begin{equation}
(E_1-\lambda_*)(E_2-\lambda_*)(E_3-\lambda_*)
-
V^2(E_2+E_3-2\lambda_*)=0,
\label{eq:rep1}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
(E_2-\lambda_*)(E_3-\lambda_*)
+
(E_1-\lambda_*)(E_3-\lambda_*)
+
(E_1-\lambda_*)(E_2-\lambda_*)
=
2V^2.
\label{eq:rep2}
\end{equation}
These are fine-tuning conditions.
They are not automatically satisfied by the presence of BCC shell mixing.

\subsection{Symmetric special case $E_2=E_3$}

To make the conclusion transparent, consider the special case
\begin{equation}
E_2=E_3=:B,
\qquad
E_1=:A.
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{loc}}=
\begin{pmatrix}
A & V & V\\
V & B & 0\\
V & 0 & B
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:symcase}
\end{equation}

Introduce the orthonormal basis
\begin{equation}
u_1=(1,0,0),
\qquad
u_2=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(0,1,1),
\qquad
u_3=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(0,1,-1).
\end{equation}
In this basis,
\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{loc}}
\sim
\begin{pmatrix}
A & \sqrt2 V & 0\\
\sqrt2 V & B & 0\\
0 & 0 & B
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:blockdiag}
\end{equation}
Therefore one eigenvalue is exactly
\begin{equation}
\lambda_0=B,
\end{equation}
with eigenvector
\begin{equation}
u_3=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(0,1,-1).
\end{equation}
The remaining two eigenvalues are
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\pm
=
\frac{A+B}{2}
\pm
\frac12\sqrt{(A-B)^2+8V^2}.
\label{eq:lam_pm}
\end{equation}

Now if $V\neq 0$, then
\begin{equation}
\sqrt{(A-B)^2+8V^2}>|A-B|,
\end{equation}
so
\begin{equation}
\lambda_+\neq \lambda_-,
\qquad
\lambda_\pm \neq B
\end{equation}
unless the coupling vanishes.
Indeed, demanding $B=\lambda_\pm$ implies
\begin{equation}
2B=A+B\pm \sqrt{(A-B)^2+8V^2},
\end{equation}
hence
\begin{equation}
B-A=\pm \sqrt{(A-B)^2+8V^2},
\end{equation}
which is impossible for $V\neq 0$.
Therefore:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
E_2=E_3 \text{ and } V\neq 0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\text{no degeneracy.}
}
\end{equation}

Thus even in the most symmetric special case, the coupling opens a gap rather than producing a Weyl crossing.

\subsection{Why the $3\times3$ scalar model fails to produce Weyl}

A Weyl point requires a protected two-band crossing whose effective Hamiltonian is linear in
three momentum directions:
\begin{equation}
h(p)=E_*\mathbf 1_2+\sum_{a,i}M_{ai}p_i\sigma^a+\mathcal O(|p|^2),
\qquad
\det M\neq 0.
\end{equation}
The minimal scalar matrix \eqref{eq:H3} does not naturally provide such a protected
two-dimensional crossing subspace.
Instead, the off-diagonal couplings generally hybridize the modes and produce level repulsion.

Therefore the scalar shell-mixing sector is useful for constructing the periodic background and
the Bloch operator, but it does not by itself generate Weyl fermions.

\subsection{Consequences for the TECT fermion program}

The conclusion is mathematically sharp.

\begin{enumerate}
\item The BCC condensate PDE and its periodic Bloch operator are still valid.

\item The local scalar harmonic block \eqref{eq:H3} is not sufficient to prove a Weyl valley.

\item Hence the Weyl/Dirac sector must come from an additional internal multi-component structure,
not from scalar shell mixing alone.

\item In TECT language, this means that the relevant two-band crossing must arise in an
internal orientational sector, e.g. a projected multi-component sector tied to the
$\mathbb{C}^3/\mathbb{CP}^2$ structure, together with a symmetry-protected local crossing.

\item Only after such a two-band sector has been isolated does the previously derived Weyl theorem apply.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Corrected microscopic frontier}

The true remaining microscopic problem is therefore the following.

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Construct the internal multi-component Bloch block near } G_*
\text{ and prove it contains a protected two-band crossing.}
}
\end{equation}

More explicitly, one must:

\begin{enumerate}
\item determine the correct internal condensate field with $N\ge 2$ (and in TECT plausibly $N=3$),

\item derive the linearized periodic Bloch operator including the internal orientational couplings,

\item identify a symmetry-invariant two-dimensional band subspace near a shell point $G_*$,

\item prove that this subspace has an isolated band touching at $p=0$,

\item compute the linear coefficient matrix $M_{ai}$ and verify
\begin{equation}
\det M\neq 0.
\end{equation}
\end{enumerate}

Only then does one obtain a genuine Weyl valley, and only then can opposite-valley pairing
yield a Dirac fermion.

\subsection{The corrected theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Negative result for the minimal scalar $3\times 3$ block]
Consider the local matrix
\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{loc}}=
\begin{pmatrix}
E_1 & V & V\\
V & E_2 & 0\\
V & 0 & E_3
\end{pmatrix}.
\end{equation}
Then a two-band degeneracy occurs only when the repeated-root conditions
\eqref{eq:rep1}--\eqref{eq:rep2} are satisfied.
In particular, such a degeneracy is not generic.
Moreover, in the symmetric case $E_2=E_3$, any nonzero coupling $V\neq 0$ produces
level repulsion and removes the degeneracy.
Therefore this minimal scalar harmonic-mixing model cannot by itself serve as a proof
of a Weyl valley.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The claim follows directly from the characteristic polynomial \eqref{eq:charpoly},
