the repeated-root criterion \eqref{eq:reproot}, and the explicit diagonalization
\eqref{eq:blockdiag}--\eqref{eq:lam_pm} in the symmetric case.
\end{proof}

\section{Final Summary}

We analyzed exactly the minimal local matrix
\[
H_{\mathrm{loc}}=
\begin{pmatrix}
E_1 & V & V\\
V & E_2 & 0\\
V & 0 & E_3
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Its characteristic polynomial is
\[
P(\lambda)
=
(E_1-\lambda)(E_2-\lambda)(E_3-\lambda)
-
V^2(E_2+E_3-2\lambda).
\]
A two-band crossing requires a repeated root of $P$, hence the simultaneous conditions
\[
P(\lambda_*)=0,
\qquad
P'(\lambda_*)=0.
\]
Therefore the degeneracy is a fine-tuned condition and is not generic.

In the special symmetric case $E_2=E_3=B$, the matrix is explicitly diagonalized into
\[
\begin{pmatrix}
A & \sqrt2 V & 0\\
\sqrt2 V & B & 0\\
0 & 0 & B
\end{pmatrix},
\]
with eigenvalues
\[
B,
\qquad
\frac{A+B}{2}\pm \frac12\sqrt{(A-B)^2+8V^2}.
\]
For any $V\neq 0$, these are nondegenerate.
Thus the coupling opens a gap rather than producing a Weyl crossing.

Hence the minimal scalar BCC shell-mixing block is insufficient for Weyl/Dirac emergence.
The true microscopic frontier is to construct the internal multi-component Bloch block
associated with the TECT orientational sector and to prove that it contains a symmetry-protected
two-band crossing with
\[
\det M\neq 0.
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Internal \texorpdfstring{$\mathbb C^3$}{C3} Bloch Block and the Minimal Protected Two-Band Sector}

\subsection{Aim}

The previous scalar $3\times 3$ harmonic-mixing model showed that
pure shell mixing does not generically produce a Weyl point.
Therefore the fermionic sector of TECT must arise from an additional internal multi-component structure.

The minimal candidate compatible with the already established TECT chain is
\begin{equation}
\Phi(x)\in \mathbb C^3,
\end{equation}
which is also the natural precursor of the projective internal manifold
\begin{equation}
\mathbb{CP}^2 \simeq \frac{SU(3)}{U(2)}.
\end{equation}

The purpose of this section is to construct the minimal local Bloch symbol acting on
$\mathbb C^3$, identify the symmetry-adapted $1\oplus 2$ decomposition near a BCC shell point,
and show how a protected two-band crossing can arise in the doublet sector.

\subsection{Microscopic linearization in an internal \texorpdfstring{$\mathbb C^3$}{C3} sector}

Let the condensate field be
\begin{equation}
\Phi(x,t)\in \mathbb C^3.
\end{equation}
After linearization around a periodic BCC-condensed background, Bloch reduction near a shell point
$G_*\in S_{BCC}$ yields a local Hermitian operator
\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{int}}(G_*+p):\mathbb C^3\to \mathbb C^3,
\qquad
|p|\ll |G_*|.
\end{equation}
We assume analyticity in $p$:
\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{int}}(G_*+p)
=
H_0 + \sum_{i=1}^3 p_i\,\Gamma_i + \mathcal O(|p|^2),
\label{eq:int_expand}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
H_0^\dagger = H_0,
\qquad
\Gamma_i^\dagger = \Gamma_i.
\end{equation}

At this stage we do not yet claim that the coefficients $(H_0,\Gamma_i)$ have been computed
ab initio from the full TECT PDE.
Rather, we ask the mathematically sharper question:

\begin{center}
\emph{
What is the minimal symmetry structure on a $\mathbb C^3$ Bloch block that can support
a protected two-band crossing?
}
\end{center}

\subsection{Distinguished direction and little-group decomposition}

Fix the shell point
\begin{equation}
G_*=\frac{q_*}{\sqrt 2}(1,1,0),
\qquad
\hat n := \frac{G_*}{|G_*|}.
\end{equation}
The point $G_*$ breaks full rotational symmetry down to the little group preserving $\hat n$.
At the level of the local internal block, the most natural decomposition is the splitting of $\mathbb C^3$
into a longitudinal line and a transverse plane:
\begin{equation}
\mathbb C^3 = \mathbb C \hat n \oplus \hat n^\perp_{\mathbb C}.
\end{equation}
Choose an orthonormal basis
\begin{equation}
e_\parallel := \hat n,
\qquad
e_1, e_2\in \hat n^\perp,
\qquad
\{e_\parallel,e_1,e_2\} \text{ orthonormal}.
\end{equation}
Then the internal space decomposes as
\begin{equation}
\mathbf 3 \to \mathbf 1 \oplus \mathbf 2,
\label{eq:1plus2}
\end{equation}
where the singlet is the longitudinal mode and the doublet is the transverse internal sector.

This is the crucial structural point:
the natural candidate for a two-band crossing is not the full $\mathbb C^3$ space,
but the transverse doublet subspace.

\subsection{Minimal symmetry constraint at the shell point}

Assume that the shell point preserves an effective internal-transverse $SO(2)$ symmetry
acting on the plane spanned by $(e_1,e_2)$.
Equivalently, in complex notation define
\begin{equation}
e_\pm := \frac{1}{\sqrt2}(e_1 \pm i e_2).
\end{equation}
Then the doublet sector carries a two-dimensional irreducible representation.

The minimal symmetry statement is that at $p=0$ the Bloch symbol commutes with the
little-group action on the transverse plane:
\begin{equation}
[H_0, R(\theta)] = 0,
\qquad
R(\theta)\big|_{\mathrm{span}\{e_1,e_2\}} \in SO(2).
\end{equation}
By Schur's lemma on the irreducible doublet block, the restriction of $H_0$ to the doublet sector
must be proportional to the identity:
\begin{equation}
H_0\big|_{\mathbf 2} = E_*\,\mathbf 1_2.
\end{equation}
Therefore, in the basis $(e_\parallel,e_1,e_2)$,
\begin{equation}
H_0=
\begin{pmatrix}
E_\parallel & 0 & 0\\
0 & E_* & 0\\
0 & 0 & E_*
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:H0form}
\end{equation}

This is the desired protected two-fold degeneracy:
it is symmetry-enforced inside the transverse doublet sector.

\subsection{Why the scalar model failed and the internal model succeeds}

In the scalar harmonic model, the $3\times3$ block had no representation-theoretic reason
to contain an invariant two-dimensional degenerate subspace.
Consequently, generic off-diagonal couplings opened gaps.

Here the situation is different.
The doublet sector in \eqref{eq:1plus2} is an invariant irreducible representation of the shell little group.
Hence the degeneracy at $p=0$ is not accidental but symmetry-protected.

This is exactly the missing structural ingredient required for Weyl emergence.

\subsection{Momentum expansion adapted to the shell geometry}

Decompose
\begin{equation}
p = p_\parallel \hat n + p_1 e_1 + p_2 e_2.
\end{equation}
The most general Hermitian linear correction compatible with the longitudinal/transverse splitting is
\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{int}}(p)
=
H_0
+
p_\parallel \Gamma_\parallel
+
p_1 \Gamma_1
+
p_2 \Gamma_2
+
\mathcal O(|p|^2).
\end{equation}

We now separate two logically distinct cases.

\paragraph{Case A: exact decoupling of singlet and doublet to linear order.}
If the singlet does not mix with the doublet at linear order, then after projection to the doublet sector
one obtains
\begin{equation}
h_{\mathbf 2}(p)
=
E_* \mathbf 1_2
+
p_\parallel A_\parallel
+
p_1 A_1
+
p_2 A_2
+
\mathcal O(|p|^2),
\label{eq:doublet1}
\end{equation}
where $A_\mu$ are Hermitian $2\times 2$ matrices.

\paragraph{Case B: weak singlet-doublet mixing.}
If the singlet mixes weakly with the doublet but remains gapped by
\begin{equation}
\Delta := E_\parallel - E_* \neq 0,
\end{equation}
then the singlet can be integrated out by Schur complement or degenerate perturbation theory.
To first order in $p$, the effective doublet Hamiltonian is still of the form \eqref{eq:doublet1},
with corrections only entering at higher order:
\begin{equation}
h_{\mathbf 2}^{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
E_* \mathbf 1_2
+
p_\parallel \widetilde A_\parallel
+
p_1 \widetilde A_1
+
p_2 \widetilde A_2
+
\mathcal O(|p|^2).
\end{equation}

Hence in either case the local valley problem reduces to a $2\times2$ Hermitian linearized symbol.

\subsection{General form of the projected doublet Hamiltonian}

Every Hermitian $2\times2$ matrix can be expanded in the Pauli basis:
\begin{equation}
\widetilde A_\mu = a_\mu \mathbf 1_2 + \sum_{b=1}^3 M_{b\mu}\sigma^b.
\end{equation}
Therefore
\begin{equation}
h_{\mathbf 2}^{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
E_* \mathbf 1_2
+
\left(\sum_{\mu=\parallel,1,2} a_\mu p_\mu\right)\mathbf 1_2
+
\sum_{b=1}^3
\left(
\sum_{\mu=\parallel,1,2} M_{b\mu} p_\mu
\right)\sigma^b
+
\mathcal O(|p|^2).
\label{eq:pauli_form}
\end{equation}
Define the $3\times3$ coefficient matrix
\begin{equation}
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
M_{1\parallel} & M_{11} & M_{12}\\
M_{2\parallel} & M_{21} & M_{22}\\
M_{3\parallel} & M_{31} & M_{32}
\end{pmatrix}.
\end{equation}
Then the Weyl criterion is exactly
\begin{equation}
\det M \neq 0.
\label{eq:weylcriterion}
\end{equation}

Thus the entire fermionic problem has been reduced to proving that the projected doublet sector
exists and that the linear coefficient matrix is nondegenerate.

\subsection{Minimal rotationally adapted form}

If, in addition, the transverse doublet respects the natural rotational structure of the shell tangent plane,
then by a suitable unitary basis change within the doublet one can bring \eqref{eq:pauli_form} to the canonical form
\begin{equation}
h_{\mathbf 2}^{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
E_* \mathbf 1_2
+
c_0(p)\mathbf 1_2
+
\lambda_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
(\alpha p_1-\beta p_2)\sigma^1
+
(\beta p_1+\alpha p_2)\sigma^2
+
\mathcal O(|p|^2),
\label{eq:canonicalweyl}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
c_0(p)=a_\parallel p_\parallel + a_1 p_1 + a_2 p_2.
\end{equation}
In this basis,
\begin{equation}
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & \alpha & -\beta\\
0 & \beta & \alpha\\
\lambda_\parallel & 0 & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\det M = \lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\end{equation}
Hence the Weyl condition becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2)\neq 0.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Protected two-band crossing theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Minimal protected doublet crossing theorem]
Let
\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{int}}(G_*+p)=H_0+\sum_{i=1}^3 p_i\Gamma_i+\mathcal O(|p|^2)
\end{equation}
be a local Hermitian Bloch symbol acting on $\mathbb C^3$ near a BCC shell point $G_*$.
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item The internal space decomposes under the shell little group as
\begin{equation}
\mathbb C^3 = \mathbf 1 \oplus \mathbf 2.
\end{equation}

\item The $p=0$ symbol commutes with the little-group action, so that
\begin{equation}
H_0=
\begin{pmatrix}
E_\parallel & 0\\
0 & E_* \mathbf 1_2
\end{pmatrix}.
\end{equation}

\item The singlet is either decoupled from the doublet to linear order, or gapped and perturbatively eliminable.

\item The projected linear doublet Hamiltonian has coefficient matrix $M$ satisfying
\begin{equation}
\det M\neq 0.
\end{equation}
\end{enumerate}

Then the doublet sector contains a Weyl point at $p=0$.
Its local effective Hamiltonian is of the form
\begin{equation}
h_{\mathbf 2}^{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
E_* \mathbf 1_2
+
\sum_{b,\mu} M_{b\mu} p_\mu \sigma^b
+
\mathcal O(|p|^2),
\end{equation}
and the topological charge is
\begin{equation}
\chi=\operatorname{sgn}(\det M).
\end{equation}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By assumptions (1)--(3), the local problem reduces to a projected $2\times2$ Hermitian symbol
on the doublet sector.
Every such symbol admits a Pauli expansion, yielding \eqref{eq:pauli_form}.
If $\det M\neq 0$, then the Pauli vector
\begin{equation}
d(p)=Mp+\mathcal O(|p|^2)
\end{equation}
is invertible to linear order.
Hence the band splitting is linear and isolated in all three directions.
This is exactly a Weyl cone.
The chirality is the degree of the map $\hat d:S^2\to S^2$, equal to $\operatorname{sgn}(\det M)$.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Dirac doubling}

Let the opposite shell point be $-G_*$.
If the corresponding doublet block satisfies the pairing relation
\begin{equation}
h_{-}(p)=-h_{+}(-p)
\end{equation}
after subtraction of the scalar part,
then the two Weyl sectors have opposite chirality and combine into a Dirac block:
\begin{equation}
H_D(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\end{equation}
Thus the full TECT fermion chain becomes

\begin{equation}
\mathbb C^3 \text{ internal block}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
(\mathbf 1\oplus \mathbf 2)\text{ decomposition}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{protected doublet crossing}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{Weyl valley}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{Dirac doubling}.
\end{equation}

\subsection{What is proven and what remains open}

What is now mathematically clear is the following:

\begin{enumerate}
\item The scalar shell-mixing mechanism is insufficient.

\item A $\mathbb C^3$ internal Bloch block naturally admits a $1\oplus2$ decomposition at a shell point.

\item The transverse doublet provides the correct carrier space for a protected two-band crossing.

\item Once the projected linear coefficient matrix is nondegenerate, Weyl follows rigorously.
\end{enumerate}

What remains open is the genuinely microscopic part:

\begin{enumerate}
\item derive the $\mathbb C^3$ internal Bloch symbol from the full TECT condensate PDE,

\item prove that the shell little group indeed enforces the $1\oplus2$ decomposition,

\item compute the projected doublet matrices explicitly,

\item verify
\begin{equation}
\det M\neq 0
\end{equation}
ab initio.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the true frontier is no longer conceptual.
It is the explicit microscopic derivation of the protected doublet block.

\section{Final Summary}

The minimal scalar harmonic block cannot generically produce Weyl points, because it lacks a
symmetry-protected two-dimensional degenerate subspace.

The correct minimal structure is an internal Bloch block acting on
\[
\mathbb C^3,
\]
which, near a BCC shell point $G_*$, naturally decomposes as
\[
\mathbb C^3 = \mathbf 1 \oplus \mathbf 2
\]
into a longitudinal singlet and a transverse doublet.

If the shell little group preserves the doublet and forces
\[
H_0\big|_{\mathbf 2}=E_*\mathbf 1_2,
\]
then the doublet carries a protected two-band crossing at $p=0$.
After projection, the local effective Hamiltonian is
\[
h_{\mathbf 2}^{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
E_* \mathbf 1_2
+
\sum_{b,\mu} M_{b\mu} p_\mu \sigma^b
+
\mathcal O(|p|^2).
\]
If
\[
\det M\neq 0,
\]
this crossing is a Weyl point, with chirality
\[
\chi=\operatorname{sgn}(\det M).
\]
If the opposite shell point $-G_*$ supplies the opposite-chirality partner, the two Weyl blocks combine into a Dirac fermion.

Hence the remaining microscopic frontier is:
derive the internal $\mathbb C^3$ Bloch block from the full TECT condensate PDE and compute the projected doublet coefficient matrix explicitly.

%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Little Group at a BCC Shell Point and the Exact Status of the \texorpdfstring{$\mathbf 3\to \mathbf 1\oplus \mathbf 2$}{3→1⊕2} Decomposition}

\subsection{Aim}

We now clarify the precise representation-theoretic status of the decomposition
\[
\mathbb C^3 = \mathbf 1 \oplus \mathbf 2
\]
near a BCC shell point.

A crucial distinction must be made between:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the \emph{kinematic orthogonal splitting}
into longitudinal and transverse subspaces, which is always true;

\item a \emph{symmetry-protected two-dimensional irreducible doublet}, which is not automatic
for the bare discrete cubic little group.
\end{enumerate}

The purpose of this section is to separate these two statements rigorously.

\subsection{Fixed shell point and stabilizer notion}

Fix
\begin{equation}
G_*=\frac{q_*}{\sqrt2}(1,1,0),
\qquad
\hat n:=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|}=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,1,0).
\end{equation}

Let \(G_{\mathrm{cubic}}\) denote the symmetry group of the condensed BCC background at the relevant linearized level.
At the purely geometric level this is a subgroup of the cubic point group \(O_h\).

Define the little group of the shell point by
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{LG}(G_*)
:=
\{ g\in G_{\mathrm{cubic}} \;:\; gG_*=G_* \}.
\end{equation}
If one instead stabilizes only the shell line \(\mathbb R G_*\), one obtains the slightly larger group
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{LG}_{\pm}(G_*)
:=
\{ g\in G_{\mathrm{cubic}} \;:\; gG_*=\pm G_* \}.
\end{equation}

The distinction between \(\mathrm{LG}\) and \(\mathrm{LG}_{\pm}\) is not essential for the orthogonal splitting below,
but it matters for the detailed representation theory.

\subsection{What is always true: longitudinal/transverse splitting}

Independently of the detailed discrete symmetry, the vector space \(\mathbb R^3\) admits the orthogonal decomposition
\begin{equation}
\mathbb R^3 = \mathbb R \hat n \oplus \hat n^\perp,
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\hat n^\perp := \{ v\in \mathbb R^3 : v\cdot \hat n = 0 \}.
\end{equation}
Complexifying,
\begin{equation}
\mathbb C^3 = \mathbb C \hat n \oplus \hat n^\perp_{\mathbb C}.
\label{eq:kin_split}
\end{equation}

This is a purely linear-algebraic fact.
It does not require any group-theoretic assumption beyond the existence of the distinguished shell direction \(\hat n\).

\begin{proposition}[Kinematic splitting]
For every nonzero shell point \(G_*\), the internal vector space \(\mathbb C^3\) decomposes canonically as
\begin{equation}
\mathbb C^3 = \mathbb C\hat n \oplus \hat n^\perp_{\mathbb C}.
\end{equation}
The first summand is the longitudinal line and the second summand is the transverse plane.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Immediate from orthogonal projection:
\begin{equation}
v=(v\cdot \hat n)\hat n + \bigl(v-(v\cdot \hat n)\hat n\bigr).
\end{equation}
The second term is orthogonal to \(\hat n\).
Complexification preserves the direct-sum decomposition.
\end{proof}

Thus the statement
\begin{equation}
\mathbf 3 \to \mathbf 1 \oplus \mathbf 2
\end{equation}
is always valid as a \emph{vector-space splitting}.
However, it is not yet a statement about irreducible representations.

\subsection{What is not automatic: protected doublet structure}

To obtain a symmetry-protected two-band crossing, one needs more than the kinematic splitting \eqref{eq:kin_split}.
One needs the transverse plane to carry a two-dimensional symmetry sector that remains degenerate at \(p=0\).

This requires that the linearized shell-point Hamiltonian \(H_0\) act on the transverse plane as
\begin{equation}
H_0\big|_{\hat n^\perp_{\mathbb C}} = E_* \mathbf 1_2.
\label{eq:doubletdeg}
\end{equation}
The question is whether \eqref{eq:doubletdeg} is forced by the exact little group of the BCC shell point.

In general, the answer is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{No. Bare discrete cubic little-group symmetry does not by itself force } \eqref{eq:doubletdeg}.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Why the bare cubic little group is insufficient}

For a generic shell direction in a cubic environment, the exact little group is discrete.
For the direction \([110]\), the stabilizer inside the cubic group is a finite subgroup, not the continuous group \(SO(2)\).

A discrete little group may preserve the transverse plane as a subspace, yet still allow the restriction of \(H_0\)
to split into two inequivalent one-dimensional pieces:
\begin{equation}
\hat n^\perp_{\mathbb C} \cong \mathbf 1 \oplus \mathbf 1
\qquad
\text{(as representations of the discrete little group).}
\end{equation}
If this happens, then symmetry permits
\begin{equation}
H_0\big|_{\hat n^\perp_{\mathbb C}}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
E_1 & 0\\
0 & E_2
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
E_1\neq E_2,
\end{equation}
and the doublet degeneracy is lost.

Therefore:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{transverse-plane invariance} \;\not\Rightarrow\; \text{transverse doublet degeneracy}.
}
\end{equation}

This is the exact reason why the previous step must be refined.

\subsection{The correct sufficient condition: emergent transverse \texorpdfstring{$SO(2)$}{SO(2)}}

The degeneracy \eqref{eq:doubletdeg} is guaranteed if the shell-point linearized problem has an effective continuous
rotational symmetry on the transverse plane.

Assume there exists a unitary representation
\begin{equation}
R(\theta):\hat n^\perp_{\mathbb C}\to \hat n^\perp_{\mathbb C},
\qquad
\theta\in [0,2\pi),
\end{equation}
of \(SO(2)\) (or \(O(2)\)) such that
\begin{equation}
[H_0,R(\theta)]=0
\qquad
\forall \theta.
\label{eq:SO2comm}
\end{equation}
Then \(H_0\) is rotation-invariant on the transverse plane.
Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
H_0\big|_{\hat n^\perp_{\mathbb C}} = E_* \mathbf 1_2.
\end{equation}

\begin{proposition}[Transverse isotropy implies protected doublet]
If the shell-point Hamiltonian \(H_0\) commutes with an effective transverse \(SO(2)\) action,
then the transverse plane is exactly degenerate:
\begin{equation}
H_0\big|_{\hat n^\perp_{\mathbb C}} = E_* \mathbf 1_2.
\end{equation}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
A Hermitian operator commuting with all planar rotations must be proportional to the identity on that plane.
This follows because the commutant of the standard \(SO(2)\) action on \(\mathbb R^2\) is generated by the identity,
and Hermiticity removes the antisymmetric generator.
After complexification the same conclusion holds.
\end{proof}

Thus the correct logic is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{effective transverse }SO(2)
\Rightarrow
\text{protected transverse doublet}.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{TECT interpretation of the extra condition}

In TECT, the natural origin of this effective transverse \(SO(2)\) is not the exact microscopic cubic symmetry itself,
but the already-emergent infrared isotropy of the condensed medium.

More precisely:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the BCC background selects a distinguished shell direction \(\hat n\);

\item the long-wavelength linearized sector splits into one longitudinal and two transverse polarizations;

\item in the infrared, cubic anisotropy is suppressed by higher-order corrections;

\item therefore the leading transverse sector acquires an effective \(SO(2)\) symmetry.
\end{enumerate}

Hence the protected doublet is not a purely crystallographic statement.
It is a statement about the \emph{infrared isotropic shell-point theory}.

\subsection{Corrected decomposition theorem}

We can now formulate the exact theorem that is justified at the present stage.

\begin{theorem}[Corrected shell-point decomposition theorem]
Let \(G_*\neq 0\) be a shell point of the BCC reciprocal star.

\begin{enumerate}
\item The internal vector space \(\mathbb C^3\) always admits the canonical orthogonal splitting
\begin{equation}
\mathbb C^3 = \mathbb C\hat n \oplus \hat n^\perp_{\mathbb C},
\qquad
\hat n=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|}.
\end{equation}

\item This yields a kinematic longitudinal/transverse decomposition
\begin{equation}
\mathbf 3 \to \mathbf 1 \oplus (\text{2D transverse plane}).
\end{equation}

\item However, the exact discrete cubic little group of \(G_*\) does not by itself force the transverse plane
to be a symmetry-protected degenerate doublet.

\item A protected two-band transverse sector is guaranteed if the shell-point Hamiltonian possesses an effective
transverse \(SO(2)\) symmetry, equivalently
\begin{equation}
H_0\big|_{\hat n^\perp_{\mathbb C}} = E_*\mathbf 1_2.
\end{equation}
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Item (1) is linear algebra.
Item (2) is simply a rephrasing of the direct sum.
Item (3) follows because a discrete little group may preserve the plane without forcing scalar action on it.
Item (4) follows from the commutant argument for the \(SO(2)\) action.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Consequences for Weyl emergence}

Once the protected transverse doublet exists, the local shell-point Hamiltonian projected to that doublet has the form
\begin{equation}
h_{\mathbf 2}(p)
=
E_* \mathbf 1_2
+
\sum_{b,\mu} M_{b\mu} p_\mu \sigma^b
+
\mathcal O(|p|^2),
\qquad
\mu\in\{\parallel,1,2\}.
\end{equation}
The Weyl condition is then
\begin{equation}
\det M\neq 0.
\end{equation}

Therefore the exact remaining nontrivial step is not the kinematic splitting itself, but proving the emergence of the
transverse \(SO(2)\) shell-point symmetry strongly enough to protect the doublet.

\subsection{True frontier after this refinement}

The microscopic frontier is now sharply localized:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{TECT PDE}
\Rightarrow
\text{infrared shell-point transverse } SO(2)
\Rightarrow
\text{protected doublet}
\Rightarrow
\det M\neq 0.
}
\end{equation}

Thus the real missing theorem is:

\begin{center}
\emph{
an infrared shell-point isotropy theorem strong enough to force
\(
H_0|_{\hat n^\perp_{\mathbb C}}=E_*\mathbf 1_2
\).
}
\end{center}

\section{Final Summary}

At a BCC shell point \(G_*\), the internal space \(\mathbb C^3\) always splits canonically as
\[
\mathbb C^3=\mathbb C\hat n\oplus \hat n^\perp_{\mathbb C},
\]
that is, into one longitudinal line and one transverse two-plane.

This statement is purely kinematic and always true.

However, the stronger statement that the transverse plane forms a symmetry-protected degenerate
two-band doublet is not automatic from the bare discrete cubic little group.
A discrete little group may preserve the transverse plane while still allowing it to split into two nondegenerate one-dimensional sectors.

Therefore the correct sufficient condition for a protected transverse doublet is an effective shell-point transverse
\(SO(2)\) symmetry:
\[
[H_0,R(\theta)]=0
\quad\Rightarrow\quad
H_0|_{\hat n^\perp_{\mathbb C}}=E_*\mathbf 1_2.
\]

Hence the true microscopic frontier is not the existence of the longitudinal/transverse splitting,
but the derivation of an infrared shell-point isotropy theorem strong enough to enforce transverse degeneracy.
Once that is obtained, the projected doublet Hamiltonian takes the Weyl form
\[
h_{\mathbf 2}(p)=E_*\mathbf 1_2+\sum_{b,\mu}M_{b\mu}p_\mu\sigma^b+\mathcal O(|p|^2),
\]
and the Weyl criterion is simply
\[
\det M\neq 0.
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{BCC Shell Valley Existence Theorem}

\begin{theorem}
Let a TECT condensate arise from a shell instability

\[
r(k)=r_0+c(|k|^2-q_*^2)^2 .
\]

Let the condensate select the BCC star

\[
S_{BCC}
=
\frac{q_*}{\sqrt2}
\{
(\pm1,\pm1,0),
(\pm1,0,\pm1),
(0,\pm1,\pm1)
\}.
\]

Then each shell vector

\[
G_* \in S_{BCC}
\]

is a stationary point of the dispersion relation.

Near the shell point

\[
k = G_* + p
\]

the quadratic kernel expands as

\[
r(k)
=
r_0
+
4c(G_*\cdot p)^2
+
\mathcal O(p^3).
\]

Thus the shell forms a degenerate manifold in the transverse directions.

If the internal sector provides a protected two-band doublet,
the projected effective Hamiltonian takes the form

\[
h(p)
=
\sum_{i,j} M_{ij} p_j \sigma^i.
\]

If

\[
\det M \neq 0
\]

the shell point is a Weyl valley.
\end{theorem}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Weyl-Coefficient Extraction from a Minimal TECT Condensate PDE Ansatz}

\subsection{Statement of scope}

We do \emph{not} claim here that the full microscopic TECT condensate PDE has already been derived uniquely from first principles.
What \emph{is} already available is the theorem-level reduction of the local Weyl problem to the three coefficients
\[
\lambda_\parallel,\qquad \alpha,\qquad \beta,
\]
together with the identity
\[
\det M=\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\]
The purpose of this section is to perform the explicit coefficient calculation for the minimal symmetry-compatible shell-enriched ansatz.

\subsection{Selected BCC shell pair and adapted frame}

Fix a selected reciprocal shell pair
\[
\pm G_* \in S_{\mathrm{BCC}},
\qquad
\hat n := \frac{G_*}{|G_*|}.
\]
Choose an orthonormal adapted frame
\[
\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\},
\qquad
e_a\cdot \hat n = 0,
\qquad
e_a\cdot e_b=\delta_{ab},
\quad a,b=1,2.
\]
Decompose the small shell-relative momentum as
\[
p = p_\parallel \hat n + p_1 e_1 + p_2 e_2.
\]

\subsection{Minimal shell-enriched first-order symbol}

On the enriched shell space
\[
H_{\mathrm{enr}}=\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}},
\]
let $\tau^i$ act on the shell-pair factor and $s_\alpha$ on the internal doublet.
The minimal axial-equivariant first-order ansatz is
\begin{equation}
L_{\min}(G_*+p)
=
E_*\,\mathbf 1_4
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel\, \tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
\alpha\bigl(
p_1 \tau^1\otimes s_1
+
p_2 \tau^1\otimes s_2
\bigr)
+
\beta\bigl(
-p_2 \tau^1\otimes s_1
+
p_1 \tau^1\otimes s_2
\bigr)
+
O(|p|^2).
\label{eq:Lmin}
\end{equation}

This is exactly the minimal shell-enriched axial ansatz.

\subsection{Derivative operators}

Define the first derivative operators at the selected shell point by
\[
K_i:=\partial_{k_i}L_{\min}(k)\big|_{k=G_*}.
\]
Using
\[
p_\parallel = \hat n_i (k_i-G_{*i}),
\qquad
p_a = e_{a i}(k_i-G_{*i}),
\]
we obtain
\begin{equation}
K_i
=
v_\parallel \hat n_i\,\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
\alpha\bigl(
e_{1i}\tau^1\otimes s_1
+
e_{2i}\tau^1\otimes s_2
\bigr)
+
\beta\bigl(
- e_{2i}\tau^1\otimes s_1
+
e_{1i}\tau^1\otimes s_2
\bigr).
\label{eq:Ki_full}
\end{equation}

Contracting with the adapted frame gives
\begin{equation}
K_\parallel := \hat n_i K_i = v_\parallel \tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2,
\end{equation}
because $\hat n\cdot e_a=0$, and
\begin{equation}
K_1 := e_{1i}K_i = \alpha\,\tau^1\otimes s_1 + \beta\,\tau^1\otimes s_2,
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
K_2 := e_{2i}K_i = -\beta\,\tau^1\otimes s_1 + \alpha\,\tau^1\otimes s_2.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Valley projection and reduced Pauli identification}

Let $P_+$ denote the projection onto the selected two-dimensional valley subspace.
Under the reduced identification of the valley Pauli basis,
\[
P_+(\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2)P_+ \mapsto \sigma^3,
\qquad
P_+(\tau^1\otimes s_1)P_+ \mapsto \sigma^1,
\qquad
P_+(\tau^1\otimes s_2)P_+ \mapsto \sigma^2,
\]
the projected first-order Hamiltonian becomes
\begin{equation}
h_+(p)
=
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
\alpha\,(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
\beta\,(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\label{eq:hplus}
\end{equation}

Therefore the transverse $2\times2$ coefficient matrix is
\begin{equation}
T=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha & -\beta\\
\beta & \alpha
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\det T = \alpha^2+\beta^2.
\label{eq:Tmat}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Explicit Weyl coefficient matrix}

Write the traceless linearized Hamiltonian as
\[
h_+^{\mathrm{lin}}(p)=\sum_{\mu,j} M_{\mu j} p_j \sigma^\mu,
\qquad
j\in\{1,2,\parallel\},
\quad
\mu\in\{1,2,3\}.
\]
Comparing with \eqref{eq:hplus}, we read off
\begin{equation}
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha & -\beta & 0\\
\beta & \alpha & 0\\
0 & 0 & v_\parallel
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:Mexplicit}
\end{equation}
Hence
\begin{equation}
\det M = v_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\label{eq:detM}
\end{equation}

This is the desired Weyl coefficient matrix extracted from the minimal TECT shell ansatz.

\subsection{Microscopic enriched tensor matching}

Now let the enriched microscopic shell coupling be
\begin{equation}
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}
=
\sum_{a=1}^3 N^a_{ij}\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\,(-i\partial_j)\otimes s_a.
\label{eq:Lenr}
\end{equation}
Near the selected shell component, the condensate gradient contributes a factor proportional to $G_{*i}$.
Define the shell-reduced vectors
\[
C^a_j := G_{*i}N^a_{ij}.
\]
Projecting these vectors onto the adapted frame gives the effective coefficients.

The longitudinal coefficient is
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_* |G_*|\, \hat n_i \hat n_j\, N^3_{ij}.
\label{eq:lambdapar}
\end{equation}

The transverse matrix entries are
\begin{equation}
T_{ab}
=
\kappa_* |G_*|\, e_{a j}\hat n_i\, N^b_{ij},
\qquad a,b=1,2.
\label{eq:Tab}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}
\Bigr),
\label{eq:alpha}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}
\Bigr).
\label{eq:beta}
\end{equation}

Thus the complete microscopic-to-Weyl dictionary is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_* |G_*|\, \hat n_i \hat n_j N^3_{ij},
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}
\Bigr),
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}
\Bigr).
}
\end{equation}

Substituting into \eqref{eq:detM}, the local Weyl criterion becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\det M
=
\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Immediate microscopic criterion}

Hence the selected BCC shell crossing is locally Weyl provided
\begin{equation}
\hat n_i \hat n_j N^3_{ij}\neq 0,
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}
\Bigr)^2
+
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}
\Bigr)^2
\neq 0.
\end{equation}
If, in addition, an opposite valley exists with the standard pairing symmetry,
then opposite-valley doubling yields the Dirac operator.

\subsection{Honest status}

What has been computed here is the full coefficient extraction for the minimal symmetry-compatible shell ansatz.
What is still open is the final first-principles derivation of the actual TECT condensate PDE and the actual microscopic computation of the tensors $N^a_{ij}$ generated by that PDE.

\section{Final Summary}

For the minimal symmetry-compatible TECT shell-enriched ansatz near a selected BCC shell vector $G_*$,
\[
L_{\min}(G_*+p)
=
E_*\,\mathbf 1_4
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel\, \tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
\alpha\bigl(
p_1 \tau^1\otimes s_1
+
p_2 \tau^1\otimes s_2
\bigr)
+
\beta\bigl(
-p_2 \tau^1\otimes s_1
+
p_1 \tau^1\otimes s_2
\bigr)
+
O(|p|^2),
\]
the projected valley Hamiltonian is
\[
h_+(p)
=
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
\alpha(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
\beta(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\]

Therefore the Weyl coefficient matrix is
\[
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha & -\beta & 0\\
\beta & \alpha & 0\\
0 & 0 & v_\parallel
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\det M=v_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\]

For the microscopic enriched tensor coupling
\[
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}
=
\sum_{a=1}^3 N^a_{ij}\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\,(-i\partial_j)\otimes s_a,
\]
the effective coefficients are
\[
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_* |G_*|\, \hat n_i \hat n_j N^3_{ij},
\]
\[
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}
\Bigr),
\]
\[
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_* |G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}
\Bigr).
\]

Hence the local BCC shell crossing is Weyl if and only if
\[
\hat n_i \hat n_j N^3_{ij}\neq 0
\]
and
\[
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}
\Bigr)^2
+
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}
\Bigr)^2
\neq 0.
\]

Thus the local Weyl/Dirac problem has been reduced to an explicit finite tensor-contraction test.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Explicit Extraction of \texorpdfstring{$N^a_{ij}$}{Nij} from a Minimal Microscopic TECT Ansatz}

\subsection{Scope}

At this stage, the theorem-level architecture is already known:
the local Weyl problem near a selected BCC shell pair is controlled by
\[
\lambda_\parallel,\qquad \alpha,\qquad \beta,
\]
with
\[
\det M=\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\]
What remains is the explicit extraction of the microscopic enriched tensors
\[
N^a_{ij}
\]
from a concrete symmetry-compatible condensate PDE ansatz.

We do not claim uniqueness of the full microscopic TECT PDE here.
Instead, we choose the minimal first-order enriched ansatz that is compatible with the BCC shell axis and the reduced internal doublet.

\subsection{Selected shell pair and adapted frame}

Fix a selected reciprocal shell pair
\[
\pm G_* \in S_{\mathrm{BCC}},
\qquad
\hat n:=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|}.
\]
Choose an orthonormal adapted frame
\[
\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\},
\qquad
e_a\cdot \hat n=0,
\qquad
e_a\cdot e_b=\delta_{ab},
\quad a,b=1,2.
\]
Write
\[
p=p_\parallel \hat n+p_1 e_1+p_2 e_2.
\]

\subsection{Minimal enriched microscopic coupling}

Consider the enriched first-order microscopic coupling
\begin{equation}
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}
=
\sum_{a=1}^3 N^a_{ij}\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\,(-i\partial_j)\otimes s_a,
\label{eq:Lenr_min}
\end{equation}
where:
\begin{itemize}
\item $\phi_0$ is the condensed BCC background,
\item $s_a$ act on the reduced internal doublet,
\item $N^a_{ij}$ are the microscopic enriched tensors.
\end{itemize}

We now choose the smallest symmetry-compatible ansatz that can generate the three effective Weyl coefficients:
\begin{equation}
N^3_{ij}=A\,\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\label{eq:N3ansatz}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^1_{ij}=B\,\hat n_i e_{1j}+C\,\hat n_i e_{2j},
\label{eq:N1ansatz}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^2_{ij}=-C\,\hat n_i e_{1j}+B\,\hat n_i e_{2j}.
\label{eq:N2ansatz}
\end{equation}

This is the minimal axial-equivariant tensor family:
\begin{itemize}
\item $A$ is the longitudinal coefficient,
\item $B$ is the transverse even coefficient,
\item $C$ is the transverse odd coefficient.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Why this ansatz is the correct minimal one}

The structure \eqref{eq:N3ansatz}--\eqref{eq:N2ansatz} is the tensor version of the operator-level ansatz
\[
\lambda_\parallel(\hat n\cdot p)\sigma^3
+
\alpha(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
\beta(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2).
\]
Indeed:
\begin{itemize}
\item $\hat n_i\hat n_j$ is the unique axial longitudinal projector,
\item $\hat n_i e_{aj}$ gives the mixed longitudinal-to-transverse shell contraction,
\item the pair $(B,C)$ is arranged so that the transverse block becomes exactly
\[
\begin{pmatrix}
B & -C\\
C & B
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
\end{itemize}

Thus \eqref{eq:N3ansatz}--\eqref{eq:N2ansatz} is the minimal microscopic realization of the already-established Weyl template.

\subsection{Microscopic-to-effective matching formulas}

The shell-level matching formulas are
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_*|G_*|\,\hat n_i\hat n_j N^3_{ij},
\label{eq:lambdamatch}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\left(
e_{1j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}
\right),
\label{eq:alphamatch}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\left(
e_{2j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}
\right).
\label{eq:betamatch}
\end{equation}

We now evaluate these explicitly.

\subsection{Longitudinal coefficient}

Using \eqref{eq:N3ansatz},
\[
\hat n_i\hat n_j N^3_{ij}
=
\hat n_i\hat n_j (A\hat n_i\hat n_j)
=
A(\hat n\cdot \hat n)^2
=
A.
\]
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel=\kappa_*|G_*|\,A.
}
\label{eq:lambdafinal}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Transverse even coefficient}

First compute
\[
e_{1j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}
=
e_{1j}\hat n_i (B\hat n_i e_{1j}+C\hat n_i e_{2j})
=
B(\hat n\cdot\hat n)(e_1\cdot e_1)
+
C(\hat n\cdot\hat n)(e_1\cdot e_2)
=
B.
\]
Similarly,
\[
e_{2j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}
=
e_{2j}\hat n_i (-C\hat n_i e_{1j}+B\hat n_i e_{2j})
=
-C(e_2\cdot e_1)+B(e_2\cdot e_2)
=
B.
\]
Thus
\[
e_{1j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}+e_{2j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}=2B,
\]
and therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha=\kappa_*|G_*|\,B.
}
\label{eq:alphafinal}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Transverse odd coefficient}

Now compute
\[
e_{2j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}
=
e_{2j}\hat n_i (B\hat n_i e_{1j}+C\hat n_i e_{2j})
=
B(e_2\cdot e_1)+C(e_2\cdot e_2)
=
C,
\]
and
\[
e_{1j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}
=
e_{1j}\hat n_i (-C\hat n_i e_{1j}+B\hat n_i e_{2j})
=
-C(e_1\cdot e_1)+B(e_1\cdot e_2)
=
-C.
\]
Hence
\[
e_{2j}\hat n_i N^1_{ij}-e_{1j}\hat n_i N^2_{ij}
=
C-(-C)=2C,
\]
and therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\beta=\kappa_*|G_*|\,C.
}
\label{eq:betafinal}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Explicit Weyl coefficient matrix}

Substituting \eqref{eq:lambdafinal}--\eqref{eq:betafinal} into the reduced Weyl block gives
\[
h_+(p)
=
\lambda_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
\alpha(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
\beta(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2).
\]
Thus the coefficient matrix is
\begin{equation}
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha & -\beta & 0\\
\beta & \alpha & 0\\
0 & 0 & \lambda_\parallel
\end{pmatrix}
=
\kappa_*|G_*|
\begin{pmatrix}
B & -C & 0\\
C & B & 0\\
0 & 0 & A
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:MABC}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3\,A(B^2+C^2).
}
\label{eq:detMABC}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Local Weyl criterion in microscopic tensor form}

Hence the selected shell crossing is locally Weyl if and only if
\begin{equation}
A\neq 0,
\qquad
(B,C)\neq (0,0).
\label{eq:ABCcriterion}
\end{equation}
Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\hat n_i\hat n_jN^3_{ij}\neq 0,
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\left(
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}+e_{2j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
\right)^2
+
\left(
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}-e_{1j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
\right)^2
\neq 0.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Interpretation}

This proves that the minimal microscopic enriched tensor family already contains the full local Weyl data:
\begin{itemize}
\item the longitudinal projector channel $\hat n_i\hat n_j\otimes s_3$ controls the coefficient $\lambda_\parallel$,
\item the transverse even channel controls $\alpha$,
\item the transverse odd channel controls $\beta$.
\end{itemize}
Therefore the Weyl problem is not an uncontrolled PDE existence problem anymore.
It is a finite microscopic coefficient test.

\subsection{Dirac consequence}

If an opposite shell valley at $-G_*$ exists with the standard pairing relation
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p),
\]
then the doubled Hamiltonian is Dirac.
Thus the only remaining genuinely microscopic steps are:
\begin{enumerate}
\item derive the actual full TECT condensate PDE,
\item compute the actual $N^a_{ij}$ generated by that PDE,
\item verify that their coefficients reduce to
\[
A\neq 0,\qquad (B,C)\neq(0,0),
\]
\item verify opposite-valley existence and pairing.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

For the minimal symmetry-compatible microscopic enriched tensors
\[
N^3_{ij}=A\,\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\]
\[
N^1_{ij}=B\,\hat n_i e_{1j}+C\,\hat n_i e_{2j},
\]
\[
N^2_{ij}=-C\,\hat n_i e_{1j}+B\,\hat n_i e_{2j},
\]
the shell-matching formulas give
\[
\lambda_\parallel=\kappa_*|G_*|\,A,
\qquad
\alpha=\kappa_*|G_*|\,B,
\qquad
\beta=\kappa_*|G_*|\,C.
\]

Hence the local Weyl coefficient matrix is
\[
M
=
\kappa_*|G_*|
\begin{pmatrix}
B & -C & 0\\
C & B & 0\\
0 & 0 & A
\end{pmatrix},
\]
and therefore
\[
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3\,A(B^2+C^2).
\]

Thus the selected BCC shell crossing is locally Weyl if and only if
\[
A\neq 0,
\qquad
(B,C)\neq(0,0).
\]

Equivalently, the local Weyl/Dirac problem has been reduced to the explicit microscopic question:
does the true TECT condensate linearization contain a nonzero longitudinal projector channel and at least one nontrivial transverse channel?

\section{Extraction of the Weyl Coefficients from a Minimal Actual TECT Operator Ansatz}

\subsection{Honest scope}

At the present stage, the full microscopic TECT condensate PDE is still not uniquely derived.
What is already available is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the reduction of the local Weyl problem to the three coefficients
\[
\lambda_\parallel,\qquad \alpha,\qquad \beta,
\]
\item the determinant formula
\[
\det M=\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2),
\]
\item and the explicit shell-level extraction formulas from the enriched microscopic tensors
\[
N^a_{ij}.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Therefore, the strongest honest next step is to choose a minimal actual symmetry-compatible operator ansatz and read off the coefficients explicitly.

\subsection{Selected shell pair and adapted frame}

Fix a selected reciprocal shell pair
\[
\pm G_* \in S_{\mathrm{BCC}},
\qquad
\hat n:=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|}.
\]
Choose an adapted orthonormal frame
\[
\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\},
\qquad
e_a\cdot \hat n=0,
\qquad
e_a\cdot e_b=\delta_{ab},
\quad a,b=1,2.
\]
Write
\[
p=p_\parallel \hat n+p_1e_1+p_2e_2.
\]

\subsection{Minimal actual operator ansatz}

The smallest symmetry-compatible first-order enriched operator is
\begin{equation}
L_{\mathrm{act}}(G_*+p)
=
E_*\,\mathbf 1_4
+
A\,(\hat n\cdot p)\,\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
B\,(p_1\tau^1\otimes s_1+p_2\tau^1\otimes s_2)
+
C\,(-p_2\tau^1\otimes s_1+p_1\tau^1\otimes s_2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\label{eq:Lact}
\end{equation}

Here:
\begin{itemize}
\item \(A\) is the longitudinal axial coefficient,
\item \(B\) is the transverse even coefficient,
\item \(C\) is the transverse odd coefficient.
\end{itemize}

This is exactly the minimal shell-enriched axial ansatz used in the explicit extraction calculation.

\subsection{First derivative operators}

Define
\[
K_i:=\partial_{k_i}L_{\mathrm{act}}(k)\big|_{k=G_*}.
\]
Using
\[
p_\parallel=\hat n_i(k_i-G_{*i}),
\qquad
p_a=e_{ai}(k_i-G_{*i}),
\]
we obtain
\begin{equation}
K_i
=
A\,\hat n_i\,\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
B\,(e_{1i}\tau^1\otimes s_1+e_{2i}\tau^1\otimes s_2)
+
C\,(-e_{2i}\tau^1\otimes s_1+e_{1i}\tau^1\otimes s_2).
\label{eq:Ki_act}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
K_\parallel:=\hat n_iK_i=A\,\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2,
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
K_1:=e_{1i}K_i=B\,\tau^1\otimes s_1+C\,\tau^1\otimes s_2,
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
K_2:=e_{2i}K_i=-C\,\tau^1\otimes s_1+B\,\tau^1\otimes s_2.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Valley projection}

Project to the selected two-band valley subspace with projector \(P_+\).
Under the reduced identification
\[
P_+(\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2)P_+\mapsto \sigma^3,
\qquad
P_+(\tau^1\otimes s_1)P_+\mapsto \sigma^1,
\qquad
P_+(\tau^1\otimes s_2)P_+\mapsto \sigma^2,
\]
the projected Hamiltonian becomes
\begin{equation}
h_+(p)
=
A\,p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
B\,(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
C\,(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\label{eq:hplusABC}
\end{equation}

Thus, immediately,
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel=A,
\qquad
\alpha=B,
\qquad
\beta=C.
\label{eq:lambalpha_beta}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Explicit Weyl matrix}

Comparing \eqref{eq:hplusABC} with
\[
h_+^{\mathrm{lin}}(p)=\sum_{\mu,j}M_{\mu j}p_j\sigma^\mu,
\qquad
j\in\{1,2,\parallel\},
\]
we read off
\begin{equation}
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
B & -C & 0\\
C & B & 0\\
0 & 0 & A
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:MABC_exact}
\end{equation}
Hence
\begin{equation}
\det M = A(B^2+C^2).
\label{eq:detMABC_exact}
\end{equation}

Therefore the local Weyl criterion is simply
\begin{equation}
A\neq 0,
\qquad
(B,C)\neq(0,0).
\label{eq:weylABC}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Matching to the enriched microscopic tensors}

Now write the enriched microscopic shell coupling as
\begin{equation}
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}
=
\sum_{a=1}^3 N^a_{ij}\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\,(-i\partial_j)\otimes s_a.
\label{eq:Lenr_again}
\end{equation}
The shell-matching formulas are
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_*|G_*|\,\hat n_i\hat n_jN^3_{ij},
\label{eq:lam_match2}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
\Bigr),
\label{eq:alpha_match2}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
\Bigr).
\label{eq:beta_match2}
\end{equation}

To realize \eqref{eq:Lact}, choose the minimal tensor ansatz
\begin{equation}
N^3_{ij}= \frac{A}{\kappa_*|G_*|}\,\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\label{eq:N3_actual}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^1_{ij}= \frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\left(
B\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
+
C\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
\right),
\label{eq:N1_actual}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^2_{ij}= \frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\left(
-C\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
+
B\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
\right).
\label{eq:N2_actual}
\end{equation}

Substituting \eqref{eq:N3_actual}--\eqref{eq:N2_actual} into \eqref{eq:lam_match2}--\eqref{eq:beta_match2} gives
\[
\lambda_\parallel=A,\qquad \alpha=B,\qquad \beta=C,
\]
exactly as required.

\subsection{Interpretation}

Thus the minimal actual operator ansatz \eqref{eq:Lact} is microscopically realized by the explicit enriched tensors
\[
N^3_{ij}\propto \hat n_i\hat n_j,
\qquad
N^1_{ij}\propto \hat n_i e_{1j},\hat n_i e_{2j},
\qquad
N^2_{ij}\propto \hat n_i e_{1j},\hat n_i e_{2j}.
\]
In other words:

\begin{itemize}
\item the longitudinal projector channel produces \(A=\lambda_\parallel\),
\item the transverse even channel produces \(B=\alpha\),
\item the transverse odd channel produces \(C=\beta\).
\end{itemize}

Hence the entire local Weyl problem is reduced to the microscopic yes/no question:
does the true TECT linearization contain these three channels with
\[
A\neq 0,
\qquad
(B,C)\neq(0,0)?
\]

\subsection{Dirac consequence}

If an opposite shell valley at \(-G_*\) exists and satisfies the standard pairing relation
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p),
\]
then the doubled theory is Dirac.
Therefore, once the actual TECT operator matches the minimal ansatz with
\[
A\neq 0,
\qquad
(B,C)\neq(0,0),
\]
the only remaining local issue is opposite-valley existence and pairing.

\section{Final Summary}

For the minimal actual symmetry-compatible TECT operator
\[
L_{\mathrm{act}}(G_*+p)
=
E_*\,\mathbf 1_4
+
A(\hat n\cdot p)\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
B(p_1\tau^1\otimes s_1+p_2\tau^1\otimes s_2)
+
C(-p_2\tau^1\otimes s_1+p_1\tau^1\otimes s_2)
+
O(|p|^2),
\]
the projected two-band valley Hamiltonian is
\[
h_+(p)
=
A\,p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
B(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
C(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\]

Therefore
\[
\lambda_\parallel=A,\qquad \alpha=B,\qquad \beta=C,
\]
and the Weyl coefficient matrix is
\[
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
B & -C & 0\\
C & B & 0\\
0 & 0 & A
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\det M=A(B^2+C^2).
\]

A microscopic tensor realization of this ansatz is
\[
N^3_{ij}= \frac{A}{\kappa_*|G_*|}\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\]
\[
N^1_{ij}= \frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\left(
B\hat n_i e_{1j}+C\hat n_i e_{2j}
\right),
\]
\[
N^2_{ij}= \frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\left(
-C\hat n_i e_{1j}+B\hat n_i e_{2j}
\right).
\]

Hence the local BCC shell crossing is Weyl if and only if
\[
A\neq 0,
\qquad
(B,C)\neq(0,0).
\]

Thus the remaining microscopic TECT task is not structural:
it is to verify that the true condensate linearization indeed generates these three channels with nonzero coefficients.

%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Projector-Field TECT PDE and Explicit Extraction of the Weyl Coefficients}

\subsection{Honest scope}

We do not yet claim a unique final microscopic TECT condensate PDE.
What is already theorem-level closed is the following:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the local BCC-shell Weyl problem reduces to the three coefficients
\[
\lambda_\parallel,\qquad \alpha,\qquad \beta,
\]
\item the determinant identity is
\[
\det M=\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2),
\]
\item and the coefficients are extracted from the microscopic enriched tensors
\[
N^a_{ij}
\]
by explicit shell contractions.
\end{enumerate}

The purpose of the present section is therefore to choose a minimal actual projector-field ansatz and compute the induced coefficients \(A,B,C\) explicitly.

\subsection{Projective internal field}

Let
\[
z(x,t)\in \mathbb C^3,
\qquad
z^\dagger z=1,
\qquad
z\sim e^{i\chi(x,t)}z,
\]
and define the rank-one projector
\[
P(x,t):=z(x,t)z^\dagger(x,t),
\qquad
P^2=P,\qquad P^\dagger=P,\qquad \Tr P=1.
\]
Thus
\[
P(x,t)\in \mathbb{CP}^2.
\]

The natural gauge-covariant derivative is
\[
D_iP:=\partial_iP-i[A_i,P],
\]
where \(A_i=A_i^aT^a\in su(3)\) is the already-emergent internal connection.

\subsection{Minimal projector-field PDE ansatz}

We now write the smallest shell-compatible first-order enriched PDE ansatz near a selected BCC shell pair.
Let \(\phi_0(x)\) denote the BCC condensate background and let
\[
\hat n:=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|},
\qquad
\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\}
\]
be the adapted shell frame.

We assume that the reduced internal doublet sector is generated by three Hermitian matrices
\[
\Sigma_3,\qquad \Sigma_1,\qquad \Sigma_2,
\]
which, after projection to the valley subspace, reduce to the Pauli basis
\[
\Sigma_3\mapsto \sigma^3,
\qquad
\Sigma_1\mapsto \sigma^1,
\qquad
\Sigma_2\mapsto \sigma^2.
\]

The minimal first-order projector-field operator is
\begin{equation}
i\partial_t \Psi
=
\Bigl[
E_*
+
\mathcal L_{\rm shell}
+
\mathcal L_{\rm enr}
\Bigr]\Psi,
\label{eq:minPDE}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\mathcal L_{\rm shell}
=
A\,(\hat n\cdot (-i\nabla))\,\Sigma_3,
\label{eq:Lshell}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\mathcal L_{\rm enr}
=
B\Bigl((e_1\cdot (-i\nabla))\Sigma_1+(e_2\cdot (-i\nabla))\Sigma_2\Bigr)
+
C\Bigl(-(e_2\cdot (-i\nabla))\Sigma_1+(e_1\cdot (-i\nabla))\Sigma_2\Bigr).
\label{eq:LenrABC}
\end{equation}

This is exactly the minimal axial-equivariant first-order ansatz:
\(A\) is the longitudinal channel,
\(B\) the transverse-even channel,
and \(C\) the transverse-odd channel.

\subsection{Microscopic tensor form}

To match with the theorem-level shell-reduction formulas, rewrite
\eqref{eq:LenrABC} in tensor form:
\begin{equation}
\delta L_{\rm enr}
=
\sum_{a=1}^3 N^a_{ij}\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\,(-i\partial_j)\otimes s_a.
\label{eq:tensorform}
\end{equation}

Near the selected shell component, the condensate gradient contributes a factor proportional to \(G_{*i}\).
Hence the shell-reduced vectors are
\[
C^a_j:=G_{*i}N^a_{ij}.
\]

The minimal tensor realization that reproduces \eqref{eq:Lshell}--\eqref{eq:LenrABC} is
\begin{equation}
N^3_{ij}=\frac{A}{\kappa_*|G_*|}\,\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\label{eq:N3proj}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^1_{ij}
=
\frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\Bigl(
B\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
+
C\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
\Bigr),
\label{eq:N1proj}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^2_{ij}
=
\frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\Bigl(
-C\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
+
B\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
\Bigr),
\label{eq:N2proj}
\end{equation}
where \(\kappa_*\) is the shell-projection normalization factor.

\subsection{Direct extraction of \texorpdfstring{$\lambda_\parallel$}{lambda}, \texorpdfstring{$\alpha$}{alpha}, \texorpdfstring{$\beta$}{beta}}

The shell-level extraction formulas are
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_*|G_*|\,\hat n_i\hat n_jN^3_{ij},
\label{eq:lamb_extract}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
\Bigr),
\label{eq:alpha_extract}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
\Bigr).
\label{eq:beta_extract}
\end{equation}

Substituting \eqref{eq:N3proj} gives
\[
\hat n_i\hat n_jN^3_{ij}
=
\frac{A}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
(\hat n\cdot\hat n)^2
=
\frac{A}{\kappa_*|G_*|},
\]
hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel=A.
}
\label{eq:lamb_eq_A}
\end{equation}

Next, using \eqref{eq:N1proj} and \eqref{eq:N2proj},
\[
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
=
\frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\Bigl(
B\,(\hat n\cdot\hat n)(e_1\cdot e_1)
+
C\,(\hat n\cdot\hat n)(e_1\cdot e_2)
\Bigr)
=
\frac{B}{\kappa_*|G_*|},
\]
and
\[
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
=
\frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\Bigl(
-C\,(\hat n\cdot\hat n)(e_2\cdot e_1)
+
B\,(\hat n\cdot\hat n)(e_2\cdot e_2)
\Bigr)
=
\frac{B}{\kappa_*|G_*|}.
\]
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha=B.
}
\label{eq:alpha_eq_B}
\end{equation}

Similarly,
\[
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
=
\frac{C}{\kappa_*|G_*|},
\qquad
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
=
-\frac{C}{\kappa_*|G_*|},
\]
so
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\beta=C.
}
\label{eq:beta_eq_C}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Projected two-band Hamiltonian}

After projection to the selected two-band valley subspace, the reduced linear Hamiltonian is
\begin{equation}
h_+(p)
=
A\,p_\parallel\sigma^3
+
B\,(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
C\,(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\label{eq:hplus_proj}
\end{equation}

Hence the Weyl coefficient matrix is
\begin{equation}
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
B & -C & 0\\
C & B & 0\\
0 & 0 & A
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:Mproj}
\end{equation}
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\det M=A(B^2+C^2).
}
\label{eq:detMproj}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Microscopic Weyl criterion}

Thus the local BCC shell crossing is Weyl if and only if
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A\neq 0,
\qquad
(B,C)\neq (0,0).
}
\label{eq:weylcritABC}
\end{equation}

Equivalently, in microscopic tensor language,
\begin{equation}
\hat n_i\hat n_jN^3_{ij}\neq 0,
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
\Bigr)^2
+
\Bigl(
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
\Bigr)^2
\neq 0.
\end{equation}

\subsection{What this proves and what it does not}

What is proved here is:
\begin{enumerate}
\item a minimal actual projector-field operator ansatz exists whose first-order coefficients are exactly \(A,B,C\),
\item its enriched microscopic tensors \(N^a_{ij}\) are explicitly known,
\item the induced Weyl matrix is explicitly
\[
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
B & -C & 0\\
C & B & 0\\
0 & 0 & A
\end{pmatrix},
\]
\item and local Weyl nondegeneracy is equivalent to
\[
A\neq 0,\qquad (B,C)\neq (0,0).
\]
\end{enumerate}

What remains open is:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the final unique derivation of the full TECT condensate PDE,
\item the proof that the actual microscopic linearization reduces to this minimal ansatz,
\item and the existence plus pairing of the opposite valley at \(-G_*\).
\end{enumerate}


\section{Final Summary}

For the minimal projector-field TECT operator
\[
i\partial_t\Psi
=
\Bigl[
E_*
+
A(\hat n\cdot(-i\nabla))\Sigma_3
+
B\bigl((e_1\cdot(-i\nabla))\Sigma_1+(e_2\cdot(-i\nabla))\Sigma_2\bigr)
+
C\bigl(-(e_2\cdot(-i\nabla))\Sigma_1+(e_1\cdot(-i\nabla))\Sigma_2\bigr)
\Bigr]\Psi,
\]
the microscopic enriched tensors are realized by
\[
N^3_{ij}=\frac{A}{\kappa_*|G_*|}\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\]
\[
N^1_{ij}=\frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\bigl(B\hat n_i e_{1j}+C\hat n_i e_{2j}\bigr),
\]
\[
N^2_{ij}=\frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\bigl(-C\hat n_i e_{1j}+B\hat n_i e_{2j}\bigr).
\]

The shell-level contraction formulas give
\[
\lambda_\parallel=A,\qquad \alpha=B,\qquad \beta=C.
\]
Therefore the projected Weyl coefficient matrix is
\[
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
B & -C & 0\\
C & B & 0\\
0 & 0 & A
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\det M=A(B^2+C^2).
\]

Hence the selected BCC shell crossing is locally Weyl if and only if
\[
A\neq 0,
\qquad
(B,C)\neq (0,0).
\]

Thus the remaining microscopic task is now precise:
derive the actual TECT condensate linearization and verify that it indeed generates the three channels corresponding to \(A\), \(B\), and \(C\).

%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Opposite-Valley Pairing and Dirac Doubling}

\subsection{Goal}

We now isolate the exact condition under which two opposite BCC shell valleys
\[
\pm G_*
\]
combine into a Dirac fermion.

The key point is that the existence of a Weyl valley at \(+G_*\) does \emph{not} by itself imply the existence of a Dirac fermion.
To obtain a Dirac block, one additionally needs an opposite valley at \(-G_*\) with the correct pairing relation.

\subsection{Local Weyl block at \texorpdfstring{$+G_*$}{+G*}}

From the previous step, the projected two-band Hamiltonian near the selected shell point \(+G_*\) is
\begin{equation}
h_+(p)
=
A\,p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
B\,(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
C\,(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2),
\label{eq:hplus_pair}
\end{equation}
where
\[
p=p_\parallel \hat n+p_1e_1+p_2e_2,
\qquad
\hat n=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|}.
\]
Its coefficient matrix is
\begin{equation}
M_+
=
\begin{pmatrix}
B & -C & 0\\
C & B & 0\\
0 & 0 & A
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\det M_+=A(B^2+C^2).
\end{equation}
If
\[
A\neq 0,\qquad (B,C)\neq (0,0),
\]
then \(+G_*\) is a Weyl valley.

\subsection{Local Weyl block at \texorpdfstring{$-G_*$}{-G*}}

Let the opposite shell vector be
\[
-G_*,
\qquad
\hat n_-:=-\hat n.
\]
Choose the opposite adapted frame
\[
\{e_1^-,e_2^-,\hat n_-\}.
\]
There is some freedom in how one chooses the transverse basis at the opposite valley.
A natural and convenient convention is
\[
e_1^- = e_1,\qquad e_2^- = -e_2,\qquad \hat n_-=-\hat n,
\]
so that the ordered frame remains right-handed.
Then the local momentum near the opposite valley is
\[
k=-G_*+p.
\]

The most general projected Hamiltonian near \(-G_*\) is
\begin{equation}
h_-(p)
=
A_-\,p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
B_-\,(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
C_-\,(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\label{eq:hminus_general}
\end{equation}

The question is: under what conditions are the coefficients related so that
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)?
\]

\subsection{What the pairing relation means explicitly}

From \eqref{eq:hplus_pair},
\[
h_+(-p)
=
-A\,p_\parallel \sigma^3
-
B\,(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
-
C\,(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\]
Therefore
\begin{equation}
-h_+(-p)
=
A\,p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
B\,(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
C\,(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\label{eq:minus_hplus}
\end{equation}
Thus the relation
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)
\]
is equivalent to
\begin{equation}
A_-=A,\qquad B_-=B,\qquad C_-=C
\label{eq:ABCpair}
\end{equation}
in a suitably matched valley basis.

So the problem reduces to whether the microscopic symmetry maps the \(+G_*\) valley to the \(-G_*\) valley while preserving the three reduced coefficients.

\subsection{Sufficient symmetry condition: unitary inversion-type pairing}

Assume there exists a unitary symmetry \(\mathcal I\) such that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(\mathcal I\) maps the shell point \(+G_*\) to \(-G_*\),
\[
\mathcal I: G_* \mapsto -G_*;
\]

\item after projection to the two-band valley subspaces, \(\mathcal I\) identifies the Pauli basis at the two valleys;

\item the microscopic linearized symbol satisfies
\[
\mathcal I\, L(G_*+p)\,\mathcal I^{-1}=L(-G_*-p).
\]
\end{enumerate}

Projecting to the two valley subspaces then gives
\[
h_-(p)=U_{\mathcal I}\, h_+(-p)\, U_{\mathcal I}^{-1},
\]
for some unitary \(U_{\mathcal I}\) acting in the two-band space.

If, moreover, the basis is chosen so that
\[
U_{\mathcal I}\sigma^\mu U_{\mathcal I}^{-1}=-\sigma^\mu
\]
for the traceless part, then
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)
\]
after subtraction of the common scalar part.

This is a sufficient condition for opposite-valley pairing.

\subsection{Sufficient antiunitary condition: time-reversal-type pairing}

Alternatively, suppose there exists an antiunitary symmetry \(\mathcal T\) such that
\[
\mathcal T\,L(G_*+p)\,\mathcal T^{-1}=L(-G_*-p).
\]
After projection, one gets
\[
h_-(p)=U_{\mathcal T}\,h_+^*(-p)\,U_{\mathcal T}^{-1}.
\]
If the reduced Hamiltonian is brought to the real canonical Weyl form or the basis is chosen so that complex conjugation acts trivially on the coefficient functions, then this again yields the standard opposite-valley relation up to unitary equivalence.

Thus either inversion-type or time-reversal-type shell pairing symmetry is sufficient.

\subsection{Microscopic tensor criterion for opposite-valley pairing}

Let the enriched microscopic coupling near \(+G_*\) be described by the tensors
\[
N^a_{ij}(+),
\]
and near \(-G_*\) by
\[
N^a_{ij}(-).
\]
The effective coefficients are
\[
A_\pm = \kappa_*|G_*|\, \hat n_\pm^i \hat n_\pm^j N^3_{ij}(\pm),
\]
\[
B_\pm = \frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{1j}^\pm \hat n_\pm^i N^1_{ij}(\pm)
+
e_{2j}^\pm \hat n_\pm^i N^2_{ij}(\pm)
\Bigr),
\]
\[
C_\pm = \frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e_{2j}^\pm \hat n_\pm^i N^1_{ij}(\pm)
-
e_{1j}^\pm \hat n_\pm^i N^2_{ij}(\pm)
\Bigr).
\]

Hence a direct finite microscopic sufficient condition for standard Dirac doubling is
\begin{equation}
A_+=A_-,
\qquad
B_+=B_-,
\qquad
C_+=C_-,
\label{eq:mic_pair}
\end{equation}
in matched opposite-valley frames.
Then automatically
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)
\]
in the canonical reduced basis.

\subsection{Dirac doubling theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Opposite-valley Dirac doubling]
Assume:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the selected shell pair \(+G_*\) and \(-G_*\) both carry nondegenerate Weyl valleys;

\item their reduced Hamiltonians satisfy the standard pairing relation
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)
\]
after subtraction of the common scalar part;

\item equivalently, in matched valley bases, the reduced coefficients satisfy
\[
A_-=A_+,\qquad B_-=B_+,\qquad C_-=C_+.
\]
\end{enumerate}
Then the doubled Hamiltonian
\begin{equation}
H_D(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}
\label{eq:HDblock}
\end{equation}
is unitarily equivalent to a massless Dirac Hamiltonian.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Write
\[
h_+(p)=d_i(p)\sigma^i,
\qquad
h_-(p)=-d_i(-p)\sigma^i.
\]
Then \eqref{eq:HDblock} becomes
\[
H_D(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
d_i(p)\sigma^i & 0\\
0 & -d_i(-p)\sigma^i
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
To linear order near the crossing, \(d_i(p)=M_{ij}p_j\), so
\[
H_D^{\rm lin}(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
M_{ij}p_j\sigma^i & 0\\
0 & -M_{ij}p_j\sigma^i
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Define the valley Pauli matrices \(\rho^\mu\) acting on the \((+,-)\) valley index. Then
\[
H_D^{\rm lin}(p)=\rho^3\otimes (M_{ij}p_j\sigma^i).
\]
After a standard Clifford-basis rearrangement, this is exactly the massless Dirac Hamiltonian
\[
H_D^{\rm lin}(p)=\Gamma^i q_i,
\qquad
\{\Gamma^i,\Gamma^j\}=2\delta^{ij},
\]
with \(q_i=M_{ij}p_j\).
\end{proof}

\subsection{Chirality cancellation and Dirac neutrality}

A single Weyl node has momentum-space topological charge
\[
\chi=\operatorname{sgn}(\det M).
\]
Under the standard opposite-valley relation, the partner node has opposite chirality:
\[
\chi_-=-\chi_+.
\]
Therefore the doubled Dirac block is topologically neutral as a whole, even though each valley separately is topologically nontrivial.

This is exactly the usual Weyl-to-Dirac doubling mechanism.

\subsection{What is automatic and what is not}

We now separate the exact logical status.

\paragraph{Automatic once pairing holds:}
\begin{itemize}
\item the doubled block is Dirac;
\item the two Weyl nodes have opposite chirality;
\item the local four-component theory is massless Dirac to linear order.
\end{itemize}

\paragraph{Not automatic:}
\begin{itemize}
\item existence of the opposite valley itself;
\item equality of the reduced coefficients \((A_-,B_-,C_-)\) and \((A_+,B_+,C_+)\);
\item the precise symmetry that enforces the pairing relation.
\end{itemize}

So the remaining opposite-valley problem is not conceptual but finite and explicit.

\subsection{Final reduction of the local Dirac problem}

The local Dirac problem is now reduced to the following finite microscopic checks:

\begin{enumerate}
\item compute the reduced coefficients \((A_+,B_+,C_+)\) at \(+G_*\);
\item compute the reduced coefficients \((A_-,B_-,C_-)\) at \(-G_*\);
\item verify the matching relation
\[
A_+=A_-,
\qquad
B_+=B_-,
\qquad
C_+=C_-;
\]
\item verify that both valleys are nondegenerate, i.e.
\[
A_\pm\neq 0,\qquad (B_\pm,C_\pm)\neq(0,0).
\]
\end{enumerate}

Once these hold, opposite-valley doubling is Dirac.

\section{Final Summary}

The local Weyl block at \(+G_*\) has the form
\[
h_+(p)
=
A\,p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
B\,(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
C\,(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\]
The opposite valley at \(-G_*\) has the analogous form
\[
h_-(p)
=
A_-\,p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
B_-\,(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
C_-\,(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\]

The standard opposite-valley pairing relation
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)
\]
is equivalent, in matched valley bases, to
\[
A_-=A_+,\qquad B_-=B_+,\qquad C_-=C_+.
\]

If both valleys are nondegenerate Weyl nodes and this pairing relation holds, then the doubled block
\[
H_D(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}
\]
is unitarily equivalent to a massless Dirac Hamiltonian.

Therefore the remaining local Dirac problem is reduced to a finite coefficient-matching check between the two opposite shell valleys.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Explicit Opposite-Valley Coefficient Matching}

\subsection{Goal}

We now compute the reduced coefficients at the opposite shell point
\[
-G_*
\]
and compare them with those at \(+G_*\).
The purpose is to verify explicitly, for the minimal symmetry-compatible microscopic tensor ansatz, that
\[
A_-=A_+,\qquad B_-=B_+,\qquad C_-=C_+,
\]
which is equivalent to the standard pairing relation
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p).
\]

\subsection{Data at the positive valley}

At \(+G_*\), we fixed the adapted orthonormal frame
\[
\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\},
\qquad
\hat n=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|}.
\]
For the minimal microscopic tensor ansatz,
\begin{equation}
N^3_{ij}= \frac{A}{\kappa_*|G_*|}\,\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\label{eq:N3plus}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^1_{ij}= \frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\left(
B\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
+
C\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
\right),
\label{eq:N1plus}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^2_{ij}= \frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\left(
-C\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
+
B\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
\right),
\label{eq:N2plus}
\end{equation}
the shell contractions gave
\[
A_+=A,\qquad B_+=B,\qquad C_+=C.
\]

\subsection{Choice of adapted frame at the negative valley}

At the opposite shell point \(-G_*\), define
\[
\hat n_-:=-\hat n.
\]
To preserve a right-handed orthonormal frame, choose
\begin{equation}
e_1^-:=e_1,
\qquad
e_2^-:=-e_2,
\qquad
\hat n_-:=-\hat n.
\label{eq:minusframe}
\end{equation}
Indeed,
\[
e_1^- \times e_2^- = e_1\times (-e_2)=-(e_1\times e_2)=-\hat n=\hat n_-.
\]

This is the natural matched opposite-valley frame.

\subsection{Minimal tensor ansatz at the negative valley}

Now write the same minimal microscopic ansatz, but in the \(-G_*\) adapted frame:
\begin{equation}
N^{3(-)}_{ij}
=
\frac{A_-}{\kappa_*|G_*|}\,\hat n^-_i \hat n^-_j,
\label{eq:N3minus_raw}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^{1(-)}_{ij}
=
\frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\left(
B_-\,\hat n^-_i e^-_{1j}
+
C_-\,\hat n^-_i e^-_{2j}
\right),
\label{eq:N1minus_raw}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^{2(-)}_{ij}
=
\frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\left(
-C_-\,\hat n^-_i e^-_{1j}
+
B_-\,\hat n^-_i e^-_{2j}
\right).
\label{eq:N2minus_raw}
\end{equation}

Substituting \eqref{eq:minusframe},
\[
\hat n^-=-\hat n,\qquad e_1^-=e_1,\qquad e_2^-=-e_2,
\]
we obtain
\begin{equation}
N^{3(-)}_{ij}
=
\frac{A_-}{\kappa_*|G_*|}\,(-\hat n_i)(-\hat n_j)
=
\frac{A_-}{\kappa_*|G_*|}\,\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\label{eq:N3minus_expanded}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^{1(-)}_{ij}
=
\frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\left(
-B_-\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
+
C_-\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
\right),
\label{eq:N1minus_expanded}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^{2(-)}_{ij}
=
\frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\left(
C_-\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
+
B_-\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
\right).
\label{eq:N2minus_expanded}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Shell contractions at the negative valley}

The shell-level extraction formulas at the negative valley are
\begin{equation}
A_-^{\mathrm{eff}}
=
\kappa_*|G_*|\,\hat n^-_i \hat n^-_j N^{3(-)}_{ij},
\label{eq:Aminus_extract}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
B_-^{\mathrm{eff}}
=
\frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e^-_{1j}\hat n^-_i N^{1(-)}_{ij}
+
e^-_{2j}\hat n^-_i N^{2(-)}_{ij}
\Bigr),
\label{eq:Bminus_extract}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
C_-^{\mathrm{eff}}
=
\frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\Bigl(
e^-_{2j}\hat n^-_i N^{1(-)}_{ij}
-
e^-_{1j}\hat n^-_i N^{2(-)}_{ij}
\Bigr).
\label{eq:Cminus_extract}
\end{equation}

We evaluate them explicitly.

\paragraph{Longitudinal coefficient.}
Using \eqref{eq:N3minus_expanded},
\[
\hat n^-_i \hat n^-_j N^{3(-)}_{ij}
=
(-\hat n_i)(-\hat n_j)\,
\frac{A_-}{\kappa_*|G_*|}\hat n_i\hat n_j
=
\frac{A_-}{\kappa_*|G_*|}.
\]
Hence
\begin{equation}
A_-^{\mathrm{eff}}=A_-.
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Transverse even coefficient.}
First,
\[
e^-_{1j}\hat n^-_i N^{1(-)}_{ij}
=
e_{1j}(-\hat n_i)\,
\frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\left(
-B_-\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
+
C_-\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
\right)
=
\frac{B_-}{\kappa_*|G_*|}.
\]
Indeed,
\[
(-\hat n_i)(-\hat n_i)=1,\qquad e_1\cdot e_1=1,\qquad e_1\cdot e_2=0.
\]
Second,
\[
e^-_{2j}\hat n^-_i N^{2(-)}_{ij}
=
(-e_{2j})(-\hat n_i)\,
\frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\left(
C_-\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
+
B_-\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
\right)
=
\frac{B_-}{\kappa_*|G_*|}.
\]
Therefore
\begin{equation}
B_-^{\mathrm{eff}}=B_-.
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Transverse odd coefficient.}
Now,
\[
e^-_{2j}\hat n^-_i N^{1(-)}_{ij}
=
(-e_{2j})(-\hat n_i)\,
\frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\left(
-B_-\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
+
C_-\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
\right)
=
\frac{C_-}{\kappa_*|G_*|},
\]
and
\[
e^-_{1j}\hat n^-_i N^{2(-)}_{ij}
=
e_{1j}(-\hat n_i)\,
\frac{1}{\kappa_*|G_*|}
\left(
C_-\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
+
B_-\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
\right)
=
-\frac{C_-}{\kappa_*|G_*|}.
\]
Hence
\begin{equation}
C_-^{\mathrm{eff}}=C_-.
\end{equation}

Thus the negative-valley shell contractions reproduce exactly the coefficients \((A_-,B_-,C_-)\) appearing in the ansatz.

\subsection{Matched opposite-valley condition}

Now suppose the microscopic opposite-valley tensor family is obtained from the same coefficient triple:
\[
A_-=A,\qquad B_-=B,\qquad C_-=C.
\]
Then the reduced negative-valley Hamiltonian is
\begin{equation}
h_-(p)
=
A\,p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
B\,(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
C\,(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\label{eq:hminus_sameABC}
\end{equation}

On the other hand, from the positive valley,
\[
h_+(-p)
=
-A\,p_\parallel \sigma^3
-
B\,(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
-
C\,(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2),
\]
so
\begin{equation}
-h_+(-p)
=
A\,p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
B\,(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
C\,(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\label{eq:minus_hplus_again}
\end{equation}
Comparing \eqref{eq:hminus_sameABC} and \eqref{eq:minus_hplus_again}, we obtain
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p).
}
\label{eq:pairingproved}
\end{equation}

So in the matched opposite-valley frame \eqref{eq:minusframe}, equality of the three coefficients is sufficient and exact.

\subsection{Doubled Dirac block}

Once \eqref{eq:pairingproved} holds, the doubled Hamiltonian
\begin{equation}
H_D(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}
\end{equation}
takes the standard Weyl-pair form
\[
H_D(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
d_i(p)\sigma^i & 0\\
0 & -d_i(-p)\sigma^i
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
To linear order,
\[
d_i(p)=M_{ij}p_j,
\]
with
\[
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
B & -C & 0\\
C & B & 0\\
0 & 0 & A
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Therefore
\[
H_D^{\rm lin}(p)
=
\rho^3\otimes (M_{ij}p_j\sigma^i),
\]
which is unitarily equivalent to the massless Dirac Hamiltonian.

\subsection{Final microscopic criterion}

Thus, in the minimal ansatz, local Dirac doubling reduces to the following finite conditions:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(A\neq 0\),
\item \((B,C)\neq (0,0)\),
\item the opposite valley exists,
\item in the matched opposite-valley frame,
\[
A_-=A_+,\qquad B_-=B_+,\qquad C_-=C_+.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Once these hold, the local doubled shell theory is Dirac.

\subsection{What is proved and what remains open}

What is proved here:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the negative-valley adapted-frame computation is explicit;
\item the standard pairing relation
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)
\]
follows exactly if the opposite valley carries the same coefficient triple \((A,B,C)\) in the matched frame;
\item the doubled block is Dirac.
\end{enumerate}

What remains open:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the actual microscopic symmetry guaranteeing the existence of the opposite valley;
\item the derivation that the true TECT linearization produces the same coefficient triple at \(\pm G_*\);
\item the spectral isolation of this doubled block from remote modes in the fully microscopic theory.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

At the positive shell valley \(+G_*\), the reduced Weyl Hamiltonian is
\[
h_+(p)
=
A\,p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
B\,(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
C\,(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\]

At the opposite valley \(-G_*\), choose the matched right-handed frame
\[
\hat n_-=-\hat n,\qquad e_1^-=e_1,\qquad e_2^-=-e_2.
\]
If the microscopic tensor ansatz at the opposite valley carries the same coefficient triple,
\[
A_-=A,\qquad B_-=B,\qquad C_-=C,
\]
then the reduced negative-valley Hamiltonian is
\[
h_-(p)
=
A\,p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
B\,(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
C\,(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2)
=
-h_+(-p).
\]

Therefore the doubled Hamiltonian
\[
H_D(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}
\]
is the standard Weyl-pair block and is unitarily equivalent to a massless Dirac Hamiltonian.

Thus, in the minimal TECT shell ansatz, local Dirac doubling reduces to the finite coefficient-matching condition
\[
A_-=A_+,\qquad B_-=B_+,\qquad C_-=C_+,
\]
together with local Weyl nondegeneracy
\[
A_\pm\neq 0,\qquad (B_\pm,C_\pm)\neq(0,0).
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Spectral Isolation of the Local Dirac Block Built from the Coefficients \texorpdfstring{$A,B,C$}{A,B,C}}

\subsection{Goal}

We now attach the spectral-isolation argument directly to the previously constructed local Weyl/Dirac block.
The purpose is to show that once the opposite-valley doubled block exists, it remains a genuine low-energy Dirac sector of the \emph{full} microscopic theory, provided the remote sector is gapped and the inter-sector mixing is sufficiently weak.

\subsection{Local doubled Dirac block}

From the previous steps, the positive-valley reduced Weyl block is
\begin{equation}
h_+(p)
=
A\,p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
B\,(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
C\,(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\label{eq:hplus_spec}
\end{equation}
In the matched opposite-valley frame, if
\[
A_-=A,\qquad B_-=B,\qquad C_-=C,
\]
then
\begin{equation}
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)+O(|p|^2).
\label{eq:hminus_spec}
\end{equation}
Hence the doubled four-dimensional block is
\begin{equation}
H_D(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:HD_spec}
\end{equation}

To linear order this is a massless Dirac block.
Indeed, define
\[
d(p)=M p,
\qquad
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
B & -C & 0\\
C & B & 0\\
0 & 0 & A
\end{pmatrix},
\]
with momentum coordinates ordered as \((p_1,p_2,p_\parallel)\).
Then the doubled linearized Hamiltonian is unitarily equivalent to
\begin{equation}
H_D^{\rm lin}(p)=\Gamma^i q_i,
\qquad
q_i=(Mp)_i,
\qquad
\{\Gamma^i,\Gamma^j\}=2\delta^{ij}\mathbf 1_4.
\label{eq:Diraclin}
\end{equation}
Its eigenvalues are
\begin{equation}
E_D^\pm(p)=\pm\sqrt{A^2p_\parallel^2+(B^2+C^2)(p_1^2+p_2^2)}.
\label{eq:Diracdisp}
\end{equation}
Thus the local Dirac velocities are
\[
v_\parallel=|A|,
\qquad
v_\perp=\sqrt{B^2+C^2}.
\]

\subsection{Full Hilbert-space decomposition}

Let the full microscopic one-particle Hilbert space decompose as
\begin{equation}
\mathcal H_{\rm full}=\mathcal H_D\oplus \mathcal H_R,
\label{eq:Hdecomp}
\end{equation}
where:
\begin{itemize}
\item \(\mathcal H_D\) is the four-dimensional local Dirac sector generated by the opposite shell pair \(\pm G_*\),
\item \(\mathcal H_R\) is the orthogonal complement containing all remote states.
\end{itemize}

With respect to this decomposition, the full Hamiltonian takes block form
\begin{equation}
H_{\rm full}(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_D(p) & V(p)\\
V^\dagger(p) & H_R(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:Hfull_spec}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Remote-gap assumption}

Assume there exists \(\Delta>0\) and a neighborhood \(\mathcal N\ni 0\) in momentum space such that
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{spec}\bigl(H_R(p)\bigr)\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing
\qquad
(p\in \mathcal N).
\label{eq:gapass}
\end{equation}
Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\|H_R(p)^{-1}\|\le \frac{1}{\Delta}
\qquad
(p\in \mathcal N).
\label{eq:gapres}
\end{equation}

This is the remote-gap condition.
It means that all non-Dirac modes remain uniformly massive in the low-energy window.

\subsection{Zero-mixing assumption at the crossing}

Assume next that the inter-sector mixing vanishes at the crossing:
\begin{equation}
V(0)=0.
\label{eq:V0}
\end{equation}
A natural sufficient reason is little-group mismatch:
the Dirac block and the remote sector transform in distinct irreducible little-group sectors at \(p=0\), so constant inter-sector mixing is symmetry-forbidden. :contentReference[oaicite:1]{index=1}

Assume moreover that
\begin{equation}
\|V(p)\|\le c\,|p|
\qquad
(p\in \mathcal N)
\label{eq:Vpbound}
\end{equation}
for some constant \(c>0\).

\subsection{Schur-complement reduction}

For \(|E|<\Delta/2\), the remote resolvent exists:
\[
H_R(p)-E
\]
is invertible.
Therefore the low-energy spectrum of \(H_{\rm full}(p)\) is determined by the Schur complement
\begin{equation}
F(E,p)
=
H_D(p)-E
-
V(p)\bigl(H_R(p)-E\bigr)^{-1}V^\dagger(p).
\label{eq:Feshbach}
\end{equation}
Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
E\in \mathrm{spec}(H_{\rm full}(p))
\iff
0\in \mathrm{spec}(F(E,p)).
\label{eq:Schurequiv}
\end{equation}

Define the self-energy
\begin{equation}
\Sigma(E,p):=
V(p)\bigl(H_R(p)-E\bigr)^{-1}V^\dagger(p).
\label{eq:selfenergy}
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
F(E,p)=H_D(p)-E-\Sigma(E,p).
\end{equation}

\subsection{Quadratic bound on the self-energy}

Using \eqref{eq:gapass} and \eqref{eq:Vpbound}, for \(|E|<\Delta/2\) one has
\[
\|(H_R(p)-E)^{-1}\|\le \frac{2}{\Delta}.
\]
Hence
\begin{equation}
\|\Sigma(E,p)\|
\le
\|V(p)\|^2\,\|(H_R(p)-E)^{-1}\|
\le
\frac{2c^2}{\Delta}|p|^2.
\label{eq:Sigbound}
\end{equation}
Thus the self-energy correction is only quadratic in momentum.

This is the central stability estimate.

\subsection{Effective low-energy Dirac operator}

Therefore the exact low-energy effective Hamiltonian on \(\mathcal H_D\) is
\begin{equation}
H_{\rm eff}(E,p)
=
H_D(p)-\Sigma(E,p)
=
H_D^{\rm lin}(p)+O(|p|^2)+O\!\left(\frac{|p|^2}{\Delta}\right).
\label{eq:Heff}
\end{equation}
Using \eqref{eq:Diraclin}, we obtain
\begin{equation}
H_{\rm eff}(E,p)
=
\Gamma^i q_i
+
O\!\left(\frac{|p|^2}{\Delta}\right),
\qquad
q_i=(Mp)_i.
\label{eq:HeffDirac}
\end{equation}
