Hence the leading linear Dirac cone is unchanged by the remote sector.
Remote states only generate subleading curvature corrections.

\subsection{Mass protection}

Spectral isolation alone does not automatically forbid an induced constant mass term.
So we also assume a residual low-energy symmetry that excludes a Dirac mass.

A sufficient condition is the existence of a grading operator \(\Gamma_5\) on \(\mathcal H_D\) such that
\begin{equation}
\{\Gamma_5,\Gamma^i\}=0,
\qquad
\Gamma_5^2=\mathbf 1_4,
\label{eq:Gamma5}
\end{equation}
and that the low-energy theory preserves this grading:
\begin{equation}
\Gamma_5 H_D(p)\Gamma_5=-H_D(p),
\qquad
\Gamma_5 V(p)\Gamma_5=-V(p).
\label{eq:chiralsym}
\end{equation}
Then the induced self-energy is also odd under \(\Gamma_5\), so no constant Dirac mass is generated.
In particular,
\begin{equation}
H_{\rm eff}(0,0)=0.
\label{eq:nomass}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Spectral-isolation theorem for the \texorpdfstring{$A,B,C$}{A,B,C} Dirac block}

\begin{theorem}[Spectral isolation of the local \(A,B,C\) Dirac block]
Assume:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the opposite shell pair \(\pm G_*\) exists and yields the doubled local Dirac block \eqref{eq:HD_spec};
\item the local Weyl coefficients satisfy
\[
A\neq 0,\qquad (B,C)\neq(0,0);
\]
\item the remote sector obeys the uniform gap condition \eqref{eq:gapass};
\item the inter-sector mixing satisfies
\[
V(0)=0,
\qquad
\|V(p)\|\le c|p|;
\]
\item a residual symmetry forbids a constant Dirac mass term.
\end{enumerate}
Then, for sufficiently small \(p\), the full microscopic Hamiltonian \(H_{\rm full}(p)\) contains a spectrally isolated four-dimensional low-energy sector whose effective Hamiltonian is
\begin{equation}
H_{\rm eff}(p)
=
\Gamma^i(Mp)_i
+
O\!\left(\frac{|p|^2}{\Delta}\right),
\label{eq:spec_theorem_result}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
B & -C & 0\\
C & B & 0\\
0 & 0 & A
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\det M=A(B^2+C^2)\neq 0.
\end{equation}
Consequently, the full theory contains a stable massless Dirac cone.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Assumptions (1) and (2) give a nondegenerate local Dirac block on \(\mathcal H_D\).
Assumption (3) makes the remote resolvent well-defined in a fixed low-energy window.
Assumption (4) implies the quadratic self-energy bound \eqref{eq:Sigbound}, so the Schur-complement effective Hamiltonian differs from the local Dirac block only by \(O(|p|^2/\Delta)\).
Assumption (5) excludes a generated constant mass term.
Hence the leading linear Dirac cone persists and remains spectrally isolated from all remote states.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Microscopic checklist in coefficient language}

In the present \(A,B,C\) language, the full local Dirac-stability problem reduces to the finite checklist:
\begin{equation}
A\neq 0,
\qquad
(B,C)\neq (0,0),
\qquad
A_-=A_+,\ B_-=B_+,\ C_-=C_+,
\label{eq:ABCcheck1}
\end{equation}
together with
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{spec}(H_R)\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing,
\qquad
V(0)=0,
\qquad
\|V(p)\|\le c|p|,
\label{eq:ABCcheck2}
\end{equation}
and a mass-forbidding symmetry.

Thus the full local Dirac problem is no longer conceptual.
It is an explicit finite coefficient-and-gap verification problem.

\subsection{What is proved and what remains open}

What is now proved:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the local doubled \(A,B,C\) shell block is Dirac;
\item remote states only modify it at order \(O(|p|^2/\Delta)\);
\item the Dirac cone is stable if the remote gap and zero-mixing conditions hold.
\end{enumerate}

What remains open:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the actual microscopic proof of the remote gap \(\Delta>0\),
\item the actual microscopic little-group mismatch implying \(V(0)=0\),
\item the final symmetry argument forbidding a generated mass term,
\item and the embedding of this isolated Dirac block into the full chiral gauge-matter bundle.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

The local Weyl coefficients extracted from the microscopic shell ansatz are
\[
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
B & -C & 0\\
C & B & 0\\
0 & 0 & A
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\det M=A(B^2+C^2).
\]
If
\[
A\neq 0,\qquad (B,C)\neq (0,0),
\]
then each valley is Weyl.
If, in addition, the opposite valley satisfies
\[
A_-=A_+,\qquad B_-=B_+,\qquad C_-=C_+,
\]
then the doubled shell block is Dirac.

Now decompose the full Hilbert space as
\[
\mathcal H_{\rm full}=\mathcal H_D\oplus \mathcal H_R,
\]
with \(\mathcal H_D\) the four-dimensional Dirac block and \(\mathcal H_R\) the remote sector.
If the remote sector is gapped,
\[
\mathrm{spec}(H_R)\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing,
\]
and the inter-sector mixing obeys
\[
V(0)=0,\qquad \|V(p)\|\le c|p|,
\]
then the Schur complement gives the effective low-energy Hamiltonian
\[
H_{\rm eff}(p)=\Gamma^i(Mp)_i+O(|p|^2/\Delta).
\]
Hence the full microscopic theory contains a stable massless Dirac cone, with only quadratic curvature corrections from remote states.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Little-Group Mismatch Implies Vanishing Constant Mixing}

\subsection{Goal}

We now prove the key representation-theoretic statement used in the spectral-isolation argument:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If the local Dirac sector and the remote sector belong to inequivalent little-group representations at }p=0,
\text{ then }V(0)=0.
}
\]

This is the precise meaning of the ``little-group mismatch'' condition appearing in the spectral-isolation checklist.

\subsection{Setup}

Let \(H_*\) denote the little group of the selected shell point \(G_*\), or more generally the residual symmetry group acting on the linearized microscopic theory at the crossing point \(p=0\).

Let the full Hilbert space decompose as
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}=\mathcal H_D\oplus \mathcal H_R,
\]
where

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\mathcal H_D\) is the four-dimensional local Dirac sector built from the opposite shell pair \(\pm G_*\),
\item \(\mathcal H_R\) is the orthogonal complement containing all remote modes.
\end{itemize}

Assume that \(H_*\) acts unitarily on \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}\), and that this action preserves the decomposition:
\[
U(h)\mathcal H_D\subset \mathcal H_D,
\qquad
U(h)\mathcal H_R\subset \mathcal H_R,
\qquad
h\in H_*.
\]
Thus \(\mathcal H_D\) and \(\mathcal H_R\) are \(H_*\)-modules.

At \(p=0\), write the full Hamiltonian in block form
\begin{equation}
H(0)=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_D(0) & V(0)\\
V(0)^\dagger & H_R(0)
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:H0block}
\end{equation}

The claim is that \(V(0)\) must vanish if the two sectors are representation-theoretically mismatched.

\subsection{Equivariance condition on the mixing block}

Assume the microscopic Hamiltonian is \(H_*\)-equivariant at \(p=0\):
\[
U(h)H(0)U(h)^{-1}=H(0)
\qquad
\forall h\in H_*.
\]
Writing this in block form relative to \(\mathcal H_D\oplus \mathcal H_R\), we obtain
\[
\begin{pmatrix}
U_D(h) & 0\\
0 & U_R(h)
\end{pmatrix}
\begin{pmatrix}
H_D(0) & V(0)\\
V(0)^\dagger & H_R(0)
\end{pmatrix}
\begin{pmatrix}
U_D(h)^{-1} & 0\\
0 & U_R(h)^{-1}
\end{pmatrix}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_D(0) & V(0)\\
V(0)^\dagger & H_R(0)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Comparing off-diagonal blocks gives
\begin{equation}
U_D(h)\,V(0)\,U_R(h)^{-1}=V(0)
\qquad
\forall h\in H_*.
\label{eq:intertwiner}
\end{equation}
Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
V(0)\in \mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D).
\label{eq:homspace}
\end{equation}

Thus the constant mixing block is not an arbitrary operator.
It must be an \(H_*\)-intertwiner.

\subsection{Schur-type vanishing criterion}

We now use the standard representation-theoretic fact:

\begin{quote}
If two \(H_*\)-modules share no common irreducible component, then the intertwiner space between them is zero.
\end{quote}

More precisely, decompose
\[
\mathcal H_D \cong \bigoplus_{\lambda} m_\lambda^D\, W_\lambda,
\qquad
\mathcal H_R \cong \bigoplus_{\lambda} m_\lambda^R\, W_\lambda,
\]
where \(W_\lambda\) runs over irreducible \(H_*\)-modules and \(m_\lambda^{D,R}\) are multiplicities.
Then
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)
\cong
\bigoplus_\lambda
\mathrm{Mat}_{m_\lambda^D\times m_\lambda^R}(\mathbb C).
\label{eq:homdecomp}
\end{equation}
Therefore,
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)=0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
m_\lambda^D m_\lambda^R=0
\ \text{for all }\lambda.
\label{eq:no_common_irrep}
\end{equation}

In words: if \(\mathcal H_D\) and \(\mathcal H_R\) have no common irreducible sector, then there is no nonzero equivariant map from \(\mathcal H_R\) to \(\mathcal H_D\).

Combining \eqref{eq:homspace} and \eqref{eq:no_common_irrep}, we immediately get \(V(0)=0\).

\subsection{Main theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Little-group mismatch implies zero constant mixing]
Let \(H_*\) be the little group at the selected shell crossing \(p=0\).
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the full microscopic Hamiltonian \(H(0)\) is \(H_*\)-equivariant;

\item the full Hilbert space decomposes into \(H_*\)-invariant subspaces
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}=\mathcal H_D\oplus \mathcal H_R;
\]

\item the \(H_*\)-modules \(\mathcal H_D\) and \(\mathcal H_R\) share no common irreducible component, i.e.
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)=0.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Then the constant inter-sector mixing vanishes:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V(0)=0.
}
\end{equation}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By \(H_*\)-equivariance of \(H(0)\), the off-diagonal block \(V(0)\) must satisfy
\[
U_D(h)V(0)U_R(h)^{-1}=V(0)
\qquad
\forall h\in H_*.
\]
Hence \(V(0)\in \mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)\).
By assumption, this intertwiner space is zero.
Therefore \(V(0)=0\).
\end{proof}

\subsection{Irreducible special case}

A particularly transparent corollary occurs when both sectors are irreducible.

\begin{corollary}[Irreducible mismatch]
If \(\mathcal H_D\) and \(\mathcal H_R\) are irreducible \(H_*\)-modules and
\[
\mathcal H_D \not\cong \mathcal H_R,
\]
then
\[
V(0)=0.
\]
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
By Schur's lemma,
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)=0
\]
for inequivalent irreducible modules.
Then the theorem applies.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Why this is the right condition for the Dirac block}

In the TECT setting, \(\mathcal H_D\) is the enriched shell block built from the opposite shell pair \(\pm G_*\).
The remote sector \(\mathcal H_R\) consists of all other microscopic modes.

If the enriched Dirac block transforms in a distinguished little-group sector that is absent from the remote states, then constant hybridization at the crossing is symmetry-forbidden.
This is exactly the content of \(V(0)=0\).

Hence the little-group mismatch is not merely heuristic.
It is the precise representation-theoretic reason why the Dirac block decouples at leading order.

\subsection{Linear mixing and the next nontrivial order}

Once \(V(0)=0\), the leading possible mixing begins at first order in momentum:
\[
V(p)=V_i p_i + O(|p|^2).
\]
Whether the linear term is allowed depends on the representation content of the momentum vector itself.

Indeed, since \(p\) transforms as the spatial little-group representation \(R_p\), the linear coefficient must belong to
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(R_p\otimes \mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D).
\]
Therefore:

\begin{itemize}
\item if this Hom-space also vanishes, then
\[
V(p)=O(|p|^2);
\]

\item if it is nonzero, then the generic situation is
\[
V(p)=O(|p|).
\]
\end{itemize}

For the spectral-isolation theorem, \(V(0)=0\) together with \(V(p)=O(|p|)\) is already sufficient, because the induced self-energy is then \(O(|p|^2/\Delta)\).

\subsection{Compatibility with the Schur-complement bound}

The spectral-isolation argument used previously assumes:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a remote gap
\[
\mathrm{spec}(H_R)\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing;
\]

\item zero constant mixing
\[
V(0)=0;
\]

\item a bound
\[
\|V(p)\|\le c|p|.
\]
\end{enumerate}

The theorem proved here gives the exact representation-theoretic origin of item (2).
Together with the remote gap, it implies that the self-energy correction satisfies
\[
\Sigma(E,p)=V(p)\bigl(H_R(p)-E\bigr)^{-1}V(p)^\dagger
=
O(|p|^2/\Delta),
\]
so the leading linear Dirac cone is preserved.

\subsection{Microscopic checklist in representation language}

Therefore the zero-mixing check is now completely explicit:

\begin{enumerate}
\item determine the little group \(H_*\) of the selected shell crossing;

\item decompose the enriched shell Dirac block \(\mathcal H_D\) into irreducible \(H_*\)-representations;

\item decompose the remote sector \(\mathcal H_R\) into irreducible \(H_*\)-representations;

\item verify that no irreducible representation appears in both.
\end{enumerate}

If that holds, then \(V(0)=0\) follows automatically.

\subsection{What is proved and what remains open}

What is now proved rigorously:
\begin{enumerate}
\item constant mixing \(V(0)\) is an \(H_*\)-intertwiner;
\item if \(\mathcal H_D\) and \(\mathcal H_R\) have no common irreducible component, then \(V(0)=0\);
\item this is the exact content of the ``little-group mismatch'' condition used in spectral isolation.
\end{enumerate}

What remains open microscopically:
\begin{enumerate}
\item identify the actual little group \(H_*\) of the TECT shell crossing in the full microscopic model;
\item decompose the actual enriched Dirac block into \(H_*\)-irreps;
\item decompose the remote sector into \(H_*\)-irreps;
\item verify the absence of common irreducible sectors.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

At the selected shell crossing \(p=0\), the constant inter-sector mixing block \(V(0)\) is not arbitrary.
If the microscopic Hamiltonian is little-group equivariant, then \(V(0)\) must satisfy
\[
U_D(h)V(0)U_R(h)^{-1}=V(0),
\qquad h\in H_*,
\]
so
\[
V(0)\in \mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D).
\]

Therefore, if the local Dirac sector \(\mathcal H_D\) and the remote sector \(\mathcal H_R\) share no common irreducible representation of the little group \(H_*\), then
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)=0,
\]
and hence
\[
V(0)=0.
\]

This is the rigorous form of the little-group mismatch criterion.
It explains why the local Dirac block decouples at the crossing point and why, together with the remote gap condition, the Schur-complement self-energy is only
\[
O(|p|^2/\Delta),
\]
so the linear Dirac cone remains stable.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Actual Little Group for the Selected BCC Axis and the Representation Content of the Local Dirac Block}

\subsection{Goal}

We now fix the actual little group of the selected BCC shell direction and clarify the exact status of the local Dirac block.

The key point is that two distinct symmetry levels must be separated:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the exact discrete cubic little group of the shell direction;
\item the infrared axial remnant acting on the transverse plane in the \(k\cdot p\) / continuum limit.
\end{enumerate}

Confusing these two levels leads to an overstatement.
The bare cubic little group does \emph{not} by itself force an ordinary two-dimensional irreducible representation.

\subsection{Selected shell axis}

Fix the BCC shell direction
\[
\hat n=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,1,0),
\qquad
G_*=|G_*|\hat n.
\]

There are two related stabilizer groups:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the stabilizer of the \emph{oriented} vector \(+ \hat n\);
\item the stabilizer of the \emph{unoriented pair} \(\{\pm \hat n\}\).
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Exact discrete cubic little groups}

At the level of the cubic point group, the exact statements are:

\begin{equation}
\mathrm{Stab}(+\hat n)\cong C_{2v},
\qquad
\mathrm{Stab}(\{\pm \hat n\})\cong D_{2h}.
\end{equation}

Moreover, all ordinary complex irreducible representations of \(C_{2v}\) and \(D_{2h}\) are one-dimensional.

Therefore:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the bare cubic little group does not force an ordinary complex 2D irrep.}
}
\end{equation}

This is the exact group-theoretic obstruction.

\subsection{What remains true at the kinematic level}

Even though the discrete cubic little group has only one-dimensional ordinary irreps, the spatial vector space still splits canonically as
\[
\mathbb R^3=\mathbb R\hat n\oplus \hat n^\perp.
\]
Hence there is always a longitudinal/transverse decomposition:
\[
\mathbf 3 \to \mathbf 1 \oplus (\text{2D transverse plane}).
\]
This is a vector-space splitting, not yet an irreducible-representation statement.

\subsection{Infrared axial remnant}

For the continuum \(k\cdot p\) analysis, the relevant symmetry is the infrared axial remnant acting on the transverse plane:
\[
\hat n^\perp.
\]
At that level, the two transverse polarizations furnish a real two-dimensional representation, equivalently a complex two-component low-energy envelope.

Thus the correct statement is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the 2D transverse structure is forced at the IR axial level, not by bare cubic ordinary irreps alone.}
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Shell-level transverse doublet}

Let
\[
\Pi_\perp=\hat n^\perp=\mathrm{span}\{e_1,e_2\}.
\]
For the selected shell direction, define the spatial witness tensors
\[
W^{(\alpha)}_{ij}=e^\alpha_i \hat n_j,
\qquad
\alpha=1,2.
\]
Under the residual shell symmetry,
\[
h\cdot \hat n=\hat n,
\qquad
h\cdot e_\alpha=R(h)_\alpha{}^\beta e_\beta,
\]
so
\[
h\cdot W^{(\alpha)}=R(h)_\alpha{}^\beta W^{(\beta)}.
\]
Hence the space
\[
W_{\mathrm{mix}}=\mathrm{span}\{W^{(1)},W^{(2)}\}=\Pi_\perp\otimes \hat n
\]
transforms as the transverse shell doublet.

This is the correct shell-level 2D representation entering the Dirac coupling test.

\subsection{Internal matching doublet}

Now let the internal-channel space contain a two-dimensional subrepresentation
\[
U_D=\mathrm{span}\{u^{(1)},u^{(2)}\}
\]
transforming in the same transverse way.
Then the tensor product
\[
W_{\mathrm{mix}}\otimes U_D
\]
contains an invariant line.
This is exactly the representation-theoretic reason why the shell couplings survive once a matching internal doublet exists.

So the true local Dirac carrier is not

\[
\text{bare cubic 2D irrep}
\]

but rather

\[
\text{transverse shell doublet} \otimes \text{matching internal doublet}.
\]

\subsection{Actual structure of the local Dirac block}

Therefore the local enriched shell Dirac block should be understood as follows:

\begin{equation}
\mathcal H_D
\cong
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}},
\end{equation}
where

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\) is the opposite-shell pair index \(\pm G_*\),
\item \(\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\) is the internal transverse doublet sector.
\end{itemize}

The first factor is not an ordinary irrep of the single-wavevector little group;
it comes from pairing the opposite valleys.
The second factor is the actual doublet carrier relevant for the shell-level Dirac theorem.

Thus the four-dimensional Dirac block is
\[
\mathcal H_D=\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{valley}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}.
\]

\subsection{What is actually proved}

We may now state the strongest honest theorem.

\begin{theorem}[Actual representation content of the local Dirac block]
For the selected BCC shell axis \(\hat n\parallel(1,1,0)\):

\begin{enumerate}
\item the exact oriented cubic stabilizer is \(C_{2v}\), and the pair stabilizer is \(D_{2h}\);

\item the ordinary complex irreducible representations of these exact discrete groups are one-dimensional, so bare cubic symmetry alone does not force a 2D ordinary irrep;

\item nevertheless, the shell analysis canonically supplies a 2D transverse plane \(\Pi_\perp\), and the infrared axial remnant acts on it as a real two-dimensional transverse representation;

\item if the microscopic enriched sector contains a matching internal transverse doublet, then the shell-coupling test closes and the local reduced block is carried by
\[
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}.
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\subsection{Consequence for the zero-mixing lemma}

This also clarifies the zero-mixing statement.
The “little-group mismatch” used in
\[
V(0)=0
\]
should not be interpreted as mismatch between ordinary bare-cubic 2D irreps.
Rather, it means mismatch between the actual shell-relevant representation sectors:

\begin{itemize}
\item the enriched Dirac block sector
\[
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}},
\]
\item and the remote sectors appearing in the microscopic linearization.
\end{itemize}

If no common shell-relevant irrep sector is shared, then
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)=0
\]
and therefore
\[
V(0)=0.
\]

\subsection{What remains open}

The remaining open microscopic problem is now sharp:

\begin{enumerate}
\item identify the actual internal doublet inside the TECT enriched linearization;
\item identify the actual remote-sector irrep content;
\item verify that the enriched Dirac block and remote sector share no common shell-relevant representation;
\item verify the remote spectral gap.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

For the selected BCC shell axis
\[
\hat n=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,1,0),
\]
the exact cubic little groups are
\[
\mathrm{Stab}(+\hat n)\cong C_{2v},
\qquad
\mathrm{Stab}(\{\pm\hat n\})\cong D_{2h}.
\]
Their ordinary complex irreducible representations are one-dimensional.
Therefore the bare cubic little group alone does not force an ordinary two-dimensional irrep.

However, the shell analysis still gives the canonical longitudinal/transverse splitting
\[
\mathbb R^3=\mathbb R\hat n\oplus \hat n^\perp,
\]
and in the infrared \(k\cdot p\) / continuum analysis the transverse plane \(\hat n^\perp\) carries the relevant axial two-dimensional representation.

Thus the local Dirac block is not built from a bare cubic 2D irrep.
It is built from
\[
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}},
\]
namely the opposite-shell pair factor times the matching internal transverse doublet.

Accordingly, the little-group mismatch condition used in the zero-mixing lemma means mismatch between this shell-relevant enriched Dirac sector and the remote-sector representation content, not mismatch between ordinary bare-cubic 2D irreps.

%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Remote-Sector Representation Checklist for the Zero-Mixing Condition}

\subsection{Goal}

The purpose of this section is to turn the abstract zero-mixing statement
\[
V(0)=0
\]
into a practical finite verification procedure.

The key theorem already proved is:

\begin{equation}
V(0)\in \mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D),
\end{equation}
so if
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)=0,
\]
then
\[
V(0)=0.
\]

What remains is to identify the shell-relevant representation content of the local Dirac block \(\mathcal H_D\) and compare it with the remote sector \(\mathcal H_R\).

\subsection{Shell-relevant form of the Dirac block}

The local Dirac block is not to be understood as a bare cubic 2D irrep.
Its actual shell-relevant structure is
\begin{equation}
\mathcal H_D
\cong
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}
\otimes
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}},
\label{eq:HDshell}
\end{equation}
where:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\) is the opposite-shell pair sector built from \(\pm G_*\),
\item \(\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\) is the matching internal transverse doublet.
\end{itemize}

Thus the representation content relevant for zero-mixing is the shell-enriched sector carried by \eqref{eq:HDshell}.

\subsection{Remote-sector decomposition}

Let the remote sector decompose into shell-relevant irreducible sectors:
\begin{equation}
\mathcal H_R
\cong
\bigoplus_{\lambda} m_\lambda^R\, W_\lambda.
\label{eq:HRdecomp}
\end{equation}
Likewise decompose the Dirac block as
\begin{equation}
\mathcal H_D
\cong
\bigoplus_{\lambda} m_\lambda^D\, W_\lambda.
\label{eq:HDdecomp}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)
\cong
\bigoplus_\lambda \mathrm{Mat}_{m_\lambda^D\times m_\lambda^R}(\mathbb C).
\label{eq:Homformula}
\end{equation}

Therefore the constant mixing vanishes if and only if
\begin{equation}
m_\lambda^D m_\lambda^R=0
\qquad
\forall \lambda.
\label{eq:nocommon}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Practical checklist}

To verify \(V(0)=0\), it is sufficient to carry out the following finite steps.

\paragraph{Step 1: fix the shell data.}
Choose the shell pair
\[
\pm G_*,
\qquad
\hat n=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|},
\]
and fix the matched adapted frames at \(\pm G_*\).

\paragraph{Step 2: identify the shell-relevant symmetry group.}
Determine the actual residual symmetry \(H_*\) acting on the selected shell sector.
At the exact cubic level this is the appropriate stabilizer subgroup; at the shell-reduction level one uses the shell-relevant axial/transverse representation data.

\paragraph{Step 3: identify the Dirac carrier.}
Construct the enriched Dirac block
\[
\mathcal H_D\cong \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}.
\]
Then decompose \(\mathcal H_D\) into irreducible \(H_*\)-sectors.

\paragraph{Step 4: identify the remote carriers.}
Decompose the remote modes into:
\begin{itemize}
\item volumetric sector,
\item non-enriched shell sectors,
\item higher-shell sectors,
\item internal sectors not belonging to the matched transverse doublet.
\end{itemize}
Then decompose each into irreducible \(H_*\)-sectors.

\paragraph{Step 5: compare the sectors.}
Check whether any irrep appearing in \(\mathcal H_D\) also appears in \(\mathcal H_R\).
If none does, then
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)=0.
\]

\paragraph{Step 6: conclude zero mixing.}
By equivariance,
\[
V(0)\in \mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D),
\]
hence
\[
V(0)=0.
\]

\subsection{Sufficient exclusion rules}

In practice, the no-common-sector condition is usually guaranteed if the remote states differ from the Dirac block in at least one of the following labels:

\begin{enumerate}
\item opposite-shell pair label is absent;
\item internal transverse-doublet label is absent;
\item parity/inversion label differs;
\item chirality or grading label differs;
\item shell-order label differs;
\item volumetric/transverse character differs.
\end{enumerate}

Any such mismatch can be enough to force the intertwiner space to vanish.

\subsection{Minimal operational theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Operational zero-mixing criterion]
Let \(H_*\) be the shell-relevant residual symmetry group at \(p=0\).
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the full Hamiltonian is \(H_*\)-equivariant at \(p=0\);
\item the local Dirac block \(\mathcal H_D\) and the remote sector \(\mathcal H_R\) are \(H_*\)-invariant;
\item after decomposition into irreducible shell-relevant sectors, \(\mathcal H_D\) and \(\mathcal H_R\) share no common irreducible component.
\end{enumerate}

Then
\[
V(0)=0.
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By equivariance, \(V(0)\) is an \(H_*\)-intertwiner from \(\mathcal H_R\) to \(\mathcal H_D\).
If there is no common irreducible sector, the intertwiner space vanishes.
Hence \(V(0)=0\).
\end{proof}

\subsection{Failure mode}

The only way the zero-mixing test can fail is if a remote mode carries the same shell-relevant representation sector as the Dirac block.
Then
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)\neq 0,
\]
and constant mixing is symmetry-allowed.

In that case, one must either:
\begin{enumerate}
\item enlarge the low-energy block to include that mode, or
\item use an additional symmetry/grading to rule out the mixing, or
\item show dynamically that the corresponding matrix element vanishes.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Connection to the master checklist}

This completes the rigorous form of the checklist item:

\[
\text{Little-group mismatch check} \Longrightarrow V(0)=0.
\]

Together with the remote gap condition and the mass-protection symmetry, this feeds directly into the spectral-isolation theorem for the local Dirac block.


\section{Final Summary}

To verify the zero-mixing condition \(V(0)=0\), one must compare the shell-relevant representation content of the local Dirac block
\[
\mathcal H_D\cong \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}
\]
with that of the remote sector
\[
\mathcal H_R.
\]

If the Hamiltonian is \(H_*\)-equivariant at \(p=0\), then the constant mixing block satisfies
\[
V(0)\in \mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D).
\]
Therefore
\[
V(0)=0
\]
whenever \(\mathcal H_D\) and \(\mathcal H_R\) share no common irreducible shell-relevant sector.

Operationally, the check is finite:
fix the shell pair, identify the shell-relevant symmetry group, decompose both the Dirac block and the remote sector into irreducible sectors, and verify that no sector appears in both.
If that holds, then zero constant mixing follows automatically.

\section{Remote-Sector Decomposition and the Practical Mismatch Checklist}

\subsection{Goal}

We now decompose the remote sector into physically meaningful components and determine, sector by sector, whether constant mixing with the local Dirac block is symmetry-allowed.

The local Dirac carrier is
\begin{equation}
\mathcal H_D
\cong
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}},
\label{eq:HDcarrier}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{itemize}
\item \(\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\) is the \(\pm G_*\) shell-pair space,
\item \(\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\) is the matched internal transverse doublet.
\end{itemize}

The question is whether any remote sector contains the same shell-relevant representation content.

\subsection{Canonical decomposition of the remote sector}

The most useful practical decomposition is
\begin{equation}
\mathcal H_R
=
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{vol}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{high}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{non\!-\!enr}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{wrong\!-\!int}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{extra}},
\label{eq:HRsplit}
\end{equation}
where:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{vol}}\): volumetric / longitudinal sector;
\item \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{high}}\): higher-shell sector not built from the selected \(\pm G_*\) pair;
\item \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{non\!-\!enr}}\): shell states without the enriched internal doublet;
\item \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{wrong\!-\!int}}\): states with internal content not matching the selected doublet;
\item \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{extra}}\): any additional sector specific to the microscopic realization.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Sector 1: volumetric sector}

The local Dirac block is built from the transverse shell sector.
Hence the volumetric sector differs already at the level of polarization character.

Therefore, unless the microscopic theory contains an accidental symmetry identifying longitudinal and transverse modes, one expects
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{vol}},\mathcal H_D)=0.
\end{equation}

So the default status of the volumetric sector is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{volumetric/longitudinal character mismatch}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
V_{\mathrm{vol}\to D}(0)=0.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Sector 2: higher-shell sector}

The Dirac block is built from the selected shell pair \(\pm G_*\).
Any state belonging to a different shell orbit carries a different shell label.
Thus, unless the microscopic Hamiltonian collapses shell labels at \(p=0\), one has a shell-label mismatch.

Therefore the default expectation is
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{high}},\mathcal H_D)=0.
\end{equation}

So:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{different shell orbit}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
V_{\mathrm{high}\to D}(0)=0.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Sector 3: non-enriched shell sector}

These are states that may live on the same shell but do not carry the enriched internal doublet needed for the Dirac block.
Thus they fail the internal matching condition.

Hence
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{non\!-\!enr}},\mathcal H_D)=0
\end{equation}
provided the internal doublet is genuinely absent.

So:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{same shell but no enriched internal doublet}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
V_{\mathrm{non\!-\!enr}\to D}(0)=0.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Sector 4: wrong internal sector}

This sector may have a nontrivial internal structure, but not the one matching the selected transverse doublet.
For example, it may carry:
\begin{itemize}
\item a singlet instead of a doublet,
\item a different doublet not identified with the shell-relevant one,
\item or a larger representation whose relevant multiplicity does not match.
\end{itemize}

If there is no common matching internal sector, then
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{wrong\!-\!int}},\mathcal H_D)=0.
\end{equation}

Thus:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{wrong internal representation}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
V_{\mathrm{wrong\!-\!int}\to D}(0)=0.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Sector 5: extra microscopic sectors}

Any additional sector must be tested case by case.
However, the same criterion applies:

\[
V(0)=0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_{\rm extra},\mathcal H_D)=0.
\]

So no new conceptual ingredient is needed.

\subsection{Compact checklist}

The practical test can be compressed into the following decision rule.

A remote sector \(\mathcal K\subset \mathcal H_R\) can mix at \(p=0\) with the Dirac block only if it matches \(\mathcal H_D\) in \emph{all} of the following shell-relevant labels:

\begin{enumerate}
\item selected shell-pair label \(\pm G_*\),
\item transverse/non-volumetric character,
\item enriched internal doublet label,
\item residual parity / grading / chirality labels, if present,
\item any extra microscopic conserved label carried by \(\mathcal H_D\).
\end{enumerate}

If even one label differs, then that sector is excluded from constant mixing.

\subsection{Practical theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Sectorwise zero-mixing criterion]
Let
\[
\mathcal H_R=\bigoplus_a \mathcal K_a
\]
be any decomposition of the remote sector into \(H_*\)-invariant sectors.
Assume the full Hamiltonian is \(H_*\)-equivariant at \(p=0\).
If each \(\mathcal K_a\) fails to match the Dirac block \(\mathcal H_D\) in at least one shell-relevant label, then
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal K_a,\mathcal H_D)=0
\qquad
\forall a,
\]
hence
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)=0
\]
and therefore
\[
V(0)=0.
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Since the decomposition is \(H_*\)-invariant,
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)
\cong
\bigoplus_a \mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal K_a,\mathcal H_D).
\]
If every summand vanishes, then the full Hom-space vanishes.
By the previously proved equivariance theorem, \(V(0)\) must belong to this Hom-space.
Hence \(V(0)=0\).
\end{proof}

\subsection{Failure mode}

The only dangerous case is when a remote sector \(\mathcal K\) shares the full shell-relevant label set of \(\mathcal H_D\).
Then
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal K,\mathcal H_D)\neq 0
\]
may occur, and constant mixing becomes symmetry-allowed.

If that happens, one must do one of the following:
\begin{enumerate}
\item enlarge the Dirac block to include \(\mathcal K\),
\item invoke an additional symmetry to forbid the matrix element,
\item or show dynamically that the matrix element vanishes.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Operational research order}

The most efficient order of execution is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item identify the enriched Dirac carrier \(\mathcal H_D\),
\item split \(\mathcal H_R\) into the four canonical sectors in \eqref{eq:HRsplit},
\item test shell-pair label mismatch,
\item test volumetric/transverse mismatch,
\item test enriched-internal-doublet mismatch,
\item then only for surviving cases test the finer grading/parity labels.
\end{enumerate}

In generic situations, the first three tests already eliminate all constant mixing channels.

\section{Final Summary}

The local Dirac block is carried by
\[
\mathcal H_D\cong \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}.
\]
To verify the zero-mixing condition \(V(0)=0\), decompose the remote sector as
\[
\mathcal H_R
=
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{vol}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{high}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{non\!-\!enr}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{wrong\!-\!int}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{extra}}.
\]

A remote sector can mix constantly with \(\mathcal H_D\) only if it matches the Dirac block in all shell-relevant labels:
the selected shell pair, transverse character, enriched internal doublet, and any additional residual labels.
If any one of these labels differs, then the corresponding intertwiner space vanishes and that sector gives no constant mixing.

Thus the practical zero-mixing test is finite and sectorwise:
check each remote sector against the Dirac carrier label set.
If all sectors mismatch in at least one label, then
\[
V(0)=0.
\]
