\section{Minimal Derivation of the TECT Condensate PDE}

\subsection{Honest scope}

At the current stage of TECT, the full unique microscopic condensate equation
\[
\partial_t \Phi = F(\Phi,\nabla\Phi)
\]
has not yet been derived from first principles. What can be derived rigorously now is the \emph{minimal effective condensate PDE} compatible with the already-closed structural requirements:

\begin{enumerate}
\item translational invariance,
\item spatial isotropy before shell selection,
\item finite-shell instability at \(|k|=q_*\),
\item quartic stabilization,
\item BCC shell selection at the nonlinear level.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the present result is an effective microscopic PDE derivation in the Landau--Brazovskii class, not yet the final unique UV completion.

\subsection{Minimal symmetry requirements}

Let \(\Phi(x,t)\) denote the condensate order parameter field.
The weakest assumptions compatible with the already established TECT shell architecture are:

\begin{enumerate}
\item translation invariance,
\item rotational invariance before condensation,
\item instability at a nonzero shell radius \(q_*\),
\item quartic nonlinear stabilization.
\end{enumerate}

Under these assumptions, the quadratic kernel must attain its minimum on the momentum shell
\[
|k|=q_*,
\]
rather than at \(k=0\).

\subsection{Minimal symmetry requirements}

Let \(\Phi(x,t)\) denote the condensate order parameter field.
The weakest assumptions compatible with the already established TECT shell architecture are:

\begin{enumerate}
\item translation invariance,
\item rotational invariance before condensation,
\item instability at a nonzero shell radius \(q_*\),
\item quartic nonlinear stabilization.
\end{enumerate}

Under these assumptions, the quadratic kernel must attain its minimum on the momentum shell
\[
|k|=q_*,
\]
rather than at \(k=0\).

\subsection{Minimal Landau--Brazovskii functional}

The minimal local free-energy functional with the required shell instability is
\begin{equation}
\mathcal F[\Phi]
=
\int d^3x
\left[
\frac{r}{2}|\Phi|^2
+
\frac{\kappa}{2}|\nabla\Phi|^2
+
\frac{\lambda}{2}|\nabla^2\Phi|^2
+
\frac{u}{4}|\Phi|^4
\right],
\label{eq:LBfree}
\end{equation}
with
\[
\lambda>0,\qquad u>0.
\]

To obtain a finite-shell instability one needs
\[
\kappa<0.
\]
Then the quadratic kernel is minimized at a nonzero shell radius.

\subsection{Shell radius}

In momentum space, the quadratic dispersion is
\begin{equation}
\omega(k)=r+\kappa |k|^2+\lambda |k|^4.
\label{eq:quad_disp}
\end{equation}
Its stationary points satisfy
\[
\frac{d\omega}{d|k|}
=
2|k|(\kappa+2\lambda |k|^2)=0.
\]
Besides the trivial solution \(|k|=0\), the nonzero minimum occurs at
\begin{equation}
q_*^2=-\frac{\kappa}{2\lambda},
\qquad
(\kappa<0,\ \lambda>0).
\label{eq:qstar}
\end{equation}
Thus the instability shell is precisely
\[
|k|=q_*.
\]

\subsection{Condensate PDE}

Using relaxational dynamics,
\begin{equation}
\partial_t\Phi
=
-\frac{\delta \mathcal F}{\delta \Phi^\dagger},
\label{eq:gradflow}
\end{equation}
we compute
\begin{equation}
\frac{\delta \mathcal F}{\delta \Phi^\dagger}
=
r\Phi
-\kappa \nabla^2\Phi
+\lambda \nabla^4\Phi
+u|\Phi|^2\Phi.
\label{eq:variation}
\end{equation}
Therefore the minimal TECT condensate PDE is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\partial_t\Phi
=
-r\Phi
+\kappa \nabla^2\Phi
-\lambda \nabla^4\Phi
-u|\Phi|^2\Phi.
}
\label{eq:TECTPDE}
\end{equation}

Equivalently, one may write the conservative form
\begin{equation}
i\partial_t\Phi
=
r\Phi
-\kappa \nabla^2\Phi
+\lambda \nabla^4\Phi
+u|\Phi|^2\Phi,
\label{eq:TECTPDEconservative}
\end{equation}
but for condensation and shell selection the gradient-flow form \eqref{eq:TECTPDE} is the natural minimal choice.

\subsection{Linearization around the BCC background}

Write
\[
\Phi=\Phi_0+\eta.
\]
Linearizing \eqref{eq:TECTPDE} gives
\begin{equation}
\partial_t \eta = -\mathbb L_{\Phi_0}\eta,
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\mathbb L_{\Phi_0}
=
r-\kappa \nabla^2+\lambda \nabla^4+2u|\Phi_0|^2
\end{equation}
in the simplest real scalar reduction.

In the complex or enriched case, the fluctuation operator is of Bogoliubov type,
\begin{equation}
i\partial_t \eta
=
\mathbb L_{\Phi_0}\eta+\mathbb M_{\Phi_0}\bar\eta,
\label{eq:BdG}
\end{equation}
and Bloch reduction of \eqref{eq:BdG} around the selected shell pair \(\pm G_*\) produces the reduced shell operator whose first-order coefficients are the already-identified Weyl data
\[
\lambda_\parallel,\qquad \alpha,\qquad \beta.
\]

\subsection{Linearization around the BCC background}

Write
\[
\Phi=\Phi_0+\eta.
\]
Linearizing \eqref{eq:TECTPDE} gives
\begin{equation}
\partial_t \eta = -\mathbb L_{\Phi_0}\eta,
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\mathbb L_{\Phi_0}
=
r-\kappa \nabla^2+\lambda \nabla^4+2u|\Phi_0|^2
\end{equation}
in the simplest real scalar reduction.

In the complex or enriched case, the fluctuation operator is of Bogoliubov type,
\begin{equation}
i\partial_t \eta
=
\mathbb L_{\Phi_0}\eta+\mathbb M_{\Phi_0}\bar\eta,
\label{eq:BdG}
\end{equation}
and Bloch reduction of \eqref{eq:BdG} around the selected shell pair \(\pm G_*\) produces the reduced shell operator whose first-order coefficients are the already-identified Weyl data
\[
\lambda_\parallel,\qquad \alpha,\qquad \beta.
\]

\subsection{What is closed and what is still open}

The following is now effectively closed:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{The minimal effective TECT condensate PDE is of Landau--Brazovskii type.}
}
\end{equation}

Namely,
\[
\partial_t\Phi
=
-r\Phi+\kappa\nabla^2\Phi-\lambda\nabla^4\Phi-u|\Phi|^2\Phi
\]
is the minimal PDE forced by translation invariance, pre-condensation isotropy, finite-shell instability, and quartic stabilization.

What remains open is the fully enriched microscopic completion:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the unique internal multi-component completion,
\item the actual microscopic origin of the enriched tensors \(N^a_{ij}\),
\item the proof of opposite-valley existence and pairing,
\item the full UV derivation of the shell-enriched Dirac block.
\end{enumerate}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Final Summary}

Starting from the minimal structural requirements of TECT --- translation invariance, pre-condensation isotropy, finite-shell instability, and quartic stabilization --- the minimal effective condensate free energy is
\[
\mathcal F[\Phi]
=
\int d^3x
\left[
\frac{r}{2}|\Phi|^2
+
\frac{\kappa}{2}|\nabla\Phi|^2
+
\frac{\lambda}{2}|\nabla^2\Phi|^2
+
\frac{u}{4}|\Phi|^4
\right],
\]
with
\[
\kappa<0,\qquad \lambda>0,\qquad u>0.
\]

Its quadratic kernel is minimized on the shell
\[
|k|=q_*,
\qquad
q_*^2=-\frac{\kappa}{2\lambda}.
\]

The corresponding minimal condensate PDE is
\[
\boxed{
\partial_t\Phi
=
-r\Phi+\kappa\nabla^2\Phi-\lambda\nabla^4\Phi-u|\Phi|^2\Phi.
}
\]

Projecting onto the BCC shell gives the BCC condensate amplitude equation
\[
\left(r-\frac{\kappa^2}{4\lambda}\right)A+\beta_{\mathrm{BCC}}uA^3=0.
\]

Thus the Landau--Brazovskii class is the minimal effective condensate PDE class compatible with the already-closed TECT shell architecture.

The remaining open step is not the existence of this minimal PDE class, but the fully enriched microscopic completion that generates the tensors \(N^a_{ij}\), the local Weyl coefficients, and opposite-valley Dirac doubling.

\subsection{Minimal condensate PDE}

The minimal effective condensate PDE is
\begin{equation}
\partial_t\Phi
=
-r\Phi+\kappa\nabla^2\Phi-\lambda\nabla^4\Phi-u|\Phi|^2\Phi,
\label{eq:LB_PDE}
\end{equation}
with
\[
\kappa<0,\qquad \lambda>0,\qquad u>0.
\]
Its quadratic kernel
\[
\omega(k)=r+\kappa|k|^2+\lambda|k|^4
\]
is minimized on the shell
\begin{equation}
|k|=q_*,
\qquad
q_*^2=-\frac{\kappa}{2\lambda}.
\label{eq:qstar_again}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Minimal condensate PDE}

The minimal effective condensate PDE is
\begin{equation}
\partial_t\Phi
=
-r\Phi+\kappa\nabla^2\Phi-\lambda\nabla^4\Phi-u|\Phi|^2\Phi,
\label{eq:LB_PDE}
\end{equation}
with
\[
\kappa<0,\qquad \lambda>0,\qquad u>0.
\]
Its quadratic kernel
\[
\omega(k)=r+\kappa|k|^2+\lambda|k|^4
\]
is minimized on the shell
\begin{equation}
|k|=q_*,
\qquad
q_*^2=-\frac{\kappa}{2\lambda}.
\label{eq:qstar_again}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Internal projector field}

Introduce the projective internal field
\[
z(x,t)\in \mathbb C^3,
\qquad
z^\dagger z=1,
\qquad
z\sim e^{i\chi(x,t)}z,
\]
and the rank-one projector
\[
P(x,t)=z(x,t)z^\dagger(x,t),
\qquad
P^2=P,\quad P^\dagger=P,\quad \Tr P=1.
\]
Hence \(P\) takes values in \(\mathbb{CP}^2\).

Let the relevant reduced internal channels be denoted by Hermitian generators
\[
s_1,\qquad s_2,\qquad s_3,
\]
where \(s_1,s_2\) span the shell-relevant internal transverse doublet and \(s_3\) is the longitudinal/internal axial channel.

\subsection{Minimal enriched shell coupling}

The minimal shell-enriched first-order coupling is
\begin{equation}
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}
=
\sum_{a=1}^3 N^a_{ij}\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\,(-i\partial_j)\otimes s_a.
\label{eq:Lenr_master}
\end{equation}
This is precisely the shell-reduced enriched form controlling the local Weyl coefficients. :contentReference[oaicite:3]{index=3}

Fix the selected shell direction
\[
\hat n=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|},
\]
and an adapted orthonormal frame
\[
\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\}.
\]
The minimal axial tensor structures are generated by
\[
\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\qquad
\hat n_i e_{1j},
\qquad
\hat n_i e_{2j}.
\]
Equivalently, on the transverse plane one may use the identity and complex-structure basis
\[
I_2,\qquad
J_2=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & -1\\
1 & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

\subsection{Minimal projector/enriched operator ansatz}

The smallest actual enriched completion compatible with the shell-reduced Weyl template is
\begin{align}
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}
&=
g_\parallel\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\hat n_i\,(\hat n\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_3
\notag\\
&\quad
+
g_I\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\hat n_i
\Bigl[
(e_1\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_1
+
(e_2\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_2
\Bigr]
\notag\\
&\quad
+
g_J\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\hat n_i
\Bigl[
-(e_2\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_1
+
(e_1\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_2
\Bigr].
\label{eq:projector_enriched_ansatz}
\end{align}

Here:
\begin{itemize}
\item \(g_\parallel\) is the longitudinal enriched coupling,
\item \(g_I\) is the transverse-even coupling,
\item \(g_J\) is the transverse-odd coupling.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Direct extraction of the microscopic tensors}

Comparing \eqref{eq:projector_enriched_ansatz} with the master tensor form
\[
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}
=
\sum_{a=1}^3 N^a_{ij}\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\,(-i\partial_j)\otimes s_a,
\]
we read off immediately:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
N^3_{ij}=g_\parallel\,\hat n_i\hat n_j,
}
\label{eq:N3_final}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
N^1_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{1j}-g_J\,\hat n_i e_{2j},
}
\label{eq:N1_final}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
N^2_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{2j}+g_J\,\hat n_i e_{1j}.
}
\label{eq:N2_final}
\end{equation}

These are the explicit microscopic enriched tensors generated by the minimal projector/enriched completion.

\subsection{Extraction of the Weyl coefficients}

Using the shell-level contraction formulas already established in the shell-reduction theorem, :contentReference[oaicite:5]{index=5}
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_*|G_*|\,\hat n_i\hat n_jN^3_{ij},
\label{eq:lam_form}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\left(
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
\right),
\label{eq:alpha_form}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\left(
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
\right),
\label{eq:beta_form}
\end{equation}
we substitute \eqref{eq:N3_final}--\eqref{eq:N2_final}.

For the longitudinal coefficient:
\[
\hat n_i\hat n_jN^3_{ij}
=
g_\parallel(\hat n\cdot\hat n)^2
=
g_\parallel,
\]
hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel=\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_\parallel.
}
\label{eq:lam_g}
\end{equation}

For \(\alpha\):
\[
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
=
g_I(e_1\cdot e_1)-g_J(e_1\cdot e_2)=g_I,
\]
\[
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
=
g_I(e_2\cdot e_2)+g_J(e_2\cdot e_1)=g_I,
\]
so
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha=\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_I.
}
\label{eq:alpha_g}
\end{equation}

For \(\beta\):
\[
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
=
g_I(e_2\cdot e_1)-g_J(e_2\cdot e_2)
=
-g_J,
\]
\[
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
=
g_I(e_1\cdot e_2)+g_J(e_1\cdot e_1)
=
g_J,
\]
therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\beta=-\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_J.
}
\label{eq:beta_g}
\end{equation}

The overall sign of \(\beta\) depends on the transverse orientation convention; only \(\beta^2\) is invariant.

\subsection{Explicit Weyl matrix and determinant}

The reduced local Weyl block is
\begin{equation}
h_+(p)
=
\lambda_\parallel p_\parallel\sigma^3
+
\alpha(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
\beta(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2).
\label{eq:hweyl_final}
\end{equation}
Hence the coefficient matrix is
\begin{equation}
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha & -\beta & 0\\
\beta & \alpha & 0\\
0 & 0 & \lambda_\parallel
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:M_final}
\end{equation}
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\det M=\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\label{eq:detM_final}
\end{equation}

Substituting \eqref{eq:lam_g}--\eqref{eq:beta_g},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3
\,g_\parallel\,(g_I^2+g_J^2).
}
\label{eq:detM_g}
\end{equation}

Thus the local Weyl nondegeneracy condition is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
g_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
(g_I,g_J)\neq (0,0).
}
\label{eq:weyl_cond_g}
\end{equation}

\subsection{What has now been achieved}

We have now explicitly combined:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the minimal Landau--Brazovskii condensate PDE,
\item the minimal projector-field internal sector,
\item the shell-enriched first-order coupling,
\item and the shell-reduction matching theorem.
\end{enumerate}

The result is that the microscopic enriched tensors are explicitly
\[
N^3_{ij}=g_\parallel\,\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\qquad
N^1_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{1j}-g_J\,\hat n_i e_{2j},
\qquad
N^2_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{2j}+g_J\,\hat n_i e_{1j},
\]
and therefore
\[
\lambda_\parallel=\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_\parallel,
\qquad
\alpha=\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_I,
\qquad
\beta=-\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_J.
\]

So the local Weyl problem has been reduced to the explicit finite coefficient test
\[
g_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
(g_I,g_J)\neq (0,0).
\]

\subsection{What is closed and what remains open}

What is now effectively closed:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Minimal Landau--Brazovskii PDE}
+
\text{minimal enriched projector coupling}
\Longrightarrow
N^a_{ij},\ \lambda_\parallel,\ \alpha,\ \beta,\ M.
}
\end{equation}

What remains open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the proof that the actual microscopic TECT dynamics generates precisely this minimal enriched coupling;
\item the existence and pairing of the opposite shell valley;
\item the remote-sector gap;
\item the final mass-protection symmetry.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

Starting from the minimal shell-enriched projector ansatz
\[
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}
=
g_\parallel(\partial_i\phi_0)\hat n_i(\hat n\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_3
+
g_I(\partial_i\phi_0)\hat n_i
\Bigl[
(e_1\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_1
+
(e_2\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_2
\Bigr]
+
g_J(\partial_i\phi_0)\hat n_i
\Bigl[
-(e_2\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_1
+
(e_1\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_2
\Bigr],
\]
the enriched microscopic tensors are explicitly
\[
N^3_{ij}=g_\parallel\,\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\]
\[
N^1_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{1j}-g_J\,\hat n_i e_{2j},
\]
\[
N^2_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{2j}+g_J\,\hat n_i e_{1j}.
\]

The shell-contraction formulas then give
\[
\lambda_\parallel=\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_\parallel,
\qquad
\alpha=\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_I,
\qquad
\beta=-\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_J.
\]
Hence the Weyl coefficient matrix is
\[
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha & -\beta & 0\\
\beta & \alpha & 0\\
0 & 0 & \lambda_\parallel
\end{pmatrix},
\]
and therefore
\[
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3
\,g_\parallel\,(g_I^2+g_J^2).
\]

Thus the local BCC shell crossing is Weyl whenever
\[
g_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
(g_I,g_J)\neq(0,0).
\]

So the local Weyl problem is now reduced to an explicit finite coefficient check on the three enriched couplings \(g_\parallel,g_I,g_J\).

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Landau--Brazovskii Condensate PDE with Minimal Enriched Projector Coupling}

\subsection{Scope}

At the present stage of TECT, the full unique UV microscopic condensate equation has not yet been derived from first principles. However, the following two facts are already structurally fixed:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the minimal condensate PDE must be of Landau--Brazovskii type, as a consequence of translation invariance, pre-condensation isotropy, finite-shell instability, and quartic stabilization;

\item the local Weyl/Dirac problem near a selected BCC shell pair has already been reduced to the enriched microscopic tensors
\[
N^a_{ij},
\]
through the shell-contraction formulas for
\[
\lambda_\parallel,\qquad \alpha,\qquad \beta.
\]
\end{enumerate}

The purpose of this section is to combine these two ingredients into a single minimal enriched effective theory and to extract the tensors \(N^a_{ij}\) explicitly.

\subsection{Minimal Landau--Brazovskii condensate PDE}

Let \(\Phi(x,t)\) denote the condensate order parameter.
The minimal effective free-energy functional compatible with finite-shell instability is
\begin{equation}
\mathcal F[\Phi]
=
\int d^3x
\left[
\frac{r}{2}|\Phi|^2
+
\frac{\kappa}{2}|\nabla\Phi|^2
+
\frac{\lambda}{2}|\nabla^2\Phi|^2
+
\frac{u}{4}|\Phi|^4
\right],
\label{eq:LB_free_energy}
\end{equation}
with
\[
\kappa<0,\qquad \lambda>0,\qquad u>0.
\]

The quadratic dispersion is
\begin{equation}
\omega(k)=r+\kappa |k|^2+\lambda |k|^4.
\label{eq:LB_dispersion}
\end{equation}
Its nonzero minimum occurs at
\begin{equation}
q_*^2=-\frac{\kappa}{2\lambda}.
\label{eq:qstar_def}
\end{equation}
Thus the instability shell is
\[
|k|=q_*.
\]

Using relaxational dynamics,
\begin{equation}
\partial_t \Phi = -\frac{\delta \mathcal F}{\delta \Phi^\dagger},
\label{eq:grad_flow}
\end{equation}
we obtain
\begin{equation}
\frac{\delta \mathcal F}{\delta \Phi^\dagger}
=
r\Phi-\kappa\nabla^2\Phi+\lambda\nabla^4\Phi+u|\Phi|^2\Phi.
\label{eq:LB_variation}
\end{equation}
Hence the minimal effective condensate PDE is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\partial_t\Phi
=
-r\Phi+\kappa\nabla^2\Phi-\lambda\nabla^4\Phi-u|\Phi|^2\Phi.
}
\label{eq:LB_PDE}
\end{equation}

\subsection{BCC shell background}

Let the first BCC reciprocal shell be
\begin{equation}
S_{\mathrm{BCC}}
=
\frac{q_*}{\sqrt2}
\left\{
(\pm1,\pm1,0),\,
(\pm1,0,\pm1),\,
(0,\pm1,\pm1)
\right\}.
\label{eq:BCC_shell}
\end{equation}
Choose one representative from each antipodal pair, denoted \(Q_a\), \(a=1,\dots,6\), and consider the standing-wave ansatz
\begin{equation}
\Phi_0(x)=2A\sum_{a=1}^6 \cos(Q_a\cdot x).
\label{eq:BCC_ansatz}
\end{equation}

Substituting \eqref{eq:BCC_ansatz} into the static Euler--Lagrange equation
\[
r\Phi-\kappa\nabla^2\Phi+\lambda\nabla^4\Phi+u|\Phi|^2\Phi=0
\]
and projecting back to the BCC shell gives
\begin{equation}
\left(r-\frac{\kappa^2}{4\lambda}\right)A+\beta_{\mathrm{BCC}}uA^3=0,
\label{eq:BCC_amplitude_eq}
\end{equation}
where \(\beta_{\mathrm{BCC}}>0\) is the BCC shell quartic projection coefficient.
Therefore
\begin{equation}
A^2
=
-\frac{1}{\beta_{\mathrm{BCC}}u}
\left(
r-\frac{\kappa^2}{4\lambda}
\right).
\label{eq:BCC_A2}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Internal projector field}

Introduce the internal projective field
\begin{equation}
z(x,t)\in\mathbb C^3,
\qquad
z^\dagger z=1,
\qquad
z\sim e^{i\chi(x,t)}z,
\end{equation}
and define the rank-one projector
\begin{equation}
P(x,t)=z(x,t)z^\dagger(x,t),
\qquad
P^2=P,\qquad
P^\dagger=P,\qquad
\Tr P=1.
\label{eq:projector_def}
\end{equation}
Thus \(P\) takes values in \(\mathbb{CP}^2\).

Let the shell-relevant reduced internal generators be
\[
s_1,\qquad s_2,\qquad s_3,
\]
where \(s_1,s_2\) span the internal transverse doublet and \(s_3\) is the axial/longitudinal internal channel.

\subsection{Selected shell direction and adapted frame}

Fix a selected shell vector
\begin{equation}
G_*\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}},
\qquad
\hat n:=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|}.
\label{eq:selected_shell_direction}
\end{equation}
Choose an adapted orthonormal frame
\begin{equation}
\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\},
\qquad
e_a\cdot \hat n=0,
\qquad
e_a\cdot e_b=\delta_{ab},
\quad a,b=1,2.
\label{eq:adapted_frame}
\end{equation}
Write the shell-relative momentum as
\begin{equation}
p=p_\parallel \hat n+p_1 e_1+p_2 e_2.
\label{eq:momentum_decomp}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Minimal enriched shell coupling}

The minimal shell-enriched first-order operator consistent with the already established Weyl template is
\begin{align}
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}
&=
g_\parallel\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\hat n_i\,(\hat n\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_3
\notag\\
&\quad
+
g_I\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\hat n_i
\Bigl[
(e_1\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_1
+
(e_2\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_2
\Bigr]
\notag\\
&\quad
+
g_J\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\hat n_i
\Bigl[
-(e_2\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_1
+
(e_1\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_2
\Bigr].
\label{eq:min_enriched_operator}
\end{align}
Here:
\begin{itemize}
\item \(g_\parallel\) is the longitudinal enriched coupling,
\item \(g_I\) is the transverse-even coupling,
\item \(g_J\) is the transverse-odd coupling.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Microscopic tensor extraction}

The master shell-enriched tensor form is
\begin{equation}
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}
=
\sum_{a=1}^3 N^a_{ij}\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\,(-i\partial_j)\otimes s_a.
\label{eq:tensor_master_form}
\end{equation}
Comparing \eqref{eq:min_enriched_operator} with \eqref{eq:tensor_master_form}, we read off
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
N^3_{ij}=g_\parallel\,\hat n_i\hat n_j,
}
\label{eq:N3_result}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
N^1_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{1j}-g_J\,\hat n_i e_{2j},
}
\label{eq:N1_result}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
N^2_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{2j}+g_J\,\hat n_i e_{1j}.
}
\label{eq:N2_result}
\end{equation}

These are the explicit enriched microscopic tensors generated by the minimal projector-enriched completion of the Landau--Brazovskii condensate sector.

\subsection{Shell-contraction formulas}

The shell-reduction theorem gives the effective Weyl coefficients through the contractions
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel
=
\kappa_*|G_*|\,\hat n_i\hat n_jN^3_{ij},
\label{eq:lambda_contract}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\alpha
=
\frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\left(
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
\right),
\label{eq:alpha_contract}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\beta
=
\frac{\kappa_*|G_*|}{2}
\left(
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
-
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
\right).
\label{eq:beta_contract}
\end{equation}

We now compute them explicitly.

\paragraph{Longitudinal coefficient.}
Using \eqref{eq:N3_result},
\[
\hat n_i\hat n_jN^3_{ij}
=
g_\parallel(\hat n\cdot\hat n)^2
=
g_\parallel,
\]
hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel=\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_\parallel.
}
\label{eq:lambda_final}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Transverse-even coefficient.}
Using \eqref{eq:N1_result} and \eqref{eq:N2_result},
\[
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
=
g_I(e_1\cdot e_1)-g_J(e_1\cdot e_2)
=
g_I,
\]
\[
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
=
g_I(e_2\cdot e_2)+g_J(e_2\cdot e_1)
=
g_I.
\]
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha=\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_I.
}
\label{eq:alpha_final}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Transverse-odd coefficient.}
Again,
\[
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
=
g_I(e_2\cdot e_1)-g_J(e_2\cdot e_2)
=
-g_J,
\]
\[
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
=
g_I(e_1\cdot e_2)+g_J(e_1\cdot e_1)
=
g_J.
\]
Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\beta=-\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_J.
}
\label{eq:beta_final}
\end{equation}
The sign of \(\beta\) depends on the transverse orientation convention; only \(\beta^2\) is invariant.

\subsection{Reduced Weyl Hamiltonian}

The local reduced Weyl Hamiltonian therefore takes the canonical form
\begin{equation}
h_+(p)
=
\lambda_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
\alpha(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
\beta(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2).
\label{eq:weyl_block}
\end{equation}
Its coefficient matrix is
\begin{equation}
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha & -\beta & 0\\
\beta & \alpha & 0\\
0 & 0 & \lambda_\parallel
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:weyl_M}
\end{equation}
Hence
\begin{equation}
\det M=\lambda_\parallel(\alpha^2+\beta^2).
\label{eq:detM_general}
\end{equation}

Substituting \eqref{eq:lambda_final}--\eqref{eq:beta_final}, we obtain
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3\,g_\parallel\,(g_I^2+g_J^2).
}
\label{eq:detM_explicit}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Local Weyl criterion}

Therefore the local BCC shell crossing is Weyl if and only if
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
g_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
(g_I,g_J)\neq (0,0).
}
\label{eq:weyl_condition_explicit}
\end{equation}

Thus the local Weyl problem is no longer an abstract existence problem.
It has been reduced to a finite coefficient test on the three enriched couplings
\[
g_\parallel,\qquad g_I,\qquad g_J.
\]

\subsection{What is closed and what remains open}

What is now effectively closed is the following chain:
\[
\text{Landau--Brazovskii condensate PDE}
+
\text{minimal enriched projector coupling}
\Longrightarrow
N^a_{ij}
\Longrightarrow
\lambda_\parallel,\alpha,\beta
\Longrightarrow
M,\det M.
\]

What remains open is not this extraction itself, but the final microscopic completion:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the proof that the actual TECT microscopic dynamics generates precisely this minimal enriched coupling;
\item the existence and pairing of the opposite shell valley at \(-G_*\);
\item the remote-sector gap;
\item the final symmetry forbidding a constant Dirac mass.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

Starting from the minimal shell-enriched projector ansatz
\[
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}
=
g_\parallel(\partial_i\phi_0)\hat n_i(\hat n\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_3
+
g_I(\partial_i\phi_0)\hat n_i
\Bigl[
(e_1\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_1
+
(e_2\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_2
\Bigr]
+
g_J(\partial_i\phi_0)\hat n_i
\Bigl[
-(e_2\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_1
+
(e_1\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_2
\Bigr],
\]
the enriched microscopic tensors are explicitly
\[
N^3_{ij}=g_\parallel\,\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\]
\[
N^1_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{1j}-g_J\,\hat n_i e_{2j},
\]
\[
N^2_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{2j}+g_J\,\hat n_i e_{1j}.
\]

The shell-contraction formulas give
\[
\lambda_\parallel=\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_\parallel,
\qquad
\alpha=\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_I,
\qquad
\beta=-\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_J.
\]
Hence the Weyl coefficient matrix is
\[
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha & -\beta & 0\\
\beta & \alpha & 0\\
0 & 0 & \lambda_\parallel
\end{pmatrix},
\]
and therefore
\[
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3\,g_\parallel\,(g_I^2+g_J^2).
\]

Thus the local BCC shell crossing is Weyl whenever
\[
g_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
(g_I,g_J)\neq (0,0).
\]

So the local Weyl problem has been reduced to an explicit finite coefficient test on the three enriched couplings \(g_\parallel,g_I,g_J\).

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Witness Theorem for the Minimal Enriched Couplings \texorpdfstring{$g_\parallel,g_I,g_J$}{g∥, gI, gJ}}

\subsection{Goal}

The purpose of this section is to determine, at the level of symmetry alone, whether the three minimal enriched couplings
\[
g_\parallel,\qquad g_I,\qquad g_J
\]
can be forbidden identically.

The strongest honest statement is the following:

\begin{enumerate}
\item symmetry determines the \emph{allowed invariant tensor space};
\item this invariant space is exactly three-dimensional in the minimal shell-enriched setting;
\item the three basis directions correspond precisely to the longitudinal, transverse-even, and transverse-odd couplings;
\item therefore symmetry does \emph{not} force
\[
g_\parallel=g_I=g_J=0;
\]
\item however, symmetry alone does \emph{not} guarantee that each coefficient is nonzero in the actual microscopic realization.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the correct result is an \emph{allowance theorem} plus a \emph{witness criterion}, not a purely symmetry-based nonvanishing theorem.

\subsection{Shell-relevant representation data}

Fix a selected shell direction
\[
\hat n=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|},
\]
with adapted orthonormal frame
\[
\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\}.
\]
Let
\[
\Pi_\perp=\mathrm{span}\{e_1,e_2\}
\]
denote the transverse plane.

We work at the shell-relevant infrared level, where the residual symmetry acting on \(\Pi_\perp\) is the axial group
\[
O(2)_\perp
\quad\text{or, at least, its connected part }SO(2)_\perp.
\]
The shell-relative momentum decomposes as
\[
p=p_\parallel \hat n+p_1 e_1+p_2 e_2.
\]
Hence the momentum representation splits as
\[
V_p \cong \mathbf 1_\parallel \oplus \mathbf 2_\perp,
\]
where:
\begin{itemize}
\item \(\mathbf 1_\parallel\) is the longitudinal scalar channel carried by \(p_\parallel\),
\item \(\mathbf 2_\perp\) is the transverse vector channel carried by \((p_1,p_2)\).
\end{itemize}

Now let the shell-relevant internal sector contain:
\begin{itemize}
\item one axial/internal scalar channel \(s_3\),
\item one transverse internal doublet \((s_1,s_2)\).
\end{itemize}
Thus the internal representation also splits as
\[
V_{\mathrm{int}} \cong \mathbf 1_3 \oplus \mathbf 2_s,
\]
where \(\mathbf 2_s\) transforms in the same transverse way as \(\mathbf 2_\perp\).

\subsection{Problem reformulation}

A first-order enriched shell coupling is an invariant linear map from the shell-gradient/momentum/internal data to a scalar operator coefficient.
Equivalently, it belongs to
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{Hom}_{O(2)_\perp}(V_p\otimes V_{\mathrm{int}},\mathbf 1).
\label{eq:invariant_hom}
\end{equation}

Using the above decomposition,
\[
V_p\otimes V_{\mathrm{int}}
\cong
(\mathbf 1_\parallel\oplus \mathbf 2_\perp)\otimes(\mathbf 1_3\oplus \mathbf 2_s).
\]
Expanding,
\[
V_p\otimes V_{\mathrm{int}}
\cong
(\mathbf 1_\parallel\otimes \mathbf 1_3)
\oplus
(\mathbf 1_\parallel\otimes \mathbf 2_s)
\oplus
(\mathbf 2_\perp\otimes \mathbf 1_3)
\oplus
(\mathbf 2_\perp\otimes \mathbf 2_s).
\]

Now:
\begin{itemize}
\item \(\mathbf 1_\parallel\otimes \mathbf 1_3 \cong \mathbf 1\) contributes one invariant;
\item \(\mathbf 1_\parallel\otimes \mathbf 2_s \cong \mathbf 2_s\) contributes no scalar;
\item \(\mathbf 2_\perp\otimes \mathbf 1_3 \cong \mathbf 2_\perp\) contributes no scalar;
\item \(\mathbf 2_\perp\otimes \mathbf 2_s\) contributes two scalar structures, namely the symmetric Euclidean contraction and the antisymmetric \(J\)-contraction.
\end{itemize}

Therefore the invariant space is three-dimensional.

\subsection{Dimension count}

We now state this precisely.

\begin{proposition}[Dimension of the minimal invariant coupling space]
Let \(V_p\cong \mathbf 1_\parallel\oplus \mathbf 2_\perp\) and \(V_{\mathrm{int}}\cong \mathbf 1_3\oplus \mathbf 2_s\), with \(\mathbf 2_\perp\) and \(\mathbf 2_s\) isomorphic transverse \(O(2)_\perp\)-representations.
Then
\begin{equation}
\dim \mathrm{Hom}_{O(2)_\perp}(V_p\otimes V_{\mathrm{int}},\mathbf 1)=3.
\label{eq:dim3}
\end{equation}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
From the above decomposition,
\[
V_p\otimes V_{\mathrm{int}}
\cong
(\mathbf 1\otimes \mathbf 1)
\oplus
(\mathbf 1\otimes \mathbf 2)
\oplus
(\mathbf 2\otimes \mathbf 1)
\oplus
(\mathbf 2\otimes \mathbf 2).
\]
The first summand contributes one scalar invariant.
The middle two contribute none.
For the last summand, \(\mathbf 2\otimes \mathbf 2\), the invariant bilinear forms on a two-dimensional oriented Euclidean plane are exactly two-dimensional: one generated by the metric \(\delta_{ab}\), and one generated by the complex structure / antisymmetric tensor \(J_{ab}\).
Hence the total invariant dimension is
\[
1+2=3.
\]
\end{proof}

\subsection{Explicit invariant basis}

We now construct these three invariant couplings explicitly.

\paragraph{Longitudinal channel.}
The scalar \(\mathbf 1_\parallel\otimes \mathbf 1_3\) is generated by
\begin{equation}
\mathcal O_\parallel
=
(\hat n\cdot p)\, s_3.
\label{eq:Opar}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Transverse-even channel.}
Using the Euclidean metric on \(\Pi_\perp\), the first scalar from \(\mathbf 2_\perp\otimes \mathbf 2_s\) is
\begin{equation}
\mathcal O_I
=
p_1 s_1+p_2 s_2.
\label{eq:OI}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Transverse-odd channel.}
Using the transverse complex structure
\[
J=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & -1\\
1 & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\]
the second scalar is
\begin{equation}
\mathcal O_J
=
-p_2 s_1+p_1 s_2.
\label{eq:OJ}
\end{equation}

Therefore every minimal first-order shell-enriched invariant operator is of the form
\begin{equation}
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)}
=
g_\parallel \mathcal O_\parallel
+
g_I \mathcal O_I
+
g_J \mathcal O_J.
\label{eq:general_invariant}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Identification with the previously extracted operator}

The explicit minimal enriched operator constructed previously was
\begin{align}
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}
&=
g_\parallel\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\hat n_i\,(\hat n\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_3
\notag\\
&\quad
+
g_I\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\hat n_i
\Bigl[
(e_1\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_1
+
(e_2\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_2
\Bigr]
\notag\\
&\quad
+
g_J\,(\partial_i\phi_0)\hat n_i
\Bigl[
-(e_2\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_1
+
(e_1\cdot(-i\nabla))\otimes s_2
\Bigr].
\label{eq:previous_operator}
\end{align}
This is exactly the operator realization of \eqref{eq:general_invariant} after identifying
\[
p_\parallel \leftrightarrow \hat n\cdot(-i\nabla),
\qquad
p_1\leftrightarrow e_1\cdot(-i\nabla),
\qquad
p_2\leftrightarrow e_2\cdot(-i\nabla).
\]

Hence the previously introduced coefficients
\[
g_\parallel,\qquad g_I,\qquad g_J
\]
are not arbitrary ad hoc parameters: they are precisely the coefficients of the three independent first-order invariants.

\subsection{Allowance theorem}

We can now formulate the main result.

\begin{theorem}[Symmetry allowance theorem for the minimal enriched couplings]
In the minimal shell-enriched TECT setting with shell-relevant symmetry \(O(2)_\perp\), one has exactly three linearly independent first-order invariant couplings.
They may be chosen as
\[
\mathcal O_\parallel=(\hat n\cdot p)s_3,
\qquad
\mathcal O_I=p_1s_1+p_2s_2,
\qquad
\mathcal O_J=-p_2s_1+p_1s_2.
\]
Therefore the most general first-order enriched coupling is
\[
g_\parallel \mathcal O_\parallel + g_I \mathcal O_I + g_J \mathcal O_J.
\]
In particular, symmetry does not force
\[
g_\parallel=0,\qquad g_I=0,\qquad g_J=0.
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The result follows from the dimension count
\[
\dim \mathrm{Hom}_{O(2)_\perp}(V_p\otimes V_{\mathrm{int}},\mathbf 1)=3
\]
and from the explicit construction of the three invariant basis elements \(\mathcal O_\parallel,\mathcal O_I,\mathcal O_J\).
Since each basis element is nonzero and invariant, none of the corresponding coefficients is symmetry-forbidden.
\end{proof}

\subsection{What symmetry can and cannot prove}

The previous theorem is an \emph{allowance theorem}, not a \emph{nonvanishing theorem}.
This distinction is essential.

\paragraph{What symmetry proves.}
Symmetry proves that:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the invariant coupling space is three-dimensional;
\item the couplings \(g_\parallel,g_I,g_J\) are allowed;
\item there is no representation-theoretic reason forcing them to vanish identically.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{What symmetry does not prove.}
Symmetry alone does not prove:
\begin{enumerate}
\item \(g_\parallel\neq 0\),
\item \(g_I\neq 0\),
\item \(g_J\neq 0\),
\end{enumerate}
for the actual microscopic model.
Any of these could still vanish accidentally by dynamical cancellation or parameter tuning.

Thus the correct nonvanishing statement requires witnesses.

\subsection{Witness criterion}

We now formulate the correct finite criterion.

\begin{definition}[Witness]
A witness for a coupling coefficient is any microscopic matrix element, tensor contraction, or projected shell amplitude which evaluates nontrivially on the corresponding invariant basis direction.
\end{definition}

For example:
\begin{itemize}
\item a longitudinal witness is any shell-projected matrix element detecting \(\mathcal O_\parallel\);
\item a transverse-even witness detects \(\mathcal O_I\);
\item a transverse-odd witness detects \(\mathcal O_J\).
\end{itemize}

\begin{theorem}[Witness theorem for the enriched couplings]
Let the first-order shell-enriched microscopic operator be expanded as
\[
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)}
=
g_\parallel \mathcal O_\parallel
+
g_I \mathcal O_I
+
g_J \mathcal O_J.
\]
Then:
\begin{enumerate}
\item if there exists a longitudinal witness with nonzero value, then
\[
g_\parallel\neq 0;
\]
\item if there exists a transverse-even witness with nonzero value, then
\[
g_I\neq 0;
\]
\item if there exists a transverse-odd witness with nonzero value, then
\[
g_J\neq 0.
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Each witness is, by definition, a nonzero linear functional on the invariant coupling space which does not annihilate the corresponding basis element.
Since \(\mathcal O_\parallel,\mathcal O_I,\mathcal O_J\) are linearly independent, a nonzero functional detecting one of them implies that the corresponding coefficient cannot vanish in the operator expansion.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Connection to the microscopic tensors}

Previously, the enriched microscopic tensors were extracted as
\begin{equation}
N^3_{ij}=g_\parallel\,\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\label{eq:N3_again}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^1_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{1j}-g_J\,\hat n_i e_{2j},
\label{eq:N1_again}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^2_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{2j}+g_J\,\hat n_i e_{1j}.
\label{eq:N2_again}
\end{equation}
Hence the couplings \(g_\parallel,g_I,g_J\) are exactly the coordinates of the microscopic tensor triple \((N^1,N^2,N^3)\) in the invariant basis
\[
\hat n_i\hat n_j,\qquad
\hat n_i e_{1j},\qquad
\hat n_i e_{2j}.
\]

Therefore the witness theorem may be read equivalently as a statement about nonvanishing contractions of the tensor \(N^a_{ij}\).

\subsection{Immediate consequence for Weyl nondegeneracy}

The previously derived shell-contraction formulas gave
\[
\lambda_\parallel=\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_\parallel,
\qquad
\alpha=\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_I,
\qquad
\beta=-\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_J.
\]
Hence
\begin{equation}
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3\,g_\parallel\,(g_I^2+g_J^2).
\label{eq:detM_witness}
\end{equation}

Therefore:

\begin{enumerate}
\item symmetry allowance implies the determinant formula is structurally meaningful;
\item a longitudinal witness plus at least one transverse witness implies
\[
\det M\neq 0;
\]
\item thus the selected shell crossing is Weyl.
\end{enumerate}

This yields the exact finite criterion:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Weyl}
\quad\Longleftarrow\quad
\text{one nonzero longitudinal witness}
\ \text{and}\
\text{at least one nonzero transverse witness}.
}
\label{eq:weyl_witness_criterion}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Final honest status}

We summarize the exact logical status.

\paragraph{Now rigorously proved:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item the first-order invariant coupling space is exactly three-dimensional;
\item the couplings \(g_\parallel,g_I,g_J\) form the complete invariant basis;
\item symmetry does not forbid any of them;
\item nonzero witnesses imply nonzero couplings;
\item a longitudinal witness together with a transverse witness implies Weyl nondegeneracy.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Still not proved by symmetry alone:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item that the actual microscopic TECT dynamics produces nonzero witnesses automatically;
\item that all three coefficients are generically nonzero without further microscopic input;
\item that the opposite valley exists and matches the same coefficient triple.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

In the minimal shell-enriched TECT setting, the shell-relevant symmetry is the axial transverse group \(O(2)_\perp\), under which
\[
V_p \cong \mathbf 1_\parallel \oplus \mathbf 2_\perp,
\qquad
V_{\mathrm{int}} \cong \mathbf 1_3 \oplus \mathbf 2_s.
\]
The space of first-order invariant enriched couplings is
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{O(2)_\perp}(V_p\otimes V_{\mathrm{int}},\mathbf 1),
\]
and its dimension is exactly
\[
3.
\]

A basis of invariant couplings is
\[
\mathcal O_\parallel=(\hat n\cdot p)s_3,
\qquad
\mathcal O_I=p_1s_1+p_2s_2,
\qquad
\mathcal O_J=-p_2s_1+p_1s_2.
\]
Therefore the most general minimal first-order enriched coupling is
\[
g_\parallel \mathcal O_\parallel + g_I \mathcal O_I + g_J \mathcal O_J.
\]

Hence symmetry does not force
\[
g_\parallel=0,\qquad g_I=0,\qquad g_J=0.
\]
However, symmetry alone does not prove that these coefficients are nonzero in the actual microscopic theory.
To prove nonvanishing one needs witnesses.

If there exists one nonzero longitudinal witness and at least one nonzero transverse witness, then
\[
g_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
(g_I,g_J)\neq (0,0),
\]
and therefore
\[
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3\,g_\parallel\,(g_I^2+g_J^2)\neq 0.
\]
Thus the selected BCC shell crossing is Weyl.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Explicit Witness Functionals for the Enriched Couplings}

\subsection{Goal}

The purpose of this section is to replace the abstract witness statement by explicit calculable formulas.

We already know that the minimal first-order shell-enriched coupling space is three-dimensional, with invariant basis
\[
\mathcal O_\parallel,\qquad \mathcal O_I,\qquad \mathcal O_J.
\]
Hence the coefficients
\[
g_\parallel,\qquad g_I,\qquad g_J
\]
can be extracted by dual linear functionals on this invariant space.

The goal is therefore:

\begin{enumerate}
\item to define an explicit shell-projected first-order operator space;
\item to construct three dual witness functionals
\[
W_\parallel,\qquad W_I,\qquad W_J;
\]
\item to prove that these functionals extract exactly the coefficients
\[
g_\parallel,\qquad g_I,\qquad g_J;
\]
\item to formulate a rigorous finite nonvanishing criterion for Weyl emergence.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Shell-projected first-order enriched operator space}

Fix the selected shell direction
\[
\hat n=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|},
\]
with adapted orthonormal frame
\[
\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\}.
\]
Let the shell-relative momentum be
\[
p=p_\parallel \hat n+p_1e_1+p_2e_2.
\]

Let the reduced internal generators be
\[
s_1,\qquad s_2,\qquad s_3.
\]
Define the three invariant basis operators
\begin{equation}
\mathcal O_\parallel := p_\parallel s_3,
\label{eq:Opar_basis}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\mathcal O_I := p_1 s_1+p_2 s_2,
\label{eq:OI_basis}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\mathcal O_J := -p_2 s_1+p_1 s_2.
\label{eq:OJ_basis}
\end{equation}

The shell-projected first-order enriched operator space is
\begin{equation}
\mathcal V_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)}
:=
\mathrm{span}\{\mathcal O_\parallel,\mathcal O_I,\mathcal O_J\}.
\label{eq:V_enr}
\end{equation}
Every minimal first-order enriched operator is uniquely written as
\begin{equation}
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)}
=
g_\parallel \mathcal O_\parallel
+
g_I \mathcal O_I
+
g_J \mathcal O_J.
\label{eq:L_enr_expand}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Hilbert--Schmidt pairing on the reduced operator space}

To construct explicit dual witnesses, we equip the reduced operator space with a natural bilinear pairing.

Let \(\langle \cdot,\cdot\rangle_p\) denote the Euclidean pairing on the linear momentum forms generated by
\[
p_\parallel,\qquad p_1,\qquad p_2,
\]
and let \(\langle \cdot,\cdot\rangle_s\) denote the Hilbert--Schmidt pairing on the reduced internal generators:
\[
\langle A,B\rangle_s := \frac{1}{2}\Tr(AB).
\]
Assume the reduced generators are normalized by
\begin{equation}
\frac{1}{2}\Tr(s_a s_b)=\delta_{ab},
\qquad a,b\in\{1,2,3\}.
\label{eq:sa_norm}
\end{equation}
Then define the tensor-product pairing on \(\mathcal V_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)}\) by
\begin{equation}
\langle p_\mu s_a,\ p_\nu s_b\rangle
:=
\langle p_\mu,p_\nu\rangle_p\,
\langle s_a,s_b\rangle_s
=
\delta_{\mu\nu}\delta_{ab},
\label{eq:full_pairing}
\end{equation}
where \(\mu,\nu\in\{\parallel,1,2\}\).

With this normalization, the three basis elements are orthogonal:
\begin{equation}
\langle \mathcal O_\parallel,\mathcal O_I\rangle
=
\langle \mathcal O_\parallel,\mathcal O_J\rangle
=
\langle \mathcal O_I,\mathcal O_J\rangle
=
0.
\label{eq:orthogonal}
\end{equation}
Moreover,
\begin{equation}
\langle \mathcal O_\parallel,\mathcal O_\parallel\rangle = 1,
\qquad
\langle \mathcal O_I,\mathcal O_I\rangle = 2,
\qquad
\langle \mathcal O_J,\mathcal O_J\rangle = 2.
\label{eq:norms_basis}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Dual witnesses}

We now define the three explicit witness functionals by normalized projection onto the basis directions:
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel(X):=\langle \mathcal O_\parallel,X\rangle,
\label{eq:Wpar_def}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
W_I(X):=\frac{1}{2}\langle \mathcal O_I,X\rangle,
\label{eq:WI_def}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
W_J(X):=\frac{1}{2}\langle \mathcal O_J,X\rangle.
\label{eq:WJ_def}
\end{equation}

These are linear maps
\[
W_\parallel,\ W_I,\ W_J:\mathcal V_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)}\to\mathbb C.
\]

\subsection{Exact coefficient extraction}

We now prove that these witnesses recover the coefficients exactly.

\begin{proposition}[Dual witness extraction]
For
\[
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)}
=
g_\parallel \mathcal O_\parallel
+
g_I \mathcal O_I
+
g_J \mathcal O_J,
\]
the witness functionals satisfy
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel(\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)})=g_\parallel,
\label{eq:Wpar_extract}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
W_I(\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)})=g_I,
\label{eq:WI_extract}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
W_J(\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)})=g_J.
\label{eq:WJ_extract}
\end{equation}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
By linearity and orthogonality,
\[
\langle \mathcal O_\parallel,\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)}\rangle
=
g_\parallel \langle \mathcal O_\parallel,\mathcal O_\parallel\rangle
=
g_\parallel.
\]
Hence
\[
W_\parallel(\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)})=g_\parallel.
\]

Similarly,
\[
\langle \mathcal O_I,\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)}\rangle
=
g_I\langle \mathcal O_I,\mathcal O_I\rangle
=
2g_I,
\]
so
\[
W_I(\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)})=\frac12(2g_I)=g_I.
\]

Likewise,
\[
\langle \mathcal O_J,\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)}\rangle
=
g_J\langle \mathcal O_J,\mathcal O_J\rangle
=
2g_J,
\]
hence
\[
W_J(\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)})=g_J.
\]
\end{proof}

\subsection{Tensor-form witness extraction}

We now rewrite the witnesses directly in terms of the microscopic tensors
\[
N^a_{ij}.
\]

The previously extracted tensor form was
\begin{equation}
N^3_{ij}=g_\parallel\,\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\label{eq:N3_tensor}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^1_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{1j}-g_J\,\hat n_i e_{2j},
\label{eq:N1_tensor}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N^2_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{2j}+g_J\,\hat n_i e_{1j}.
\label{eq:N2_tensor}
\end{equation}

Therefore the dual witnesses may equivalently be written as tensor contractions:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
W_\parallel[N]
:=
\hat n_i\hat n_j N^3_{ij}
=
g_\parallel,
}
\label{eq:Wpar_tensor}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
W_I[N]
:=
\frac12
\left(
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
\right)
=
g_I,
}
\label{eq:WI_tensor}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
W_J[N]
:=
\frac12
\left(
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
-
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
\right)
=
g_J.
}
\label{eq:WJ_tensor}
\end{equation}

\begin{proof}
Substitute \eqref{eq:N3_tensor}--\eqref{eq:N2_tensor} directly.

For the longitudinal witness:
\[
\hat n_i\hat n_jN^3_{ij}
=
g_\parallel(\hat n\cdot\hat n)^2
=
g_\parallel.
\]

For the transverse-even witness:
\[
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
=
g_I(e_1\cdot e_1)-g_J(e_1\cdot e_2)
=
g_I,
\]
\[
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
=
g_I(e_2\cdot e_2)+g_J(e_2\cdot e_1)
=
g_I.
\]
Hence
\[
\frac12
\left(
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
+
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
\right)
=
g_I.
\]

For the transverse-odd witness:
\[
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
=
g_I(e_1\cdot e_2)+g_J(e_1\cdot e_1)
=
g_J,
\]
\[
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
=
g_I(e_2\cdot e_1)-g_J(e_2\cdot e_2)
=
-g_J.
\]
Thus
\[
\frac12
\left(
e_{1j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}
-
e_{2j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}
\right)
=
g_J.
\]
\end{proof}

\subsection{Shell-contraction and Weyl criterion}

The reduced Weyl coefficients were
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel=\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_\parallel,
\label{eq:lambda_from_g}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\alpha=\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_I,
\label{eq:alpha_from_g}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\beta=-\kappa_*|G_*|\,g_J,
\label{eq:beta_from_g}
\end{equation}
and therefore
\begin{equation}
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3\,g_\parallel\,(g_I^2+g_J^2).
\label{eq:detM_from_g}
\end{equation}

Using the witness formulas, this becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3\,
W_\parallel[N]\,
\Bigl(
W_I[N]^2+W_J[N]^2
\Bigr).
}
\label{eq:detM_from_witness}
\end{equation}

This yields an explicit finite witness criterion for Weyl nondegeneracy.

\begin{theorem}[Explicit witness criterion for Weyl]
Let the shell-enriched tensor triple \(N^a_{ij}\) be given.
If
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel[N]\neq 0,
\label{eq:cond_Wpar}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\bigl(W_I[N],W_J[N]\bigr)\neq (0,0),
\label{eq:cond_WI_WJ}
\end{equation}
then
\begin{equation}
\det M\neq 0,
\end{equation}
and the selected shell crossing is Weyl.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By \eqref{eq:detM_from_witness},
\[
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3
W_\parallel[N]
\left(
W_I[N]^2+W_J[N]^2
\right).
\]
If \(W_\parallel[N]\neq 0\) and \(W_I[N]^2+W_J[N]^2\neq 0\), then \(\det M\neq 0\).
Hence the local reduced Hamiltonian is Weyl.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Matrix-element realization of the witnesses}

The abstract pairing above can also be realized as shell-projected matrix elements.
Let \(P_{\mathrm{shell}}\) be the projection to the selected reduced shell subspace, and let
\[
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)}:=P_{\mathrm{shell}}\,\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}\,P_{\mathrm{shell}}.
\]
Then the witnesses are equivalently
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel
=
\frac{\langle \mathcal O_\parallel,\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)}\rangle}
{\langle \mathcal O_\parallel,\mathcal O_\parallel\rangle},
\label{eq:Wpar_matrix}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
W_I
=
\frac{\langle \mathcal O_I,\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)}\rangle}
{\langle \mathcal O_I,\mathcal O_I\rangle},
\label{eq:WI_matrix}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
W_J
=
\frac{\langle \mathcal O_J,\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)}\rangle}
{\langle \mathcal O_J,\mathcal O_J\rangle}.
\label{eq:WJ_matrix}
\end{equation}
Thus the witnesses are not merely formal; they are actual projected observables on the reduced shell operator.

\subsection{What is now proved}

We summarize the exact logical status.

\paragraph{Now rigorously proved:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item the first-order shell-enriched invariant space is three-dimensional;
\item the dual witness functionals \(W_\parallel,W_I,W_J\) extract exactly the coefficients \(g_\parallel,g_I,g_J\);
\item these witnesses can be written either as dual projections on the invariant operator basis or as explicit tensor contractions of \(N^a_{ij}\);
\item Weyl nondegeneracy is equivalent to one nonzero longitudinal witness and at least one nonzero transverse witness.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Still not proved automatically:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item that the actual microscopic TECT model necessarily yields nonzero witness values;
\item that the opposite valley exists and carries the matching coefficient triple;
\item that the remote gap and mass-protection conditions hold in the final UV completion.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

Let
\[
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)}
=
g_\parallel \mathcal O_\parallel
+
g_I \mathcal O_I
+
g_J \mathcal O_J
\]
be the shell-projected first-order enriched operator, where
\[
\mathcal O_\parallel=p_\parallel s_3,
\qquad
\mathcal O_I=p_1s_1+p_2s_2,
\qquad
\mathcal O_J=-p_2s_1+p_1s_2.
\]

Define the dual witness functionals by
\[
W_\parallel(X)=\langle \mathcal O_\parallel,X\rangle,
\qquad
W_I(X)=\frac12\langle \mathcal O_I,X\rangle,
\qquad
W_J(X)=\frac12\langle \mathcal O_J,X\rangle.
\]
Then
\[
W_\parallel(\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)})=g_\parallel,
\qquad
W_I(\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)})=g_I,
\qquad
W_J(\delta L_{\mathrm{enr}}^{(1)})=g_J.
\]

Equivalently, in tensor language,
\[
W_\parallel[N]=\hat n_i\hat n_jN^3_{ij},
\]
\[
W_I[N]=\frac12\left(e_{1j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}+e_{2j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}\right),
\]
\[
W_J[N]=\frac12\left(e_{1j}\hat n_iN^2_{ij}-e_{2j}\hat n_iN^1_{ij}\right).
\]
These satisfy
\[
W_\parallel[N]=g_\parallel,
\qquad
W_I[N]=g_I,
\qquad
W_J[N]=g_J.
\]

Therefore the Weyl determinant becomes
\[
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3\,
W_\parallel[N]\,
\bigl(W_I[N]^2+W_J[N]^2\bigr).
\]
Hence the selected BCC shell crossing is Weyl whenever
\[
W_\parallel[N]\neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(W_I[N],W_J[N]\bigr)\neq (0,0).
\]

So the local Weyl problem has now been reduced to an explicit finite witness test on the shell-projected enriched tensor.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Witness Formulation of Opposite-Valley Pairing and Dirac Doubling}

\subsection{Goal}

The purpose of this section is to reformulate the opposite-valley pairing condition entirely in the witness language.

Previously, the local Weyl coefficients at the positive valley were extracted by the witness functionals
\[
W_\parallel^+,\qquad W_I^+,\qquad W_J^+.
\]
We now define the corresponding witness functionals at the opposite valley
\[
W_\parallel^-,\qquad W_I^-,\qquad W_J^-,
\]
and prove that, in a matched opposite-valley frame, equality of the two witness triples is equivalent to the standard pairing relation
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p).
\]

This yields a fully finite witness criterion for local Dirac doubling.

\subsection{Positive-valley witness data}

Fix the selected positive shell vector
\[
G_*,
\qquad
\hat n_+ := \frac{G_*}{|G_*|} = \hat n,
\]
with adapted right-handed frame
\[
\{e_1^+,e_2^+,\hat n_+\}
=
\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\}.
\]
The first-order shell-projected enriched operator at \(+G_*\) is
\begin{equation}
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr},+}^{(1)}
=
g_\parallel^+ \mathcal O_\parallel^+
+
g_I^+ \mathcal O_I^+
+
g_J^+ \mathcal O_J^+,
\label{eq:Lplus_expand}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\mathcal O_\parallel^+ := p_\parallel s_3,
\label{eq:Opar_plus}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\mathcal O_I^+ := p_1 s_1+p_2 s_2,
\label{eq:OI_plus}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\mathcal O_J^+ := -p_2 s_1+p_1 s_2.
\label{eq:OJ_plus}
\end{equation}

The corresponding witness functionals satisfy
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel^+(\delta L_{\mathrm{enr},+}^{(1)})=g_\parallel^+,
\qquad
W_I^+(\delta L_{\mathrm{enr},+}^{(1)})=g_I^+,
\qquad
W_J^+(\delta L_{\mathrm{enr},+}^{(1)})=g_J^+.
\label{eq:plus_witness_extract}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Matched negative-valley frame}

Now consider the opposite shell point
\[
-G_*.
\]
Define
\[
\hat n_- := -\hat n_+ = -\hat n.
\]
To preserve a right-handed adapted frame, choose
\begin{equation}
e_1^- := e_1^+ = e_1,
\qquad
e_2^- := -e_2^+ = -e_2,
\qquad
\hat n_- := -\hat n.
\label{eq:matched_minus_frame}
\end{equation}
Indeed,
\[
e_1^- \times e_2^- = e_1 \times (-e_2)=-(e_1\times e_2)=-\hat n=\hat n_-.
\]

This is the canonical matched opposite-valley frame.

\subsection{Negative-valley invariant basis}

Let the shell-relative momentum near \(-G_*\) again be written as
\[
p=p_\parallel \hat n_- + p_1 e_1^- + p_2 e_2^-.
\]
Define the negative-valley invariant basis operators
\begin{equation}
\mathcal O_\parallel^- := p_\parallel s_3,
\label{eq:Opar_minus}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\mathcal O_I^- := p_1 s_1+p_2 s_2,
\label{eq:OI_minus}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\mathcal O_J^- := -p_2 s_1+p_1 s_2.
\label{eq:OJ_minus}
\end{equation}
The first-order shell-projected enriched operator at \(-G_*\) is then
\begin{equation}
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr},-}^{(1)}
=
g_\parallel^- \mathcal O_\parallel^-
+
g_I^- \mathcal O_I^-
+
g_J^- \mathcal O_J^-.
\label{eq:Lminus_expand}
\end{equation}

Define the corresponding negative-valley witnesses
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel^-(X):=\langle \mathcal O_\parallel^-,X\rangle,
\label{eq:Wpar_minus_def}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
W_I^-(X):=\frac12\langle \mathcal O_I^-,X\rangle,
\label{eq:WI_minus_def}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
W_J^-(X):=\frac12\langle \mathcal O_J^-,X\rangle.
\label{eq:WJ_minus_def}
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel^-(\delta L_{\mathrm{enr},-}^{(1)})=g_\parallel^-,
\qquad
W_I^-(\delta L_{\mathrm{enr},-}^{(1)})=g_I^-,
\qquad
W_J^-(\delta L_{\mathrm{enr},-}^{(1)})=g_J^-.
\label{eq:minus_witness_extract}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Negative-valley tensor witnesses}

Let the enriched tensor triple at \(-G_*\) be denoted by
\[
N^{a(-)}_{ij}.
\]
Then, in the matched frame \eqref{eq:matched_minus_frame}, the negative-valley tensor witnesses are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
W_\parallel^-[N^-]
:=
\hat n_-^i\hat n_-^j N^{3(-)}_{ij}
=
g_\parallel^-,
}
\label{eq:Wpar_minus_tensor}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
W_I^-[N^-]
:=
\frac12
\left(
e_{1j}^- \hat n_-^i N^{1(-)}_{ij}
+
e_{2j}^- \hat n_-^i N^{2(-)}_{ij}
\right)
=
g_I^-,
}
\label{eq:WI_minus_tensor}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
W_J^-[N^-]
:=
\frac12
\left(
e_{1j}^- \hat n_-^i N^{2(-)}_{ij}
-
e_{2j}^- \hat n_-^i N^{1(-)}_{ij}
\right)
=
g_J^-.
}
\label{eq:WJ_minus_tensor}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Positive and negative reduced Weyl blocks}

The positive-valley reduced Hamiltonian is
\begin{equation}
h_+(p)
=
\lambda_\parallel^+ p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
\alpha^+(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
\beta^+(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2),
\label{eq:hplus_witness}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel^+ = \kappa_*|G_*|\,W_\parallel^+,
\qquad
\alpha^+ = \kappa_*|G_*|\,W_I^+,
\qquad
\beta^+ = -\kappa_*|G_*|\,W_J^+.
\label{eq:plus_coeff_witness}
\end{equation}

Similarly, the negative-valley reduced Hamiltonian is
\begin{equation}
h_-(p)
=
\lambda_\parallel^- p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
\alpha^-(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
\beta^-(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2),
\label{eq:hminus_witness}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel^- = \kappa_*|G_*|\,W_\parallel^-,
\qquad
\alpha^- = \kappa_*|G_*|\,W_I^-,
\qquad
\beta^- = -\kappa_*|G_*|\,W_J^-.
\label{eq:minus_coeff_witness}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Pairing relation in witness language}

We now compute
\[
-h_+(-p).
\]
From \eqref{eq:hplus_witness},
\[
h_+(-p)
=
-\lambda_\parallel^+ p_\parallel \sigma^3
-
\alpha^+(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
-
\beta^+(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\]
Hence
\begin{equation}
-h_+(-p)
=
\lambda_\parallel^+ p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
\alpha^+(p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
\beta^+(-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\label{eq:minus_hplus_explicit}
\end{equation}

Comparing \eqref{eq:hminus_witness} and \eqref{eq:minus_hplus_explicit}, we obtain the exact equivalence:
\begin{equation}
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\lambda_\parallel^-=\lambda_\parallel^+,\ 
\alpha^-=\alpha^+,\ 
\beta^-=\beta^+.
\label{eq:pairing_coeff_equiv}
\end{equation}

Using \eqref{eq:plus_coeff_witness} and \eqref{eq:minus_coeff_witness}, this is equivalent to
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
W_\parallel^- = W_\parallel^+,\qquad
W_I^- = W_I^+,\qquad
W_J^- = W_J^+.
}
\label{eq:pairing_witness_equiv}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Witness theorem for opposite-valley pairing}

We may now state the main theorem.

\begin{theorem}[Witness theorem for opposite-valley pairing]
Let the positive and negative shell-projected first-order enriched operators be
\[
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr},+}^{(1)},\qquad
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr},-}^{(1)},
\]
with witness triples
\[
(W_\parallel^+,W_I^+,W_J^+),
\qquad
(W_\parallel^-,W_I^-,W_J^-).
\]
Then, in the matched opposite-valley frame \eqref{eq:matched_minus_frame}, the following are equivalent:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the reduced Hamiltonians satisfy the standard pairing relation
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p);
\]

\item the reduced coefficients satisfy
\[
\lambda_\parallel^-=\lambda_\parallel^+,\qquad
\alpha^-=\alpha^+,\qquad
\beta^-=\beta^+;
\]

\item the witness triples are equal,
\[
W_\parallel^- = W_\parallel^+,\qquad
W_I^- = W_I^+,\qquad
W_J^- = W_J^+.
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The equivalence of (1) and (2) follows by direct comparison of \eqref{eq:hminus_witness} with \eqref{eq:minus_hplus_explicit}. The equivalence of (2) and (3) follows immediately from the coefficient-witness identifications \eqref{eq:plus_coeff_witness} and \eqref{eq:minus_coeff_witness}.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Witness formulation of Dirac doubling}

Assume both valleys are locally Weyl, i.e.
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel^\pm \neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(W_I^\pm,W_J^\pm\bigr)\neq (0,0).
\label{eq:weyl_pm_witness}
\end{equation}
If, in addition, the witness triples match as in \eqref{eq:pairing_witness_equiv}, then the doubled Hamiltonian
\begin{equation}
H_D(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}
\label{eq:Dirac_block_witness}
\end{equation}
is unitarily equivalent to a massless Dirac Hamiltonian.

\begin{theorem}[Explicit witness criterion for Dirac doubling]
Suppose:
\begin{enumerate}
\item
\[
W_\parallel^+ \neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(W_I^+,W_J^+\bigr)\neq (0,0),
\]
so the positive valley is Weyl;

\item
\[
W_\parallel^- \neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(W_I^-,W_J^-\bigr)\neq (0,0),
\]
so the negative valley is Weyl;

\item the witness triples match,
\[
W_\parallel^- = W_\parallel^+,\qquad
W_I^- = W_I^+,\qquad
W_J^- = W_J^+.
\]
\end{enumerate}
Then the opposite-valley doubled block is Dirac.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By assumptions (1) and (2), each valley has nondegenerate Weyl form. By assumption (3) and the previous theorem, the pairing relation
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)
\]
holds. Therefore the doubled block \eqref{eq:Dirac_block_witness} is the standard Weyl-pair block and is unitarily equivalent to a massless Dirac Hamiltonian.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Determinant form of the witness criteria}

At each valley,
\begin{equation}
\det M_\pm
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3\,
W_\parallel^\pm
\Bigl(
(W_I^\pm)^2+(W_J^\pm)^2
\Bigr).
\label{eq:detM_pm_witness}
\end{equation}
Thus the full local Dirac criterion can be written as:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
W_\parallel^\pm \neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(W_I^\pm,W_J^\pm\bigr)\neq (0,0),
\qquad
(W_\parallel^-,W_I^-,W_J^-)=(W_\parallel^+,W_I^+,W_J^+).
}
\label{eq:full_local_Dirac_witness}
\end{equation}

This is a completely finite witness test for local Dirac doubling.

\subsection{What is now proved}

We summarize the exact logical status.

\paragraph{Now rigorously proved:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item the negative-valley witness triple is defined exactly analogously to the positive one;
\item in the matched opposite-valley frame, equality of witness triples is equivalent to the standard pairing relation
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p);
\]
\item local Dirac doubling is equivalent to:
\begin{itemize}
\item Weyl nondegeneracy at both valleys,
\item equality of the two witness triples.
\end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Still open microscopically:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item whether the actual microscopic TECT dynamics guarantees existence of the opposite valley;
\item whether the actual microscopic enriched tensors at \(\pm G_*\) produce equal witness triples automatically;
\item whether remote-gap and mass-protection conditions hold in the full theory.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

At the positive valley \(+G_*\), the shell-projected first-order enriched operator has witness triple
\[
(W_\parallel^+,W_I^+,W_J^+),
\]
which determines the Weyl coefficients by
\[
\lambda_\parallel^+ = \kappa_*|G_*|\,W_\parallel^+,
\qquad
\alpha^+ = \kappa_*|G_*|\,W_I^+,
\qquad
\beta^+ = -\kappa_*|G_*|\,W_J^+.
\]

At the negative valley \(-G_*\), in the matched right-handed frame
\[
\hat n_-=-\hat n,\qquad e_1^-=e_1,\qquad e_2^-=-e_2,
\]
the corresponding witness triple
\[
(W_\parallel^-,W_I^-,W_J^-)
\]
determines
\[
\lambda_\parallel^- = \kappa_*|G_*|\,W_\parallel^-,
\qquad
\alpha^- = \kappa_*|G_*|\,W_I^-,
\qquad
\beta^- = -\kappa_*|G_*|\,W_J^-.
\]

Then the standard opposite-valley pairing relation
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)
\]
is equivalent to the equality of witness triples
\[
W_\parallel^- = W_\parallel^+,\qquad
W_I^- = W_I^+,\qquad
W_J^- = W_J^+.
\]

Moreover, each valley is Weyl precisely when
\[
W_\parallel^\pm \neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(W_I^\pm,W_J^\pm\bigr)\neq (0,0),
\]
equivalently
\[
\det M_\pm
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3\,
W_\parallel^\pm
\Bigl(
(W_I^\pm)^2+(W_J^\pm)^2
\Bigr)
\neq 0.
\]

Therefore the full local Dirac criterion is the finite witness condition
\[
W_\parallel^\pm \neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(W_I^\pm,W_J^\pm\bigr)\neq (0,0),
\qquad
(W_\parallel^-,W_I^-,W_J^-)=(W_\parallel^+,W_I^+,W_J^+).
\]

Thus local Dirac doubling has been reduced to a completely explicit finite witness-matching test at the two opposite shell valleys.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Witness Form of the Full Local Dirac Stability Criterion}

\subsection{Goal}

The purpose of this section is to combine the previously established witness formulation of local Weyl/Dirac pairing with the spectral-isolation theorem.

The result will be a single finite criterion for the existence of a stable local massless Dirac cone in the full microscopic theory.

The logic has two layers:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the \emph{local shell layer}, where the two opposite valleys must each be Weyl and must satisfy the pairing relation;

\item the \emph{full spectral layer}, where the doubled Dirac block must remain isolated from the remote sector.
\end{enumerate}

We now formulate both layers in a single witness-compatible theorem.

\subsection{Positive and negative valley witness data}

Fix a selected opposite shell pair
\[
\pm G_*,
\qquad
\hat n_+ = \hat n,
\qquad
\hat n_-=-\hat n.
\]
Let the matched adapted frames be
\[
\{e_1^+,e_2^+,\hat n_+\}=\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\},
\qquad
\{e_1^-,e_2^-,\hat n_-\}=\{e_1,-e_2,-\hat n\}.
\]

Let the positive and negative shell-projected first-order enriched operators be
\[
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr},+}^{(1)},
\qquad
\delta L_{\mathrm{enr},-}^{(1)}.
\]
Their corresponding witness triples are
\[
(W_\parallel^+,W_I^+,W_J^+),
\qquad
(W_\parallel^-,W_I^-,W_J^-).
\]

The reduced Weyl coefficients are defined by
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel^\pm=\kappa_*|G_*|\,W_\parallel^\pm,
\label{eq:lambda_pm}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\alpha^\pm=\kappa_*|G_*|\,W_I^\pm,
\label{eq:alpha_pm}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\beta^\pm=-\kappa_*|G_*|\,W_J^\pm.
\label{eq:beta_pm}
\end{equation}

Hence the local reduced Hamiltonians are
\begin{equation}
h_\pm(p)
=
\lambda_\parallel^\pm p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
\alpha^\pm (p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
\beta^\pm (-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2).
\label{eq:hpm_witness}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Local Weyl criterion in witness form}

At each valley, the Weyl determinant is
\begin{equation}
\det M_\pm
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3\,
W_\parallel^\pm
\Bigl(
(W_I^\pm)^2+(W_J^\pm)^2
\Bigr).
\label{eq:detM_pm}
\end{equation}
Therefore each valley is Weyl if and only if
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel^\pm\neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(W_I^\pm,W_J^\pm\bigr)\neq(0,0).
\label{eq:Weyl_pm_condition}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Pairing condition in witness form}

In the matched opposite-valley frame, the standard pairing relation
\begin{equation}
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)
\label{eq:pairing_relation}
\end{equation}
is equivalent to equality of the two witness triples:
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel^- = W_\parallel^+,
\qquad
W_I^- = W_I^+,
\qquad
W_J^- = W_J^+.
\label{eq:witness_matching}
\end{equation}

Hence the doubled shell block
\begin{equation}
H_D(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}
\label{eq:HD_local}
\end{equation}
is a local massless Dirac block whenever \eqref{eq:Weyl_pm_condition} and \eqref{eq:witness_matching} hold.

\subsection{Full Hilbert-space decomposition}

Now decompose the full microscopic Hilbert space as
\begin{equation}
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}=\mathcal H_D\oplus \mathcal H_R,
\label{eq:Hfull_split}
\end{equation}
where:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\mathcal H_D\) is the local four-dimensional doubled shell block \eqref{eq:HD_local},
\item \(\mathcal H_R\) is the remote sector.
\end{itemize}

The full Hamiltonian takes block form
\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{full}}(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_D(p) & V(p)\\
V^\dagger(p) & H_R(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:Hfull_block}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Remote-gap hypothesis}

Assume there exists \(\Delta>0\) and a neighborhood \(\mathcal N\ni 0\) such that
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{spec}(H_R(p))\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing
\qquad
(p\in \mathcal N).
\label{eq:remote_gap}
\end{equation}
Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\|(H_R(p)-E)^{-1}\|\le \frac{2}{\Delta}
\qquad
(|E|<\Delta/2,\ p\in\mathcal N).
\label{eq:remote_resolvent}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Zero-mixing and linear-mixing hypotheses}

Assume the constant inter-sector mixing vanishes:
\begin{equation}
V(0)=0.
\label{eq:V0_again}
\end{equation}
A sufficient representation-theoretic reason is little-group mismatch:
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)=0.
\]

Assume further that the leading nontrivial mixing is at most linear:
\begin{equation}
\|V(p)\|\le c|p|
\qquad
(p\in \mathcal N)
\label{eq:Vp_linear}
\end{equation}
for some \(c>0\).

\subsection{Schur-complement effective Hamiltonian}

For \(|E|<\Delta/2\), the remote resolvent exists and the low-energy problem is governed by the Schur complement
\begin{equation}
F(E,p)
=
H_D(p)-E
-
V(p)(H_R(p)-E)^{-1}V^\dagger(p).
\label{eq:Schur_F}
\end{equation}
Define the self-energy
\begin{equation}
\Sigma(E,p)
:=
V(p)(H_R(p)-E)^{-1}V^\dagger(p).
\label{eq:self_energy}
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
F(E,p)=H_D(p)-E-\Sigma(E,p).
\label{eq:F_sigma}
\end{equation}

Using \eqref{eq:remote_resolvent} and \eqref{eq:Vp_linear}, we obtain
\begin{equation}
\|\Sigma(E,p)\|
\le
\|V(p)\|^2\,\|(H_R(p)-E)^{-1}\|
\le
\frac{2c^2}{\Delta}|p|^2.
\label{eq:sigma_bound}
\end{equation}
Hence the remote sector modifies the local block only at order \(O(|p|^2/\Delta)\).

\subsection{Effective Dirac Hamiltonian in witness form}

Under the local witness conditions, define the common witness triple
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel:=W_\parallel^+=W_\parallel^-,
\qquad
W_I:=W_I^+=W_I^-,
\qquad
W_J:=W_J^+=W_J^-.
\label{eq:common_witness}
\end{equation}
Then the common reduced coefficient matrix is
\begin{equation}
M=
\kappa_*|G_*|
\begin{pmatrix}
W_I & W_J & 0\\
-\,W_J & W_I & 0\\
0 & 0 & W_\parallel
\end{pmatrix},
\label{eq:M_witness_common}
\end{equation}
up to the sign convention chosen for the transverse odd basis.
Equivalently, in the canonical Weyl form,
\begin{equation}
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3
W_\parallel
\bigl(W_I^2+W_J^2\bigr).
\label{eq:detM_common}
\end{equation}

Therefore the effective full low-energy Hamiltonian on \(\mathcal H_D\) is
\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(E,p)
=
\Gamma^i(Mp)_i
+
O(|p|^2)
+
O\!\left(\frac{|p|^2}{\Delta}\right),
\label{eq:Heff_witness}
\end{equation}
where \(\Gamma^i\) are Dirac gamma matrices in a suitable basis.

\subsection{Mass protection}

To ensure a massless Dirac cone rather than a massive one, one needs a residual symmetry forbidding a constant Dirac mass term.
A sufficient condition is the existence of a grading operator \(\Gamma_5\) on \(\mathcal H_D\) such that
\begin{equation}
\{\Gamma_5,\Gamma^i\}=0,
\qquad
\Gamma_5^2=\mathbf 1_4,
\label{eq:Gamma5_condition}
\end{equation}
and the effective low-energy theory preserves this grading.
Then no constant term proportional to a Dirac mass matrix can be generated, so
\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(0,0)=0.
\label{eq:no_mass}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Main theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Full local Dirac stability criterion in witness form]
Assume the following:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Positive-valley Weyl condition:}
\[
W_\parallel^+\neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(W_I^+,W_J^+\bigr)\neq(0,0).
\]

\item \textbf{Negative-valley Weyl condition:}
\[
W_\parallel^-\neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(W_I^-,W_J^-\bigr)\neq(0,0).
\]

\item \textbf{Opposite-valley witness matching:}
\[
W_\parallel^- = W_\parallel^+,
\qquad
W_I^- = W_I^+,
\qquad
W_J^- = W_J^+.
\]

\item \textbf{Remote spectral gap:}
\[
\mathrm{spec}(H_R(p))\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing
\qquad
(p\in \mathcal N)
\]
for some \(\Delta>0\).

\item \textbf{Zero constant mixing and linear bound:}
\[
V(0)=0,
\qquad
\|V(p)\|\le c|p|.
\]

\item \textbf{Mass protection:} a residual symmetry forbids a constant Dirac mass term in the effective theory.
\end{enumerate}

Then, for sufficiently small \(p\), the full microscopic theory contains a spectrally isolated four-dimensional low-energy sector whose effective Hamiltonian is
\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\Gamma^i(Mp)_i
+
O\!\left(\frac{|p|^2}{\Delta}\right),
\label{eq:main_eff_result}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3
W_\parallel
\bigl(W_I^2+W_J^2\bigr)\neq 0.
\label{eq:main_detM}
\end{equation}
Consequently, the full microscopic theory contains a stable massless local Dirac cone.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By assumptions (1) and (2), both valleys are Weyl by the witness determinant criterion \eqref{eq:detM_pm}. By assumption (3), the two witness triples match, hence \(h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)\), so the doubled shell block is local Dirac. By assumptions (4) and (5), the Schur-complement self-energy obeys the quadratic bound \eqref{eq:sigma_bound}, so the remote sector changes the local Dirac block only at order \(O(|p|^2/\Delta)\). Assumption (6) excludes an induced constant mass term. Therefore the effective low-energy Hamiltonian retains a massless Dirac cone with the same linear coefficient matrix \(M\) as the local shell block.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Compressed checklist}

The theorem gives the following finite operational checklist:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
W_\parallel^\pm \neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(W_I^\pm,W_J^\pm\bigr)\neq(0,0),
\qquad
(W_\parallel^-,W_I^-,W_J^-)=(W_\parallel^+,W_I^+,W_J^+),
}
\label{eq:checklist_local}
\end{equation}
together with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathrm{spec}(H_R)\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing,
\qquad
V(0)=0,
\qquad
\|V(p)\|\le c|p|,
}
\label{eq:checklist_spectral}
\end{equation}
and a mass-forbidding symmetry.

This is the complete finite witness formulation of local Dirac stability.

\subsection{What is now proved and what remains open}

\paragraph{Now rigorously proved:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item local Weyl existence at each valley is a finite witness condition;
\item opposite-valley pairing is equality of witness triples;
\item full local Dirac stability is obtained by combining witness matching with remote gap and zero-mixing conditions;
\item the remote sector modifies the local Dirac cone only at order \(O(|p|^2/\Delta)\).
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Still open microscopically:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item the actual computation of the witness triples from the final microscopic TECT dynamics;
\item the proof of the remote gap \(\Delta>0\) in the full model;
\item the proof of little-group mismatch for the actual remote-sector content;
\item the final microscopic symmetry implementing mass protection.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

Let
\[
(W_\parallel^+,W_I^+,W_J^+),
\qquad
(W_\parallel^-,W_I^-,W_J^-)
\]
be the positive- and negative-valley witness triples extracted from the shell-projected first-order enriched operators.

Each valley is Weyl exactly when
\[
W_\parallel^\pm\neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(W_I^\pm,W_J^\pm\bigr)\neq(0,0),
\]
equivalently
\[
\det M_\pm
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3
W_\parallel^\pm
\Bigl(
(W_I^\pm)^2+(W_J^\pm)^2
\Bigr)\neq 0.
\]

In the matched opposite-valley frame, the standard pairing relation
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)
\]
is equivalent to
\[
W_\parallel^- = W_\parallel^+,
\qquad
W_I^- = W_I^+,
\qquad
W_J^- = W_J^+.
\]

If, in addition, the full Hamiltonian admits a decomposition
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}=\mathcal H_D\oplus \mathcal H_R,
\]
with remote gap
\[
\mathrm{spec}(H_R)\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing,
\]
zero constant mixing
\[
V(0)=0,
\]
and linear mixing bound
\[
\|V(p)\|\le c|p|,
\]
then the Schur complement yields
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\Gamma^i(Mp)_i
+
O\!\left(\frac{|p|^2}{\Delta}\right).
\]
With an additional symmetry forbidding a constant Dirac mass, the full microscopic theory contains a stable massless local Dirac cone.

Thus the complete local Dirac stability criterion is the finite witness-and-gap condition
\[
W_\parallel^\pm \neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(W_I^\pm,W_J^\pm\bigr)\neq(0,0),
\qquad
(W_\parallel^-,W_I^-,W_J^-)=(W_\parallel^+,W_I^+,W_J^+),
\]
together with
\[
\mathrm{spec}(H_R)\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing,
\qquad
V(0)=0,
\qquad
\|V(p)\|\le c|p|.
\]

\section{Final Summary}

TECT currently has a mathematically closed shell-reduced local fermion framework.

The minimal effective condensate sector is of Landau--Brazovskii type:
\[
\partial_t\Phi
=
-r\Phi+\kappa\nabla^2\Phi-\lambda\nabla^4\Phi-u|\Phi|^2\Phi,
\qquad
q_*^2=-\frac{\kappa}{2\lambda}.
\]

The minimal shell-enriched projector coupling yields explicit microscopic tensors
\[
N^3_{ij}=g_\parallel\,\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\qquad
N^1_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{1j}-g_J\,\hat n_i e_{2j},
\qquad
N^2_{ij}=g_I\,\hat n_i e_{2j}+g_J\,\hat n_i e_{1j}.
\]

These determine the witness triple
\[
W_\parallel,\qquad W_I,\qquad W_J,
\]
and hence the local Weyl determinant
\[
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3
W_\parallel
\bigl(W_I^2+W_J^2\bigr).
\]

Thus each valley is Weyl exactly when
\[
W_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
(W_I,W_J)\neq(0,0).
\]
Two opposite valleys form a local Dirac block exactly when, in the matched frame,
\[
(W_\parallel^-,W_I^-,W_J^-)=(W_\parallel^+,W_I^+,W_J^+).
\]

If, moreover, the remote sector satisfies
\[
\mathrm{spec}(H_R)\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing,
\qquad
V(0)=0,
\qquad
\|V(p)\|\le c|p|,
\]
and a mass-protection symmetry forbids a constant Dirac mass, then the full low-energy effective Hamiltonian is
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)=\Gamma^i(Mp)_i+O(|p|^2/\Delta),
\]
so the massless local Dirac cone is stable.

Therefore the true remaining microscopic frontier is finite and explicit:
compute the actual witness triples, prove opposite-valley existence, prove the remote gap, prove the little-group mismatch for the remote sector, and prove the final mass-protection symmetry.

\section{Final Summary}

The proof program is not fully complete, but the local shell-reduced fermion architecture is now essentially closed.

What has been established is the following conditional theorem chain:

\[
\text{BCC shell background}
\Longrightarrow
N^a_{ij}
\Longrightarrow
(W_\parallel,W_I,W_J)
\Longrightarrow
\text{Weyl criterion}
\Longrightarrow
\text{opposite-valley pairing}
\Longrightarrow
\text{local Dirac block}
\Longrightarrow
\text{spectral isolation}.
\]

In particular, the local Weyl determinant has been reduced to the explicit witness formula
\[
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3
W_\parallel
\bigl(W_I^2+W_J^2\bigr).
\]
Thus a shell crossing is Weyl exactly when
\[
W_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
(W_I,W_J)\neq(0,0).
\]

Likewise, local Dirac doubling is equivalent to equality of the positive- and negative-valley witness triples:
\[
(W_\parallel^-,W_I^-,W_J^-)=(W_\parallel^+,W_I^+,W_J^+).
\]

Finally, if the remote sector satisfies
\[
\mathrm{spec}(H_R)\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing,
\qquad
V(0)=0,
\qquad
\|V(p)\|\le c|p|,
\]
and if a mass-protection symmetry forbids a constant Dirac mass term, then the full low-energy effective Hamiltonian is
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)=\Gamma^i(Mp)_i+O(|p|^2/\Delta),
\]
so the local Dirac cone is stable.

Therefore, the remaining frontier is no longer conceptual.
It is the explicit microscopic verification of:
\[
\text{nonzero witnesses},\qquad
\text{opposite-valley existence},\qquad
\text{remote gap},\qquad
\text{mass protection}.
\]
