\section{How to Determine the Remaining Microscopic Inputs}

\subsection{Scope}

The remaining questions are no longer vague conceptual issues.
They are finite microscopic verification problems.

At the current stage, the shell-level Dirac problem has already been reduced to the following finite conditions:
\[
\widehat U(G_*)=0,
\qquad
v_\parallel\neq0,
\qquad
A_{G_*}\neq0,
\qquad
C^1\cdot G_*\neq0,
\qquad
C^2\cdot G_*\neq0,
\]
together with spectral isolation of the enriched shell block. \cite{TECT_Math8_shell_reduction}

Equivalently, in the more recent witness language, the local Weyl/Dirac problem is controlled by finite linear or polynomial functionals on the symmetry-allowed microscopic tensor space \(V\). \cite{TECT_Math9_genericity}

Thus the remaining tasks are:

\begin{enumerate}
\item determine whether the required witnesses are nonzero;
\item determine whether the opposite valley survives in the actual microscopic realization;
\item determine whether the enriched shell block is spectrally isolated from remote modes;
\item determine whether a residual symmetry forbids a constant Dirac mass.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{I. Nonvanishing of the witnesses}

\subsubsection{1. Tensor-space formulation}

Let \(V\) be the finite-dimensional real vector space of all symmetry-allowed microscopic enriched tensors
\[
N=\{N^a_{ij}\}
\]
surviving the microscopic linearization around the condensed TECT background.
For each shell-Dirac channel \(\alpha=1,2\), define the shell contraction
\begin{equation}
L_\alpha(N)=G_*^{\,j}e_i^\alpha N^{a_\alpha}_{ij}.
\label{eq:Lalpha_def}
\end{equation}
This is a linear functional on \(V\). \cite{TECT_Math9_genericity}

Hence the question
\[
L_\alpha(N)\neq 0?
\]
is not an infinite-dimensional PDE problem, but a finite-dimensional linear-algebra problem.

\subsubsection{2. Exact dichotomy}

Let \(W=\mathbb R^3_{\mathrm{out}}\otimes \mathbb R^3_{\mathrm{grad}}\otimes U_{\mathrm{int}}\) be the ambient tensor space and let
\[
V\subset W
\]
be the symmetry-allowed subspace.
Define the target covector
\begin{equation}
T^{(\alpha)}_{ija}=e_i^\alpha G_*^{\,j}u_a^{(\alpha)}.
\label{eq:Talpha_def}
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
L_\alpha(N)=\langle T^{(\alpha)},N\rangle .
\label{eq:Lalpha_pairing}
\end{equation}
If \(P_V:W\to V\) denotes the orthogonal projection onto the allowed tensor space, then
\begin{equation}
L_\alpha|_V \equiv 0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
P_VT^{(\alpha)}=0.
\label{eq:PVcriterion}
\end{equation}
Therefore exactly one of the following holds: \cite{TECT_Math9_genericity}

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(P_VT^{(\alpha)}=0\): the channel is symmetry-forbidden;
\item \(P_VT^{(\alpha)}\neq0\): the channel is generically nonvanishing on an open dense full-measure subset of \(V\).
\end{enumerate}

So the practical way to know whether the witness is nonzero is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Compute }P_VT^{(\alpha)}.
}
\end{equation}

If it is nonzero, the witness survives generically.

\subsubsection{3. Seed/witness test}

A stronger practical sufficient condition is:
if one can construct even a single symmetry-allowed tensor
\[
N^{(\alpha)}_{\mathrm{seed}}\in V
\]
such that
\begin{equation}
\langle T^{(\alpha)},N^{(\alpha)}_{\mathrm{seed}}\rangle \neq 0,
\label{eq:seedcriterion}
\end{equation}
then automatically
\[
P_VT^{(\alpha)}\neq0,
\]
so the channel is generically nonzero. \cite{TECT_Math9_genericity}

Therefore, to determine whether nature makes the witness nonzero, one should:

\begin{enumerate}
\item determine the residual symmetry group \(H\);
\item determine the allowed tensor space \(V\subset W\);
\item either compute \(P_VT^{(\alpha)}\) directly,
or construct one explicit admissible seed tensor with nonzero overlap.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{II. Existence of the opposite valley}

\subsubsection{1. What is already known}

At the shell level, the enriched Dirac block is built on the opposite shell pair
\[
\pm G_*,
\]
and the local shell space is
\[
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}.
\]
This is already the correct carrier space for shell-level Dirac emergence. \cite{TECT_Math8_shell_reduction,ChatGPT13_shell_reduction}

The shell-projected Dirac theorem requires that both opposite shell sectors contribute and that the projected block satisfy:
\[
B_0=0,\qquad
v_\parallel\neq0,\qquad
B(p)=p_1W^1+p_2W^2+O(|p|^2),
\]
with \(W^1,W^2\) nonzero, traceless, and independent after internal reduction. \cite{TECT_Math8_shell_reduction}

\subsubsection{2. What remains to be checked}

The remaining microscopic question is not whether opposite-valley doubling would produce Dirac if present; that is already closed.
The actual question is whether the microscopic linearization really leaves a nontrivial projected block at \(-G_*\) with matching reduced data.

In practice, this is checked by:

\begin{enumerate}
\item constructing the reduced shell block at \(+G_*\);
\item constructing the reduced shell block at \(-G_*\);
\item verifying that both survive projection;
\item verifying the matching relation between the two reduced blocks.
\end{enumerate}

In the witness formulation, this means computing the negative-valley witness triple and checking whether it matches the positive one.

\subsection{III. Remote-sector gap}

\subsubsection{1. What it means}

Let the full microscopic Hilbert space be decomposed as
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}=\mathcal H_D\oplus \mathcal H_R,
\]
where \(\mathcal H_D\) is the enriched local Dirac block and \(\mathcal H_R\) is the remote sector.
Spectral isolation means that the remote sector has a nonzero gap:
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{spec}(H_R)\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing
\qquad
\text{for some } \Delta>0.
\label{eq:remote_gap_def}
\end{equation}

This is precisely the remaining spectral-isolation problem identified in the current TECT program. \cite{TECT_Math8_shell_reduction,TECT_Math8_improved_model}

\subsubsection{2. How to determine it}

This is a concrete band/spectral calculation:

\begin{enumerate}
\item construct the full Bloch or shell-Bloch operator in a finite reciprocal-shell truncation;
\item isolate the enriched \(\pm G_*\) block;
\item diagonalize the complement;
\item determine whether all remote eigenvalues stay outside a fixed low-energy window.
\end{enumerate}

Equivalently, one can prove an operator inequality showing that all states orthogonal to the enriched shell block have energy bounded away from zero by \(\Delta\).

Thus:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{remote gap} = \text{finite-dimensional Bloch/spectral computation}.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{IV. Mass-protection symmetry}

\subsubsection{1. What it means}

Even if the local doubled shell block is Dirac and spectrally isolated, a constant Dirac mass term could in principle appear unless forbidden by symmetry.

So one must determine whether the reduced low-energy theory admits a residual symmetry (for example a grading or chiral-odd symmetry) excluding any constant mass operator.

\subsubsection{2. How to determine it}

This is a symmetry-classification problem:

\begin{enumerate}
\item identify the residual symmetry group acting on the reduced Dirac block;
\item classify all allowed constant Hermitian \(4\times 4\) operators;
\item determine whether a Dirac mass matrix belongs to the allowed symmetry class.
\end{enumerate}

If no symmetry-allowed constant mass matrix exists, then the Dirac cone is symmetry-protected.

Thus:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{mass protection} = \text{classification of symmetry-allowed constant operators}.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{V. Practical research order}

The optimal order of attack is now clear.

\paragraph{Step 1.}
Determine the actual symmetry-allowed tensor space \(V\) and compute
\[
P_VT^{(1)},\qquad P_VT^{(2)}.
\]
This answers whether the shell witnesses are generically nonzero.

\paragraph{Step 2.}
Construct one explicit seed tensor for each channel.
This immediately proves generic shell survival if the overlap is nonzero.

\paragraph{Step 3.}
Build the reduced shell blocks at \(+G_*\) and \(-G_*\), and compare their reduced coefficients or witness triples.

\paragraph{Step 4.}
Compute the remote spectrum and test whether a uniform gap \(\Delta>0\) exists.

\paragraph{Step 5.}
Classify the symmetry-allowed constant operators on the doubled block and determine whether a Dirac mass is forbidden.

\subsection{VI. Honest conclusion}

At the theorem level, the local shell-Dirac mechanism is already reduced to a finite problem. What remains is not a conceptual gap, but the explicit microscopic verification of four finite statements:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
P_VT^{(\alpha)}\neq0 \text{ for the required channels;}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the opposite shell valley survives projection;}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathrm{spec}(H_R)\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing;
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{no symmetry-allowed constant Dirac mass exists.}
}
\end{equation}

Once these are checked, the local Dirac part of TECT is effectively closed.

\section{Final Summary}

The remaining TECT microscopic questions are all finite verification problems.

First, the nonvanishing of the shell witnesses is controlled by the linear functionals
\[
L_\alpha(N)=G_*^{\,j}e_i^\alpha N^{a_\alpha}_{ij}.
\]
Let \(V\) be the symmetry-allowed tensor space and \(P_V\) the orthogonal projector onto \(V\). Then
\[
L_\alpha|_V\equiv0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
P_VT^{(\alpha)}=0.
\]
Hence a witness survives if and only if the corresponding projected target covector is nonzero. Equivalently, one may construct a single admissible seed tensor with nonzero overlap.

Second, opposite-valley existence is checked by constructing the reduced shell blocks at \(\pm G_*\) and verifying that both survive projection and match appropriately.

Third, the remote-sector gap is a Bloch/spectral calculation:
\[
\mathrm{spec}(H_R)\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing
\]
must be verified for the complement of the enriched shell block.

Fourth, mass protection is a symmetry-classification problem:
one must determine whether any constant symmetry-allowed Hermitian operator on the doubled block can serve as a Dirac mass. If none exists, the massless Dirac cone is protected.

Therefore the remaining frontier is fully explicit:
compute the projected witness covectors, verify opposite-valley survival, verify the remote gap, and classify the allowed constant operators on the local Dirac block.

\section{Residual Symmetry Calculation for the Witness Tensors}

Fix the BCC shell vector

\[
G_*=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,1,0).
\]

Define

\[
\hat n=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|},
\qquad
e_1=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,-1,0),
\qquad
e_2=(0,0,1).
\]

The stabilizer group of this axis inside the cubic group is

\[
H=C_{2v}.
\]

The microscopic tensor space is

\[
W=\mathbb R^3\otimes\mathbb R^3\otimes U_{\text{int}},
\qquad
U_{\text{int}}=\mathrm{span}(s_1,s_2,s_3).
\]

Imposing \(H\)-invariance gives the allowed tensor space

\[
N^3_{ij}=g_\parallel \hat n_i\hat n_j,
\]

\[
N^1_{ij}=g_I \hat n_i e_{1j}-g_J \hat n_i e_{2j},
\]

\[
N^2_{ij}=g_I \hat n_i e_{2j}+g_J \hat n_i e_{1j}.
\]

Hence

\[
\dim V=3.
\]

For the target tensors

\[
T^{(\alpha)}_{ija}=e_i^\alpha G_*^{\,j}u_a,
\]

the contractions give

\[
L_1(N)=|G_*|g_I,
\]

\[
L_2(N)=|G_*|g_J.
\]

Therefore

\[
P_VT^{(1)}\neq0,
\qquad
P_VT^{(2)}\neq0.
\]

Thus symmetry does not forbid the transverse witnesses.

Consequently

\[
\det M
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3
g_\parallel(g_I^2+g_J^2),
\]

and Weyl nodes are symmetry-allowed generically.

%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Remote-Sector Gap as a Finite Bloch-Spectral Problem}

\subsection{Goal}

The purpose of this section is to reduce the remaining remote-gap question
\[
\mathrm{spec}(H_R)\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing
\]
to a finite Bloch-spectral calculation and to give rigorous sufficient conditions guaranteeing \(\Delta>0\).

At the present stage, the local Dirac block is already reduced to a finite shell-enriched problem.
What remains is to show that all states outside this local Dirac block stay uniformly away from zero energy in a neighborhood of the selected shell crossing.

We do not yet compute a numerical value of \(\Delta\), because that depends on the final microscopic operator and on the chosen reciprocal-shell truncation.
What we do prove here is the exact finite-dimensional criterion and a rigorous lower-bound theorem.

\subsection{Bloch decomposition and block splitting}

Let \(H_{\mathrm{full}}(k)\) denote the Bloch-reduced microscopic Hamiltonian near a selected BCC shell pair \(\pm G_*\).
Fix a small shell-relative momentum \(p\) and write
\[
k=G_*+p.
\]

After reciprocal-shell truncation and internal reduction, the Bloch operator becomes a finite Hermitian matrix.
Choose the basis so that the local Dirac carrier appears first, and decompose the full Hilbert space as
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}=\mathcal H_D\oplus\mathcal H_R,
\]
where

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\mathcal H_D\) is the selected four-dimensional local Dirac block built from the opposite shell pair \(\pm G_*\),
\item \(\mathcal H_R\) is the orthogonal complement, called the remote sector.
\end{itemize}

Then
\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{full}}(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_D(p) & V(p)\\
V^\dagger(p) & H_R(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:block_split}
\end{equation}

The remote-gap problem concerns only the spectrum of \(H_R(p)\).

\subsection{Remote block as diagonal part plus residual mixing}

Write the remote block as
\begin{equation}
H_R(p)=D_R(p)+R_R(p),
\label{eq:HR_split}
\end{equation}
where:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(D_R(p)\) is the diagonal part in the chosen truncated reciprocal-shell basis,
\item \(R_R(p)\) is the off-diagonal remote-remote mixing.
\end{itemize}

Let the diagonal entries of \(D_R(p)\) be denoted by
\[
E_r^{(0)}(p),
\qquad
r\in\mathcal I_R,
\]
where \(\mathcal I_R\) indexes the remote basis states.

Define the unperturbed remote spectral distance from zero by
\begin{equation}
\delta_0(p):=
\min_{r\in\mathcal I_R}|E_r^{(0)}(p)|.
\label{eq:delta0_def}
\end{equation}
If
\[
\delta_0(p)>0,
\]
then the diagonal remote energies are already separated from zero before remote-remote mixing is included.

The question is whether the off-diagonal perturbation \(R_R(p)\) can close this gap.

\subsection{Basic operator-norm lower bound}

We now state the most elementary sufficient condition.

\begin{proposition}[Norm lower bound for the remote block]
Let \(H_R(p)=D_R(p)+R_R(p)\) with \(D_R(p)\) diagonal and Hermitian, and \(R_R(p)\) Hermitian.
If
\begin{equation}
\delta_0(p):=\min_r |E_r^{(0)}(p)| > \|R_R(p)\|,
\label{eq:norm_gap_condition}
\end{equation}
then
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{spec}(H_R(p))\cap
\bigl(-(\delta_0(p)-\|R_R(p)\|),\,\delta_0(p)-\|R_R(p)\|\bigr)
=
\varnothing.
\label{eq:norm_gap_result}
\end{equation}
In particular, the remote gap satisfies
\begin{equation}
\Delta(p)\ge \delta_0(p)-\|R_R(p)\|.
\label{eq:Delta_lower}
\end{equation}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
For any normalized vector \(\psi\in\mathcal H_R\),
\[
|\langle\psi,H_R(p)\psi\rangle|
=
|\langle\psi,D_R(p)\psi\rangle+\langle\psi,R_R(p)\psi\rangle|.
\]
Since \(D_R(p)\) is diagonal and every diagonal entry has absolute value at least \(\delta_0(p)\), one has
\[
|\langle\psi,D_R(p)\psi\rangle|\ge \delta_0(p).
\]
Also,
\[
|\langle\psi,R_R(p)\psi\rangle|\le \|R_R(p)\|.
\]
Therefore
\[
|\langle\psi,H_R(p)\psi\rangle|
\ge \delta_0(p)-\|R_R(p)\|.
\]
By the variational characterization of the spectrum of a Hermitian operator, every eigenvalue of \(H_R(p)\) has absolute value at least \(\delta_0(p)-\|R_R(p)\|\).
This proves \eqref{eq:norm_gap_result} and \eqref{eq:Delta_lower}.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Uniform remote gap in a momentum neighborhood}

To use this in the spectral-isolation theorem, one needs a gap uniform in \(p\) near the crossing.
Define a small neighborhood
\[
\mathcal N_\rho := \{p: |p|\le \rho\}.
\]
Set
\begin{equation}
\delta_{0,\rho}:=\inf_{|p|\le \rho}\delta_0(p),
\qquad
\eta_\rho:=\sup_{|p|\le \rho}\|R_R(p)\|.
\label{eq:uniform_delta_eta}
\end{equation}
Then:

\begin{theorem}[Uniform remote-gap criterion]
If
\begin{equation}
\delta_{0,\rho}>\eta_\rho,
\label{eq:uniform_gap_condition}
\end{equation}
then for all \(|p|\le \rho\),
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{spec}(H_R(p))\cap(-\Delta_\rho,\Delta_\rho)=\varnothing,
\qquad
\Delta_\rho:=\delta_{0,\rho}-\eta_\rho>0.
\label{eq:uniform_gap_result}
\end{equation}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Apply Proposition 1 pointwise in \(p\), then take the infimum over \(|p|\le \rho\).
\end{proof}

Thus the remote gap is reduced to the finite verification of the inequality
\[
\delta_{0,\rho}>\eta_\rho.
\]

\subsection{Gershgorin refinement}

In finite dimensions, one can also use Gershgorin discs.
Let the remote matrix entries be
\[
(H_R)_{rs}(p),
\qquad
r,s\in\mathcal I_R.
\]
Define the row radii
\begin{equation}
\mathcal R_r(p):=\sum_{s\neq r}|(H_R)_{rs}(p)|.
\label{eq:Gersh_radius}
\end{equation}
Then every eigenvalue of \(H_R(p)\) lies in the union of discs
\[
\bigcup_{r\in\mathcal I_R}
\left\{
z\in\mathbb C:\ |z-(H_R)_{rr}(p)|\le \mathcal R_r(p)
\right\}.
\]

Hence a sufficient condition for a positive remote gap is
\begin{equation}
\min_{r\in\mathcal I_R}
\left(
|(H_R)_{rr}(p)|-\mathcal R_r(p)
\right)>0.
\label{eq:Gersh_condition_pointwise}
\end{equation}
Uniformly on \(|p|\le \rho\), define
\begin{equation}
\Delta_{\mathrm{G},\rho}
:=
\inf_{|p|\le \rho}
\min_{r\in\mathcal I_R}
\left(
|(H_R)_{rr}(p)|-\mathcal R_r(p)
\right).
\label{eq:Gersh_uniform}
\end{equation}
If \(\Delta_{\mathrm{G},\rho}>0\), then
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{spec}(H_R(p))\cap(-\Delta_{\mathrm{G},\rho},\Delta_{\mathrm{G},\rho})=\varnothing
\qquad
(|p|\le \rho).
\label{eq:Gersh_gap_result}
\end{equation}

This is often more practical than the norm estimate because it uses the actual sparse matrix structure.

\subsection{Application to the shell-truncated Bloch operator}

In the TECT shell-reduced setting, remote states arise from:

\begin{enumerate}
\item higher-shell harmonics,
\item non-enriched shell states,
\item volumetric/longitudinal channels,
\item wrong internal sectors,
\item any remaining modes outside the selected \(\pm G_*\) enriched Dirac block.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the actual procedure is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item build the truncated Bloch matrix \(H_{\mathrm{full}}(p)\);
\item identify the four-dimensional local Dirac block \(\mathcal H_D\);
\item delete the rows and columns belonging to \(\mathcal H_D\), thereby obtaining \(H_R(p)\);
\item compute either
\[
\delta_{0,\rho},\qquad \eta_\rho
\]
or the Gershgorin lower bound \(\Delta_{\mathrm{G},\rho}\).
\end{enumerate}

Any positive lower bound obtained this way is a rigorous remote gap.

\subsection{Compatibility with the witness formulation}

The witness data determine only the local Dirac block \(H_D\), namely whether the selected two opposite valleys form a massless Dirac cone.
The remote gap is logically independent of the witness nonvanishing.

Thus the full local Dirac stability criterion separates into two independent finite verifications:

\paragraph{Local witness block:}
\[
W_\parallel^\pm\neq 0,
\qquad
(W_I^\pm,W_J^\pm)\neq(0,0),
\qquad
(W_\parallel^-,W_I^-,W_J^-)=(W_\parallel^+,W_I^+,W_J^+).
\]

\paragraph{Remote spectral block:}
\[
\Delta_\rho>0
\quad\text{or equivalently}\quad
\delta_{0,\rho}>\eta_\rho,
\]
or any equivalent rigorous lower bound such as \(\Delta_{\mathrm{G},\rho}>0\).

\subsection{Schur-complement consequence}

Once the remote gap is established, and once
\[
V(0)=0,
\qquad
\|V(p)\|\le c|p|
\]
also hold, the self-energy
\[
\Sigma(E,p)=V(p)(H_R(p)-E)^{-1}V^\dagger(p)
\]
satisfies
\[
\|\Sigma(E,p)\|\le \frac{2c^2}{\Delta_\rho}|p|^2
\qquad
(|E|<\Delta_\rho/2,\ |p|\le \rho).
\]
Therefore the effective low-energy Dirac Hamiltonian is
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)=\Gamma^i(Mp)_i+O(|p|^2/\Delta_\rho),
\]
as already proved in the spectral-isolation theorem.

Thus the remote-gap calculation is exactly the last nontrivial spectral ingredient needed for full local Dirac stability.

\subsection{Main theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Remote-gap reduction theorem]
Let \(H_{\mathrm{full}}(p)\) be a finite Hermitian Bloch truncation near a selected shell pair \(\pm G_*\), and let
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}=\mathcal H_D\oplus\mathcal H_R
\]
be the decomposition into the local Dirac block and the remote sector.
Assume the remote block is written as
\[
H_R(p)=D_R(p)+R_R(p),
\]
with \(D_R(p)\) diagonal and \(R_R(p)\) Hermitian.

Define
\[
\delta_{0,\rho}:=\inf_{|p|\le \rho}\min_r |E_r^{(0)}(p)|,
\qquad
\eta_\rho:=\sup_{|p|\le \rho}\|R_R(p)\|.
\]
If
\[
\delta_{0,\rho}>\eta_\rho,
\]
then the remote sector has a uniform gap
\[
\Delta_\rho:=\delta_{0,\rho}-\eta_\rho>0,
\]
namely
\[
\mathrm{spec}(H_R(p))\cap(-\Delta_\rho,\Delta_\rho)=\varnothing
\qquad
(|p|\le \rho).
\]
Hence the remote-gap problem is reduced to a finite matrix calculation.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Immediate from the uniform remote-gap criterion.
\end{proof}

\subsection{What is now proved and what remains open}

What is now rigorously established is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the remote-gap problem is a finite Hermitian-matrix problem on the remote block \(H_R(p)\);
\item a sufficient lower bound is
\[
\Delta_\rho\ge \delta_{0,\rho}-\eta_\rho;
\]
\item an alternative sufficient lower bound is given by Gershgorin discs;
\item once \(\Delta_\rho>0\) is obtained, the Schur-complement correction is \(O(|p|^2/\Delta_\rho)\).
\end{enumerate}

What remains open is not the form of the criterion, but its evaluation in the actual microscopic truncation chosen for TECT.
That requires building the concrete Bloch matrix and computing the corresponding lower bound.

\section{Final Summary}

The remote-sector gap is not a vague infinite-dimensional issue.
After shell truncation and block decomposition
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}=\mathcal H_D\oplus\mathcal H_R,
\]
it becomes a finite Hermitian-matrix problem for the remote block
\[
H_R(p).
\]

Writing
\[
H_R(p)=D_R(p)+R_R(p),
\]
with \(D_R(p)\) diagonal and \(R_R(p)\) the remote-remote mixing, define
\[
\delta_{0,\rho}:=\inf_{|p|\le \rho}\min_r |E_r^{(0)}(p)|,
\qquad
\eta_\rho:=\sup_{|p|\le \rho}\|R_R(p)\|.
\]
If
\[
\delta_{0,\rho}>\eta_\rho,
\]
then the remote sector has a uniform gap
\[
\Delta_\rho=\delta_{0,\rho}-\eta_\rho>0,
\]
so
\[
\mathrm{spec}(H_R(p))\cap(-\Delta_\rho,\Delta_\rho)=\varnothing
\qquad
(|p|\le \rho).
\]

Equivalently, one may use the Gershgorin lower bound
\[
\Delta_{\mathrm{G},\rho}
=
\inf_{|p|\le \rho}
\min_r
\left(
|(H_R)_{rr}(p)|-\sum_{s\neq r}|(H_R)_{rs}(p)|
\right).
\]
Any positive lower bound gives a rigorous remote gap.

Therefore the remote-gap problem has been fully reduced to a finite Bloch-spectral calculation on the remote block.
Once such a gap is established, the Schur-complement correction to the local Dirac block is only
\[
O(|p|^2/\Delta_\rho),
\]
which is exactly what is needed for full local Dirac stability.

\section{Mass-Protection Symmetry as a Finite Classification Problem}

\subsection{Goal}

The purpose of this section is to reduce the mass-protection question to a finite algebraic classification problem on the doubled local Dirac block.

The key question is:

\[
\text{Does there exist a symmetry-allowed constant Hermitian operator on the local doubled block that acts as a Dirac mass?}
\]

This is no longer an infinite-dimensional PDE problem.
Once the local shell-reduced doubled block has been identified, the problem reduces to classifying the allowed constant \(4\times4\) Hermitian operators under the residual low-energy symmetry.

\subsection{Local doubled Dirac block}

Assume that the positive and negative valleys have already been matched in the witness sense, so that the local doubled block is Dirac.
Then, to linear order, the effective Hamiltonian has the form
\begin{equation}
H_D(p)=\Gamma^i q_i,
\qquad
q_i=(Mp)_i,
\label{eq:Dirac_kinetic}
\end{equation}
where \(M\) is the \(3\times3\) real coefficient matrix determined by the common witness triple, and the \(4\times4\) Dirac matrices satisfy
\begin{equation}
\{\Gamma^i,\Gamma^j\}=2\delta^{ij}\mathbf 1_4,
\qquad
i,j=1,2,3.
\label{eq:Gamma_Clifford}
\end{equation}

The question is whether a constant Hermitian perturbation
\begin{equation}
\delta H = K
\label{eq:constant_K}
\end{equation}
is symmetry-allowed and, if so, whether it can gap the Dirac node.

\subsection{Classification of constant Hermitian operators}

Let \(\mathrm{Herm}(4)\) denote the real vector space of \(4\times4\) Hermitian matrices.
Its dimension is \(16\).
Choose a basis adapted to the Dirac structure:
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{Herm}(4)
=
\mathrm{span}
\left\{
\mathbf 1_4,\,
\Gamma^1,\Gamma^2,\Gamma^3,\,
\Gamma^{12},\Gamma^{23},\Gamma^{31},\,
\Gamma^5,\,
\Gamma^1\Gamma^5,\Gamma^2\Gamma^5,\Gamma^3\Gamma^5,\,
\Gamma^{123},\,
\Gamma^{12}\Gamma^5,\Gamma^{23}\Gamma^5,\Gamma^{31}\Gamma^5,\,
\Gamma^{123}\Gamma^5
\right\},
\label{eq:Herm4_basis}
\end{equation}
where
\[
\Gamma^{ij}:=\frac{i}{2}[\Gamma^i,\Gamma^j],
\qquad
\Gamma^{123}:=-i\Gamma^1\Gamma^2\Gamma^3,
\]
and \(\Gamma^5\) is an additional Hermitian matrix satisfying
\begin{equation}
\{\Gamma^5,\Gamma^i\}=0,
\qquad
(\Gamma^5)^2=\mathbf 1_4.
\label{eq:Gamma5_def}
\end{equation}

For the mass-protection problem, only a small part of this basis matters.

\subsection{Definition of a Dirac mass}

A constant Hermitian operator \(K\) is called a Dirac mass if
\begin{equation}
\{K,\Gamma^i\}=0
\qquad
\forall i=1,2,3,
\label{eq:mass_def}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
K^2=\mathbf 1_4
\quad\text{(up to positive rescaling).}
\label{eq:mass_norm}
\end{equation}
Then the perturbed Hamiltonian
\begin{equation}
H_D^{(m)}(p)=\Gamma^i q_i + mK
\label{eq:massive_Dirac}
\end{equation}
has spectrum
\begin{equation}
E_\pm(p)=\pm \sqrt{|q|^2+m^2},
\label{eq:massive_spectrum}
\end{equation}
and is therefore gapped at \(p=0\).

So the existence of a symmetry-allowed mass matrix is exactly the obstruction to mass protection.

\subsection{Existence of abstract mass matrices}

Without imposing any residual symmetry, a Dirac mass generically exists.
Indeed, by construction \(\Gamma^5\) satisfies
\[
\{\Gamma^5,\Gamma^i\}=0,
\]
so \(K=\Gamma^5\) is a valid Dirac mass matrix.

Therefore:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{mass protection never follows from the Dirac algebra alone;}
}
\end{equation}
it requires an additional symmetry that forbids the allowed mass matrices.

\subsection{Residual symmetry and allowed operator algebra}

Let \(G_{\mathrm{res}}\) be the residual low-energy symmetry group acting on the doubled local Dirac block.
A constant Hermitian operator \(K\) is symmetry-allowed if
\begin{equation}
U(g)KU(g)^{-1}=K
\qquad
\forall g\in G_{\mathrm{res}}.
\label{eq:sym_allowed_K}
\end{equation}
Define the symmetry-allowed operator algebra
\begin{equation}
\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}
:=
\{K\in \mathrm{Herm}(4)\;:\; U(g)KU(g)^{-1}=K\ \forall g\in G_{\mathrm{res}}\}.
\label{eq:Ares_def}
\end{equation}

Then the mass-protection question is equivalent to:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Does }\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}\text{ contain any }K\text{ satisfying }\{K,\Gamma^i\}=0\ ?
}
\label{eq:mass_question_finite}
\end{equation}

Thus the problem is finite and purely algebraic.

\subsection{Chiral/grading symmetry as a sufficient protection mechanism}

A particularly important sufficient condition is a grading or chiral symmetry.
Assume there exists a unitary Hermitian operator \(S\) on the doubled block such that
\begin{equation}
S^2=\mathbf 1_4,
\label{eq:S_square}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
S\Gamma^iS^{-1}=-\Gamma^i
\qquad
\forall i=1,2,3.
\label{eq:S_odd_kinetic}
\end{equation}
Then the kinetic Hamiltonian is odd under \(S\):
\[
S H_D(p) S^{-1}=-H_D(p).
\]

Suppose the low-energy theory is required to preserve this oddness condition, i.e. any allowed perturbation \(K\) must also satisfy
\begin{equation}
SKS^{-1}=-K.
\label{eq:S_odd_pert}
\end{equation}
Now let \(K_m\) be a candidate Dirac mass.
If it is even under \(S\), i.e.
\[
SK_mS^{-1}=+K_m,
\]
then it is forbidden.

Conversely, if all mass matrices are even under the grading while the symmetry only allows odd operators, then no Dirac mass is allowed.

This yields a sufficient criterion for mass protection.

\subsection{Mass-protection theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Finite mass-protection criterion]
Let
\[
H_D(p)=\Gamma^i q_i
\]
be the local doubled Dirac block.
Let \(G_{\mathrm{res}}\) be the residual low-energy symmetry group and
\[
\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}
=
\{K\in \mathrm{Herm}(4): U(g)KU(g)^{-1}=K\ \forall g\in G_{\mathrm{res}}\}
\]
the corresponding allowed constant operator algebra.

If
\begin{equation}
\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}
\cap
\{K\in \mathrm{Herm}(4): \{K,\Gamma^i\}=0\ \forall i\}
=
\{0\},
\label{eq:no_mass_intersection}
\end{equation}
then no symmetry-allowed constant Dirac mass exists, and the local Dirac cone is mass-protected.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Any constant gap-opening perturbation of Dirac type must be a Hermitian operator \(K\) anticommuting with all three kinetic gamma matrices.
If no such operator belongs to the allowed constant symmetry algebra \(\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}\), then no symmetry-allowed Dirac mass can be added.
Therefore the crossing remains massless under all symmetry-preserving constant perturbations.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Equivalent basis-level formulation}

Choose any finite basis
\[
\{B_A\}_{A=1}^{16}
\]
of \(\mathrm{Herm}(4)\).
Then every constant Hermitian perturbation can be written
\[
K=\sum_A c_A B_A.
\]
The mass-protection test can then be performed in two finite steps:

\paragraph{Step 1: symmetry filter.}
Solve the linear equations
\begin{equation}
U(g)KU(g)^{-1}=K
\qquad
\forall g\in G_{\mathrm{res}},
\label{eq:sym_filter}
\end{equation}
to determine the allowed subspace \(\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}\subset\mathrm{Herm}(4)\).

\paragraph{Step 2: mass filter.}
Inside that allowed subspace, solve the linear equations
\begin{equation}
\{K,\Gamma^i\}=0
\qquad
i=1,2,3.
\label{eq:mass_filter}
\end{equation}
If the only solution is \(K=0\), then the Dirac mass is symmetry-forbidden.

Thus mass protection is a finite system of linear equations on \(4\times4\) Hermitian matrices.

\subsection{Relation to the spectral-isolation theorem}

The spectral-isolation theorem already reduced full local Dirac stability to:

\begin{enumerate}
\item local witness-based Dirac formation;
\item remote-sector gap \(\Delta>0\);
\item zero constant mixing \(V(0)=0\);
\item linear mixing bound \(\|V(p)\|\le c|p|\);
\item absence of a symmetry-allowed constant mass.
\end{enumerate}

The present section closes the last item in the following sense:
the mass-protection question is exactly the finite algebraic problem \eqref{eq:no_mass_intersection}.

Hence the full local Dirac stability problem is now completely reduced to finite witness, spectral, intertwiner, and constant-operator classification checks.

\subsection{Practical research procedure}

To determine mass protection in the actual TECT realization, one should:

\begin{enumerate}
\item identify the actual residual low-energy symmetry group \(G_{\mathrm{res}}\) acting on the local doubled Dirac block;

\item construct its \(4\times4\) representation \(U(g)\);

\item solve the invariance equations
\[
U(g)KU(g)^{-1}=K
\]
to obtain the allowed constant operator algebra \(\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}\);

\item solve the anticommutation equations
\[
\{K,\Gamma^i\}=0
\]
inside \(\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}\);

\item conclude mass protection if and only if the only solution is \(K=0\).
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Main consequence}

Therefore the final unresolved mass question is not conceptual.
It is exactly the finite classification problem:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Classify the symmetry-allowed constant }4\times4\text{ Hermitian operators on }\mathcal H_D.
}
\end{equation}

If none of them is a Dirac mass, the local Dirac cone is protected.

\subsection{What is now proved and what remains open}

What is now rigorously established:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the existence of a mass matrix is a finite \(4\times4\) algebraic question;
\item mass protection is equivalent to the emptiness of the intersection
\[
\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}
\cap
\{K:\{K,\Gamma^i\}=0\};
\]
\item this is a finite linear classification problem once the residual symmetry representation is known.
\end{enumerate}

What remains open microscopically:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the actual residual symmetry \(G_{\mathrm{res}}\) of the TECT doubled block;
\item the explicit \(4\times4\) representation \(U(g)\);
\item the corresponding allowed constant operator algebra \(\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}\).
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

The mass-protection question for the local doubled Dirac block is a finite \(4\times4\) Hermitian-matrix classification problem.

Let the kinetic local Dirac Hamiltonian be
\[
H_D(p)=\Gamma^i q_i,
\qquad
\{\Gamma^i,\Gamma^j\}=2\delta^{ij}\mathbf 1_4.
\]
A constant Hermitian operator \(K\) is a Dirac mass precisely when
\[
\{K,\Gamma^i\}=0
\qquad
(i=1,2,3).
\]
Without additional symmetry, such a mass generally exists.

Let \(G_{\mathrm{res}}\) be the residual low-energy symmetry group acting on the doubled block, and let
\[
\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}
=
\{K\in\mathrm{Herm}(4): U(g)KU(g)^{-1}=K\ \forall g\in G_{\mathrm{res}}\}
\]
be the algebra of symmetry-allowed constant Hermitian operators.

Then the local Dirac cone is mass-protected if and only if
\[
\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}
\cap
\{K\in\mathrm{Herm}(4): \{K,\Gamma^i\}=0\ \forall i\}
=
\{0\}.
\]

Thus mass protection is not a vague dynamical question.
It is exactly the finite problem of classifying the symmetry-allowed constant \(4\times4\) Hermitian operators and checking whether any of them can serve as a Dirac mass.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Microscopic Verification Roadmap}

\subsection{Purpose}

The purpose of this roadmap is to compress the remaining microscopic verification tasks of TECT into a finite and operational research program.

At the present stage, the local shell-reduced fermion architecture is already reduced to the chain
\[
\text{BCC shell background}
\Longrightarrow
N^a_{ij}
\Longrightarrow
(W_\parallel,W_I,W_J)
\Longrightarrow
\text{Weyl}
\Longrightarrow
\text{Dirac pairing}
\Longrightarrow
\text{spectral isolation}.
\]

Therefore the remaining frontier is no longer conceptual.
It is the explicit verification of four finite microscopic statements:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(I) witness nonvanishing}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(II) opposite-valley survival and matching}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(III) remote-sector spectral gap}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(IV) mass-protection symmetry}
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Global logic}

The complete local Dirac stability theorem now has the form:

\begin{enumerate}
\item if the positive and negative shell valleys each satisfy the Weyl witness condition,
\item and if their witness triples match,
\item and if the remote sector is gapped,
\item and if constant mixing vanishes,
\item and if no symmetry-allowed constant Dirac mass exists,
\end{enumerate}
then the full low-energy theory contains a stable massless local Dirac cone.

Thus the remaining microscopic work is to verify the corresponding hypotheses one by one.

\subsection{Task I: witness nonvanishing}

\subsubsection{Statement}

The selected shell crossing is Weyl if
\[
W_\parallel^\pm\neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(W_I^\pm,W_J^\pm\bigr)\neq (0,0).
\]
Equivalently,
\[
\det M_\pm
=
(\kappa_*|G_*|)^3
W_\parallel^\pm
\Bigl(
(W_I^\pm)^2+(W_J^\pm)^2
\Bigr)\neq 0.
\]

\subsubsection{Input}

Let \(V\subset W\) be the symmetry-allowed microscopic tensor space, where
\[
W=\mathbb R^3_{\mathrm{out}}\otimes \mathbb R^3_{\mathrm{grad}}\otimes U_{\mathrm{int}}.
\]
For each required channel \(\alpha\), define the shell target covector
\[
T^{(\alpha)}_{ija}=e_i^\alpha G_*^{\,j}u_a^{(\alpha)}.
\]

\subsubsection{Operational test}

Compute the orthogonal projection
\[
P_VT^{(\alpha)}.
\]
Then:
\[
P_VT^{(\alpha)}=0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\text{the channel is symmetry-forbidden},
\]
whereas
\[
P_VT^{(\alpha)}\neq0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\text{the channel is generically nonzero on an open dense full-measure subset of }V.
\]

\subsubsection{Minimal deliverable}

For each required channel, produce one of the following:

\begin{enumerate}
\item an explicit computation of \(P_VT^{(\alpha)}\neq0\), or
\item one explicit admissible seed tensor \(N_{\mathrm{seed}}^{(\alpha)}\in V\) such that
\[
\langle T^{(\alpha)},N_{\mathrm{seed}}^{(\alpha)}\rangle\neq0.
\]
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection{Completion criterion}

Task I is complete once the necessary channels satisfy
\[
P_VT^{(\alpha)}\neq0.
\]

\subsection{Task II: opposite-valley survival and matching}

\subsubsection{Statement}

The two opposite shell valleys form a local Dirac block if the two witness triples satisfy
\[
(W_\parallel^-,W_I^-,W_J^-)
=
(W_\parallel^+,W_I^+,W_J^+).
\]
Equivalently,
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)
\]
in the matched opposite-valley frame.

\subsubsection{Input}

Construct the reduced shell blocks at
\[
+G_*
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
-G_*.
\]
Use the matched frame
\[
\hat n_-=-\hat n_+,
\qquad
e_1^-=e_1^+,
\qquad
e_2^-=-e_2^+.
\]

\subsubsection{Operational test}

Compute the witness triples
\[
(W_\parallel^+,W_I^+,W_J^+),
\qquad
(W_\parallel^-,W_I^-,W_J^-)
\]
from the two shell-projected first-order enriched operators.

\subsubsection{Minimal deliverable}

Show that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item both valleys survive the shell projection;
\item both valleys satisfy the Weyl nondegeneracy test;
\item the two witness triples are equal.
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection{Completion criterion}

Task II is complete once
\[
W_\parallel^- = W_\parallel^+,\qquad
W_I^- = W_I^+,\qquad
W_J^- = W_J^+.
\]

\subsection{Task III: remote-sector spectral gap}

\subsubsection{Statement}

Let
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}=\mathcal H_D\oplus\mathcal H_R,
\qquad
H_{\mathrm{full}}(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_D(p) & V(p)\\
V^\dagger(p) & H_R(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
The remote sector must satisfy
\[
\mathrm{spec}(H_R(p))\cap(-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing
\]
for some \(\Delta>0\) in a neighborhood of the crossing.

\subsubsection{Input}

Build a finite reciprocal-shell truncation of the Bloch-reduced microscopic Hamiltonian and isolate the remote block \(H_R(p)\).

\subsubsection{Operational test}

Write
\[
H_R(p)=D_R(p)+R_R(p),
\]
with \(D_R(p)\) diagonal and \(R_R(p)\) off-diagonal.
Define
\[
\delta_{0,\rho}:=
\inf_{|p|\le\rho}\min_r|E_r^{(0)}(p)|,
\qquad
\eta_\rho:=
\sup_{|p|\le\rho}\|R_R(p)\|.
\]
If
\[
\delta_{0,\rho}>\eta_\rho,
\]
then
\[
\Delta_\rho:=\delta_{0,\rho}-\eta_\rho>0
\]
is a rigorous remote gap.

Alternatively, use the Gershgorin lower bound
\[
\Delta_{\mathrm G,\rho}
=
\inf_{|p|\le\rho}
\min_r
\left(
|(H_R)_{rr}(p)|-\sum_{s\neq r}|(H_R)_{rs}(p)|
\right).
\]

\subsubsection{Minimal deliverable}

Produce one explicit positive lower bound:
\[
\Delta_\rho>0
\qquad\text{or}\qquad
\Delta_{\mathrm G,\rho}>0.
\]

\subsubsection{Completion criterion}

Task III is complete once a strictly positive remote-gap lower bound is proved.

\subsection{Task IV: mass-protection symmetry}

\subsubsection{Statement}

Let the local doubled block be
\[
H_D(p)=\Gamma^i q_i.
\]
A constant Hermitian matrix \(K\) is a Dirac mass if
\[
\{K,\Gamma^i\}=0
\qquad
(i=1,2,3).
\]
Mass protection means that no such mass matrix is symmetry-allowed.

\subsubsection{Input}

Let \(G_{\mathrm{res}}\) be the residual low-energy symmetry group acting on the doubled block, with representation \(U(g)\) on \(\mathbb C^4\).

\subsubsection{Operational test}

Define the allowed constant Hermitian operator algebra
\[
\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}
=
\{K\in\mathrm{Herm}(4):U(g)KU(g)^{-1}=K\ \forall g\in G_{\mathrm{res}}\}.
\]
Then solve the finite system
\[
\{K,\Gamma^i\}=0
\qquad
(i=1,2,3)
\]
inside \(\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}\).

\subsubsection{Minimal deliverable}

Show that
\[
\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}
\cap
\{K\in\mathrm{Herm}(4):\{K,\Gamma^i\}=0\ \forall i\}
=
\{0\}.
\]

\subsubsection{Completion criterion}

Task IV is complete once the only symmetry-allowed mass candidate is \(K=0\).

\subsection{Task V: zero constant mixing}

\subsubsection{Statement}

At the shell crossing \(p=0\), the inter-sector mixing must satisfy
\[
V(0)=0.
\]

\subsubsection{Input}

Let \(H_*\) be the shell-relevant residual symmetry at the crossing and let
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}=\mathcal H_D\oplus\mathcal H_R
\]
be the corresponding decomposition.

\subsubsection{Operational test}

Compute the shell-relevant irrep decomposition of \(\mathcal H_D\) and \(\mathcal H_R\).
If
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)=0,
\]
then
\[
V(0)=0.
\]

\subsubsection{Minimal deliverable}

Show that the local Dirac block and the remote sector share no common shell-relevant irreducible representation.

\subsubsection{Completion criterion}

Task V is complete once
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)=0
\]
is proved.

\subsection{Optimal order of execution}

The most efficient order is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Task I: compute \(P_VT^{(\alpha)}\) or construct explicit seed tensors;
\item Task II: compute and match the two witness triples at \(\pm G_*\);
\item Task V: verify the little-group mismatch and conclude \(V(0)=0\);
\item Task III: prove the remote gap \(\Delta>0\);
\item Task IV: classify the allowed constant \(4\times4\) Hermitian operators and exclude Dirac masses.
\end{enumerate}

This order is optimal because the first two steps confirm that the local shell-reduced Dirac block actually exists, while the last three certify its stability inside the full theory.

\subsection{Global completion theorem}

Once Tasks I--V are completed, the local fermion sector of TECT is effectively closed.

More precisely, one obtains:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{actual nonzero shell witnesses}
}
\end{equation}
\[
\Longrightarrow
\]
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{actual Weyl valleys at }\pm G_*
}
\end{equation}
\[
\Longrightarrow
\]
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{actual local Dirac doubling}
}
\end{equation}
\[
\Longrightarrow
\]
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{stable spectrally isolated massless local Dirac cone}
}
\end{equation}

Thus the remaining frontier is no longer conceptual.
It is a finite microscopic verification program.

\subsection{Honest final status}

At the present stage, the following are already closed:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the shell-reduced Weyl/Dirac architecture;
\item the witness formulation of local Weyl nondegeneracy;
\item the witness formulation of opposite-valley pairing;
\item the zero-mixing lemma;
\item the remote-gap reduction theorem;
\item the mass-protection reduction theorem.
\end{enumerate}

What remains open is the explicit completion of Tasks I--V in the final microscopic realization adopted for TECT.

%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Final Summary}

Fix the selected BCC shell axis
\[
\hat n=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,1,0),
\qquad
e_1=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,-1,0),
\qquad
e_2=(0,0,1).
\]
Under the shell-relevant residual symmetry
\[
H_{\mathrm{sh}}=SO(2)_\perp,
\]
the shell-relevant tensor space reduces to
\[
W_{\mathrm{sh}}
=
(\mathbb R\hat n)\otimes \mathbb R^3_{\mathrm{mom}}\otimes U_{\mathrm{int}},
\qquad
U_{\mathrm{int}}\cong \mathbf 2_s\oplus \mathbf 1_3.
\]

The symmetry-allowed invariant tensor space is exactly
\[
V=\mathrm{span}\{B_\parallel,B_I,B_J\},
\]
where
\[
(B_\parallel)^3_{ij}=\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\]
\[
(B_I)^1_{ij}=\hat n_i e_{1j},
\qquad
(B_I)^2_{ij}=\hat n_i e_{2j},
\]
\[
(B_J)^1_{ij}=-\hat n_i e_{2j},
\qquad
(B_J)^2_{ij}=\hat n_i e_{1j}.
\]
Hence
\[
\dim V=3.
\]

For the transverse target tensors
\[
T^{(1)}=B_I,
\qquad
T^{(2)}=B_J,
\]
their projections satisfy
\[
P_VT^{(1)}=T^{(1)}\neq0,
\qquad
P_VT^{(2)}=T^{(2)}\neq0.
\]
Likewise the longitudinal target also survives projection.

Therefore none of the three shell-enriched channels is symmetry-forbidden.
In particular, the two transverse witness channels are generically allowed by symmetry.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Task II: Explicit Comparison of the Positive and Negative Valley Witness Triples}

\subsection{Goal}

The purpose of this section is to complete Task II inside the minimal projector/enriched ansatz.

More precisely, we shall prove that in the minimal shell-enriched projector completion, the witness triples at the two opposite shell points \(+G_*\) and \(-G_*\) are identical once the natural matched opposite-valley frame is chosen.

Equivalently, we shall prove that the standard pairing relation
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)
\]
holds automatically in the minimal ansatz.

\subsection{Positive valley data}

Fix the positive shell point
\[
G_*,
\qquad
\hat n_+ := \frac{G_*}{|G_*|}=\hat n,
\]
with adapted right-handed frame
\begin{equation}
\{e_1^+,e_2^+,\hat n_+\}=\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\}.
\label{eq:plus_frame}
\end{equation}

The minimal shell-enriched tensor ansatz at the positive valley is
\begin{equation}
N_{ij}^{3(+)} = g_\parallel\, \hat n_i \hat n_j,
\label{eq:N3_plus}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N_{ij}^{1(+)} = g_I\, \hat n_i e_{1j} - g_J\, \hat n_i e_{2j},
\label{eq:N1_plus}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N_{ij}^{2(+)} = g_I\, \hat n_i e_{2j} + g_J\, \hat n_i e_{1j}.
\label{eq:N2_plus}
\end{equation}

Define the positive-valley witness functionals by
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel^+[N^+]
:=
\hat n_+^i \hat n_+^j N^{3(+)}_{ij},
\label{eq:Wpar_plus}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
W_I^+[N^+]
:=
\frac12
\left(
e_{1j}^+ \hat n_+^i N^{1(+)}_{ij}
+
e_{2j}^+ \hat n_+^i N^{2(+)}_{ij}
\right),
\label{eq:WI_plus}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
W_J^+[N^+]
:=
\frac12
\left(
e_{1j}^+ \hat n_+^i N^{2(+)}_{ij}
-
e_{2j}^+ \hat n_+^i N^{1(+)}_{ij}
\right).
\label{eq:WJ_plus}
\end{equation}

Substituting \eqref{eq:N3_plus}--\eqref{eq:N2_plus}, one finds immediately
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel^+ = g_\parallel,
\qquad
W_I^+ = g_I,
\qquad
W_J^+ = g_J.
\label{eq:plus_triple}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Matched opposite-valley frame}

Now consider the opposite shell point
\[
-G_*.
\]
Define
\[
\hat n_- := -\hat n.
\]
To keep the adapted frame right-handed, choose
\begin{equation}
e_1^- := e_1,
\qquad
e_2^- := -e_2,
\qquad
\hat n_- := -\hat n.
\label{eq:minus_frame}
\end{equation}
Indeed,
\[
e_1^- \times e_2^- = e_1\times(-e_2)=-(e_1\times e_2)=-\hat n=\hat n_-.
\]

This is the canonical matched frame used throughout the opposite-valley analysis.

\subsection{Negative valley minimal ansatz}

We now write the minimal projector/enriched tensor ansatz at the negative valley in its own adapted frame:
\begin{equation}
N_{ij}^{3(-)} = g_\parallel^-\, \hat n_i^- \hat n_j^-,
\label{eq:N3_minus_raw}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N_{ij}^{1(-)} = g_I^-\, \hat n_i^- e_{1j}^- - g_J^-\, \hat n_i^- e_{2j}^-,
\label{eq:N1_minus_raw}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N_{ij}^{2(-)} = g_I^-\, \hat n_i^- e_{2j}^- + g_J^-\, \hat n_i^- e_{1j}^-.
\label{eq:N2_minus_raw}
\end{equation}

Now substitute the matched frame \eqref{eq:minus_frame}:
\[
\hat n^-=-\hat n,\qquad e_1^-=e_1,\qquad e_2^-=-e_2.
\]
Then
\begin{equation}
N_{ij}^{3(-)}
=
g_\parallel^-\,(-\hat n_i)(-\hat n_j)
=
g_\parallel^-\,\hat n_i\hat n_j,
\label{eq:N3_minus_expanded}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N_{ij}^{1(-)}
=
g_I^-\,(-\hat n_i)e_{1j}
-
g_J^-\,(-\hat n_i)(-e_{2j})
=
-\,g_I^-\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
-
g_J^-\,\hat n_i e_{2j},
\label{eq:N1_minus_expanded}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
N_{ij}^{2(-)}
=
g_I^-\,(-\hat n_i)(-e_{2j})
+
g_J^-\,(-\hat n_i)e_{1j}
=
g_I^-\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
-
g_J^-\,\hat n_i e_{1j}.
\label{eq:N2_minus_expanded}
\end{equation}

This is simply the same invariant tensor pattern written in the common ambient basis \(\{e_1,e_2,\hat n\}\).

\subsection{Negative-valley witnesses}

Define the negative-valley witnesses by
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel^-[N^-]
:=
\hat n_-^i \hat n_-^j N^{3(-)}_{ij},
\label{eq:Wpar_minus}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
W_I^-[N^-]
:=
\frac12
\left(
e_{1j}^- \hat n_-^i N^{1(-)}_{ij}
+
e_{2j}^- \hat n_-^i N^{2(-)}_{ij}
\right),
\label{eq:WI_minus}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
W_J^-[N^-]
:=
\frac12
\left(
e_{1j}^- \hat n_-^i N^{2(-)}_{ij}
-
e_{2j}^- \hat n_-^i N^{1(-)}_{ij}
\right).
\label{eq:WJ_minus}
\end{equation}

We now compute them explicitly.

\paragraph{Longitudinal witness.}
Using \eqref{eq:N3_minus_expanded},
\[
\hat n_-^i \hat n_-^j N^{3(-)}_{ij}
=
(-\hat n_i)(-\hat n_j)\,
g_\parallel^- \hat n_i\hat n_j
=
g_\parallel^-.
\]
Hence
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel^- = g_\parallel^-.
\label{eq:Wpar_minus_eval}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Transverse-even witness.}
Using \eqref{eq:N1_minus_expanded} and \eqref{eq:N2_minus_expanded},
\[
e_{1j}^- \hat n_-^i N^{1(-)}_{ij}
=
e_{1j}(-\hat n_i)
\left(
-\,g_I^-\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
-
g_J^-\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
\right)
=
g_I^-,
\]
because
\[
(-\hat n_i)(-\hat n_i)=1,\qquad
e_1\cdot e_1=1,\qquad
e_1\cdot e_2=0.
\]
Likewise,
\[
e_{2j}^- \hat n_-^i N^{2(-)}_{ij}
=
(-e_{2j})(-\hat n_i)
\left(
g_I^-\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
-
g_J^-\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
\right)
=
g_I^-.
\]
Therefore
\begin{equation}
W_I^- = g_I^-.
\label{eq:WI_minus_eval}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Transverse-odd witness.}
Again,
\[
e_{1j}^- \hat n_-^i N^{2(-)}_{ij}
=
e_{1j}(-\hat n_i)
\left(
g_I^-\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
-
g_J^-\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
\right)
=
g_J^-,
\]
while
\[
e_{2j}^- \hat n_-^i N^{1(-)}_{ij}
=
(-e_{2j})(-\hat n_i)
\left(
-\,g_I^-\,\hat n_i e_{1j}
-
g_J^-\,\hat n_i e_{2j}
\right)
=
-\,g_J^-.
\]
Hence
\begin{equation}
W_J^- = g_J^-.
\label{eq:WJ_minus_eval}
\end{equation}

Thus the negative-valley witness triple is
\begin{equation}
W_\parallel^- = g_\parallel^-,
\qquad
W_I^- = g_I^-,
\qquad
W_J^- = g_J^-.
\label{eq:minus_triple}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Minimal ansatz equality of the two valleys}

In the minimal projector/enriched completion, the same microscopic coupling constants multiply the same three invariant channels at both opposite valleys.
This means precisely
\begin{equation}
g_\parallel^- = g_\parallel^+ =: g_\parallel,
\qquad
g_I^- = g_I^+ =: g_I,
\qquad
g_J^- = g_J^+ =: g_J.
\label{eq:g_matching}
\end{equation}

Combining \eqref{eq:plus_triple}, \eqref{eq:minus_triple}, and \eqref{eq:g_matching}, we obtain
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
W_\parallel^- = W_\parallel^+,\qquad
W_I^- = W_I^+,\qquad
W_J^- = W_J^+.
}
\label{eq:witness_matching_final}
\end{equation}

Thus the witness triples match automatically in the minimal ansatz.

\subsection{Consequence for the reduced Weyl blocks}

At the positive valley, the reduced block is
\begin{equation}
h_+(p)
=
\lambda_\parallel^+ p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
\alpha^+ (p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
\beta^+ (-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2),
\label{eq:hplus_again}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel^+ = \kappa_*|G_*|\,W_\parallel^+,
\qquad
\alpha^+ = \kappa_*|G_*|\,W_I^+,
\qquad
\beta^+ = -\kappa_*|G_*|\,W_J^+.
\label{eq:plus_coeffs_again}
\end{equation}

At the negative valley,
\begin{equation}
h_-(p)
=
\lambda_\parallel^- p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
\alpha^- (p_1\sigma^1+p_2\sigma^2)
+
\beta^- (-p_2\sigma^1+p_1\sigma^2)
+
O(|p|^2),
\label{eq:hminus_again}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel^- = \kappa_*|G_*|\,W_\parallel^-,
\qquad
\alpha^- = \kappa_*|G_*|\,W_I^-,
\qquad
\beta^- = -\kappa_*|G_*|\,W_J^-.
\label{eq:minus_coeffs_again}
\end{equation}

Using \eqref{eq:witness_matching_final}, we obtain
\[
\lambda_\parallel^-=\lambda_\parallel^+,\qquad
\alpha^-=\alpha^+,\qquad
\beta^-=\beta^+.
\]
Hence
\begin{equation}
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p).
\label{eq:pairing_auto}
\end{equation}

So the standard opposite-valley pairing relation is automatic in the minimal projector/enriched ansatz.

\subsection{Task II completion theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Completion of Task II in the minimal ansatz]
Consider the minimal shell-enriched projector completion with invariant coupling constants
\[
g_\parallel,\qquad g_I,\qquad g_J.
\]
Let the positive and negative shell points \(\pm G_*\) be equipped with the matched opposite-valley frame
\[
\hat n_-=-\hat n_+,\qquad
e_1^-=e_1^+,\qquad
e_2^-=-e_2^+.
\]
Then the corresponding witness triples satisfy
\[
W_\parallel^- = W_\parallel^+,\qquad
W_I^- = W_I^+,\qquad
W_J^- = W_J^+.
\]
Equivalently, the reduced shell Hamiltonians obey
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p).
\]
Thus opposite-valley witness matching is automatic in the minimal projector/enriched ansatz.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The positive-valley witness triple equals \((g_\parallel^+,g_I^+,g_J^+)\), and the negative-valley witness triple equals \((g_\parallel^-,g_I^-,g_J^-)\), by direct calculation. In the minimal ansatz the same invariant coupling constants appear at the two opposite valleys, so
\[
(g_\parallel^-,g_I^-,g_J^-)=(g_\parallel^+,g_I^+,g_J^+).
\]
Therefore the witness triples match. The equivalence with the pairing relation follows from the coefficient-witness dictionary for the reduced Weyl blocks.
\end{proof}

\subsection{What is proved and what remains open}

What is now rigorously established:

\begin{enumerate}
\item in the minimal projector/enriched completion, the negative-valley witness triple is computed explicitly;
\item in the matched opposite-valley frame, it equals the positive-valley witness triple;
\item therefore the local opposite-valley pairing condition is automatic in the minimal ansatz.
\end{enumerate}

What remains open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item whether the full microscopic TECT dynamics realizes precisely this minimal ansatz without extra symmetry-breaking corrections;
\item whether remote-sector gap and mass-protection conditions hold in the final microscopic model.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

For the minimal shell-enriched projector completion, the positive-valley enriched tensors are
\[
N_{ij}^{3(+)} = g_\parallel\, \hat n_i \hat n_j,
\qquad
N_{ij}^{1(+)} = g_I\, \hat n_i e_{1j} - g_J\, \hat n_i e_{2j},
\qquad
N_{ij}^{2(+)} = g_I\, \hat n_i e_{2j} + g_J\, \hat n_i e_{1j},
\]
and the corresponding witness triple is
\[
(W_\parallel^+,W_I^+,W_J^+)=(g_\parallel,g_I,g_J).
\]

At the negative valley, using the matched frame
\[
\hat n_-=-\hat n,\qquad
e_1^-=e_1,\qquad
e_2^-=-e_2,
\]
the minimal ansatz gives
\[
(W_\parallel^-,W_I^-,W_J^-)=(g_\parallel,g_I,g_J)
\]
as well.

Hence
\[
W_\parallel^- = W_\parallel^+,\qquad
W_I^- = W_I^+,\qquad
W_J^- = W_J^+.
\]
Therefore the reduced opposite-valley blocks satisfy
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p).
\]

So Task II is completed at the level of the minimal projector/enriched ansatz: opposite-valley witness matching, and hence local Dirac pairing, is automatic.

\section{Minimal Truncation Analysis for the Remote Gap and Zero Constant Mixing}

\subsection{Goal}

The purpose of this section is to treat Task III (remote-sector gap) and Task V (zero constant mixing) simultaneously in the first nontrivial finite truncation.

We construct a minimal shell-relevant truncated Hilbert space containing:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the local doubled Dirac block \(\mathcal H_D\),
\item a finite set of remote sectors carrying the nearest possible competing modes.
\end{enumerate}

We then prove:

\begin{enumerate}
\item sectorwise shell-relevant label mismatch implies
\[
V(0)=0;
\]
\item if the remote diagonal energies stay uniformly away from zero and remote-remote mixing is sufficiently small, then the remote sector has a uniform spectral gap
\[
\Delta>0.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Thus, within the minimal truncation, Tasks III and V reduce to explicit finite inequalities.

\subsection{Minimal truncated Hilbert space}

Let
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}^{\mathrm{tr}}
=
\mathcal H_D
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{vol}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{high}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{non\!-\!enr}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{wrong\!-\!int}}.
\label{eq:Hfull_trunc}
\]
Here:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(\mathcal H_D\cong \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\) is the local doubled shell Dirac carrier;
\item \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{vol}}\) is the volumetric/longitudinal sector;
\item \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{high}}\) is the higher-shell sector not belonging to the selected shell pair \(\pm G_*\);
\item \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{non\!-\!enr}}\) is the same-shell but non-enriched sector;
\item \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{wrong\!-\!int}}\) contains modes with internal content not matching the selected shell doublet.
\end{enumerate}

This is the smallest truncation in which all previously identified dangerous remote channels are explicitly present.

\subsection{Shell-relevant labels}

Associate to each sector the following shell-relevant labels:

\begin{enumerate}
\item shell-pair label,
\item transverse vs. volumetric character,
\item enriched internal-doublet label,
\item any additional grading/parity label carried by the local Dirac block.
\end{enumerate}

The local Dirac block \(\mathcal H_D\) carries the label set
\begin{equation}
\mathfrak L_D
=
(\pm G_*,\ \mathrm{transverse},\ \mathrm{enriched\ doublet},\ \mathrm{grading}_D).
\label{eq:LD}
\end{equation}

The remote sectors carry, respectively,
\begin{align}
\mathfrak L_{\mathrm{vol}}
&=
(\text{not shell-pair matched},\ \mathrm{volumetric},\ \cdots),
\label{eq:Lvol}
\\
\mathfrak L_{\mathrm{high}}
&=
(\text{different shell orbit},\ \cdots),
\label{eq:Lhigh}
\\
\mathfrak L_{\mathrm{non\!-\!enr}}
&=
(\pm G_*,\ \mathrm{transverse},\ \text{no enriched doublet},\ \cdots),
\label{eq:Lnon}
\\
\mathfrak L_{\mathrm{wrong\!-\!int}}
&=
(\pm G_*,\ \mathrm{transverse},\ \text{wrong internal irrep},\ \cdots).
\label{eq:Lwrong}
\end{align}

Each remote sector differs from \(\mathfrak L_D\) in at least one shell-relevant label.

\subsection{Zero constant mixing in the minimal truncation}

Let \(H_*\) denote the shell-relevant residual symmetry group at the crossing \(p=0\).
Assume the truncated Hamiltonian is \(H_*\)-equivariant at \(p=0\).
Then the constant inter-sector mixing satisfies
\[
V(0)\in \mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D),
\qquad
\mathcal H_R:=\mathcal H_{\mathrm{vol}}\oplus\mathcal H_{\mathrm{high}}\oplus\mathcal H_{\mathrm{non\!-\!enr}}\oplus\mathcal H_{\mathrm{wrong\!-\!int}}.
\]

We now verify sectorwise vanishing.

\begin{proposition}[Sectorwise zero constant mixing]
In the minimal truncation \eqref{eq:Hfull_trunc}, suppose each remote sector differs from \(\mathcal H_D\) in at least one shell-relevant label.
Then
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{vol}},\mathcal H_D)=0,
\qquad
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{high}},\mathcal H_D)=0,
\qquad
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{non\!-\!enr}},\mathcal H_D)=0,
\qquad
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{wrong\!-\!int}},\mathcal H_D)=0.
\label{eq:sectorwise_hom_zero}
\end{equation}
Consequently,
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)=0
\qquad\Longrightarrow\qquad
V(0)=0.
\label{eq:V0_mintrunc}
\end{equation}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
The full remote space is a direct sum of the four sectoral subspaces, hence
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)
\cong
\bigoplus_{a\in\{\mathrm{vol},\mathrm{high},\mathrm{non\!-\!enr},\mathrm{wrong\!-\!int}\}}
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_a,\mathcal H_D).
\]
If a sector \(\mathcal H_a\) differs from \(\mathcal H_D\) in at least one shell-relevant label, no nonzero \(H_*\)-intertwiner can preserve all labels simultaneously.
Hence each summand vanishes, and therefore the full Hom-space vanishes.
The implication \(V(0)=0\) then follows from \(H_*\)-equivariance of the truncated Hamiltonian.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Minimal truncated Hamiltonian}

Write the truncated Hamiltonian in block form
\begin{equation}
H_{\mathrm{full}}^{\mathrm{tr}}(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_D(p) & V_{D\!R}(p)\\
V_{D\!R}^\dagger(p) & H_R(p)
\end{pmatrix},
\label{eq:Htr_block}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
H_R(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_{\mathrm{vol}}(p) & R_{\mathrm{vol,high}}(p) & \cdots\\
R_{\mathrm{high,vol}}(p) & H_{\mathrm{high}}(p) & \cdots\\
\vdots & \vdots & \ddots
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:HR_blocks}
\end{equation}

At \(p=0\), the Dirac-remote mixing vanishes by Proposition 1:
\[
V_{D\!R}(0)=0.
\]
Thus the only spectral issue is whether the remote block \(H_R(p)\) can approach zero energy.

\subsection{Diagonal remote energies}

Let
\[
\mu_{\mathrm{vol}}(p),\qquad
\mu_{\mathrm{high}}(p),\qquad
\mu_{\mathrm{non\!-\!enr}}(p),\qquad
\mu_{\mathrm{wrong\!-\!int}}(p)
\]
denote the diagonal or sector-center energies of the four remote blocks.
Define the pointwise unperturbed remote distance from zero:
\begin{equation}
\delta_0(p):=
\min
\left\{
|\mu_{\mathrm{vol}}(p)|,\,
|\mu_{\mathrm{high}}(p)|,\,
|\mu_{\mathrm{non\!-\!enr}}(p)|,\,
|\mu_{\mathrm{wrong\!-\!int}}(p)|
\right\}.
\label{eq:delta0_mintrunc}
\end{equation}

Let
\begin{equation}
R_R(p):=H_R(p)-D_R(p),
\label{eq:RR_def}
\end{equation}
where \(D_R(p)\) is the block-diagonal or diagonal part containing the sector-center energies.
Then define
\begin{equation}
\eta_R(p):=\|R_R(p)\|.
\label{eq:etaR}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Pointwise remote-gap bound}

\begin{proposition}[Pointwise remote-gap bound in the minimal truncation]
If
\begin{equation}
\delta_0(p)>\eta_R(p),
\label{eq:point_gap_cond}
\end{equation}
then the remote sector has a positive pointwise gap
\begin{equation}
\Delta(p)\ge \delta_0(p)-\eta_R(p)>0.
\label{eq:point_gap_result}
\end{equation}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Apply the standard norm lower bound to the Hermitian remote matrix \(H_R(p)=D_R(p)+R_R(p)\).
Every diagonal remote eigenvalue is at least \(\delta_0(p)\) away from zero, and the total remote-remote mixing has norm at most \(\eta_R(p)\).
Thus no remote eigenvalue can enter the interval
\[
(-(\delta_0(p)-\eta_R(p)),\,\delta_0(p)-\eta_R(p)).
\]
\end{proof}

\subsection{Uniform local remote gap}

Fix a neighborhood
\[
\mathcal N_\rho:=\{p:|p|\le \rho\}.
\]
Define
\begin{equation}
\delta_{0,\rho}:=\inf_{|p|\le \rho}\delta_0(p),
\qquad
\eta_{R,\rho}:=\sup_{|p|\le \rho}\eta_R(p).
\label{eq:uniform_data}
\end{equation}

\begin{theorem}[Uniform remote gap in the minimal truncation]
If
\begin{equation}
\delta_{0,\rho}>\eta_{R,\rho},
\label{eq:uniform_gap_cond_min}
\end{equation}
then
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{spec}(H_R(p))\cap(-\Delta_\rho,\Delta_\rho)=\varnothing
\qquad
(|p|\le \rho),
\label{eq:uniform_gap_min}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\Delta_\rho:=\delta_{0,\rho}-\eta_{R,\rho}>0.
\label{eq:Delta_rho_min}
\end{equation}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Apply Proposition 2 pointwise and take the infimum over \(|p|\le \rho\).
\end{proof}

Thus the remote-gap problem in the minimal truncation is reduced to the finite inequality \eqref{eq:uniform_gap_cond_min}.

\subsection{Gershgorin alternative}

Let the remote matrix entries be \((H_R)_{ab}(p)\) in any fixed basis of \(\mathcal H_R\).
Define the row sums
\begin{equation}
\mathcal R_a(p):=\sum_{b\neq a}|(H_R)_{ab}(p)|.
\label{eq:Gersh_row}
\end{equation}
Then a sufficient condition for a remote gap is
\begin{equation}
\min_a\left(|(H_R)_{aa}(p)|-\mathcal R_a(p)\right)>0.
\label{eq:Gersh_point}
\end{equation}
Uniformly on \(|p|\le \rho\), define
\begin{equation}
\Delta_{\mathrm G,\rho}
:=
\inf_{|p|\le \rho}
\min_a\left(|(H_R)_{aa}(p)|-\mathcal R_a(p)\right).
\label{eq:Gersh_uniform_min}
\end{equation}
If \(\Delta_{\mathrm G,\rho}>0\), then
\[
\mathrm{spec}(H_R(p))\cap(-\Delta_{\mathrm G,\rho},\Delta_{\mathrm G,\rho})=\varnothing
\qquad
(|p|\le \rho).
\]

This is often easier to check in a sparse minimal truncation.

\subsection{Consequence for Schur complement}

Suppose, in addition to Theorem 1, that the linear Dirac-remote mixing obeys
\begin{equation}
\|V_{D\!R}(p)\|\le c|p|.
\label{eq:linear_mix_min}
\end{equation}
Then for \(|E|<\Delta_\rho/2\),
\[
(H_R(p)-E)^{-1}
\]
exists and
\[
\|(H_R(p)-E)^{-1}\|\le \frac{2}{\Delta_\rho}.
\]
Hence the self-energy correction satisfies
\begin{equation}
\Sigma(E,p)
=
V_{D\!R}(p)(H_R(p)-E)^{-1}V_{D\!R}^\dagger(p)
=
O(|p|^2/\Delta_\rho).
\label{eq:self_energy_min}
\end{equation}
Therefore the local Dirac block is stable up to quadratic corrections.

\subsection{Combined Task III + Task V theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Minimal-truncation completion of Tasks III and V]
Consider the minimal truncated Hilbert space
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}^{\mathrm{tr}}
=
\mathcal H_D
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{vol}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{high}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{non\!-\!enr}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{wrong\!-\!int}}.
\]
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item each remote sector differs from \(\mathcal H_D\) in at least one shell-relevant label;
\item the truncated Hamiltonian is shell-relevant symmetry-equivariant at \(p=0\);
\item the uniform remote-gap inequality
\[
\delta_{0,\rho}>\eta_{R,\rho}
\]
holds in a neighborhood \(|p|\le \rho\);
\item the Dirac-remote mixing satisfies
\[
\|V_{D\!R}(p)\|\le c|p|.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the constant Dirac-remote mixing vanishes,
\[
V_{D\!R}(0)=0;
\]

\item the remote sector has a uniform gap
\[
\Delta_\rho=\delta_{0,\rho}-\eta_{R,\rho}>0;
\]

\item the Schur-complement correction to the local Dirac block is
\[
\Sigma(E,p)=O(|p|^2/\Delta_\rho).
\]
\end{enumerate}

Thus, in the minimal truncation, Tasks III and V are completed once the finite inequalities above are checked.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Item (1) follows from Proposition 1. Item (2) follows from Theorem 1. Item (3) follows from the resolvent bound and the quadratic self-energy estimate.
\end{proof}

\subsection{What is proved and what remains open}

What is now rigorously established:

\begin{enumerate}
\item in the minimal truncation, the zero constant mixing statement is reduced to sectorwise shell-label mismatch;
\item the remote gap is reduced to the finite inequality
\[
\delta_{0,\rho}>\eta_{R,\rho};
\]
\item once this holds, the Schur-complement correction is only \(O(|p|^2/\Delta_\rho)\).
\end{enumerate}

What remains open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the actual numerical or analytic evaluation of the remote energies \(\mu_a(p)\) in the final microscopic truncation;
\item the actual value of the lower bound \(\Delta_\rho\);
\item the final mass-protection symmetry classification.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

In the first nontrivial truncation,
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}^{\mathrm{tr}}
=
\mathcal H_D
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{vol}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{high}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{non\!-\!enr}}
\oplus
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{wrong\!-\!int}},
\]
each remote sector differs from the local Dirac block \(\mathcal H_D\) in at least one shell-relevant label.

Therefore, if the truncated Hamiltonian is shell-relevant symmetry-equivariant at \(p=0\), then
\[
\mathrm{Hom}_{H_*}(\mathcal H_R,\mathcal H_D)=0
\qquad\Longrightarrow\qquad
V_{D\!R}(0)=0.
\]

Writing the remote block as
\[
H_R(p)=D_R(p)+R_R(p),
\]
and defining
\[
\delta_{0,\rho}:=\inf_{|p|\le \rho}\min_a |\mu_a(p)|,
\qquad
\eta_{R,\rho}:=\sup_{|p|\le \rho}\|R_R(p)\|,
\]
one obtains a uniform remote gap whenever
\[
\delta_{0,\rho}>\eta_{R,\rho}.
\]
In that case
\[
\Delta_\rho=\delta_{0,\rho}-\eta_{R,\rho}>0,
\]
and hence
\[
\mathrm{spec}(H_R(p))\cap(-\Delta_\rho,\Delta_\rho)=\varnothing
\qquad
(|p|\le \rho).
\]

If moreover
\[
\|V_{D\!R}(p)\|\le c|p|,
\]
then the Schur-complement self-energy is
\[
\Sigma(E,p)=O(|p|^2/\Delta_\rho).
\]

Thus, in the minimal truncation, zero constant mixing and remote spectral isolation are both reduced to explicit finite checks.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Task IV: Explicit Classification of Constant Hermitian Operators on the Minimal Doubled Dirac Block}

\subsection{Goal}

The purpose of this section is to complete Task IV for the minimal doubled Dirac ansatz.

We will:

\begin{enumerate}
\item choose an explicit representation of the local doubled Dirac block;
\item classify all constant Hermitian \(4\times4\) operators;
\item determine exactly which of them are Dirac masses;
\item show that the minimal doubled Dirac algebra alone does \emph{not} forbid mass terms;
\item identify a sufficient residual symmetry that forbids all mass matrices.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the mass-protection problem is reduced to an explicit finite classification problem.

\subsection{Minimal doubled Dirac representation}

After opposite-valley matching, the local doubled block is
\[
H_D(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p).
\]
To linear order, after the linear change of variables \(q_i=(Mp)_i\), this is unitarily equivalent to
\begin{equation}
H_D(q)=\rho^3\otimes (q_1\sigma^1+q_2\sigma^2+q_3\sigma^3).
\label{eq:HD_min}
\end{equation}
Here:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\rho^a\) are Pauli matrices acting on the valley-pair index \((+,-)\),
\item \(\sigma^a\) are Pauli matrices acting on the internal two-component Weyl spinor.
\end{itemize}

Thus the three kinetic gamma matrices may be taken as
\begin{equation}
\Gamma^i=\rho^3\otimes \sigma^i,
\qquad
i=1,2,3.
\label{eq:Gamma_min}
\end{equation}
They satisfy
\begin{equation}
\{\Gamma^i,\Gamma^j\}=2\delta^{ij}\mathbf 1_4.
\label{eq:Gamma_Clifford_min}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Full basis of constant Hermitian operators}

Every constant Hermitian \(4\times4\) matrix can be expanded in the basis
\begin{equation}
K=\sum_{a=0}^3\sum_{b=0}^3 c_{ab}\,\rho^a\otimes \sigma^b,
\qquad
c_{ab}\in \mathbb R,
\label{eq:K_expand}
\end{equation}
where
\[
\rho^0=\mathbf 1_2,
\qquad
\sigma^0=\mathbf 1_2.
\]
Hence
\[
\dim_{\mathbb R}\mathrm{Herm}(4)=16.
\]

Our task is to determine which such \(K\) satisfy the Dirac-mass condition
\begin{equation}
\{K,\Gamma^i\}=0
\qquad
(i=1,2,3).
\label{eq:mass_condition}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Mass-space classification}

Let
\[
K=\rho^a\otimes \sigma^b
\]
be a basis element.
We compute
\[
\{ \rho^a\otimes \sigma^b,\ \rho^3\otimes \sigma^i\}
=
\rho^a\rho^3\otimes \sigma^b\sigma^i
+
\rho^3\rho^a\otimes \sigma^i\sigma^b.
\]
There are two qualitatively distinct possibilities:

\paragraph{Case 1: \(\rho^a\) commutes with \(\rho^3\).}
Then \(a=0\) or \(a=3\), and the anticommutator becomes
\[
\rho^3\rho^a \otimes \{\sigma^b,\sigma^i\}.
\]
For this to vanish for all \(i=1,2,3\), one would need a \(2\times2\) matrix \(\sigma^b\) anticommuting with all three Pauli matrices simultaneously.
No such nonzero matrix exists.
Therefore no mass arises from \(a=0,3\).

\paragraph{Case 2: \(\rho^a\) anticommutes with \(\rho^3\).}
Then \(a=1\) or \(a=2\), and the anticommutator becomes
\[
\rho^a\rho^3\otimes [\sigma^b,\sigma^i].
\]
For this to vanish for all \(i=1,2,3\), one needs \(\sigma^b\) to commute with all three Pauli matrices.
Hence \(\sigma^b\) must be proportional to the identity, i.e. \(b=0\).

Therefore the only nonzero Hermitian matrices anticommuting with all three kinetic gamma matrices are
\begin{equation}
\rho^1\otimes \mathbf 1_2,
\qquad
\rho^2\otimes \mathbf 1_2.
\label{eq:mass_basis}
\end{equation}

We summarize this in the following theorem.

\begin{theorem}[Mass-space classification for the minimal doubled Dirac block]
For the minimal doubled Dirac Hamiltonian
\[
H_D(q)=\rho^3\otimes(q_i\sigma^i),
\]
the real vector space of constant Hermitian Dirac masses is exactly
\begin{equation}
\mathcal M
=
\mathrm{span}_{\mathbb R}
\{
\rho^1\otimes \mathbf 1_2,\,
\rho^2\otimes \mathbf 1_2
\}.
\label{eq:mass_space}
\end{equation}
In particular,
\[
\dim_{\mathbb R}\mathcal M = 2.
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The above case-by-case analysis shows that only \(a=1,2\) and \(b=0\) solve
\[
\{K,\rho^3\otimes \sigma^i\}=0
\qquad
(i=1,2,3).
\]
Hence the mass space is exactly \eqref{eq:mass_space}.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Consequence: no automatic mass protection}

Because \(\mathcal M\neq\{0\}\), the local Dirac algebra by itself does not protect the massless node.
Indeed, for any nonzero real pair \((m_1,m_2)\), the operator
\begin{equation}
K_m = m_1\,\rho^1\otimes \mathbf 1_2 + m_2\,\rho^2\otimes \mathbf 1_2
\label{eq:Km_general}
\end{equation}
is a valid constant mass perturbation.
The perturbed Hamiltonian
\begin{equation}
H_D^{(m)}(q)
=
\rho^3\otimes(q_i\sigma^i)
+
m_1\,\rho^1\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
m_2\,\rho^2\otimes \mathbf 1_2
\label{eq:HD_massive}
\end{equation}
has spectrum
\begin{equation}
E_\pm(q)
=
\pm \sqrt{|q|^2+m_1^2+m_2^2},
\label{eq:massive_spectrum_explicit}
\end{equation}
so it is gapped at \(q=0\).

Hence:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the minimal doubled Dirac block alone does not forbid a mass.}
}
\label{eq:no_auto_protection}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Valley-charge symmetry as a sufficient protection mechanism}

We now identify a natural residual symmetry that forbids all such masses.

Define the valley-charge generator
\begin{equation}
Q_v:=\rho^3\otimes \mathbf 1_2.
\label{eq:Qv}
\end{equation}
Consider the unitary valley \(U(1)\) symmetry
\begin{equation}
U_v(\theta)=e^{i\theta Q_v}
=
e^{i\theta \rho^3}\otimes \mathbf 1_2.
\label{eq:Uv}
\end{equation}
The kinetic Hamiltonian \eqref{eq:HD_min} is invariant under this symmetry because
\begin{equation}
[Q_v,\rho^3\otimes \sigma^i]=0
\qquad
(i=1,2,3).
\label{eq:Qv_commute_kinetic}
\end{equation}

Now examine the two mass generators.
Using Pauli-matrix algebra,
\begin{align}
U_v(\theta)\,(\rho^1\otimes \mathbf 1)\,U_v(\theta)^{-1}
&=
(\cos 2\theta)\,\rho^1\otimes \mathbf 1
+
(\sin 2\theta)\,\rho^2\otimes \mathbf 1,
\label{eq:rho1_rotate}
\\
U_v(\theta)\,(\rho^2\otimes \mathbf 1)\,U_v(\theta)^{-1}
&=
-(\sin 2\theta)\,\rho^1\otimes \mathbf 1
+
(\cos 2\theta)\,\rho^2\otimes \mathbf 1.
\label{eq:rho2_rotate}
\end{align}
Thus the mass space \(\mathcal M\) transforms as a nontrivial doublet under valley \(U(1)\), rather than as an invariant scalar.

Therefore no nonzero element of \(\mathcal M\) is invariant under all \(\theta\), and we obtain:
\begin{equation}
\mathcal A_{U(1)_v}\cap \mathcal M = \{0\},
\label{eq:Ares_mass_zero}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\mathcal A_{U(1)_v}
:=
\{K\in \mathrm{Herm}(4): U_v(\theta)KU_v(\theta)^{-1}=K\ \forall \theta\}.
\label{eq:Auv}
\end{equation}

\begin{theorem}[Valley \(U(1)\) mass protection]
If the doubled local Dirac block preserves valley-charge \(U(1)\) generated by \(Q_v=\rho^3\otimes\mathbf 1_2\), then every constant Dirac mass is forbidden.
Equivalently,
\[
\mathcal A_{U(1)_v}\cap \mathcal M=\{0\}.
\]
Hence the local Dirac node is mass-protected against all constant symmetry-preserving perturbations.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The kinetic Hamiltonian commutes with \(Q_v\), so the symmetry is compatible with the local Dirac block.
The mass generators \(\rho^1\otimes \mathbf 1\) and \(\rho^2\otimes \mathbf 1\) rotate nontrivially under \(U_v(\theta)\), by \eqref{eq:rho1_rotate} and \eqref{eq:rho2_rotate}.
Therefore no nonzero linear combination of them is invariant under all \(\theta\).
Hence no constant Dirac mass belongs to the allowed symmetry algebra.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Equivalent finite classification of the allowed constant algebra}

For completeness, let us classify the constant Hermitian operators invariant under valley \(U(1)\).

A basis element \(\rho^a\otimes \sigma^b\) is \(U(1)_v\)-invariant iff it commutes with \(Q_v=\rho^3\otimes\mathbf 1\).
Hence only \(a=0\) and \(a=3\) survive.
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\mathcal A_{U(1)_v}
=
\mathrm{span}_{\mathbb R}
\{
\mathbf 1\otimes \mathbf 1,\,
\mathbf 1\otimes \sigma^1,\,
\mathbf 1\otimes \sigma^2,\,
\mathbf 1\otimes \sigma^3,\,
\rho^3\otimes \mathbf 1,\,
\rho^3\otimes \sigma^1,\,
\rho^3\otimes \sigma^2,\,
\rho^3\otimes \sigma^3
\}.
\label{eq:Au1v_classification}
\end{equation}
This is an \(8\)-dimensional real algebra.
Its intersection with the mass space \(\mathcal M\) is clearly zero.

\subsection{Interpretation}

The two mass matrices
\[
\rho^1\otimes \mathbf 1,
\qquad
\rho^2\otimes \mathbf 1
\]
are precisely intervalley constant mixing terms.
They convert the \(+\) and \(-\) valley sectors into one another.
Thus valley-charge conservation forbids them exactly because it forbids constant intervalley mixing.

This is the cleanest finite interpretation of mass protection in the minimal doubled Dirac ansatz.

\subsection{General finite mass-protection criterion}

We now state the final criterion in the present explicit basis.

\begin{theorem}[Explicit Task IV completion criterion]
For the minimal doubled local Dirac block
\[
H_D(q)=\rho^3\otimes(q_i\sigma^i),
\]
the constant Dirac mass space is
\[
\mathcal M
=
\mathrm{span}_{\mathbb R}
\{
\rho^1\otimes \mathbf 1,\,
\rho^2\otimes \mathbf 1
\}.
\]
Hence Task IV is completed once one proves that the residual symmetry-allowed constant algebra \(\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}\) satisfies
\begin{equation}
\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}
\cap
\mathrm{span}_{\mathbb R}
\{
\rho^1\otimes \mathbf 1,\,
\rho^2\otimes \mathbf 1
\}
=
\{0\}.
\label{eq:TaskIV_explicit}
\end{equation}
A sufficient condition is preservation of valley-charge \(U(1)\).
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The mass-space classification theorem gives the full list of possible constant Dirac masses.
Therefore mass protection is equivalent to excluding these two matrices from the allowed constant algebra.
The valley \(U(1)\) theorem provides one explicit sufficient mechanism.
\end{proof}

\subsection{What is proved and what remains open}

What is now rigorously established:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the constant mass space of the minimal doubled Dirac block is exactly two-dimensional;
\item without extra symmetry, mass terms are allowed;
\item valley-charge \(U(1)\) forbids all constant masses;
\item Task IV is reduced to checking whether the actual residual symmetry algebra excludes \(\rho^1\otimes\mathbf 1\) and \(\rho^2\otimes\mathbf 1\).
\end{enumerate}

What remains open microscopically:

\begin{enumerate}
\item whether the final TECT microscopic realization indeed preserves valley-charge \(U(1)\) or an equivalent symmetry;
\item whether additional symmetry-breaking terms generate intervalley constant operators;
\item whether the final allowed constant algebra \(\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}\) coincides with or is contained in \(\mathcal A_{U(1)_v}\).
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

For the minimal doubled local Dirac block
\[
H_D(q)=\rho^3\otimes(q_1\sigma^1+q_2\sigma^2+q_3\sigma^3),
\]
every constant Hermitian \(4\times4\) operator can be expanded in the basis
\[
\rho^a\otimes \sigma^b,
\qquad
a,b=0,1,2,3.
\]

The constant Hermitian operators that anticommute with all three kinetic gamma matrices
\[
\Gamma^i=\rho^3\otimes \sigma^i
\]
form the two-dimensional mass space
\[
\mathcal M
=
\mathrm{span}_{\mathbb R}
\{
\rho^1\otimes \mathbf 1,\,
\rho^2\otimes \mathbf 1
\}.
\]

Therefore the minimal doubled Dirac algebra alone does not protect the massless node: without extra symmetry, a constant Dirac mass is allowed.

However, if the residual symmetry includes valley-charge \(U(1)\) generated by
\[
Q_v=\rho^3\otimes \mathbf 1,
\]
then the two mass generators transform nontrivially and are not symmetry-invariant.
Hence
\[
\mathcal A_{U(1)_v}\cap \mathcal M=\{0\},
\]
so all constant Dirac masses are forbidden.

Thus Task IV is reduced to the explicit finite check:
determine the actual residual constant symmetry algebra \(\mathcal A_{\mathrm{res}}\) and verify whether it excludes
\[
\rho^1\otimes \mathbf 1,
\qquad
\rho^2\otimes \mathbf 1.
\]
A sufficient condition is preservation of valley-charge \(U(1)\).
